Q1. All planets move in elliptical orbits with the Sun situated at one of the foci of the ellipse. This law was given by
    (A) Maxwell
    (B) Newton
    (C) Einstein
    (D) Kepler   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: In the 17th century, Johannes Kepler discovered that the orbits along which the planets travel around the Sun are ellipses with the Sun at one focus, and described this in his first law of planetary motion.

Q2. According to Kepler‚Äôs Law of areas:
    (A) Only the line that joins earth to the sun sweeps equal areas in equal intervals of time
    (B) The line that joins any planet to the sun sweeps equal areas in equal intervals of time   <-- CORRECT
    (C) Only the line that joins mars to the earth sweeps equal areas in equal intervals of time
    (D) The line that joins any planet to the earth sweeps equal areas in equal intervals of time
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Kepler law of areas describes the speed of a planet travelling in an elliptical orbit around the sun. A planet‚Äôs orbital speed changes, depending on how far it is from the Sun. The closer a planet is to the Sun, the stronger the Sun‚Äôs gravitational pull on it, and the faster the planet moves. The farther it is from the Sun, the weaker the Sun‚Äôs gravitational pull, and the slower it moves in its orbit. So, the statement of Kepler's law of area is "The line joining the sun to the planet sweeps out equal areas in equal interval of time". i.e. areal velocity is constant.

Q3. According to Kepler‚Äôs Law of periods, the ________ of the time period of revolution of a planet is proportional to the cube of the _________ of the ellipse traced out by the planet.
    (A) cube, semi-major axis
    (B) square, semi-minor axis
    (C) square, semi-major axis   <-- CORRECT
    (D) cube, semi-minor axis
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Kepler's 3^rd Law: T^2 = a^3. Kepler's 3rd law is a mathematical formula. It means that if you know the period of a planet's orbit (T = how long it takes the planet to go around the Sun), then you can determine that planet's distance from the Sun (a is the length of the semimajor axis of the planet's orbit)

Q4. The area $\Delta $A swept out by a planet of mass m in time interval $\Delta $t is related to the angular momentum by:
    (A) $\Delta {{A}}/\Delta {\rm{t}}$ = $L / (2 m)$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\Delta {\bf{A}}/\Delta {\rm{t}}$ = $GL / (2 m)$
    (C) $\Delta {{A}}/\Delta {\rm{t}}$ = $2L / m$
    (D) $\Delta {{A}}/\Delta {\rm{t}}$ =$ gL / (2 m)$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: The law of areas can be understood as the consequence of conservation of angular momentum which is valid for any central force such that the force on the planet is along the vector joining the sun and the planet. Let the sun be at the origin and let the position and momentum of the planet be denoted by r and p respectively. Then the area swept out by the planet of mass m in the time interval Œît is given by ŒîA = $\frac{1}{2}$ (r $\times$ vŒît).
Hence $\frac{\Delta{A}}{\Delta{t}}$ = $\frac{1}{2}$(r $\times$ p)/m = $\frac{L}{2m}$
where v is the velocity, L is the angular momentum equal to (r $\times$ p).

Q5. Newton‚Äôs law of universal gravitation states that the gravitational force of attraction between any two particles of masses m_1 and m_2 separated by a distance r has the magnitude equal to:
    (A) $\left| {\overrightarrow {\rm{F}} } \right| =\mathrm{G} \frac{\mathrm{m}_1}{\mathrm{r}^2}$
    (B) $\left| {\overrightarrow {\rm{F}} } \right| =\mathrm{G} \frac{\mathrm{m}_2}{\mathrm{r}^2}$
    (C) $\left| {\overrightarrow {\rm{F}} } \right| =\mathrm{G} \frac{\mathrm{m}_1 \mathrm{~m}_2}{\mathrm{r}^2}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\left| {\overrightarrow {\rm{F}} } \right| = \frac{\mathrm{m}_1 \mathrm{~m}_2}{\mathrm{r}^2}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: According to Newton's law of universal gravitation, the force of attraction between two objects is directly proportional to the product of two masses and inversely proportional to the square of the distance between them.

Q6. The direction of the universal gravitational force between particles of masses ${{\rm{m}}_{\rm{1}}}$ and ${{\rm{m}}_{\rm{2}}}$ is:
    (A) towards ${{\text{m}}_{\text{2}}}$ on ${{\text{m}}_{\text{1}}}$ and towards ${{\rm{m}}_{\rm{1}}}$ on ${{\rm{m}}_{\rm{2}}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) towards ${{\rm{m}}_{\rm{2}}}$
    (C) towards the center of the earth
    (D) towards ${{\rm{m}}_{\rm{1}}}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Since the gravitational force is attractive in nature, one Force will be on m_1 which will be directed towards m_ 2. i.e., ${\overrightarrow F _{12}} = G{{{m_1}{m_2}} \over {{r_{21}}^2}}$ and other force will be on m_2 which will be directed towards m_ 1 i.e., ${\overrightarrow F _{21}} = -G{{{m_1}{m_2}} \over {{r_{12}}^2}}$
Clearly, It can be seen that ${\overrightarrow F _{12}} = - {\overrightarrow F _{21}}$.

Q7. A central force is always directed:
    (A) at a varying angle to the position vector of the point of application of the force with respect to the fixed point
    (B) along the position vector of the point of application of the force with respect to the fixed point   <-- CORRECT
    (C) at a fixed angle to the position vector of the point of application of the force with respect to the fixed point
    (D) perpendicular to the position vector of the point of application of the force with respect to the fixed point
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The motion of a particle under a central force F always remains in the plane defined by its initial position and velocity. This may be seen by symmetry. Since the position r, velocity v, and force F all lie in the same plane, there is never an acceleration perpendicular to that plane, because that would break the symmetry between "above" the plane and "below" the plane.
To demonstrate this mathematically, it suffices to show that the angular momentum of the particle is constant. This angular momentum L is defined by the equation
L = r $\times$ p = r $\times$ mv
as angular momentum is conserved, the torque vanishes as F is zero or along r
$dL\over dt$= $r \times F$ = 0
central forces always satisfy this condition. A central force is always directed towards the center.

Q8. The gravitational force on point mass m_1 due to point mass m_2, m_3 & m_4 is the____________________of gravitational forces exerted by m2m2, m3m3 and m4.
    (A) vector sum   <-- CORRECT
    (B) difference
    (C) product
    (D) scalar sum
    Answer: A
    Explanation: According to properties of gravitational force, the force between the particles is independent of the presence or absence of other particles; so the principle of superposition is valid i.e. force on a particle due to a number of particles is the resultant of forces due to individual particles i.e., $\overrightarrow {{F_1}} + \overrightarrow {{F_2}} + \overrightarrow {{F_3}} + .........$

Q9. To find the resultant gravitational force acting on the particle m due to a number of masses we need to use the principle of
    (A) maximal action
    (B) superposition   <-- CORRECT
    (C) no action
    (D) least action
    Answer: B
    Explanation: According to properties of gravitational force, gravitational force between the particles is independent of the presence or absence of other particles; so the principle of superposition is valid i.e. force on a particle due to number of particles is the resultant of forces due to individual particles i.e.$\overrightarrow {{F_1}} + \overrightarrow {{F_2}} + \overrightarrow {{F_3}} + .........$

Q10. The force of attraction between a hollow spherical shell of uniform density and a point mass situated outside is just as if the entire mass of the shell is
    (A) equally concentrated at four points on a square of the shell.
    (B) concentrated at the centre of the shell.   <-- CORRECT
    (C) equally concentrated at opposite ends of the diameter of the sphere.
    (D) equally concentrated at three points on a triangle of the shell.
    Answer: B
    Explanation: According to Shell's theorem, If a particle of mass m is located outside a spherical shell of mass M at, for instance, point P, the shell attracts the particle as though the mass of the shell were concentrated at its centre. Thus, as far as the gravitational force acting on a particle outside the shell is concerned, a spherical shell acts no differently from the solid spherical distributions of mass.

Q11. The force of attraction due to a hollow spherical shell of uniform density, on a point mass situated inside it, is
    (A) negative
    (B) one
    (C) positive
    (D) zero   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The gravitational force acting by a spherically symmetric shell upon a point mass inside it, is the vector sum of gravitational forces acted by each part of the shell, and this vector sum is equal to zero. That is, a mass m within a spherically symmetric shell of mass M will feel no net force

Q12. In Cavendish‚Äôs experiment:
    (A) net torque on bar AB having two small lead spheres due to gravitational forces is positive
    (B) torque on bar AB having two small lead spheres due to gravitational forces is balanced by the restoring torque of the wire   <-- CORRECT
    (C) the net force on bar AB having two small lead spheres due to gravitational forces is negative
    (D) the net force on bar AB having two small lead spheres due to gravitational forces is positive
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Cavendish's apparatus for experimentally determining the value of G involved a light, rigid rod about 2-feet long. Two small lead spheres were attached to the ends of the rod and the rod was suspended by a thin wire. When the rod becomes twisted, the torsion of the wire begins to exert a torsional force that is proportional to the angle of rotation of the rod. The more twist of the wire, the more the system pushes backward to restore itself towards the original position.

Q13. The value of the gravitational constant G is
    (A) G = 6.67$ \times $${\rm{1}}{0^{ - {\rm{11}}}}$N m$^{\rm{2}}$/kg$^{\rm{2}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) G = 6.67$ \times $${\rm{1}}{0^{ - {\rm{11}}}}$ N m/kg$^{\rm{2}}$
    (C) G = 6.67$ \times $${\rm{1}}{0^{ - {\rm{11}}}}$ m$^{\rm{2}}$/kg$^{\rm{2}}$
    (D) G = 6.67$ \times $${\rm{1}}{0^{ - {\rm{11}}}}$ N m$^{\rm{2}}$/kg
    Answer: A
    Explanation: The value of G was experimentally determined by Lord Henry Cavendish using a torsion balance. Cavendish brought two large lead spheres near the smaller spheres attached to the rod. Since all masses attract, the large spheres exerted a gravitational force upon the smaller spheres and twisted the rod a measurable amount. Once the torsional force balanced the gravitational force, the rod and spheres came to rest and Cavendish was able to determine the gravitational force of attraction between the masses. Cavendish expressed his result in terms of the density of the Earth.
$G = g{{R_{earth}^2} \over {{M_{earth}}}} = {{3g} \over {4\pi {R_{earth}}{\rho _{earth}}}}$
After converting to SI units, Cavendish's value for the Earth's density, 5.448 g cm^‚àí3, gives
G = 6.74√ó10^‚àí11 m^3 kg^‚Äì1 s^‚àí2
Today, the currently accepted value is 6.67259 x 10^-11 N m^2/kg^2.

Q14. The acceleration due to gravity at a height h in terms of mass of earth M_E, radius of the earth R_E and gravitational constant G is
    (A) $g(h)=\frac{G M_E}{\left(R_E+2 h\right)^2}$
    (B) $g(h)=\frac{M_E}{\left(R_E+h\right)^2}$
    (C) $g(h)=\frac{G M_E}{\left(R_E+h\right)^2}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $g(h)=\frac{G m M_E}{\left(R_E+h\right)^2}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: We know by Newton's law of gravitation, the force on the body of mass m, situated at height h to the surface of the earth of mass M_E is given by:
$F=G \frac{M_e m}{(r+h)^2}$ ...(i)
We also know F = mass $\times$ acceleration(here acceleration is the acceleration due to gravity)
$\Rightarrow$ F = mg ...(ii)
Equating (i) & (ii), we get
$g=G \frac{M_e}{(r+h)^2}$

Q15. The acceleration due to gravity at a depth d in terms of g(acceleration due to gravity) at radius of the earth R_E is
    (A) $g(d) = g(1 + \frac{d}{2R_E})$
    (B) $g(d) = g(1 + \frac{d}{R_E})$
    (C) $g(d) = g(1 - \frac{2d}{R_E})$
    (D) $g(d) = g(1 - \frac{d}{ R_E})$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Acceleration due to gravity at the surface of the earth of radius R_E
$g = G\frac{M}{{{R_E^2}}} = \frac{4}{3}\pi \rho GR_E$ ...(1)
Acceleration due to gravity at depth d from the surface of the earth 
$g(d) = \frac{4}{ 3}\pi \rho G(R - d)$ ...(2)
From (1) & (2), we get
$g(d) = g(1 - \frac{d}{R_E})$

Q1. The formula for gravitational potential energy associated with two particles of masses m_1 and m_2 separated by distance r is given by
    (A) ${\rm{v}} = - \frac{G{m_1}{m_2}}{r}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) ${\rm{v}} = - \frac{G{m_1}}{r}$
    (C) $\mathrm{v}=-\frac{G m_1 m_2}{2 r}$
    (D) $\mathrm{v}=-\frac{G m_2}{r}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Change in gravitational potential energy of a system is defined as the -ve of the work done by the gravitational force as the configuration of the system is changed.
${U_f}\; - {\rm{ }}{U_i}\; = {\rm{ }}{W_{gr}}=V(Potential Energy)$ 
Change in gravitational potential energy of two point masses m_1 and m_2 as their separation is changed from r_1 to r_2 is given by
$U\left(r_2\right)-U\left(r_1\right)=G m_1 m_2\left[\frac{1}{r_1}-\frac{1}{r_2}\right]$ 
If, at infinite separation, gravitational potential energy is assumed to be zero, then the gravitational potential energy of the above two point mass system at separation r,
$U(r) = - G\frac{{m_1}{m_2}} {r}$ 
$\therefore V = - G\frac{{m_1}{m_2}} { r}$

Q2. The minimum velocity with which a body should be projected from the surface of the earth so as it goes out of the gravitational field of earth and never return to earth is known as
    (A) Higher speed
    (B) escape velocity   <-- CORRECT
    (C) average speed
    (D) escape acceleration
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Escape velocity is the minimum velocity with which a body should be projected from the surface of earth so as it goes out of gravitational field of earth and never return to earth.
${V_{escape}} = \sqrt {{{2GM} \over R}} $
V_escape=11184 m/sec approximate to 11.2 km/sec

Q3. For the earth the escape speed in terms of g, the acceleration due to gravity and R_E, the radius of the earth is
    (A) $\sqrt {5g{R_E}} $
    (B) $\sqrt {g{R_E}} $
    (C) $\sqrt {2g} $
    (D) $\sqrt {2g{R_E}} $   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The work done to displace a body from the surface of earth (r = R_e) to infinity ( $r = \infty $ ) is
$W = G{{Mm} \over {R_e{\rm{ }}}}$
If v_e is the required escape velocity then kinetic energy which should be given to the body is ${1 \over 2}{\rm{ }}m{v_e}^2$
$\therefore {1 \over 2}{\rm{ }}m{v_e}^2 = G{{Mm} \over R_e}$
$ \Rightarrow {v_e} = \sqrt {2g{R_e}} $

Q4. For earth satellites, the centripetal force is provided by
    (A) strong interactions
    (B) gravitational force   <-- CORRECT
    (C) forces exerted by firing rockets on satellite
    (D) electrical forces
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Gravity supplies the necessary centripetal force to hold a satellite in orbit about the earth. Setting the gravity force from the universal law of gravity equal to the required centripetal force yields the equation of motion of satellites in the circular orbit.
i.e., ${F_{gravity}} = {F_{centripetal}}$
$ \Rightarrow {v_{orbital}} = \sqrt {{{GM} \over r}} $

Q5. Time period of an earth satellite very close to the surface of earth is given by
    (A) $T_0=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{R_E}{g}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $T_0=\pi \sqrt{\frac{R_E}{g}}$
    (C) $T_0=\pi \sqrt{\frac{R_E}{2 g}}$
    (D) $T_0=\pi \sqrt{\frac{2 R_E}{g}}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: It is the time taken by satellite to go once around the earth
$\therefore \mathrm{T}=\frac{\text { Circumference of the orbit }}{\text { orbitalvelocity }}$
$ \Rightarrow T = \frac{{2\pi r}}{\nu } = 2\pi \sqrt {\frac{r}{{GM}}} $ ${\text{[As }}\nu {\text{ = }}\sqrt {\frac{{GM}}{r}{\text{ }}} ]$ 
Since Satellite is very close to the surface of earth, so here, we can take r = R (Radius of earth)
$ \Rightarrow T = 2\pi \sqrt {\frac{{{R^3}}}{{GM}}} $
$ \Rightarrow T = 2\pi \sqrt {\frac{{{R^3}}}{{g{R^2}}}}$ $[GM = g{R^2}]$
$ \Rightarrow T = 2\pi \sqrt {\frac{{{R}}}{{g}}}$

Q6. The total energy of a circularly orbiting satellite is
    (A) zero
    (B) negative   <-- CORRECT
    (C) positive large
    (D) positive small
    Answer: B
    Explanation: When a satellite revolves around a planet in its orbit, it possesses both potential energy (due to its position against the gravitational pull of earth) and kinetic energy (due to orbital motion).
$ \begin{gathered} {\text{Total Energy = Potential Energy(U) + Kinetic Energy(K)}} \hfill \\ {\text{ = }}\frac{{ - GMm}}{r} + \frac{{GMm}}{{2r}} \hfill \\ {\text{ = }}\frac{{ - GMm}}{{2r}} \hfill \\ \end{gathered} $
Hence we can say that total energy of circularly orbiting satellite is negative.

Q7. For a circularly orbiting satellite the potential energy:
    (A) is negative, and has a magnitude thrice that of the positive kinetic energy.
    (B) is positive, and having a magnitude twice that of the positive kinetic energy.
    (C) is negative, and has a magnitude twice that of the positive kinetic energy.   <-- CORRECT
    (D) is negative, and having a magnitude twice that of the negative kinetic energy.
    Answer: C
    Explanation: In the case of circularly orbiting satellite,
Potential Energy = ${\text{ }}\frac{{ - GMm}}{r} = \frac{{ - {L^2}}}{{m{r^2}}}$
Kinetic Energy = $\frac{G M m}{2 r}=\frac{L^{2}}{2 m r^{2}}$ 
Comparing both, we can say that the potential energy is negative and has a magnitude twice that of the positive kinetic energy.

Q8. For Geostationary Satellites:
    (A) a circular orbit is in the equatorial plane of the earth and period of rotation is 24 hrs   <-- CORRECT
    (B) circular orbit is in the equatorial plane of the earth and period of rotation is 34 hrs
    (C) a circular orbit is in the longitudinal plane of the earth and period of rotation is 20 hrs
    (D) circular orbit is in the equatorial plane of the earth and period of rotation is 14 hrs
    Answer: A
    Explanation: If there is a satellite in the equatorial plane rotating in the direction of earth‚Äôs rotation from west to east, then for an observer, on the earth, the angular velocity of the satellite will be $({\omega _s} - {\omega _E})$ 
The time interval between the two consecutive appearances overhead will be,
$T = \frac{{2\pi }}{{{\omega _s} - {\omega _E}}} = \frac{{{T_s}{T_E}}}{{{T_E} - {T_s}}}$ 
If ${\omega _s} = {\omega _E}$, T = $\infty$, i.e. satellite will appear stationary relative to earth. Such satellites are called geostationary satellites and will have a period of rotation as the period of rotation of earth i.e 24 Hrs.

Q9. Geostationary Satellites:
    (A) are not useful for communication applications
    (B) needless powerful rockets to put them into larger distance orbits
    (C) need more powerful rockets to put them into smaller distance orbits
    (D) need more powerful rockets to put them into larger distance orbits   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The orbital velocity of the satellite depends on its altitude above Earth. The nearer Earth, the faster the required orbital velocity. At an altitude of 200 kilometers, the required orbital velocity is just over 27,400 kph. To maintain an orbit that is 35,786 km above Earth, the satellite must orbit at a speed of about 11,300 kmph. That orbital speed and distance permit the satellite to make one revolution in 24 hours. To achieve his speed, Judging from the mass of fuel needed, it stands to reason that you'd use a more powerful launch vehicle with a bi-propellant upper stage to get the satellite to GEO.

Q10. Polar satellites:
    (A) are low altitude satellites, and go around the poles of the earth in a north-south direction   <-- CORRECT
    (B) are high altitude satellites, and go around the equator of the earth in a north-east direction
    (C) are low altitude satellites, and go around the equator of the earth in a north-east direction
    (D) are high altitude satellites, and go around the equator of the earth in a west-east direction
    Answer: A
    Explanation: A polar-orbiting satellite closely parallels the earth's meridian lines. It passes over the north and south poles each revolution. As the earth rotates to the east beneath the satellite, each pass monitors an area to the west of the previous pass at intervals of roughly 90 to 100 minutes. These strips can be pieced together to produce a picture of a larger area. They are used in monitoring the weather, observing the Earth‚Äôs surface, military uses including spying.

Q11. Which of the following statements correct?
    (A) Acceleration due to gravity is independent of mass of the earth.
    (B) The formula -G Mm$(\frac {1}{r_2}- \frac {1}{r_1})$ is less accurate than the formula mg(r_2 - r_1) for the difference of potential energy.
    (C) Acceleration due to gravity decreases with increasing altitude.   <-- CORRECT
    (D) Acceleration due to gravity increases with increasing depth .
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The acceleration due to gravity due to a body at point P on the surface of earth is
g = $ G{M \over {{R^2}}}$ ...(i)
Let the body is placed to point Q at a height h abovr the surface of earth, then acceleration due to gravity:
g_h = $ G{M \over {{{(R + h)}^2}}}$ ...(ii)
Dividing (i) & (ii), we get
${{{g_h}} \over g} = {{{R^2}} \over {{{(R + h)}^2}}}$
By simplifying and expanding through binomial theorem, we get
g_h = $ g(1 - {{2h} \over R})$
From above expression we can clearly say that with increase in altitiude h, the value of acceleration due to gravity decreases.

Q12. Suppose there existed a planet that went around the sun twice as fast as the earth. What would be its orbital size as compared to that of the earth?
    (A) Smaller by a factor of 0.5
    (B) Larger by a factor of 1.11
    (C) Larger by a factor of 1.23
    (D) Smaller by a factor of 0.63   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The time period of revolution of the earth around sun T_e = 1 Year
The time period of revolution of the planet around the sun, T_p = 0.5 Year
Orbital size of earth, r_e = 1 A.U
Orbital size of the planet,r_p =?
Applying Kepler's third law we get: 
${{{r_p}^3} \over {{r_e}^3}} = {{{T_p}^2} \over {{T_e}^2}}$ $ \Rightarrow {{{r_p}^3} \over {{r_e}^3}} = {\left( {{{{1 \over 2}} \over 1}} \right)^2} = {1 \over 4}$ 
$ \Rightarrow {{{r_p}} \over {{r_e}}} = {\left( {{1 \over 4}} \right)^{1 \over3}}$
$\Rightarrow {r_p} = {\left( {{1 \over 4}} \right)^{1\over 3}}{r_e}$
As r_e= 1 A.U (Given)
$ \Rightarrow {r_p} = {\left( {{1 \over 4}} \right)^{1\over 3}}$
$\Rightarrow$ r_p = 0.63 A.U.

Q13. A typical adult human has a mass of about 70 kg. What force does a full moon exert on such a human when it is directly overhead with its centre 378,000 km away?
    (A) 2.4 $ \times $ 10^-3 N   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 3.1 $ \times $ 10^-6 N
    (C) 2.0 $ \times $ 10^-6 N
    (D) 1.4 $ \times $ 10^‚àí6 N
    Answer: A
    Explanation: The force exerted by the full moon on the human
$F = G\frac{{Mm}}{{{r^2}}}$ 
Here G is the gravitational constant, m is the mass of the human, M is the mass of the moon. r is the separation distance between moon and human.
Substitute Value Of G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^‚àí11N.m^2/Kg^2, m = 70 kg, M = 7.35 $\times$ 10^22, r = 3.78 $\times$ 10^8
$\Rightarrow $ F = $\frac{\left(6.67 \times 10^{-11} N . m^{2} / K g^{2}\right) \times(70 K g) \times\left(7.35 \times 10^{22} K g\right)}{\left(3.78 \times 10^{8} m\right)^{2}}$
$\Rightarrow $$F=\frac{3.43 \times 10^{3} \times 10^{-11} \times 10^{22}}{14.29 \times 10^{16}} N$
$\Rightarrow$ F = 2.4 $\times$ 10^‚àí3N

Q14. A particle of mass 3m is located 1.00 m from a particle of mass m. Where should you put a third mass M so that the net gravitational force on M due to the two masses is exactly zero?
    (A) 0.534 m from the mass m
    (B) 0.234 m from the mass 3m
    (C) 0.63 m from the mass 3m   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 0.69 m from the mass m
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Let x be the distance of the third particle from the mass 3m. then (1 - x) is the distance will be the distance from mass m.
If we require that net force on another mass M be zero, then we must have the following:
$\frac{{GM(3m)}}{{{x^2}}} = \frac{{GM(m)}}{{{{\left( {1 - x} \right)}^2}}}$
$\Rightarrow \frac{3}{{{x^2}}} = \frac{1}{{{{\left( {1 - x} \right)}^2}}}$
$\Rightarrow {x^2} = 3 \times {\left( {1 - x} \right)^2}$
$\Rightarrow x = \sqrt {3 \times {{\left( {1 - x} \right)}^2}} = \sqrt 3 (1 - x) $
$\Rightarrow x = \frac{{\sqrt 3 }}{{1 + \sqrt 3 }} = 0.63$
$\therefore$ The particle is 0.63 m away from 3m.

Q15. At what distance above the surface of the earth is the acceleration due to the earth‚Äôs gravity 0.980 m/s^2 if the acceleration due to gravity at the surface has magnitude 9.8 m/s^2.
    (A) 1.18 $ \times $ 10^7 m
    (B) 1.37 $ \times $ 10^7 m   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 0.98 $ \times $ 10^7 m
    (D) 1.48 $ \times $ 10^7 m
    Answer: B
    Explanation: We know:
$g^{\prime}=g\left(\frac{R^{2}}{(R+h)^{2}}\right)$
or 
g' = $g\left(\frac{R^{2}}{r^{2}}\right)$
where R = Radius of earth = 6371 km = 6371$\times$ 10^3 m
r = R + h
g = Acceleration due to gravity on the surface of earth
g‚Ä≤ = Acceleration due to gravity at height h
Putting above values in the relation, we get:
$r^{2}=\frac{9.8 \times\left(6371 \times 10^{3}\right)^{2}}{0.98}$
$\Rightarrow r=\sqrt{10} \times 6371 \times 10^{3}$
$\Rightarrow$ r = 20146.87 $\times$ 10^3
We know r = R + h
$\Rightarrow$ h = r - R
$\Rightarrow h=20132.36 \times 10^{3}-6371 \times 10^{3}$
$\Rightarrow$ h = 10^3(20132.36 - 6371)
$\Rightarrow$ h = 13761.36 $\times$ 10^3 = 1.37 $\times$ 10^7m

Q1. Titania, the largest moon of the planet Uranus, has $\frac 1 8$ the radius of the earth and $\frac {1} {1700}$ the mass of the earth. What is the acceleration due to gravity at the surface of Titania? Data: G = 6.67$ \times $ 10^-11 N m$^{\rm{2}}$/kg$^{\rm{2}}$, R_E = 6.38 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{\rm{6}}}$m, m_E = 5.97 $ \times $ 10^24 kg.
    (A) 0.43 m/s$^{\rm{2}}$ Hz
    (B) 0.28 m/s^2
    (C) 0.18 m/s^2
    (D) 0.37 m/s^2   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $g_{t}=G \frac{M_{t}}{R_{t}^{2}}, g_{E}=G \frac{M_{E}}{R_{E}^{2}}=9.8 \mathrm{m} / \mathrm{s}^{2}$
$\Rightarrow g_{t}=G \frac{\left(\frac{M_{E}}{1700}\right)}{\left(\frac{R_{E}}{8}\right)^{2}} $
$=\frac{64}{1700} G \frac{M_{E}}{R_{E}^{2}}=\frac{64}{1700} \times g_{E}$
= 0.37m/s^2

Q2. Titania, the largest moon of the planet Uranus, has $\frac 1 8$ the radius of the earth and $\frac {1} {1700}$ the mass of the earth. What is the average density of Titania? Data: G = 6.67$ \times $ 10^-11 Nm$^{\rm{2}}$/kg$^{\rm{2}}$, R_E = 6.38 $ \times $ 10^6 m, ${{\rm{M}}_{\rm{E}}}$ = 5.97 $ \times $ 10^24kg.
    (A) 1900 kg/m^3
    (B) 2300 kg/m^3
    (C) 1700 kg/m$^{\rm{3}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 2700 kg/m^3
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The density of Titania = $\frac{{{\text{Mass Of Titania}}}}{{{\text{Volume Of Titania}}}} = \frac{{\frac{{{{\text{M}}_E}}}{{1700}}}}{{\frac{4}{3}\pi {{\left( {\frac{{{R_E}}}{8}} \right)}^3}}}$
Here, mass of earth (M_E = 5.97 $\times$ 10^24kg) and radius of earth(R_E = 6.38 $\times$ 10^6m) 
$= \frac{{\frac{{{\text{5}}{\text{.97}} \times {\text{1}}{{\text{0}}^{24}}}}{{1700}}kg}}{{\frac{4}{3}\pi {{\left( {\frac{{6.38 \times {{10}^6}}}{8}m} \right)}^3}}} $
${\text{ = }}\frac{{{\text{3}}{\text{.5}} \times {\text{1}}{{\text{0}}^{21}}kg}}{{\frac{4}{3} \times 3.14 \times {{\left( {0.79 \times {{10}^6}m} \right)}^3}}}$
$= \frac{{{\text{3}}{\text{.5}} \times {\text{1}}{{\text{0}}^{21}}kg}}{{2.064 \times {{10}^{18}}{m^3}}}$
= 1.7 $\times$ 10^3kg/m^3 = 1700 kg/m^3

Q3. A rocket is fired from the earth towards the sun. At what distance from the earth‚Äôs centre is the gravitational force on the rocket zero? Mass of the sun = 2 $ \times $ 10^30 kg, mass of the earth = 6 $ \times $ 10^24 kg. Neglect the effect of other planets etc. (orbital radius = 1.5 $ \times $ 10^11 m).
    (A) 3.2 $ \times $ 10^8 m
    (B) 2.2 $ \times $ 10^8 m
    (C) 2.6 $ \times $ 10^8 m   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 2.8 $ \times $ 10^8 m
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Mass of the Sun,M_s = 2 $\times$ 10^30 kg
Mass of the Earth, M_e = 6 $\times$ 10^24 kg
Orbital radius, r = 1.5 $\times$ 10^11 m
Mass Of rocket = m
Let x be the distance from the centre of the earth where the gravitational forces acting on satellite P under the influence of Sun & the Earth as:
$G \frac{M_{s} m}{(r-x)^{2}}=G \frac{M_{e} m}{x^{2}}$
$[{(r-x) \over x}]^{2}=\frac{M_{s}}{M_{e}}$
$\frac{(r-x)}{x}=\left[\frac{2 \times 10^{30}}{6 \times 10^{24}}\right]^{\frac{1}{2}}$ = 577.35
$\Rightarrow$ 1.5 $\times$ 10^11 ‚àí x = 577.35x
$\Rightarrow$ 577.35x = 1.5 $\times$ 10^11
$\Rightarrow x=\frac{1.5 \times 10^{11}}{578.35}$ = 2.6 $\times$ 10^8m

Q4. How will you ‚Äòweigh the sun‚Äô, that is estimate its mass? The mean orbital radius of the earth around the sun is 1.5 $ \times $ 10^8 km.
    (A) 2 $ \times $ 10^30 kg   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 2.2 $ \times $ 10^30 kg
    (C) 1.8 $ \times $ 10^30 kg
    (D) 2.4 $ \times $ 10^30 kg
    Answer: A
    Explanation: R = Radius Of Orbit Of Earth = 1.5 $\times$ 10^8 km = 1.5 $\times$ 10^11m
T = Time Period Of Earth Around The Sun = 365 Days 
= 365 $\times$ 24 $\times$ 60 $\times$ 60 sec
G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^11 Nm^2kg^-2 From the relation,
$\frac{R}{T^{2}}=\frac{G M_{s}}{4 \pi^{2} R^{2}}$ where M_s = Mass of sun = ?
$\Rightarrow \mathrm{M}_{s}=\frac{4 \pi^{2} \mathrm{R}^{3}}{\mathrm{T}^{2} G}$
$\Rightarrow \mathrm{M}_{s}=\frac{4 \times 9.87 \times\left(1.5 \times 10^{11}\right)^{3}}{(365 \times 24 \times 60 \times 60)^{2} \times 6.67 \times 10^{-11}} $ kg
$\Rightarrow$ M_s = 2.0 $\times$ 10^30 kg
$\therefore
$ Mass of sun = 2.0 $\times$ 10^30 kg

Q5. A Saturn year is 29.5 times the earth year. How far is the Saturn from the sun if the earth is 1.50 $ \times $ 10^8 km away from the sun?
    (A) 1.43 $ \times $ 10^12 m   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 1.13 $ \times $ 10^12 m
    (C) 1.53 $ \times $ 10^12 m
    (D) 1.23 $ \times $ 10^12 m
    Answer: A
    Explanation: The distance of the earth from the sun, re = 1.5 $\times$ 10^8 km = 1.5 $\times$ 10^11m
The time period of the earth = T_e
The time period of the Saturn = T_s = 29.5 T_e
The distance of Saturn from the sun = r_s
From Kepler's third law of planetary motion, we have
$\mathrm{T}=\left(\frac{4 \pi r^{3}}{G M}\right)^{\frac{1}{2}}$
For Saturn & Sun, we can write
$\frac{r_{s}^{3}}{r_{e}^{3}}=\frac{T_{s}^{2}}{T_{e}^{2}}$
r_s = $r_{e}\left(\frac{\mathrm{T}_{s}}{\mathrm{T}_{e}}\right)^{\frac{2}{3}}$
$r_{s}=1.5 \times 10^{11}\left(\frac{29.5 \mathrm{T}_{e}}{\mathrm{T}_{e}}\right)^{\frac{2}{3}}$
= 1.5 $\times$ 10^11$(29.5)^{2\over3}$^_ = 14.32 $\times$ 10^11m
Hence, the distance between Saturn and the sun is 1.43 $\times$ 10^12m

Q6. A body weighs 63 N on the surface of the earth. What is the gravitational force on it due to the earth at a height equal to half the radius of the earth?
    (A) 180 N
    (B) 128 N
    (C) 28 N   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 18 N
    Answer: C
    Explanation: ${g_h} = g{\left( {1 + \frac{h}{R}} \right)^{ - 2}}$
Given, h = $\frac{R}{2}$
$ \therefore {g_h} = g{\left( {1 + \frac{{\frac{{\text{R}}}{2}}}{R}} \right)^{ - 2}}$
$\Rightarrow \frac{{{g_h}}}{g} = \frac{1}{{{{\left( {1 + \frac{1}{2}} \right)}^2}}} = \frac{1}{{{{\left( {\frac{3}{2}} \right)}^2}}} = {\left( {\frac{2}{3}} \right)^2} = \frac{4}{9}$
Let m = mass of the body
If W and W_h be its weight at earth's surface and at a height h above the earth's surface, then
W = mg
${\text{and }}{{\text{W}}_h} = m{g_h} = m \times \frac{4}{9}g = \frac{4}{9}mg$
$ \Rightarrow {{\text{W}}_h} = m{g_h} = \frac{4}{9} \times 63 $ = 28N

Q7. A body weighed 250 N on the surface. Assuming the earth to be a sphere of uniform mass density, how much would it weigh halfway down to the centre of earth?
    (A) 195 N
    (B) 240 N
    (C) 210 N
    (D) 125 N   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Weight of a body of mass m at the surface of earth's surface, W = mg = 250 N.
Body of mass m is located at depth,
d = $\frac{1}{2}$Re
where Re = Radius of earth
Acceleration due to gravity at depth g(d)is given by the relation:
g(d) = $\left(1-\frac{d}{R_{e}}\right) g$
$=\left(1-\frac{R_{e}}{2 \times R_{e}}\right) g=\frac{1}{2} g$
Weight of the body at depth d,
W' = mg(d)
$=\mathrm{m} \times \frac{1}{2} g=\frac{1}{2} m g=\frac{1}{2} W$
$=\frac{1}{2} \times 250$ = 125N

Q8. A rocket is fired vertically with a speed of 5 km sec^-1 ^ ^ from the earth‚Äôs surface. How far from the earth does the rocket go before returning to the earth? Mass of the earth = 6.0 $ \times $ 10^24 kg; mean radius of the earth = 6.4 $ \times $ 10^6 m; G = 6.67 $ \times $ 10^-11 N m^2 kg^-2.
    (A) 8.0 $ \times $ 10^6 m from the earth‚Äôs center   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 7.5 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{\rm{6}}}$ m from the earth‚Äôs center
    (C) 8.5 $ \times $ 10^6 m from the earth‚Äôs centre
    (D) 9.0 $ \times $ 10^6 m from the earth‚Äôs centre
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Work done in bringing a mass m against a mass M at a distance 'x' through a distance dx 
= Gravitational force $\times$ Distance = $\frac{\mathrm{GMm}}{x^{2}} d x$
Work done in bringing it from $\infty$ to r = GMm$\left[\frac{1}{\infty}-\frac{1}{R}\right]$
$=-\mathrm{GMm}\left[\frac{1}{R}-\frac{1}{\infty}\right]$
Therefore Gravitational Potential Energy, $W =\frac{-\mathrm{GMm}}{R}$
Initial Total Energy = Kinetic Energy + Potential Energy
$=\frac{1}{2} \mathrm{mv}^{2}-\frac{\mathrm{GMm}}{R}$
Final Total Energy = 0 - $\frac{\mathrm{GMm}}{(R+h)}$
Applying law of conservation of energy:
$\frac{1}{2} \mathrm{mv}^{2}-\frac{\mathrm{GMm}}{R}=0-\frac{\mathrm{GMm}}{(R+h)}$
$\frac{\mathrm{GMm}}{(R+h)}=\frac{\mathrm{GMm}}{R}-\frac{1}{2} \mathrm{mv}^{2}$
$\frac{G M}{(R+h)}=\frac{1}{R}\left(G M-\frac{1}{2} R v^{2}\right)$
$\frac{(R+h)}{R}=\frac{G M}{\left(G M-\frac{1}{2} R v^{2}\right)}$
$(R+h)=\frac{G M R}{\left(G M-\frac{1}{2} R v^{2}\right)}$
h = $\frac{G M R}{\left(G M-\frac{1}{2} \mathrm{Rv}^{2}\right)}-R$
$h=\frac{\frac{1}{2} R^{2} v^{2}}{\left(G M-\frac{1}{2} R v^{2}\right)}$
$h=\frac{\left[\frac{1}{2} \times\left(6.4 \times 10^{6}\right)^{2} \times\left(5 \times 10^{3}\right)^{2}\right]}{\left[\left(6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 6 \times 10^{24}\right)-\left(\frac{1}{2} \times 6.4 \times 10^{6} \times\left(5 \times 10^{3}\right)^{2}\right]\right.} $m
$=\frac{\left(512 \times 10^{18}\right)}{40 \times 10^{13}-80 \times 10^{12}}$
=1.6 √ó 10^6m
Height achieved by the rocket with respect to the centre of the Earth = Re + h
= 6.4 $\times$ 10^6 + 1.6 $\times$ 10^6 = 8 $\times$ 10^6 m.

Q9. The escape speed of a projectile on the earth‚Äôs surface is 11.2 km sec^-1. A body is projected out with thrice this speed. What is the speed of the body far away from the earth? Ignore the presence of the sun and other planets.
    (A) 31.6 km/sec   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 28.7 km/s
    (C) 29.7 km/s
    (D) 32.7 km/s
    Answer: A
    Explanation: The escape velocity of a projectile from the Earth, V_esc = 11.2 km/s
Projection velocity of the projectile, V_p = 3V_esc
Mass of the projectile = m
The velocity of the projectile far away from the Earth = V_f
The total energy of the projectile on the earth$= \frac{1}{2}m{v_p}^2 - \frac{1}{2}m{v_{esc}}^2$ 
The gravitational potential energy of the projectile far away from the Earth is zero.
The total energy of the projectile far away from the Earth = $\frac{1}{2}m{v_f}^2$ 
From the law of conservation of energy, we have
$= \frac{1}{2}m{v_p}^2 - \frac{1}{2}m{v_{esc}}^2 = \frac{1}{2}m{v_f}^2$ 
${v_f} = \sqrt {{v_p}^2 - {v_{esc}}^2}$ 
$ = \sqrt {{{\left( {3{v_{esc}}} \right)}^2} - {{\left( {{v_{esc}}} \right)}^2}}$ 
$= \sqrt {{{\left( {3{v_{esc}}} \right)}^2} - {{\left( {{v_{esc}}} \right)}^2}}$
$=\sqrt 8 {v_{esc}}$
$=\sqrt 8 \times 11.2 $ = 31.68 km/sec

Q10. A satellite orbits the earth at a height of 400 km above the surface. How much energy must be expended to rocket the satellite out of the earth‚Äôs gravitational influence? Mass of the satellite = 200 kg; mass of the earth = 6.0 $ \times $ 10^24 kg; radius of the earth = 6.4 $ \times $ 10^6 m; G = 6.67 $ \times $ 10^-11 N m^2 kg^-2.
    (A) 6.5 $ \times $ 10^9 J
    (B) 6.0 $ \times $ 10^9 J
    (C) 6.2 $ \times $ 10^9 J
    (D) 5.9 $ \times $ 10^9 J   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Mass of the Earth, M = 6.0 $\times$ 10^24kg
Mass of the satellite, m = 200 kg
Radius of the Earth, Re = 6.4 $\times$ 10^6m Universal gravitational constant, G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^-11N m^2 kg^‚àí2
Height of the satellite, h = 400 km = 4 $\times$ 10^5m = 0.4 $\times$ 106m
Total energy of the satellite at height = $1\over2$mv^2 + $\left(\frac{-G M_{e} m}{R_{e}+h}\right)$
Orbital velocity of the satellite, $v=\sqrt{\frac{G M_{e}}{R_{e}+h}}$
Total energy at height, $h=\frac{1}{2} m\left(\frac{G M_{e}}{R_{e}+h}\right)-\frac{G M_{e} m}{R_{e}+h}=-\frac{1}{2}\left(\frac{-G M_{e} m}{R_{e}+h}\right)$
The negative sign indicates that the satellite is bound to the Earth. 
This is called the bound energy of the satellite. The energy required to send the satellite out of its orbit = ... (Bound energy)
$=\frac{1}{2} \frac{G M_{e} m}{\left(R_{e}+h\right)}$
$=\frac{1}{2} \times \frac{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 6.0 \times 10^{24} \times 200}{\left(6.4 \times 10^{6}+0.4 \times 10^{6}\right)}$
$=\frac{1}{2} \times \frac{6.67 \times 6 \times 2 \times 10^{15}}{6.8 \times 10^{6}}$ = 5.9 $\times$ 10^9J

Q11. For a satellite to be in a circular orbit 780 km above the surface of the earth, what orbital speed must it be given?
    (A) 7360 m/s
    (B) 7460 m/s   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 7260 m/s
    (D) 7160 m/s
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Mass of the Earth, M_e = $6.0 \times {10^{24}}$kg
The radius of the Earth, Re = $6.4 \times {10^6}$m
Universal gravitational constant, G = $6.67 \times {10^{ - 11}}\;N{\text{ }}{m^2}{\text{ }}k{g^{ - 2}}$
Height of the satellite, h = 780 km = 780 $\times$ 10^3m = 0.78 $\times$ 10^6m
Orbital velocity of the satellite, v = $\sqrt {\frac{{G{M_e}}}{{{R_e} + h}}}$
$= \sqrt {\frac{{6.67 \times {{10}^{ - 11}} \times 6.0 \times {{10}^{24}}}}{{6.4 \times {{10}^6} + 0.78 \times {{10}^6}}}}$
$= \sqrt {\frac{{40 \times {{10}^{13}}}}{{7.18 \times {{10}^6}}}}$
$= \sqrt {5.57 \times {{10}^7}} $
$= {10^3} \times \sqrt {5.57 \times 10}$
$= 7.46 \times {10^3}$
= 7460 m/sec

Q12. For a satellite to be in a circular orbit 780 km above the surface of the earth, what is the period of the orbit (in hours)?
    (A) 1.98 hr
    (B) 1.65 hr   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 1.88 hr
    (D) 1.78 hr
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Mass of the Earth, M_e = 6.0 $\times$ 10^24 kg
The radius of the Earth, Re =$ \;6.4 \times {10^6}m$
Universal gravitational constant, G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^-11Nm^2 kg^-2
Height of the satellite, h = 780 km = 780 $\times$ 10^3m = 0.78 $\times$ 10^6 m
Time Period of the satellite, T = $2\pi \sqrt {\frac{{{{({R_e} + h)}^3}}}{{G{M_e}}}}$
$= 2 \times \frac{{22}}{7} \times \sqrt {\frac{{{{\left( {6.4 \times {{10}^6} + 0.78 \times {{10}^6}} \right)}^3}}}{{6.67 \times {{10}^{ - 11}} \times 6.0 \times {{10}^{24}}}}}$
$= 2 \times \frac{{22}}{7} \times \sqrt {\frac{{{{\left( {7.18 \times {{10}^6}} \right)}^3}}}{{40 \times {{10}^{13}}}}}$
$= 2 \times \frac{{22}}{7} \times \sqrt {9 \times {{10}^5}}$
$= 2 \times \frac{{22}}{7} \times 948$
= 2 $\times$ 22 $\times$ 135.42
= 5958.85 sec = 1.65 hr

Q13. Two satellites are in circular orbits around a planet that has radius 9.00 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{\rm{6}}}$ m. One satellite has mass 68.0 kg, orbital radius 5.00 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{\rm{7}}}$ m, and orbital speed 4800 m/s. The second satellite has a mass 84.0 kg and an orbital radius of 3.00 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{\rm{7}}}$ m. What is the orbital speed of this second satellite?
    (A) 6122 m/s
    (B) 6152 m/s
    (C) 6132 m/s
    (D) 6192 m/s   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: We know $T=\frac{2 \pi R}{v}$
$\Rightarrow \frac{\left(\frac{2 \pi R_{1}}{v_{1}}\right)^{2}}{R_{1}^{3}}=\frac{\left(\frac{2 \pi R_{2}}{v_{2}}\right)^{2}}{R_{2}^{3}}$
$\Rightarrow \frac{4 \pi^{2}}{v_{1}^{2} R_{1}}=\frac{4 \pi^{2}}{v_{2}^{2} R_{2}}$
$\Rightarrow v_{1}^{2} R_{1}=v_{2}^{2} R_{2}$
$\Rightarrow \frac{v_{1}^{2} R_1}{R_{2}}=v_{2}^{2}$
$\Rightarrow \sqrt{\frac{v_{1}^{2} R_1}{R_{2}}}=v_{2}$
Here v_1 = 4800 m/sec; R_1 = 5 $\times$ 10^7m, R_2 = 3 $\times$ 10^7m, v^2 = ?
$\Rightarrow \sqrt{\frac{(4800)^{2} \times 5 \times 10^{7}}{3 \times 10^{7}}}=v_{2}$
$\Rightarrow$ 4800 $\times$ 1.29 = 6192 m/sec.

Q14. Deimos, a moon of Mars, is about 12 km in diameter with mass 2.0 $ \times $ 10^15 kg. Suppose you are stranded alone on Deimos and want to play a one-person game of baseball. You would be the pitcher, and you would be the batter! With what speed would you have to throw a baseball so that it would go into a circular orbit just above the surface and return to you so you could hit it?
    (A) 5.1 m/s
    (B) 4.9 m/s
    (C) 4.7 m/s   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 4.3 m/s
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $v=\sqrt{\frac{G M}{r}}$
Here, G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^‚àí11N m^2 kg^‚àí2
M = 2 $\times$ 10^15 kg
$r=\frac{12000}{2}$ = 6000
$\Rightarrow v=\sqrt{\frac{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 2 \times 10^{15}}{6000}}$
$\Rightarrow$ v = 4.7 m/sec

Q15. Deimos, a moon of Mars, is about 12 km in diameter with mass 2.0 $ \times $ 10^15 kg. Suppose you are stranded alone on Deimos and want to play a one-person game of baseball. You would be the pitcher, and you would be the batter! You have to throw a baseball so that it would go into a circular orbit just above the surface and return to you so you could hit it. How long (in hours) after throwing the ball should you be ready to hit it?
    (A) 2.11 hr
    (B) 2.03 hr
    (C) 2.35 hr
    (D) 2.20 hr   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $T=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{r^{3}}{G M}}$
Here,G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^-11Nm^2kg^‚àí2
M = 2 $\times$ 10^15 kg
$r=\frac{12000}{2}$ = 6000m
$\Rightarrow T=2 \times \frac{22}{7} \times \sqrt{\frac{(6000)^{3}}{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 2 \times 10^{15}}}$
$\Rightarrow T=2 \times \frac{22}{7} \times \sqrt{\frac{216 \times 10^{9}}{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 2 \times 10^{15}}}$
$\Rightarrow T=2 \times \frac{22}{7} \times \sqrt{\frac{216 \times 10^{9}}{13.34 \times 10^{4}}}$
$\Rightarrow T=2 \times \frac{22}{7} \times \sqrt{\frac{216 \times 10^{9}}{13.34 \times 10^{4}}}$
$\Rightarrow T=2 \times \frac{22}{7} \times \sqrt{16.2 \times 10^{5}}$
$\Rightarrow T=2 \times \frac{22}{7} \times \sqrt{1.6 \times 10^{6}}$
$\Rightarrow$ T = 2 $\times$ $\frac{{22}}{7}$ $\times$ 1.26 $\times$10^3
$\Rightarrow$ T = 7920 sec 
$\Rightarrow$ T = 2.2 hr

Q1. Two stars each of one solar mass (= 2 $ \times $ 10^30 kg) are approaching each other for a head-on collision. When they are a distance 10^9 km, their speeds are negligible. What is the speed with which they collide? The radius of each star is 10^4 km. assume the stars to remain undistorted until they collide. (Use the known value of G).
    (A) 2.8 $ \times $ 10^6 m/s
    (B) 2.2 $ \times $ 10^6 m/s
    (C) 2.6 $ \times $ 10^6 m/s   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 1.6 $ \times $ 10^6 m/s
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Let $\vec v_1$ and $\vec v_2$ be the velocities of two stars when they collide. 
According to the law of conservation of momentum,
$M{{\vec v}_1} + M{{\vec v}_2} = 0$
$\Rightarrow {{\vec v}_1} = - {{\vec v}_2}$
$\Rightarrow {v_1} = {v_2} = v$
According to law of conservation of energy,
${\text{2}}\left[ {\frac{1}{2}M{v^2}} \right] = G{M^2}\left[ {\frac{1}{{{r_2}}} - \frac{1}{{{r_1}}}} \right]$
We Have
Mass of the star,M = 2 $\times$ 10^30 kg
Distance between the stars, r_1 = 10^9 km = 10^12 m
Radius of star, r = 10^4km = 10^7m
The distance between two stars when they collide is,
r_2 = 2r = 2 $\times$ 10^7m
$\therefore$ The speed with which the stars collide is given by,
$v = \sqrt {GM\left[ {\frac{1}{{{r_2}}} - \frac{1}{{{r_1}}}} \right]}$
$v = \sqrt {6.67 \times {{10}^{ - 11}} \times 2 \times {{10}^{30}} \times \left[ {\frac{1}{{2 \times {{10}^7}}} - \frac{1}{{{{10}^{12}}}}} \right]}$
v = 2.6 $\times$ 10^6m/sec

Q2. Two heavy spheres each of mass 100 kg and radius 0.10 m are placed 1.0 m apart on a horizontal table. What is the gravitational force and potential at the midpoint of the line joining the centers of the spheres? Is an object placed at that point in equilibrium? If so, is the equilibrium stable or unstable?
    (A) 0, 2.7 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{ - {\rm{8}}}}$ J/kg, stable
    (B) 0, 1.9 $ \times $ 10^‚àí8 J/kg, unstable
    (C) 0, 1.9 $ \times $ 10^‚àí8 J/kg stable
    (D) 0, 2.7 $ \times $ 10^-8 J/kg, unstable   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Here, G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^‚àí11N m^2 kg^‚àí2
M = 100 kg
R = 0.1 m
Distance between the two spheres d = 1.0 m
Suppose that the distance of either sphere from the midpoint of the line joining their centre is r. Then
r = $d\over2$ = 0.5 m
The gravitational field at the midpoint due to two spheres will be equal and opposite.
Hence, the resultant gravitational field at the midpoint = 0
The gravitational potential at the midpoint.
$=\left(\frac{-G M}{r}\right) \times 2$
$=-\frac{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 100 \times 2}{0.5}$ 
= ‚àí2.7 $\times$ 10^‚àí8 Jkg^-1
As the effective force on the body placed at the midpoint is zero, so the body is in equilibrium, if the body is displaced a little towards either mass body from its equilibrium position, it will not return to its initial position of equilibrium. Hence, the body is in unstable equilibrium.

Q3. A geostationary satellite orbits the earth at a height of nearly 36,000 km from the surface of the earth. What is the potential due to the earth‚Äôs gravity at the site of this satellite? (Take the potential energy at infinity to be zero).Mass of the earth = 6.0$ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{{\rm{24}}}}$kg, radius = 6400 km.
    (A) -9.9 $ \times $ 10^6 J/kg
    (B) -8.4 $ \times $ 10^6 J/kg
    (C) -8.7 $ \times $ 10^6 J/kg
    (D) -9.4 $ \times $ 10^6 J/kg   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Mass of the earth, M = 6.0 $\times$ 10^24kg.
Radius of the earth, R = 6400 km = 6.4 $\times$ 10^6m.
Height of a geostationary satellite from the surface of the earth,
h = 36000 km = 3.6 √ó 10^7 m
Gravitational potential energy due to earth's gravity at height h,
${\text{ = }} - \frac{{GM}}{{(R + h)}}$
$= - \frac{{6.67 \times {\text{1}}{{\text{0}}^{ - 11}} \times 6 \times {{10}^{24}}}}{{3.6 \times {{10}^7} + 0.64 \times {{10}^7}}}$
$= - \frac{{6.67 \times 6 \times {{10}^6}}}{{4.24}} = - 9.4 \times {10^6}{\text{ J/kg}}$

Q4. A spaceship is stationed on Mars. How much energy must be expended on the spaceship to launch it out of the solar system? Mass of the space ship = 1000 kg; the mass of the sun = 2 $ \times $ 10^30 kg; mass of mars = 6.4 $ \times $ 10^23 kg; radius of mars = 3395 km; radius of the orbit of mars = 2.28 $ \times $ 10^8 km; G = 6.67 $ \times $ 10^11 N m^2 kg^-2.
    (A) 5.66 $ \times $ 10^11 J
    (B) 5.96 $ \times $ 10^11 J   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 5.86 $ \times $ 10^11 J
    (D) 5.5 $ \times $ 10^11 J
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Mass of the spaceship is given by, m_s = 1000 kg
Mass of the Sun is given by, M = 2 $\times$ 10^30kg
Mass of Mars is given by, M_m = 6.4 $\times$ 10^23kg
Orbital radius of Mars, r = 3395 km = 3.395 $\times$ 10^6m
Universal gravitational constant, G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^-11 Nm^2kg^-2
Potential energy of the spaceship due to gravitational attraction of the Sun = - $\frac{GM{m_s}}{R}$
Potential energy of the spaceship due to gravitational attraction of the Mars = - $\frac{{G{M_m}{m_s}}}{r}$
Since the spaceship is stationed on Mars, its velocity and hence, its kinetic energy will be zero.
Total energy of the spaceship = $- \frac{{GM{m_s}}}{R}{\text{ - }}\frac{{G{M_m}{m_s}}}{r}$
$= - G{m_s}\left[ {\frac{M}{R} + \left( {\frac{{{M_m}}}{r}} \right)} \right]$
The negative sign indicates that the system is in bound state.
Energy required for launching the spaceship out of the solar system
= - Total energy of the spaceship = $G{m_s}\left[ {\frac{M}{R} + \left( {\frac{{{M_m}}}{r}} \right)} \right] $$= 6.67 \times {10^{ - 11}} \times {10^3} \times \left( {\frac{{2 \times {{10}^{30}}}}{{2.28 \times {{10}^{11}}}} + \frac{{6.4 \times {{10}^{23}}}}{{3.395 \times {{10}^6}}}} \right) $
= 6.67 $\times$ 10^-8 (87.72 $\times$ 10^17 + 1.88 $\times$ 10^17) 
= 6.67 $\times$ 10^-8 $\times$ 89.50 $\times$ 10^17
= 596.96 $\times$ 10^9
= 5.96 $\times$ 10^11 J

Q5. A rocket is fired ‚Äòvertically‚Äô from the surface of mars with a speed of 2 km s^‚àí1. If 20 percent of its initial energy is lost due to Martian atmospheric resistance, how far will the rocket go from the surface of Mars before returning to it? Mass of mars = 6.4 $ \times $ 10^23 kg; radius of mars = 3395 km; G = 6.67 $ \times $ 10^‚àí11 N m^2 kg^-2.
    (A) 495 km   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 435 km
    (C) 465 km
    (D) 525 km
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Initial velocity of the rocket, v = 2 km/s = 2 $\times$ 10^3 m/s
Mass of Mars, M = 6.4 $\times$ 10^23 kg
Radius of Mars, R = 3395 km = 3.395 $\times$ 10^6 m
Universal gravitational constant, G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^‚àí11 N m^2 kg^‚àí2
Mass of the rocket = m , Initial kinetic energy of the rocket = $1\over2$mv^2
Initial potential energy of the rocket = $-GMm\over r$
Total initial energy = $1\over 2$mv^2 - $GMm\over r$
If 20% of initial kinetic energy is lost due to Martian atmospheric resistance, then only 80 % of its kinetic energy helps in reaching a height.
Total initial energy available = $\frac{80}{100} \times \frac{1}{2} m v^{2}-\frac{G M m}{R}=0.4 m v^{2}-\frac{G M m}{R}$
The maximum height reached by rocket = h
At this height, the velocity and hence, the kinetic energy of the rocket will become zero. The total energy of the rocket at height h = $-G M m \over {R + h }$
Applying the law of conservation of energy for the rocket, we can write: 
$0.4 m v^{2}-\frac{G M m}{R}=\frac{-G M m}{(R+h)}$
$0.4 m v^{2}=\frac{G M m}{R}-\frac{G M m}{(R+h)}$
$0.4 v^2=\operatorname{GM}\left(\frac{1}{R}-\frac{1}{(R+h)}\right)$
$\Rightarrow \frac{R+h}{h}=\frac{G M}{0.4 v^{2} R}$
$\frac{R}{h}+1=\frac{G M}{0.4 v^{2} R}$
$\frac{R}{h}=\frac{G M}{0.4 v^{2} R}-1$
$h=\frac{R}{\frac{G M}{0.4 v^{2} R}-1}$
$h=\frac{0.4 v^{2} R^{2}}{G M-0.4 v^{2} R}$
$=\frac{0.4 \times (2 \times 10^3)^2\times \left(3.395 \times 10^{6}\right)^{2} }{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 6.4 \times 10^{23}-0.4 \times (2\times 10^3)^2 \times \left(3.395 \times 10^{6}\right) }$
$=\frac{18.442 \times 10^{18}}{42.688 \times 10^{12}-5.432 \times 10^{12}}=\frac{18.442}{37.256} \times 10^{6}$
= 495 $\times$ 10^3m = 495 km

Q6. The star Cancri is 57 light-years from the earth and has a mass of 0.85 times that of our sun. A planet has been detected in a circular orbit around Cancri with an orbital radius equal to 0.11 times the radius of the earth‚Äôs orbit around the sun. What is the orbital speed? The mean orbital radius of the earth around the sun is 1.5 $ \times $ 10^8 km.
    (A) 82700 m/s   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 87700 m/s
    (C) 85700 m/s
    (D) 92700 m/s
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $v=\sqrt{\frac{G M}{r}}$
G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^‚àí11Nm^2kg^‚àí2
Mass of sun = Ms = 1.99 $\times$ 10^30kg
Mass of planet which revolve around star Cancri,
M = 0.85 $\times$ 1.99 $\times$ 10^30 kg
r = 0.11 $\times$ 1.5 $\times$ 10^8km = 0.11 $\times$ 1.5 $\times$ 10^11m
$\Rightarrow v=\sqrt{\frac{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 0.85 \times 1.99 \times 10^{30}}{0.11 \times 1.5 \times 10^{11}}}$
$\Rightarrow$ v = 8.269 $\times$ 10^4 m/sec = 82700 m/sec

Q7. The star Cancri is 57 light-years from the earth and has a mass of 0.85 times that of our sun. A planet has been detected in a circular orbit around Cancri with an orbital radius equal to 0.11 times the radius of the earth‚Äôs orbit around the sun. What is the orbital period of the planet of Cancri? The mean orbital radius of the earth around the sun is 1.5 $ \times $ 10^8 km.
    (A) 10.1 days
    (B) 14.5 days   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 12.3 days
    (D) 15.5 days
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $v=\sqrt{\frac{G M}{r}}$
G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^‚àí11Nm^2kg^‚àí2
Mass of sun = M_s = 1.99 $\times$ 10^30kg
Mass of planet which revolve around star Cancri,
M = 0.85 $\times$ 1.99 $\times$10^30kg ,r = 0.11 $\times$ 1.5 $\times$10^8km = 0.11 $\times$ 1.5 $\times$10^11m·πÅ
$\Rightarrow v=\sqrt{\frac{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 0.85 \times 1.99 \times 10^{30}}{0.11 \times 1.5 \times 10^{11}}}$
‚áív = 8.269 $\times$ 10^4 m/sec = 82700 m/sec
we know, T =
$\Rightarrow T=\frac{2 \times \frac{22}{7} \times 0.11 \times 1.5 \times 10^{11}}{82700}$
$\Rightarrow T=\frac{1.0371 \times 10^{11}}{82700}$
$\Rightarrow$ T = 12540 $\times $ 10^2sec = 1254000 sec
$\Rightarrow$T = $1254000 \over 3600$hr = 348.33 hr
$\Rightarrow$T = $348.33\over24$days = 14.51 days

Q8. On October 15, 2001, a planet was discovered orbiting around the star HD 68988. Its orbital distance was measured to be 10.5 million kilometers from the center of the star, and its orbital period was estimated at 6.3 days. What is the mass of HD 68988?
    (A) 2.4 $ \times $ 10^30 kg
    (B) 2.2 $ \times $ 10^30 kg
    (C) 2.1 $ \times $ 10^30 kg
    (D) 2.3 $ \times $ 10^30 kg   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Following is the equation for the period T:
$T=\frac{2 \pi r^{3 / 2}}{\sqrt{G m}}$ or $T^{2}=\frac{4 \pi^{2} r^{3}}{G m}$
Solving this equation for the mass m, we get mass of the star HD68988 as follows:
$m=\frac{4 \pi^{2} r^{3}}{T^{2} G}=\frac{4 \pi^{2}\left(10.5 \times 10^{9} m\right)^{3}}{\left(5.443 \times 10^{5} s\right)^{2}\left(6.673 \times 10^{-11} N . m^{2} / k g^{2}\right)}$ = 2.3 $\times$ 10^30kg

Q9. A uniform, solid, 1000.0-kg sphere has a radius of 5.00 m. Find the gravitational force this sphere exerts on a 2.00-kg point mass placed at a distance of 5.01 m from the center of the sphere?
    (A) 5.31 $ \times $ 10^‚àí9 N   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 5.41 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{ - {\rm{9}}}}$ N
    (C) 5.52 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{ - {\rm{9}}}}$ N
    (D) 5.63 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{ - {\rm{9}}}}$ N
    Answer: A
    Explanation: We know the gravitational force
$F=\frac{G M m}{r^{2}}$
Here G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^‚àí11Nm^2kg^‚àí2
M = 1000 kg
m = 2.0 kg
r = 5.01 m
$\Rightarrow F=\frac{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 1000 \times 2}{(5.01)^{2}}$
$\Rightarrow F=\frac{13.34 \times 10^{8}}{25.10}$
$\Rightarrow$ F = $\frac{13.34 \times 10^{8}}{25.10}$
$\Rightarrow$ F = 0.531 $\times$ 10^‚àí8 = 5.31 $\times$ 10^‚àí9N

Q10. A uniform, solid, 1000.0-kg sphere has a radius of 5.00 m. Find the gravitational force this sphere exerts on a 2.00-kg point mass placed at a distance of 2.50 m from the center of the sphere?
    (A) 2.67 $ \times $ 10^‚àí9 N   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 2.27 $ \times $ 10^‚àí9 N
    (C) 2.47 $ \times $ 10^‚àí9 N
    (D) 2.07 $ \times $ 10^‚àí9 N
    Answer: A
    Explanation: In this case, at r = 2.50 m, only a fraction of mass M is located,
so first, we calculate a mass for the position r = 2.50 
since density is uniform, So
$\sigma = \sigma$'
$\frac{M}{\frac{4}{3} \pi R^{3}}=\frac{M^{\prime}}{\frac{4}{3} \pi r^{3}}$
Here R = 5 m, r = 2.50 m
$\Rightarrow \frac{1000}{(5)^{3}}=\frac{M^{\prime}}{(2.50)^{3}}$
$\Rightarrow M^{\prime}=\frac{1000 \times 2.5 \times 2.5 \times 2.5}{125}$
$\Rightarrow$ M‚Ä≤ = 125 kg
We know the gravitational force
F = $GM‚Ä≤m\over r^2$
Here G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^‚àí11Nm^2kg^‚àí2
M‚Ä≤ = 125 kg m = 2.0 kg
r = 2.5 m
$\Rightarrow F=\frac{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 125 \times 2}{(2.5)^{2}}$
$\Rightarrow F=\frac{1667.5 \times 10^{-11} \times 10^{2}}{625}$
$\Rightarrow$ F = 2.668 $\times$ 10^‚àí9 = 2.67 $\times$ 10^‚àí9N

Q11. The acceleration due to gravity at the North Pole of Neptune is approximately 10.7 m/s^2. Neptune has a mass 1.0 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{{\rm{26}}}}$ kg and radius 2.5 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{\rm{4}}}$ km and rotates once around its axis in about 16 h. What is the gravitational force on a 5.0-kg object at the north pole of Neptune?
    (A) 55.8 N
    (B) 56 N
    (C) 54.5 N
    (D) 53.5 N   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Gravitational Force
F = mg
$\Rightarrow$ F = 5 $\times$ 10.7 = 53.5 N

Q12. The acceleration due to gravity at the North Pole of Neptune is approximately 10.7 m/s^2. Neptune has a mass 1.0 $ \times $ 10^26 kg and radius 2.5 $ \times $ 10^4 km and rotates once around its axis in about 16 h. What is the apparent weight of a 5.0-kg object at Neptune‚Äôs equator?
    (A) 50 N
    (B) 52 N   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 49 N
    (D) 51 N
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Apparent weight at equator = m(g - a)
g = 10.7m/sec^2
a = centripetal accel. at equator = R$\omega^2$
216 hr = 57600s
$\omega=\frac{2 \pi}{T}=\frac{2 \pi}{57600 s}=1.1 \times 10^{-4} \mathrm{rad} / \mathrm{sec}$
a = R$\omega^2$ = 25 $\times$ 10^6m $\times$ (1.1 $\times$ 10^‚àí4)^2 
= 0.297 $\approx$ 0.30m/sec^2
App. weight = m(g - a) = 5(10.7 - 0.3) = 52 N

Q13. Neutron stars, such as the one at the center of the Crab Nebula, have about the same mass as our sun but have a much smaller diameter. If you weigh 675 N on the earth, what would you weigh at the surface of a neutron star that has the same mass as our sun and a diameter of 20 km?
    (A) 9.36 $ \times $ 10^13 N
    (B) 9.16 $ \times $ 10^13 N   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 9.06 $ \times $ 10^13 N
    (D) 9.26 $ \times $ 10^13 N
    Answer: B
    Explanation: W = 675 N 
Radius of Neutron star = $20\over 2$ = 10 km = 10000 m
We need to find g on the Neutron star, so:
g_neutron = $GM\over r^2$
M = 1.99 $\times$ 10^30kg
r = 10000 m
$\Rightarrow g_{\text {neutron }}=\frac{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 1.99 \times 10^{30}}{(10000)^{2}}$
$\Rightarrow$ g_neutron = 667 $\times$ 199 $\times$ 10^7 
$\Rightarrow$ g_neutron = 132733 $\times$ 10^7
Weight on earth = 675 N
$\Rightarrow$ mg_earth = 675 N (Given)
$\Rightarrow$ m = 675/ 9.8 = 68.87 kg 
Weight on saturn = m $\times$ g_neutron = 68.87 $\times$ 132733 $\times$ 10^7 = 9.16 $\times$ 10^13 N

Q14. Moon has a mass of 7.36 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{{\rm{22}}}}$ kg and a radius of 1.74 $ \times $ 10^6 m. Calculate the acceleration due to gravity on the moon.
    (A) 1.82 m/s^2
    (B) 1.22 m/s^2
    (C) 1.42 m/s^2
    (D) 1.62 m/s^2   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: We know
g = $GM\over r^2$
G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^‚àí11 Nm^2kg^‚àí2
M = 7.36 $\times$ 1022kgr = 1.74 $\times$ 10^6 m
$\Rightarrow$ $\mathrm{g}=\frac{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 7.36 \times 10^{22}}{\left(1.74 \times 10^{6}\right)^{2}}$
$\Rightarrow$ g = $\frac{6.67 \times 7.36 \times 10^{11}}{1.74 \times 1.74 \times 10^{12}}$
$\Rightarrow$ g = 1.62 m/sec^2.

Q15. The space shuttle releases a 470-kg communications satellite while in an orbit that is 280 km above the surface of the Earth. A rocket engine on the satellite boosts it into a geosynchronous orbit, which is an orbit in which the satellite stays directly over a single location on the Earth. How much energy did the engine have to provide?
    (A) 1.29 $ \times $ 10^10 J
    (B) 1.39 $ \times $ 10^10 J
    (C) 1.09 $ \times $ 10^10 J
    (D) 1.19 $ \times $ 10^10 J   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Period of the rocket in geosynchronous orbit is the same as that of the earth:
That is T = 1day = 24hours = 24 $\times$ 60 $\times$ 60 sec = 8.64 $\times$ 10^4 s
From Kepler's 3^rd law
 T^2 = K_Er^3_GS
Where K_E = $\frac{{4{\pi ^2}}}{{G{M_E}}}$ = 9.89 $\times$ 10^-14s^2/m^3
Therefore the geosynchronous radius is
 ${r_{GS}} = \root 3 \of {\frac{{{T^2}}}{{{K_E}}}} = \root 3 \of {\frac{{{{\left( {8.64 \times {{10}^4}} \right)}^2}}}{{9.89 \times {{10}^{ - 14}}}}} $ = 4.23 $\times$ 10^7m
Because the initial position before the boost is 280 km = 2.8 $\times$ 10^5 m
and the radius of the Earth is 6,370 km = 6.37 $\times$ 10^6 m
Therefore r_i = R_E + 2.80 $\times$ 10^5 m = 6.65 $\times$ 10^6 m
The total energy needed to boost the satellite at the geosynchronous radius is the difference of total energy before and after the boost 
$E = - \frac{{G{M_E}{m_s}}}{2}\left( {\frac{1}{{{r_{GS}}}} - \frac{1}{{{r_i}}}} \right)$
$= - \frac{{6.67 \times {{10}^{ - 11}} \times 5.98 \times {{10}^{24}} \times 470}}{2}$$\left( {\frac{1}{{4.23 \times {{10}^7}}} - \frac{1}{{6.65 \times {{10}^6}}}} \right) $= 1.19 $\times$ 10^10J

Q1. Calculate the escape speed from the Earth for a 5000-kg spacecraft. mass of the earth = 6.0 $ \times $ 10^24 kg; radius of the earth = 6.4 $ \times $ 10^6 m; G = 6.67 $ \times $ 10^‚àí11 N m^2 kg$^{-{\rm{2}}}$.
    (A) 1.32 $ \times $ 10^4 m/s
    (B) 1.12 $ \times $ 10^4 m/s   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 1.52 $ \times $ 10^4 m/s
    (D) 1.72 $ \times $ 10^4 m/s
    Answer: B
    Explanation: We know, V_esc = $\sqrt{\frac{2 G M}{R}}$
Here G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^-11Nm^2kg^-2
M = 6 $\times$ 10^24 kg
R = 6.4 $\times$ 10^6 m
$\Rightarrow V_{e s c}=\sqrt{\frac{2 G M}{R}}$
$=\sqrt{\frac{2 \times 6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 6 \times 10^{24}}{6.4 \times 10^{6}}}$
$=\sqrt{\frac{12 \times 6.67 \times 10^{7}}{6.4}}$
= 1.12 $\times$ 10^4 m/sec

Q2. Determine the kinetic energy a 5000-kg spacecraft must have in order to escape the Earth‚Äôs gravitational field. Mass of the earth = 6.0 $ \times $ 10^24 kg; radius of the earth = 6.4 $ \times $ 10^6 m; G = 6.67 $ \times $ 10^-11 N m^2 kg^-2.
    (A) 3.04 $ \times $ 10^11J
    (B) 3.34 $ \times $ 10^11J
    (C) 3.53 $ \times $ 10^11J
    (D) 3.14 $ \times $ 10^11J   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Kinetic energy required for spacecraft to escape the earth‚Ä≤s gravitational field
$=\frac{1}{2} m_{s} v_{e s c}^{2}$
We know, $V_{e s c}=\sqrt{\frac{2 G M}{R}}$
Here G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^-11Nm^2kg^-2
M = 6 $\times$ 10^24 kg
R = 6.4 $\times$ 10^6 m
$\Rightarrow V_{e s c}=\sqrt{\frac{2 G M}{R}}$
$=\sqrt{\frac{2 \times 6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 6 \times 10^{24}}{6.4 \times 10^{6}}}$
$=\sqrt{\frac{12 \times 6.67 \times 10^{7}}{6.4}}$
= 1.12 $\times$ 10^4 m/sec. Kinetic energy required for spacecraft to escape the earth‚Ä≤s gravitational field = ${1\over 2}m_sv^2_{esc}$
Here m_s = mass of spacecraft = 5000 kg
$\therefore$ K.E = (1/2) $\times$ 5000 $\times$ (1.12 $\times$ 10^4 )^2J
$\Rightarrow$ K.E = 2500 $\times$( 1.12 $\times$ 10^4 )^2J
$\Rightarrow$ K.E = 3.14 $\times$ 10^11 J

Q3. The asteroid Toro has a radius of about 5.0 km. Assuming that the density of Toro is the same as that of the earth (5.5 g/cm3) find its total mass and find the acceleration due to gravity at its surface. Mass of the earth = 6.0 $ \times $ 10^24 kg; radius of the earth = 6.4 $ \times $ 10^6 m; G = 6.67 $ \times $ 10^‚àí11 N m^2 kg^-2.
    (A) 2.7 $ \times $ 10^15 kg, 0.0077 m/s^2
    (B) 2.9 $ \times $ 10^15 kg, 0.0077 m/s^2   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 2.5 $ \times $ 10^15 kg, 0.0077 m/s^2
    (D) 3.1 $ \times $ 10^15 kg, 0.0077 m/s^2
    Answer: B
    Explanation: We can calculate the mass of Toro by mass density relationship:
 Mass = Volume $\times$ Density
 $\Rightarrow M_{t o r o}=\frac{4}{3} \pi R_{t o r o}^{3} \times \rho_{t o r o}$
 Here R_toro = 5.0 km = 5000 m
 œÅtoro = 5.5 g/cm^3 = 55 $\times$ 10^2kg/m3
 $\Rightarrow M_{t o r o}=\frac{4}{3} \pi(5000)^{3} \times 55 \times 10^{2}$
 $\Rightarrow M_{\text {toro}}=\frac{4}{3} \times \frac{22}{7} \times 125 \times 10^{9} \times 55 \times 10^{2}$
 $\Rightarrow$ M_toro = 2.9 $\times$ 10^15 kg
 we can find the acceleration due to gravity at the surface of Toro by the following relation:
 $$$g=\frac{G M_{\text {toro}}}{R_{\text {toro}}^{2}}$
 $\Rightarrow g=\frac{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 2.9 \times 10^{15}}{(5000)^{2}}$
 $\Rightarrow g=\frac{19.34 \times 10^{4}}{25 \times 10^{6}}$
 $\Rightarrow$ g = 0.77 $\times$ 10^‚àí2 = 0.0077 m/sec^2.

Q4. The asteroid Toro has a radius of about 5.0 km. assuming that the density of Toro is the same as that of the earth (5.5 g/cm^3) Suppose an object is to be placed in a circular orbit around Toro, with a radius just slightly larger than the asteroid‚Äôs radius. What is the speed of the object?. Mass of the earth = 6.0 $ \times $ 10^24 kg; radius of the earth = 6.4 $ \times $ 10^6 m; G = 6.67 $ \times $ 10^-11 Nm^2 kg^-2.
    (A) 6.8 m/s
    (B) 6.4 m/s
    (C) 6.2 m/s   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 6.6 m/s
    Answer: C
    Explanation: We know Orbital velocity:
V_orbital = $=\sqrt{\frac{G M_{t o r o}}{r}}$ .....(1)
We can calculate the mass of Toro by mass density relationship:
Mass = Volume $\times$ Density
$\Rightarrow$ $M_{\text {toro}}=\frac{4}{3} \pi R_{\text {toro}}^{3} \times \rho_{\text {toro}}$
Here R_toro = 5.0 km = 5000 m 
$\rho_{\text {toro}}=5.5 \mathrm{g} / \mathrm{cm}^{3}=55 \times 10^{2} \mathrm{kg} / \mathrm{m}^{3}$
$\Rightarrow $ M_toro = (4/3)$\pi$(5000)^3 $\times$ 55 $\times$ 10^2
$\Rightarrow $ M_toro = (4/3) $\times$ (22/7) $\times$ 125 $\times$ 10^9 $\times$ 55 $\times$ 10^2 $\Rightarrow $ M_toro = 2.9 $\times$ 10^15kg
Putting Value Of M_toro,G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^-11and r $\approx$ R_toro = 5000 in Eqn (1)
$V_{o r b i t a l}=\sqrt{\frac{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 2.9 \times 10^{15}}{5000}}$
$\Rightarrow V_{\text {orbital}}=\sqrt{\frac{19.34 \times 10^{4}}{5 \times 10^{3}}}$
$\Rightarrow V_{\text {orbital}}=\sqrt{3.886 \times 10}$
$\Rightarrow V_{\text {orbital}}=\sqrt{38.86}$ = 6.2m/sec

Q5. The effective value of acceleration due to gravity is $\frac{g}{4}$ at the depth(in respect of R = radius of earth):
    (A) $\frac{{3R}}{4}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\frac{R}{4}$
    (C) $\frac{R}{2}$
    (D) R
    Answer: A
    Explanation: we know :
g‚Ä≤ = $g\left( {1 - \frac{d}{R}} \right)$
$ \Rightarrow \frac{g}{4} = g\left( {1 - \frac{d}{R}} \right)$
$\Rightarrow d = \frac{{3R}}{4}$

Q6. Consider a spacecraft in an elliptical orbit around the earth. At the low point, or perigee, of its orbit, it is 400 km above the earth‚Äôs surface; at the high point, or apogee, it is 4000 km above the earth‚Äôs surface. What is the period of the spacecraft‚Äôs orbit?
    (A) 7910 s   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 6910 s
    (C) 7510 s
    (D) 7310 s
    Answer: A
    Explanation: At Apogee(High Point):
R_P = R_E + h_P = 6.38 $\times$ 10^6m + 400 $\times$ 10^3m = 6.38 $\times$ 10^6m + 0.40 $\times$ 10^6m
At Perigee(Low Point):
R_a = R_E + h_a = 6.38 $\times$ 10^6m + 4000 $\times$ 10^3m 
= 6.38 $\times$ 10^6m + 4 $\times$ 10^6m = 10.38 $\times$ 10^6 m
$\mathrm{a}=\frac{R_{p}+R_{a}}{2}=8.58 \times 10^{6} \mathrm{m}$
$\mathrm{T}=\frac{2 \pi \mathrm{a}^{\frac{3}{2}}}{\sqrt{\mathrm{GM}}_{E}}$; M_E= 5.97 $\times$ 10^24Kg;G = 6.67 $\times$ 10^‚àí11Nm^2kg^-2
$\Rightarrow T=\frac{2 \times \frac{22}{7} \times\left(8.58 \times 10^{6}\right)^{\frac{3}{2}}}{\sqrt{6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 5.97 \times 10^{24}}}$
$\Rightarrow$ T = 7.91 $\times$ 10^3sec = 7910 sec

Q7. Consider a spacecraft in an elliptical orbit around the earth. At the low point, or perigee, of its orbit, it is 400 km above the earth‚Äôs surface; at the high point, or apogee, it is 4000 km above the earth‚Äôs surface. Using conservation of angular momentum, find the ratio of the spacecraft‚Äôs speed at perigee to its speed at apogee.
    (A) 1.22
    (B) 1.32
    (C) 1.64
    (D) 1.53   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: At Apogee(High Point):
R_a = R_E + h_a = 6.38 $\times$ 10^6m + 4000 $\times$ 10^3m
= 6.38 $\times$ 10^6m + 4 $\times$ 10^6m 
= 10.38 $\times$ 10^6m
At Perigee(Low Point):
R_p = R_E + h_p
= 6.38 $\times$ 10^6m + 400 $\times$ 10^3m
= 6.38 $\times$ 10^6m + 0.4 $\times$ 10^6m 
= 6.78 $\times$ 10^6 m
a = $\frac{R_{p}+R_{a}}{2}$ = 8.58 $\times$ 10^6m
Angular momentum, L is conserved because no torque acts on on the system i.e.
L = mv_aR_a = mv_pR_p
Thus, $\frac{v_{p}}{v_{a}}=\frac{R_{a}}{R_{p}}=\frac{10.38 \times 10^{6}}{6.78 \times 10^{6}}$ = 1.531

Q8. Consider a spacecraft in an elliptical orbit around the earth. At the low point, or perigee, of its orbit, it is 400 km above the earth‚Äôs surface; at the high point, or apogee, it is 4000 km above the earth‚Äôs surface. Using conservation of energy, find the speed at perigee and the speed at apogee.
    (A) 3510 m/s (apogee), 7430 m/s (perigee)
    (B) 5510 m/s (perigee), 8430 m/s (apogee)
    (C) 5510 m/s (apogee), 8430 m/s (perigee)   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 4510 m/s (apogee), 8430 m/s (perigee)
    Answer: C
    Explanation: At Apogee(High Point):
R_P = R_E + h_P = 6.38 $\times$ 10^6m + 400 $\times$ 10^3m 
= 6.38 $\times$ 10^6m + 0.40 $\times$ 10^6m
At Perigee(Low Point):
R_a = R_E + h_a = 6.38 $\times$ 10^6m + 4000 $\times$ 10^3m
= 6.38 $\times$ 10^6m + 4 $\times$ 10^6m 
= 10.38 $\times$ 10^6 m
$a=\frac{R_{p}+R_{a}}{2}$ = 8.58 $\times$ 10^6m
Angular momentum, L is conserved because no torque acts on the system
i.e L = mv_aR_a= mv_pR_p
Thus $\frac{v_{p}}{v_{a}}=\frac{R_{a}}{R_{p}}$ = 1.513
Energy is conserved because non - conservative work is zero.
Thus K_a + U_a = K_p + U_p 
$\Rightarrow$ K_p - K_a = U_a - U_p
$\Rightarrow \frac{1}{2} m v_{p}^{2}-\frac{1}{2} m v_{a}^{2}=\frac{-G M m}{R_{a}}-\left(\frac{-G M m}{R_{p}}\right)$
$\Rightarrow v_{p}^{2}-v_{a}^{2}=2 G M\left(\frac{1}{R_{P}}-\frac{1}{R_{a}}\right)$
But, As we know $\frac{v_{p}}{v_{a}}$ $\Rightarrow$ v_p = 1.531 $\times$ v_a - (1)
Thus, $\left[(1.531)^{2}-1\right] v_{a}^{2}=2 G M\left(\frac{1}{R_{p}}-\frac{1}{R_{a}}\right)$
Solving for V_a , we get 
v_a = 5.51 $\times$ 10^3m/sec = 5510 m/sec 
Put value of v_a in eqn(1),
we get v_p = 8.43 $\times$ 10^3m/sec = 8430 m/sec

Q9. Consider a spacecraft in an elliptical orbit around the earth. At the low point, or perigee, of its orbit, it is 400 km above the earth‚Äôs surface; at the high point, or apogee, it is 4000 km above the earth‚Äôs surface. Using conservation of energy, find the speed at perigee and the speed at apogee. It is necessary to have the spacecraft escape from the earth completely.
    (A) 7840 m/sec(perigee),5760 m/sec(apogee)
    (B) 10840 m/sec(perigee),8760 m/sec(apogee)   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 9840 m/sec(perigee),9760 m/sec(apogee)
    (D) 8840 m/sec(perigee),6760 m/sec(apogee)
    Answer: B
    Explanation: To escape Earth, we need total energy of zero.
(E_final = 0 because U $\longrightarrow$ 0 as R $\longrightarrow$ $\infty$ and K $\longrightarrow$ 0 as v = 0 at R $\longrightarrow$ $\infty$)
So,
K_p + U_p = 0
Looking for the new velocity at perigee;
$\frac{1}{2} m v^{2}_{p, e s c a p c}=\frac{G M m}{R_{p}}$
$v_{p, \text { escape }}=\sqrt{\frac{2G M}{R_{p}}}=\sqrt{\frac{2\times 6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 5.97 \times 10^{24}}{6.78 \times 10^{6}}}$
= 1.084 $\times$ 10^4m/sec = 10840 m/sec
The similar calculation at apogee gives
$v_{a, \text {escape}}=\sqrt{\frac{2G M}{R_{a}}}=\sqrt{\frac{2\times6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 5.97 \times 10^{24}}{10.38 \times 10^{6}}}$
= 8.76 $\times$ 10^3m/sec = 8760 m/sec

Q10. Consider a spacecraft in an elliptical orbit around the earth. At the low point, or perigee, of its orbit, it is 400 km above the earth‚Äôs surface; at the high point, or apogee, it is 4000 km above the earth‚Äôs surface. It is necessary to have the spacecraft escape from the earth completely. If the spacecraft‚Äôs rockets are fired at perigee, by how much would the speed have to be increased to achieve this? If the spacecraft‚Äôs rockets are fired at apogee, by how much would the speed have to be increased to achieve this?
    (A) 3410 m/sec(perigee), 6761 m/sec(apogee)
    (B) 5410 m/sec(perigee), 8761 m/sec(apogee)
    (C) 2410 m/sec(perigee),8761 m/sec(apogee)   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 4410 m/sec(perigee), 7761 m/sec(apogee)
    Answer: C
    Explanation: To escape Earth, we need total energy of zero. (E_final = 0 because U $\rightarrow$ 0 as R $\rightarrow$ $\infty$ and K $\rightarrow$ 0 as v = 0 at R $\rightarrow$ $\infty$)
So,
K_p + U_p = 0
Looking for new velocity at perigee
$\frac{1}{2} m v_{p, e s c a p e}^{2}=\frac{G M m}{R_{p}}$
$v_{p, \text { escape }}=\sqrt{\frac{2G M}{R_{p}}}=\sqrt{\frac{2\times6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 5.97 \times 10^{24}}{6.78 \times 10^{6}}}$
= 1.084 √ó 104m/sec = 10840 m/sec Similar calculation at apogee gives
$v_{a, \text {escape}}=\sqrt{\frac{2G M}{R_{a}}}=\sqrt{\frac{2\times 6.67 \times 10^{-11} \times 5.97 \times 10^{24}}{10.38 \times 10^{6}}}$ = 8.76 $\times$ 10^3m/sec = 8760 m/sec
If the rocket are fired at perigee,
Increase of speed = v_p,escape - v_p = 2.41 $\times$ 10^3m/sec = 2410 m/sec 
If the rocket are fired at apogee,
Increase of speed = v_a,escape - v_p = 8.761 $\times$ 10^3 m/sec = 8761 m/sec

Q11. A body of mass m is taken from the earth surface to the height h equal to the radius of the earth, the increase in potential energy will be:
    (A) 2mgR
    (B) mgR
    (C) $\frac{1}{4}$mgR
    (D) $\frac{1}{2}$mgR   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: P.E of the body on surface of the earth is givem n by = $\frac {-GMm}{R}$
P.E of body at a height h from the surface of earth is give by= $\frac {-GMm}{(R+h)}$
Difference in P.E is given by = $\frac {-GMm}{(R+h)}-\frac {-GMm}{R}$
= GMm$(\frac {1}{R}-\frac {1}{(R+h)})$
= $mgR^2 \frac {h}{(R(R+h))}$
Because, g = $\frac {GM}{R^2}$
When h = R
Change in P.E = $\frac {1}{2}$

Q12. If the radius of earth reduces by 4% and density remains the same then escape velocity will be:
    (A) Reduce by 2%
    (B) Increase by 2%
    (C) Increase by 4%
    (D) Reduce by 4%   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Escape velocity v_e $\propto$R$\sqrt \rho$ and if density remains constant $\Rightarrow$ $ {v_e}{\text{ }}\propto$R
So if the radius reduces by 4% then escape velocity also reduces by 4%

Q13. An object in the shape of a thin ring has radius 'a' and mass M. A uniform sphere with mass m and radius R is placed with its center at a distance x to the right of the center of the ring, along a line through the center of the ring, and perpendicular to its plane. What is the gravitational force that the sphere exerts on the ring-shaped object?
    (A) $\frac{\operatorname{Gmx}}{\left(\mathrm{a}^2+\mathrm{x}^2\right)^{\frac{3}{2}}}$
    (B) $\frac{\text { GMm }}{\left(\mathrm{a}^2+\mathrm{x}^2\right)^{\frac{3}{2}}}$
    (C) $\frac{\text { GMmx }}{\left(\mathrm{a}^2+\mathrm{x}^2\right)^{\frac{3}{2}}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{\text { GMmx }}{\left(\mathrm{a}^2+2 \mathrm{x}^2\right)^{\frac{3}{2}}}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Consider a mass segment dM = $\left(\frac{M}{2 \pi a}\right) d l=\left(\frac{M}{2 \pi a}\right) a d \phi$ on the ring. Whereas $\phi$is the integrated angle. The potential energy between this segment and the mass m is given by
dU = $-G \frac{m d M}{\sqrt{a^{2}+x^{2}}}=-G \frac{m \frac{M}{2 \pi} d \phi}{\sqrt{a^{2}+x^{2}}}$
So that
$U=\int d U=-\int_{0}^{2 \pi} G \frac{m \frac{M}{2 \pi} d \phi}{\sqrt{a^{2}+x^{2}}}$
$=-G \frac{m M}{\sqrt{a^{2}+x^{2}}}$
$F=-\frac{d U}{d x}=-\frac{d}{d x}\left[-G \frac{m M}{\sqrt{a^{2}+x^{2}}}\right]=\frac{G M m x}{\left(a^{2}+x^{2}\right)^{\frac{3}{2}}}$

Q14. If a film of width L is stretched in the longitudinal direction a distance d by force F, surface tension is given by:
    (A) S = $\frac{F}{2L}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) S = $\frac{F}{4L}$
    (C) S = $\frac{F}{L}$
    (D) S = $\frac{F}{3L}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Let L be the width of the film. Since the film has two surfaces, the total length along which the surface force acts on the slider is 2L. The surface tension S in the film is defined as the ratio of the surface force F to the length d (perpendicular to the force) along which the force acts
$S=\frac{F}{d}$
Hence, in the case, d = 2L
$S=\frac{F}{2L}$

Q15. Derive an expression for the work required to move an Earth satellite of mass m from a circular orbit of radius 2 ${{\rm{R}}_{\rm{E}}}$ to one of radius 3 ${{\rm{R}}_{\rm{E}}}$.
    (A) $\frac{G M_E}{12 R_E}$
    (B) $\frac{G M_E m}{24 R_E}$
    (C) $\frac{G m}{24 R_E}$
    (D) $\frac{G M_E m}{12 R E}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The energy of a satellite in low orbit:
$E_{L}=\frac{-G M m}{2 r}=\frac{-G M_{E} m}{2\left(2 R_{E}\right)}=\frac{-G M_{E} m}{4 R_{E}} $
The energy of a satellite in high orbit:
$$$E_{H}=\frac{-G M m}{2 r}=\frac{-G M_{E} m}{2\left(3 R_{E}\right)}=\frac{-G M_{E} m}{6 R_{E}}$
Energy required to move a satellite from the orbit of radius 2R_E to orbit of radius 3R_E:
$=\Delta E$ = E_H ‚àí E_L = $\frac{-G M_{E} m}{6 R_{E}}-\left(\frac{-G M_{E} m}{4 R_{E}}\right) \Rightarrow \Delta E=\frac{G M_{E} m}{12 R_{E}}$
