Q1. A perfectly rigid body is one:
    (A) which starts flowing like water on the application of force.
    (B) which does not move on the application of force.
    (C) whose shape and size do not change on application of force.   <-- CORRECT
    (D) whose shape and size change on application of force.
    Answer: C
    Explanation: A perfectly rigid body is hypothetical in nature but for some phenomena ( in rotational bodies ) we assume bodies are perfectly rigid i.e. the intermolecular forces are always in equilibrium irrespective of the external forces due to which their shape and size are constant.

Q2. Elasticity is the property of a body, by virtue of which:
    (A) it changes size and shape when the force is applied and stays in that shape when the applied force is removed.
    (B) it tends to regain its original size and shape when the applied force is removed.   <-- CORRECT
    (C) it is distorted or stretches without the application of force.
    (D) it remains in original size and shape when the force is applied.
    Answer: B
    Explanation: When an external force is applied to the solid bodies, the solid bodies get deformed. The atoms or molecules are displaced from their equilibrium positions causing a change in the interatomic (or intermolecular) distances. When the deforming force is removed, the interatomic forces tend to drive them back to their original positions. Thus the body regains its original shape and size.

Q3. For the earth the escape speed in terms of g, the acceleration due to gravity and R_E, the radius of the earth is
    (A) $\sqrt {g{R_E}} $
    (B) $\sqrt {2g} $
    (C) $\sqrt {2g{R_E}} $   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\sqrt {5g{R_E}} $
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The work done to displace a body from the surface of earth (r = R_e) to infinity ( $r = \infty $ ) is
$W = G{{Mm} \over {R_e{\rm{ }}}}$
If v_e is the required escape velocity then kinetic energy which should be given to the body is ${1 \over 2}{\rm{ }}m{v_e}^2$
$\therefore {1 \over 2}{\rm{ }}m{v_e}^2 = G{{Mm} \over R_e}$
$ \Rightarrow {v_e} = \sqrt {2g{R_e}} $

Q4. The importance of the elastic behavior of materials is:
    (A) that it is useful in building sling shots.
    (B) that it enables a safe and sound design of bridges, buildings, machinery parts.   <-- CORRECT
    (C) that it gives methods for understanding materials.
    (D) that it is useful in making springs.
    Answer: B
    Explanation: More the elastic a material is, more it has the property to regain its original position which is required in construction works.

Q5. When a solid is deformed:
    (A) only the atoms or molecules at some points move from their equilibrium position.
    (B) the atoms or molecules do not move from their equilibrium position.
    (C) only the atoms or molecules of the surface move from their equilibrium position.
    (D) all the atoms or molecules are displaced from their equilibrium positions causing a change in interatomic (or intermolecular) distances.   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: External force permanently disturbed the equilibrium position of the interatomic (or intermolecular) forces between the particles of solid bodies.

Q6. Stress is:
    (A) three-point average of forces
    (B) force per unit length
    (C) restoring force per unit area   <-- CORRECT
    (D) total applied force
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Stress is defined as restoring force per unit area.

Q7. The S.I. unit of stress is:
    (A) Pascal   <-- CORRECT
    (B) Joule
    (C) Watt
    (D) Newton
    Answer: A
    Explanation: As Stress = $Force \over Area$
Putting units, we get
Stress = N/m^2
= Nm^-2 or Pascal 
So SI Unit of Stress is Pascal

Q8. If a wire has an initial length L and becomes L + $\Delta {\rm{L}}$ on the application of force, the longitudinal strain is given by:
    (A) $\frac{\Delta L}{3L}$
    (B) $\frac{\Delta L}L$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac{\Delta L}{2L}$
    (D) $\Delta {\rm{L}}$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The longitudinal strain is defined as the ratio of change in length $\Delta$L and natural length L i.e. longitudinal strain = $\frac{\triangle L}L$

Q9. Referring to figure shearing strain is defined as:
    (A) cot$\;\theta$
    (B) sin$\;\theta$
    (C) tan$\;\theta$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) cos$\;\theta$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Shear strain is measured as a change in angle between lines that were originally perpendicular.Thus from the above figure
Shearing strain $ = \frac{{\Delta x}}{L}$
and in a triangle, $\frac{{\Delta x}}{L} = \tan \theta $
thus shearing strain = tan$ \theta $

Q10. The volumetric strain is defined as K:
    (A) as the ratio of change in volume ($\Delta $V) to thrice the original volume V.
    (B) as the ratio of change in volume ($\Delta $V) to the original volume V.   <-- CORRECT
    (C) as the ratio of change in volume ($\Delta $V) to twice the original volume V.
    (D) as the change in volume $\Delta $V.
    Answer: B
    Explanation: By definition of volumetric strain, it is the ratio of change in volume of the body to its original volume 
= $\Delta V \over V$
Hence volume strain is defined 
as the ratio of change in volume ($\Delta $V) to the original volume V.

Q11. According to Hooke‚Äôs law:
    (A) for small deformations, the stress is proportional to the square of strain.
    (B) for large deformations, the stress and strain are proportional to each other.
    (C) for small deformations, the stress and strain are inversely proportional to each other.
    (D) for small deformations, the stress and strain are proportional to each other.   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: By definition of Hooke's law within the elastic limit, the stress developed is directly proportional to the strain produced in a body stress $\propto $ strain.

Q12. With reference to figure the elastic zone is:
    (A) oa   <-- CORRECT
    (B) bc
    (C) cd
    (D) ab
    Answer: A
    Explanation: In region 'oa' strain is very small and stress is proportional to the strain. In this region the Hooke's law is valid i.e. if the applied force is removed at any point between ' o ' to 'a' the body will acquire its natural length. Thus this region is called the elastic zone.

Q13. With reference to figure the plastic zone is:
    (A) ab
    (B) cd   <-- CORRECT
    (C) oa
    (D) bc
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The plastic zone represents where the body does not regain its original shape and size.
This is due to crossing of the elastic limit beyond the yield point. 
Hence the plastic zone should be 'cd'.

Q14. With reference to figure the yield point, corresponds to:
    (A) c   <-- CORRECT
    (B) d
    (C) b
    (D) a
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Beyond point 'c' in the wire starts showing an increase in the strain without an increase in stress. Thus, the point 'c' at which the wire yields to the applied stress and begins to flow down is called the yield point.

Q15. With reference to figure the yield, strength point corresponds to:
    (A) C
    (B) D
    (C) B   <-- CORRECT
    (D) A
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The yield point is a point on the curve where the body deforms with a negligible amount of stress.
Hence it should be B

Q1. With reference to the figure, the tensile stress corresponds to stress at the point.
    (A) E
    (B) C
    (C) D   <-- CORRECT
    (D) B
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The point D on the graph is the ultimate tensile strength of the material. Beyond this point, additional strain is produced even by a reduced applied force and fracture occurs at point E.
After point D thinning of wire started and finally the wire breaks.
So D shows tensile strength.

Q2. Elastomers are materials:
    (A) which can be stretched without corresponding stress
    (B) which cannot be stretched to cause large strains
    (C) which can be stretched to cause large strains   <-- CORRECT
    (D) which cannot be stretched to beyond the elastic limit
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Substances like tissue of aorta (Present in the heart), rubber, etc. which can be stretched to cause large strains are called elastomers. They show a large plastic range in the stress-strain curve.

Q3. Young‚Äôs modulus Y is:
    (A) the ratio of shear stress $\left( \sigma \right)$ to the longitudinal strain $\left( {\theta {\rm{ }}} \right)$.
    (B) the ratio of shear strain $\left( {\theta {\rm{ }}} \right)$ to the longitudinal stress $\left( \sigma \right)$.
    (C) the ratio of longitudinal strain $\left( {\varepsilon {\rm{ }}} \right)$ to the tensile (or compressive) stress $\left( \sigma \right)$.
    (D) the ratio of tensile (or compressive) stress $\left( \sigma \right)$ to the longitudinal strain $\left( {\varepsilon {\rm{ }}} \right)$.   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The ratio of stress and strain is called modulus of elasticity is the characteristic of the material. they are (a) Young's modulus (b) Shear Modulus (c) Bulk modulus
Young's Modulus is denoted by Y and is given by the ratio of tensile (or compressive) stress $\left( \sigma \right)$ to the longitudinal strain $\left( {\varepsilon {\rm{ }}} \right)$ 
Y = tensile stress/longitudinal strain
Y = $ \sigma \over \epsilon$

Q4. Shear modulus or modulus of rigidity is:
    (A) the ratio of shearing stress to the corresponding shearing strain.   <-- CORRECT
    (B) the ratio of shearing strain to the corresponding shearing stress.
    (C) the ratio of shearing stress to the corresponding lateral strain.
    (D) the ratio of longitudinal stress to the corresponding shearing strain.
    Answer: A
    Explanation: The ratio of shearing stress to the corresponding shearing strain is called the shear modulus of the material and is represented by G. It is also called the modulus of rigidity.
G = $shearing \ stress (\sigma) \over shearing \ strain$

Q5. Bulk modulus is:
    (A) the ratio of longitudinal stress to the corresponding hydraulic strain.
    (B) the ratio of lateral stress to the corresponding hydraulic strain.
    (C) the ratio of hydraulic strain to the corresponding hydraulic stress.
    (D) the ratio of hydraulic stress to the corresponding hydraulic strain.   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: When a body is submerged in a fluid, it undergoes hydraulic stress (equal in magnitude to the hydraulic pressure). This leads to a decrease in the volume of the body thus producing a strain called volume strain. The ratio of hydraulic stress to the corresponding hydraulic strain is called bulk modulus. It is denoted by symbol B.
Hence, bulk modulus = hydraulic or normal stress/ volumetric strain
$B = {- p\over ({\delta V \over V})}$ 
The negative sign indicates the fact that with an increase in pressure, a decrease in volume occurs.

Q6. For a rope of yield strength S_y loaded in tension with weight Mg the minimum area A of the rope should be:
    (A) A $ \geq $ $\frac{M g}{3 S_y}$
    (B) $\mathrm{A} \geq \frac{M g}{\mathrm{~S}_{\mathrm{y}}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) A $ \geq $ $\frac{M g}{2 S_y}$
    (D) A $ \geq $ MgS_y
    Answer: B
    Explanation: We know that Yield strength times the Area gives the weight of the body.
As Yield strength = $\frac{\text { weight of body }}{\text { area }}$
For safety purposes
Yield strength $ \geq $ $\frac{\text { weight of body }}{\text { area }}$
${{\rm{S}}_{\rm{y}}}$ $ \geq $ $\frac{Mg}{A}$
A $ \geq $ $\frac{Mg}{{\rm{S}}_{\rm{y}}}$

Q7. For a bar of length l, breadth b, and depth d as shown in the figure the deflection $\delta $ is given by:
    (A) $\delta=\frac{W l^3}{\left(4 b d^3\right)}$
    (B) $\delta=\frac{W l^3}{\left(4 b d^2 Y\right)}$
    (C) $\delta=\frac{W l^2}{\left(4 b d^3 Y\right)}$
    (D) $\delta=\frac{W l^3}{\left(4 b d^3 Y\right)}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: From $\delta=\frac{W l^3}{\left(4 b d^3 Y\right)}$ , we see that to reduce the bending for a given load, one should use material with a large Young's modulus Y. For a given material, increasing the depth d rather than the breadth b is more effective in reducing the bending, since $\delta$ is proportional to d^-3 and only to b^-1. But on increasing the depth, unless the load is exactly at the right place, the deep bar may bend as shown in the question. To avoid this, a common compromise is the cross-sectional shape is chosen. This shape reduces the weight of the beam without sacrificing the strength and hence reduces the cost.

Q8. Rectangular section is rarely used in beams because:
    (A) stresses are always plastic in rectangular section
    (B) stresses are uniform in a rectangular section
    (C) less material in kilos is required
    (D) more material in kilos is required   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: A pillar with rounded ends supports less load than that with a distributed shape at the ends.

Q9. Columns are loaded in:
    (A) shear
    (B) tension
    (C) compression   <-- CORRECT
    (D) hydraulic stress
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Stress on the columns compresses it.

Q10. Material is said to be ductile if:
    (A) the material cross-section is not significantly reduced at failure.
    (B) the material breaks suddenly at little elongation.
    (C) a large amount of plastic deformation takes place between the elastic limit and the fracture point.   <-- CORRECT
    (D) a fracture occurs soon after the elastic limit is passed.
    Answer: C
    Explanation: If the ultimate strength and fracture points are close in the stress-strain curve, the material is said to be brittle. If they are far apart, the material is said to be ductile and show a large plastic range.

Q11. Material is said to be brittle if:
    (A) a fracture occurs soon after the elastic limit is passed.   <-- CORRECT
    (B) the material cross-section is significantly reduced at failure.
    (C) a large amount of plastic deformation takes place between the elastic limit and the fracture point.
    (D) material elongates a lot before finally breaking.
    Answer: A
    Explanation: If the ultimate strength and fracture points are close, it means a very small plastic range beyond the elastic limit, the material is said to be brittle.

Q12. A steel wire of length 4.7 m and cross-sectional area 3.0 $ \times $ 10^-5 m^2 stretch by the same amount as a copper wire of length 3.5 m and cross-sectional area of 4.0 $ \times $ 10^‚àí5 m^2 under a given load. What is the ratio of Young‚Äôs modulus of steel to that of copper?
    (A) 2.0
    (B) 1.2
    (C) 1.8   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 1.6
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Length of the steel wire, L_1 = 4.7 m
Area of cross-section of the steel wire, A1 = 3.0 $\times$ 10^-5 m^2
Length of the copper wire, L_2 = 3.5 m
Area of cross-section of the copper wire, A_2 = 4.0 $\times$ 10^-5 m^2
Change in length = $\Delta$L_1 = $\Delta$L_2 = $\Delta$L
Force applied in both the cases = F
Young‚Äôs modulus of the steel wire:
Y_1 = ($F_1 \over A_1$) ($L_1 \over \Delta L_1$)
= ($F \over 3 \times 10^{-5}$) ($4.7 \over \Delta L$) ‚Ä¶.(i)
Young‚Äôs modulus of the copper wire:
Y_2 = ($F_2 \over A_2$) ($L_2 \over ŒîL_2 $)
= ($F \over 4 \times 10^{-5}$) ($3.5 \over \Delta L$) ‚Ä¶.(ii)
Dividing (i) by (ii), we get:
$Y_1 \over Y_2$ = $(4.7 \times 4 \times 10^{-5}) \over (3 \times 10^{-5} \times 3.5)$
= 1.79 : 1
The ratio of Young‚Äôs modulus of steel to that of copper is 1.8:1.

Q13. The figure shows the strain-stress curve for a given material. What is Young‚Äôs modulus?
    (A) 9.5 $ \times $ 10^10 N/m^2
    (B) 6.5 $ \times $ 10^10 N/m^2
    (C) 8.5 $ \times $ 10^10 N/m^2
    (D) 7.5 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{{\rm{1}}0}}$ ${\rm{N}}/{{\rm{m}}^{\rm{2}}}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: It is clear from the given graph that for stress 150 $\times$ 10^6 N/m^2, the strain is 0.002
Young's modulus Y = $\frac{stress}{strain}$
=$150 \times 10^{6} \over 0.002$
=7.5 $\times$ 10^10N/m^2

Q14. The figure shows the strain-stress curve for a given material. What is the approximate yield strength for this material?
    (A) 3.2 $ \times $ 10^8 N/m^2
    (B) 3.6 $ \times $ 10^8 N/m^2
    (C) 3.0 $ \times $ 10^8 N/m^2   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 3.4 $ \times $ 10^8 N/m^2
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The yield strength of a material is the maximum stress that the material can sustain without crossing the elastic limit. 
It is clear from the given graph that the approximate yield strength of this material is 300 $\times$ 10^6N/m^2 or 3 $\times$ 10^8N/m^2

Q15. A steel rod 2.0 m long has a cross-sectional area of 0.30 cm^2. It is hung by one end from a support, and a 550-kg milling machine is hung from its other end. Determine the stress on the rod?
    (A) 1.6 $ \times $ 10^8 Pa
    (B) 1.2 $ \times $ 10^8 Pa
    (C) 1.8 $ \times $ 10^8 Pa   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 1.4 $ \times $ 10^8 Pa
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Stress = $F\over A$
F = m $\times$ a
F = 550Kg $\times$ 9.8m/s^2 = 5390N
A = 0.30cm^2 = 0.30 $\times$ 10^-4
Stress = $F\over A$
Stress = $5390\over 0.30$ $\times$ 10^-4 = 1.8 $\times$ 10^8Pa

Q1. A steel rod 2.0 m long has a cross-sectional area of 0.30 cm^2. It is hung by one end from a support, and a 550-kg milling machine is hung from its other end. Determine the resulting strain.
    (A) 8.0 $ \times $ 10^-4
    (B) 10.0 $ \times $ 10^-4
    (C) 9.0 $ \times $ 10^-4   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 7.0 $ \times $ 10^-4
    Answer: C
    Explanation: young‚Ä≤s modulus y = $\frac{\text {stress}}{\text {strain}}$
$\Rightarrow$Strain = $\frac{\operatorname{stress}}{y}$
stress = $\frac{\text {restoring force}}{\text {area}}$
In a given case restoring force will be tension, which will be equal to the weight ‚Ä≤mg‚Ä≤ of the machine. using the standard value of young modulus for steel, y = 2 $\times$ 10^11N/m^2
strain = $\frac{m g}{A \times y}$
$\Rightarrow$ $\frac{550 \times 9.8 \times 10^{4}}{0.30 \times 2 \times 10^{11}}$ = 8.9 $\times$ 10^‚àí4 $\approx$ 9.0 $\times$10^‚àí4

Q2. A steel rod 2.0 m long has a cross-sectional area of 0.30 cm^2. It is hung by one end from a support, and a 550-kg milling machine is hung from its other end. Determine the elongation. Take Young's modulus of steel as 20 $ \times $ 10^10 Pa?
    (A) 2.0 mm
    (B) 1.8 mm   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 1.6 mm
    (D) 2.2 mm
    Answer: B
    Explanation: young‚Ä≤s modulus y = $\frac{{{\text{stress}}}}{{{\text{strain}}}}$ $\Rightarrow \text {strain}=\frac{\text {stress}}{y}$
$\operatorname{stress} =\frac{\text {restoring force}}{\text {area}}$
in a given case restoring force will be tension, which will be equal to the weight ‚Ä≤mg‚Ä≤ of the machine. Using the standard value of young modulus for steel y = 2 $\times$ 10^11N/m^2
strain = $\frac{m g}{A \times y} \Rightarrow \frac{550 \times 9.8 \times 10^{4}}{0.30 \times 2 \times 10^{11}}$ = 8.9 √ó 10^‚àí4 $\approx$ 9.0 $\times$ 10^‚àí4
for given case strain will be longitudinal i.e. $\Delta$LL $\Rightarrow$ $\Delta$LL = 9 $\times$ 10^ - 4(given L = 2.0m) $\Rightarrow$ $\Delta$L = 2.0 $\times$ 9.0 $\times$ 10^‚àí4 $\Delta$L = 1.8mm

Q3. A hydraulic press contains 0.25 m^3 (250 L) of oil. Find the decrease in the volume of the oil when it is subjected to a pressure increase $\Delta {\rm{p}}$ = 1.6 $ \times $ 10^7 Pa(about 160 atm or 2300 psi). The bulk modulus of the oil is B = 5.0 $ \times $ 10^9 Pa?
    (A) -0.31%
    (B) -0.30 %
    (C) -0.33%
    (D) -0.32%   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $\Delta$V = $-V_0\Delta p\over B$ = $-(0.25 m^3 \times 1.6 \times 10^7 Pa)\over5.0 \times 10^9 Pa $
= ‚àí8.0 $\times$ 10^-4 m^3 = ‚àí 0.80L = -8.0 $\times$10^-4 m^3 = -0.80L
Even through the pressure increase is very large, the fractional change in volume is very small.
‚à¥‚ñ≥V/V_0 = $-8\times 10^{-4}\over 0.25$ = -0.0032 or -0.32%

Q4. Two wires of diameter 0.25 cm, one made of steel and the other made of brass are loaded as shown in Figure. The unloaded length of steel wire is 1.5 m and that of brass wire is 1.0 m. Compute the elongation of the brass wire.
    (A) 1.2 $ \times $ 10^-4 m
    (B) 1.1 $ \times $ 10^-4 m
    (C) 1.3 $ \times $ 10^-4 m   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 1.4 $ \times $ 10^-4 m
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Diameter of the wires, d = 0.25 m
Hence, the radius of the wires, r = $\frac{d}{2}$ = 0.125 cm
Length of the steel wire, L_1 = 1.5 m
Length of the brass wire, L_2 = 1.0 m
total restoring force acting on brass wire i.e. tension T_2 = m_2g = 6g
Total restoring force exerted on the steel wire:
T_1 = m_1g + T_2 = (4 + 6) g = 10 $\times$ 9.8 = 98 N
Total restoring force on the brass wire:
T_2 = 6 $\times$ 9.8 = 58.8 N
Young‚Äôs modulus for brass: Y_2
$\Delta$L_2 = Change in the length of the brass wire
A_2 = Area of cross-section of the brass wire = $\pi$r_2^2
Y_2 = $\frac{stress}{strain}$ = $\frac{T_{2} \times L_{2}}{A_{2} \times \Delta L_{2}}$
$\Rightarrow \Delta L_{2}=\frac{58.8 \times 1.0}{3.14 \times\left(0.125 \times 10^{-2}\right)^{2} \times 0.91 \times 10^{11}}$
Elongation of the brass wire = 1.3 $\times$ 10^‚Äì4 m.

Q5. The edge of an aluminum cube is 10 cm long. One face of the cube is firmly fixed to a vertical wall. A mass of 100 kg is then attached to the opposite face of the cube. The shear modulus of aluminum is 25 GPa. What is the vertical deflection of this force?
    (A) 8.0 $ \times $ 10^-6 m
    (B) 4.0 $ \times $ 10^-7 m   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 2.0 $ \times $ 10^-6 m
    (D) 6.0 $ \times $ 10^-6 m
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Edge of the aluminium cube, L = 10 cm = 0.1 m
The mass attached to the cube, m = 100 kg
Shear modulus ($\eta$) of aluminium = 25 GPa = 25 $\times$ 10^9 Pa
shear modulus ($\eta$) = $\frac{shear\;stress}{shear\;strain}=\frac{T\times L}{A\times\Delta L}$
Where,
T = restoring force = Applied force(F) = mg = 100 $\times$ 9.8 = 980 N
A = Area of one of the faces of the cube = 0.1 $\times$ 0.1 = 0.01 m^2
$\Delta$L = Vertical deflection of the cube
shear modulus ($\eta$) = $\frac{shear\;stress}{shear\;strain}=\frac{T\times L}{A\times\Delta L}$
$\Delta L=\frac{T \times L}{\eta \times A}=\frac{980 \times 0.1}{10^{-2} \times 25 \times 10^{9}}$
$\therefore $ $\Delta$L = 3.92 $\times$ 10^‚àí7m $\approx $ 4.0 $\times$ 10^‚àí7m

Q6. A circular steel wire 2.00 m long must stretch no more than 0.25 cm when a tensile force of 400 N is applied to each end of the wire. What minimum diameter is required for the wire?
    (A) 1.9 mm
    (B) 1.43 mm   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 2.48 mm
    (D) 12.4 mm
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Let Young's modulus of steel wire Y
$Y=\frac{s t r e s s}{s t r a i n}=\frac{T \times L}{A \times \Delta L}=$
$A=\frac{T \times L}{Y \times \Delta L}=\frac{400 \times 2.0}{2.0 \times 10^{11} \times 0.25 \times 10^{-2}}$
$\pi$R^2 = 1600 $\times$ 10^‚àí9 .....(A = $\pi$R^2)R = 7.13 $\times$ 10^‚àí4m
diameter D = 2R = 2 $\times$ 7.13 $\times$ 10^‚àí4
D = 1.427 $\times$ 10^‚àí3m$\approx$1.43mm

Q7. A metal rod that is 4.00 m long and 0.500 cm^2 in cross-sectional area is found to stretch 0.20 cm under a tension of 5000 N. What is Young‚Äôs modulus for this metal?
    (A) 2.5 $ \times $ 10^10 Pa
    (B) 2.6 $ \times $ 10^12 Pa
    (C) 2.0 $ \times $ 10^11 Pa   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 2.7 $ \times $ 10^13 Pa
    Answer: C
    Explanation: young modulus = $y=\frac{stress \operatorname{}}{\operatorname{strain} }=\frac{F \times L}{A \times \Delta L}$
$=\frac{5000 \times 4.0}{0.50 \times 10^{-4} \times 0.20 \times 10^{-2}}$
yv = 2.0 $\times$ 10^11N/m^2
y = 2.0 $\times$ 10^11Pa

Q8. In constructing a large mobile, an artist hangs an aluminum sphere of mass 6.0 kg from a vertical steel wire 0.50 m long and 2.5 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 cm^2 in a cross-sectional area. On the bottom of the sphere, he attaches a similar steel wire, from which he hangs a brass cube of mass 10.0 kg. Compute the tensile strain?
    (A) 3.2 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 upper, 2.3 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 lower
    (B) 3.1 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 upper, 2.0 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 lower   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 3.4 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 upper, 2.5 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 lower
    (D) 3.3 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 upper, 2.4 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 lower
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Given mass of aluminium sphere m_sph = 6.0 KG
length of steel wire L_steel = 0.50 m area of steel wire A_steel = 2.5 $\times$ 10^-3 cm^2
mass of cube m_cube = 10 KG
net restoring force in lower wire i.e. tension T_2 will be equal to weight
of cube i.e. T_2 = m_cube = 10g
net restoring force in upper wire i.e. tension T_1
T_1 = m_sph + T_2 = 6g + 10g = 16g
standard value for young modulus of steel y = 2 $\times$ 10^11 N/m^2
y = $\frac{\text { tensile stress }}{\text { tensile strain }}$ for lower wire
$\operatorname{strain}_{l w}=\frac{\text {tensile stress}}{y}=\frac{10 \times 9.8}{2.5 \times 10^{-3} \times 10^{-4} \times 2.0 \times 10^{11}}$
$\text {strain}_{l w} = 1.96 \times 10^{-3} \approx 2.0 \times 10^{-3} $ $\rightarrow$(1)
now for upper wire
$\operatorname{strain}_{u p} = \frac{\text {tensile stress}}{y} = \frac{16 \times 9.8}{2.5 \times 10^{-3} \times 10^{-4} \times 2.0 \times 10^{11}}$
strain_up = 3.14 $\times$ 10^‚àí3 $\Rightarrow$ (2)

Q9. In constructing a large mobile, an artist hangs an aluminum sphere of mass 6.0 kg from a vertical steel wire 0.50 m long and 2.5 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 cm^2 in a cross-sectional area. On the bottom of the sphere, he attaches a similar steel wire, from which he hangs a brass cube of mass 10.0 kg. Compute the elongation show calculations for strain in the upper wire?
    (A) 1.5 mm upper, 1.1 mm lower
    (B) 1.7 mm upper, 1.0 mm lower
    (C) 1.6 mm upper, 1.0 mm lower   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 1.4 mm upper, 1.0 mm lower
    Answer: C
    Explanation: given mass of aluminium sphere m_sph = 6.0 kg
length of steel wire L_steel = 0.50 m
area of steelwire A_steel = 2.5 $\times$ 10^‚àí3 cm^2
mass of cube m_cube = 10kg
net restoring force in lower wire i.e. tension T_2 will be equal to weight of cubei.e.T_2 = m_cube g = 10 $\times$ 9.8 N
net restoring force inupperwirei.e.tensionT_1
T_1 = m_sph g + T_2 = (10 + 6) $\times$ 9.8 N
standard value for young modulus of steel y = 2 $\times$ 10^11N/m^2
$y=\frac{\text {tensile stress}}{\text {tensile strain}}$
for lower wire 
$\text {strain}_{l w}=\frac{\text {tensile stress}}{y}$ = $\frac{10 \times 9.8}{2.5 \times 10^{-3} \times 10^{-4} \times 2.0 \times 10^{11}}$
strain_lw = 1.96 $\times$ 10^‚àí3 $\approx$ 2.0 $\times$ 10^‚àí3
$\frac{\Delta L_{l w}}{L l_{w}}=2.0 \times 10^{-3} $ $\Rightarrow \Delta L_{l w}=0.50 \times 2.0 \times 10^{-3}$
$\Delta $L_lw = 1.0 mm $\rightarrow$ (1) 
now for upper wire
strain_up = $\frac{\text {tensile stress}}{y}$ = $\frac{16 \times 9.8}{2.5 \times 10^{-3} \times 10^{-4} \times 2.0 \times 10^{11}}$
strain_up = $3.14 \times 10^{-3} \rightarrow(2)$
$\frac{\Delta L_{w p}}{L_{w p}}=3.14 \times 10^{-3} $ $\Rightarrow \Delta L_{u p}=0.50 \times 3.14 \times 10^{-3}$
$\Delta$L_up = 1.57 mm $\approx$ 1.6 mm

Q10. A specimen of oil having an initial volume of 600 cm^3 is subjected to a pressure increase of 3.6 $ \times $ 10^6 Pa and the volume is found to decrease by 0.45 cm^3 what is the bulk modulus of the material?
    (A) 4.4 $ \times $ 10^9 Pa
    (B) 4.6 $ \times $ 10^9 Pa
    (C) 4.8 $ \times $ 10^9 Pa   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 5.0 $ \times $ 10^9 Pa
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Bulk modulus is defined as B = $- P\Delta V \over V$
here P is volume stress which is equal to the pressure 
given P = 3.6 $\times$ 10^6 pa
$\Delta$V = ‚àí0.45cm^3
V = 600cm^3
$B=-\frac{3.6 \times 10^{6}}{-0.45 \over 600}$
B = 4.8 $\times$ 10^9pa

Q11. A specimen of oil having an initial volume of 600 cm^3 s subjected to a pressure increase of 3.6 $ \times $ 10^6 Pa and the volume is found to decrease by 0.45 cm^3 what is the compressibility of the material?
    (A) 2.1 $ \times $ 10^-10 Pa^‚àí1   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 2.3 $ \times $ 10^-10 Pa$^{ - {\rm{1}}}$
    (C) 1.7 $ \times $ 10^-10 Pa$^{ - {\rm{1}}}$
    (D) 1.9 $ \times $ 10^-10 Pa$^{ - {\rm{1}}}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Compressibility (K) is defined as reciprocal of bulk modulus
bulk modulus is defined as B = $‚àíP\over {\Delta V\over V}$
here P is volume stress which is equal to pressure given P = 3.6 $\times$ 10^6pa
$\Delta$V = ‚àí0.45cm^3
V = 600cm^3
$B=-\frac{3.6 \times 10^{6}}{-0.45 / 600}$
B = 4.8 $\times$ 10^9 pa
K = 1/B = $1\over 4.8 \times 10^9$ = 2.1 $\times$ 10^-10 Pa^-1

Q12. A copper cube measures 6.00 cm on each side. The bottom face is held in place by very strong glue to a flat horizontal surface, while a horizontal force F is applied to the upper face parallel to one of the edges. How large must F be to cause the cube to deform by 0.250 mm? (shear modulus of copper = 4.4 $ \times $ 10^10 Pa)
    (A) 6.6 $ \times $ 10^5 N   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 6.4 $ \times $ 10^5 N
    (C) 6.3 $ \times $ 10^5 N
    (D) 6.5 $ \times $ 10^5 N
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Shear modulus ($\eta$) is given as Œ∑ = $\frac{\text {shear stress}}{\text {shear strain}}=\frac{F \over A}{x \over h}$ $\rightarrow$ (1)
given for copper $\eta$ = 4.4 $\times$10^10pa x = 0.250mm h = 6.0cm
side of cube a = 6.0cm
area A = a $\times$ a = 6.0 $\times$ 6.0 = 36.0cm^2 from
equation 1
F = $\frac{\eta \times A \times x}{h}=\frac{4.4 \times 10^{10} \times 36 \times 10^{-4} \times 0.250 \times 10^{-3}}{6.01 \times 10^{-2}}$
F = 6.6 $\times$ 10^5N

Q13. A lead cube measures 6.00 cm on each side. The bottom face is held in place by very strong glue to a flat horizontal surface, while a horizontal force F is applied to the upper face parallel to one of the edges. How large must F be to cause the cube to deform by 0.250 mm? (Shear modulus of lead = 0.6 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{{\rm{1}}0}}$Pa)
    (A) 0.9 $\times$ 10^5 N   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 1.6 $\times$ 10^5 N
    (C) 2.0 $\times$ 10^5 N
    (D) 1.4$\times$ 10^5 N
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Shear modulus(Œ∑) is given as Œ∑ = shear stress / shear strain = $\frac{F \over A}{x \over h}$ .....(1)
given for Lead $\eta$ = 4.4 $\times$ 10^10pa
x = 0.250mm, h = 6.0 cm, side of cube 'a' = 6.0cm
area A = a $\times$ a = 6.0 $\times$ 6.0 = 36.0cm^2 
from equation (1)
$F=\frac{\eta \times A \times x}{h}=\frac{0.6 \times 10^{10} \times 36 \times 10^{-4} \times 0.250 \times 10^{-3}}{6.0 \times 10^{-2}}$
F = 0.9 $\times$ 10^5N

Q14. Four identical hollow cylindrical columns of mild steel support a big structure of mass 50,000 kg. The inner and outer radii of each column are 30 and 60 cm respectively. Assuming the load distribution to be uniform, calculate the compression strain of each column? Take Young's modulus of steel as 20 $ \times $ 10^10 Pa
    (A) 7.0 $ \times $ 10^-7
    (B) 7.95 $ \times $ 10^-6
    (C) 7.224 $ \times $ 10^-7   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 7.1 $ \times $ 10^-6
    Answer: C
    Explanation: young modulus y = 2.0 $\times$10^11N/m^2
also $y = \frac{\text {stress}}{\text {strain}}$ 
strain = $ \frac{\text {stress}}{y} = \frac{F}{A \times y}$....(1)
if we assume uniform distribution of weight then weight on one cylinder(m) = $\frac{50000}{4}$ = 12500 kg restoring force F = mg = 122500 N
area of each cylinder A = $\pi$(r_2^2 - r^2_1)
A = 3.14 $\times$ (60^2 - 30^2) $\times$ 10^ - 4 = 0.8478m^2
thus from (1) we get ,strain = $\frac{122500 \times 9.8}{0.28 \times 20 \times 10^{10}}$
strain = 7.224 $\times$ 10^ -7

Q1. A piece of copper having a rectangular cross-section of 15.2 mm $ \times $ 19.1 mm is pulled in tension with 44,500 N force, producing only elastic deformation. Calculate the resulting strain. (Take Young's modulus of copper as 11 $ \times $10^10Pa)
    (A) 0.14 $\times$ 10^‚àí2   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 0.04 $\times$ 10^‚àí2
    (C) 0.06 $\times$ 10^‚àí2
    (D) 0.11 $\times$ 10^‚àí2
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Given for copper cross-section = 15.2mm $\times$ 19.1mm
thus, cross-sectional area(A) = 15.2 $\times$ 19.1mm^2 = 2.9 $\times$ 10^‚àí4m^2
restoring force tension(T) = 44500N
young modulus of copper (y) = 11 $\times$ 10^10pa
also $y=\frac{s t r e s s}{s t r a i n} $ $\Rightarrow$ strain = $\frac{s t r e s s}{y} $
strain $=\frac{44500}{2.9 \times 10^{-4} \times 11 \times 10^{10}}$
strain = 0.1394 $\times$ 10^-2 $\approx$ 0.14 $\times$ 10^-2

Q2. A steel cable with a radius of 1.5 cm supports a chairlift at a ski area. If the maximum stress is not to exceed 10^8 Nm^‚àí2, what is the maximum load the cable can support?
    (A) 6.97$ \times $ 10^4 N
    (B) 6.87$ \times $ 10^4 N
    (C) 7.17$ \times $ 10^4 N
    (D) 7.07$ \times $ 10^4 N   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Radius of the steel cable, r = 1.5 cm = 0.015 m
Maximum allowable stress = 10^8 Nm^-2
Maximum stress = $Maximum \ force \over Area \ of \ cross-section$
$\therefore$ Maximum force = Maximum stress $\times$ Area of cross-section
= 10^8 $\times $ $\pi$ (0.015)^2
= 7.065 $\times $ 10^4 N
Hence, the cable can support the maximum load of 7.07 $\times $ 10^4 N.

Q3. A rigid bar of mass 15 kg is supported symmetrically by three wires each 2.0 m long. Those at each end are of copper and the middle one is of iron. Determine the ratios of their diameters if each is to have the same tension?
    (A) $\frac{D_{\text {copper }}}{D_{\text {iron }}}$ = 1.55
    (B) $\frac{D_{\text {copper }}}{D_{\text {iron }}}$ = 1.45
    (C) $\frac{\mathrm{D}_{\text {copper }}}{\mathrm{D}_{\text {iron }}}$ = 1.25   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{\mathrm{D}_{\text {copper }}}{\mathrm{D}_{\text {irun }}}$ = 1.35
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Given condition is that tension in all three wires should be the same thus for the same tension, the extension $\Delta L$ in all three wires will be the same as the natural length of all three wires is the same i.e. 2.0 m, strain in all three wires will be equal. so young modulus of wires
$y=\frac{s t r e s s}{s t r a i n}=\frac{F \times L}{A \times \Delta L}=\frac{F \times L}{\pi r^{2} \times \Delta L} $ (D = 2r)
$y \propto \frac{1}{D^{2}}$
$\Rightarrow \frac{y_{\text {copper}}}{y_{\text {iron}}}=\frac{D^{2} \text { iron }}{D^{2} \text { copper }}$
$$$\frac{D_{\text {copper }}}{D_{\text {iron }}}=\sqrt{\frac{y_{\text {iron }}}{y_{\text {copper }}}}=\sqrt{\frac{19 \times 10^{10}}{12 \times 10^{10}}}$
$\frac{D_{\text {copper }}}{D_{\text {iron }}}$ = 1.25

Q4. A 14.5 kg mass, fastened to the end of a steel wire of unstretched length 1.0 m, is whirled in a vertical circle with an angular velocity of 2 rev/s at the bottom of the circle. The cross-sectional area of the wire is 0.065 cm^2. Calculate the elongation of the wire when the mass is at the lowest point of its path.
    (A) 1.9 mm   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 1.4 mm
    (C) 1.1 mm
    (D) 0.8 mm
    Answer: A
    Explanation: given mass m = 14.5 kg
length of steel wire L = 1.0m
angular velocity $\omega$ = 2rev/s $\Rightarrow$ 2 $\times$ 2$\pi$rad/s = 4$\pi$rad/s cross sectional area of steel wire = 0.065cm^2 = 0.065 $\times$10^‚àí4m^2
let us consider $\Delta$L be the elongation in steel wire when mass is at lowest point during its motion in vertical circle and T be the tension in wire apply in g_net force = centripetal force
T ‚àí mg = m$\omega$^2r
T = (14.5 $\times$ 9.8) + 14.5 $\times$ (4$\pi$)^2 $\times$ 1
T = 2429.5N now young modulus of steel wire y = 2.0 $\times$ 10^11pa
also $\mathrm{y}=\frac{\mathrm{stress}}{\operatorname{strain}}=\frac{\mathrm{T} \times \mathrm{L}}{\mathrm{A} \times \Delta \mathrm{L}}$
$\Delta \mathrm{L}=\frac{\mathrm{T} \times \mathrm{L}}{\mathrm{A} \times \mathrm{y}}=\frac{2429.5 \times 1.0}{0.065 \times 10^{-4} \times 2.0 \times 10^{11}}$
$\Delta$L = 1.9 $\times$ 10^‚àí3m = 1.9mm

Q5. Compute the bulk modulus of water from the following data: Initial volume = 100.0 litre, Pressure increase = 100.0 atm (1 atm = 1.013 $ \times $ 10^5 Pa), Final volume = 100.5 litre
    (A) 2.126 $ \times $ 10^9 Pa
    (B) 2.326 $ \times $ 10^9 Pa
    (C) 2.226 $ \times $ 10^9 Pa
    (D) 2.026 $ \times $ 10^9 Pa   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Bulk modulus is given by, B = $\frac{\Delta P}{\Delta {V\over V}}$
given $\Delta P$ = 100 atm = 100 $\times$ 1.013 $\times$ 10^5pa
V_INITIAL = 100.0 lit 
V_FINAL = 100.5 lit 
$\Delta$V = V_FINAL ‚àí V_INITIAL = 100.5 ‚àí 100.0 = 0.5
$B=\frac{100 \times 1.013 \times 10^{5}}{0.5 / 100.0}$
B = 2.026 $\times$ 10^9 pa

Q6. What is the density of water at a depth where pressure is 80.0 atm, given that its density at the surface is 1.03 $ \times $ 10^3 kg m^‚àí3?
    (A) 1.054 $ \times $ 10^3 kg/m^3
    (B) 1.094 $ \times $ 10^3 kg/m^3
    (C) 1.074 $ \times $ 10^3 kg/m^3
    (D) 1.034 $ \times $ 10^3 kg/m^3   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Let the given depth be h.
Pressure at the given depth, p = 80.0 atm = 80 $\times$ 1.01 $\times$ 10^5 Pa
The density of water at the surface, œÅ_1 = 1.03 $\times$ 10^3kg m^-3
Let œÅ_2 be the density of water at the depth h.
Let V_1 be the volume of water of mass m at the surface.
Let V_2 be the volume of water of mass m at the depth h.
Let ŒîV be the change in volume.
$\Delta V=V_1-V_2\;\;$ = $m\left[\frac1{\rho_1}-\frac1{\rho_2}\right]$
volumetric = strain = $\;\;\frac{\Delta V}{V_1}=m\left[\frac1{\rho_1}-\frac1{\rho_2}\right]\times\frac{\rho_1}m$
$ {\Delta V\over{ V_{1}}}={1-\frac{\rho_{1}}{\rho_{2}} \quad }$$ \rightarrow$ (1) 
bulk modulus B = $\frac{\frac{P}{\Delta V}}{V_{1}} $
$\frac{\Delta V}{V_{1}}=\frac{P}{B}$ but compressibility 1 B of water is 45.8 $\times$ 10^-11pa^-1
‚ñ≥V/V_1=80√ó1.013√ó10^5√ó45.8√ó10^-11
 =3.71√ó10^-3 .....(ii)
For equations (i) and (ii), we get:
1‚àí(œÅ_1/œÅ_2)=3.71√ó10^-3
œÅ_2=1.03√ó10^3/[1‚àí(3.71√ó10^-3)]
 =1.034√ó10^3kgm^-3
Therefore, the density of water at the given depth (h) is 1.034√ó10^3kgm^-3

Q7. Compute the fractional change in volume of a glass slab, when subjected to a hydraulic pressure of 10 atm. The bulk modulus of glass 37 GPa?
    (A) 0.273 $\times$ 10^-4   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 0.373 $\times$ 10^-4
    (C) 0.573 $\times$ 10^-4
    (D) 0.473 $\times$ 10^-4
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Bulk modulus is given by B = $\frac{\frac{P}{\Delta V}}{V}$
given B = 37 Gpa = 37 $\times$ 10^9pa
P = 10atm = 10 $\times$ 1.013 $\times$ 10^5atm
$\frac{\Delta V}{V}=\frac{P}{B}=\frac{10 \times 1.013 \times 10^{5}}{37 \times 10^{9}} $
$\frac{\Delta V}{V}=0.273 \times 10^{-4}$

Q8. Determine the volume contraction of a solid copper cube, 10 cm on an edge, when subjected to a hydraulic pressure of 7.0 $ \times $ 10^6 Pa. The bulk modulus of copper 140 GPa?
    (A) 0.12 cm^3
    (B) 0.05 cm^3   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 0.26 cm^3
    (D) 0.08 cm^3
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Length of an edge of the solid copper cube, l = 10 cm = 0.1 m
Hydraulic pressure, p = 7.0 $\times$ 10^6 Pa
The bulk modulus of copper, B = 140 $\times$ 10^9 Pa
bulk modulus B = $\frac{P}{\frac{\Delta V}{V}}$
$\Delta V=\frac{P V}{B}=\frac{7.0 \times 10^{6} \times(0.1)^{3}}{140 \times 10^{9}}$
$\Delta$V = 0.05 $\times$ 10^-6m^3 = 0.05cm^3

Q9. How much should the pressure on a liter of water be changed to compress it by 0.10 percent? The bulk modulus of water 2.2 GPa?
    (A) 2.0 $ \times $ 10^6 N/m^2
    (B) 2.2 $ \times $ 10^6 N/m^2   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 2.4 $ \times $ 10^6 N/m^2
    (D) 2.6 $ \times $ 10^6 N/m^2
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Bulk modulus B = $\Delta P \over {\Delta V\over V}$
given V = 1 lit
bulkmodulus B = 2.2Gpa = 2 .2 $\times$ 10^9pa
$\Delta$V = 0.10%
$\Delta P=B \times \frac{\Delta V}{V}$
$\Delta P=2.2 \times 10^{9} \times \frac{0.10}{100 \times 1}$ = 2.2 $\times$ 10^6 N/m^2

Q10. Anvils made of single crystals of diamond, with the shape as shown in Figure, are used to investigate the behaviour of materials under very high pressures. Flat faces at the narrow end of the anvil have a diameter of 0.50 mm, and the wide ends are subjected to a compression force of 50,000 N. What is the pressure at the tip of the anvil?
    (A) 2.1 $ \times $ 10^11 Pa
    (B) 2.5 $ \times $ 10^11 Pa   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 2.9 $ \times $ 10^11 Pa
    (D) 3.2 $ \times $ 10^11 Pa
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Given compression force F = 50000 N
Radius will be r = $\frac{diameter}{2}$ = $\frac{0.50}{2}$ = 0.25 mm = 0.25 $\times$ 10^-3 m
Area at which compression is applied A = $\pi$r^2 = 3.14 $\times$ (0.25 $\times$ 10^-3)^2 = 0.2 $\times$ 10^-6 m^2 
Pressure at the tip P = $\frac{Force}{area}$ = $\frac{50000}{0.2 \times 10^{-6}}$
P = 2.5 $\times$ 10^11 pa

Q11. A rod of length 1.05 m having negligible mass is supported at its ends by two wires of steel (wire A) and aluminum (wire B) of equal lengths as shown in Figure. The cross-sectional areas of wires A and B are 1.0 ${\rm{m}}{{\rm{m}}^{\rm{2}}}$ and 2.0 mm^2, respectively. At what point along the rod should a mass m be suspended in order to produce equal stresses? Take Young's modulus of steel as 200 GPa, for aluminum 70 GPa.
    (A) 0.6 m from steel wire
    (B) 0.8 m from steel wire
    (C) 0.7 m from steel wire   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 0.9 m from steel wire
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Let L be the length of each of the wires A and B. Also given cross-sectional area of wire A_steel = 1mm^2 cross sectional area of B_al = 2mm^2
young modulus for steel y_steel = 200 Gpa = 2 $\times$ 10^11 N/m^2
young modulus of aluminium y_al = 70Gpa = 7 $\times$ 10^10 N/m^2
Let after placing the mass m weight on lower ends of wire be F_1 and F_2 then stress on wires A and B will be $\frac{F_1}{A_{steel}}$ and $\frac{F_2}{A_{al}}$
Now given condition is stress should be equal thus
$\frac{F_{1}}{A_{\text { steel }}}=\frac{F_{2}}{A_{a l}}$ $\Rightarrow$ $\frac{F_{1}}{F_{2}}=\frac{A_{s t e e l}}{A_{a l}} \rightarrow(1)$
if mass m is placed at a distance x and y from two wires then
F_1x = F_2y
$\frac{F_1}{F_2}$ = $\frac{y}{x}$ $\rightarrow$(2)
From equation 1 and 2
$\frac{y}{x}=\frac{A_{\text {steel}}}{A_{a l}} \Rightarrow x=\frac{A_{a l}}{A_{\text {steel}}} y $ $\rightarrow$ (3)
also given x + y = 1.05 (total length of rod)
y = 1.05 - x $\rightarrow$ (4)
thus from 3 and 4
$x=\frac{A_{a l}}{A_{s t e e l}}(1.05-x) $ $\Rightarrow x A_{s t e e l}=A_{a l} 1.05-A_{a l} x$
$x\left(A_{\text {steel}}+A_{a l}\right)=A_{a l} \times 1.05 $ $\Rightarrow x=\frac{2 \times 10^{-6} \times 1.05}{(2+1) \times 10^{-6}}$
x = 0.7 m 
Thus mass should be placed 0.7m from steel wire.

Q12. A rod of length 1.05 m having negligible mass is supported at its ends by two wires of steel (wire A) and aluminum (wire B) of equal lengths as shown in Figure. The cross-sectional areas of wires A and B are 1.0 ${\rm{m}}{{\rm{m}}^{\rm{2}}}$ and 2.0 mm^2, respectively. At what point along the rod should a mass m be suspended in order to produce equal strains in both steel and aluminum wires. Take Young's modulus of steel as 200 GPa, for aluminum 70 GPa
    (A) 0.44 m from steel wire
    (B) 0.42 m from steel wire
    (C) 0.43 m from steel wire   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 0.40 m from steel wire
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Let L be the length of each wire. 
cross-sectional area of steel A_steel = 2mm^2
young modulus of steel y_steel = 200 Gpa= 2 $\times$ 10^11 pa
cross-sectional area of aluminum A_al = 1mm^2
young modulus of aluminium y_al = 70 Gpa =7 $\times$ 10^10 pa
Let after placing the mass m wight on lower ends of wire be F_1 and F_2 then stress on wires A and B will be $\frac{f_1}{A_{steel}}$ and $\frac{f_2}{A_{al}}$ 
Now given condition is strain should be equal thus
Young modulus y = $\frac{stress}{strain}$ $\Rightarrow$ strain = $\frac{stress}{y}$
Strain_st = $\frac{\operatorname{stress}_{s t}}{y_{steel}}=\frac{F_{1}}{A_{s t e e l} \times y_{s t e e l}}$ and strain_al = $\frac{stress_{al}}{y_{al}}$
for equal strains $\Rightarrow$ strain_st = strain_al
$\frac{F_{1}}{A_{s t e e l} \times y_{s t e e l}}=\frac{F_{2}}{A_{a l} \times y_{a l u m i n i u m}}$ $\Rightarrow$ $\frac{F_{1}}{F_{2}}=\frac{A_{\text {stel}} \times y_{\text { steel }}}{A_{\text {al}} \times y_{a l}} \rightarrow(1)$
if mass m is placed at a distance x and y from two wires then
F_1x = F_2y
$\frac{F_1}{F_2} $ = $\frac{y}{x}$ $\rightarrow$(2)
From equation 1 and 2
$\frac{y}{x}$ = $\frac{A_{\text {steel}} \times y_{\text {steel}}}{A_{\text {al}} \times y_{a l}}$ $\Rightarrow$ x = $\frac{A_{a l} \times y_{a l}}{A_{s t e e l} \times y_{s t e e l}} y$ $\rightarrow$ (3)
also given x + y = 1.05 (total length of rod)
y = 1.05 - x $\Rightarrow$ (4)
thus from 3 and 4
x = $\frac{A_{a l} \times y_{a l}}{A_{s t e e l} \times y_{s t e e l}}(1.05-x)$ $\Rightarrow$ $x A_{s t e e l} \times y_{s t e e l}=A_{a l} y_{a l} 1.05-A_{a l} y_{a l} x$
$x\left(A_{s t e e l} y_{s t e el} +A_{a l} y_{a l}\right)=A_{a l} \times y_{a l} \times 1.05 \Rightarrow x=\frac{2 \times 10^{-6} \times 7 \times 10^{10} \times 7 \times 10^{10} \times 1.05}{\left(2 \times 2 \times 10^{11}+1 \times 7 \times 10^{10}\right) \times 10^{-6}}$
x = 0.43 m
Thus mass should be placed 0.43 m from steel wire.

Q13. A mild steel wire of length 1.0 m and cross-sectional area 0.50 $ \times $ 10^-2 cm^2 is stretched, well within its elastic limit, horizontally between two pillars. A mass of 100 g is suspended from the mid-point of the wire. Calculate the depression at the midpoint.
    (A) 1.074 $\times$ 10^- 2 m   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 1.5 $\times$ 10^-2 m
    (C) 0.40 $\times$ 10 ^-2 m
    (D) 0.90 $\times$ 10^-2 m
    Answer: A
    Explanation: given length of wire L = 1.0 m
area A = 0.50 $\times$ 10^-2cm^2 = 0.5 $\times$ 10^‚àí6m^2
mass suspended = 100g = 0.1 kg 
using standard value for young modulus of steely = 2.0 $\times$ 10^11N/m^2
depression at midpoint $x ={ l ({Mg\over {YA}})}^{1/3}$
$x = 0.5 ({0.1 \times 10 \over (2 \times 10 ^11)(0.50 \times 10 ^{-6})})^{1/3}$
x = 1.074 $\times$ 10^-2 m

Q14. Two strips of metal are riveted together at their ends by four rivets, each of diameter 6.0 mm. What is the maximum tension that can be exerted by the riveted strip if the shearing stress on the rivet is not to exceed 6.9 $ \times $ 10^7 Pa? Assume that each rivet is to carry one-quarter of the load:
    (A) 6.5 $\times$ 10^3N
    (B) 7.0 $\times$ 10^3 N
    (C) 7.2 $\times$ 10^3 N
    (D) 7.8 $\times$ 10^3 N   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: if the riveted strip is subjected to a stretching load W, the tensile force i.e. tension in each strip(equal to W)
let A be the area of each rivet on which the shearing force acts.
shearing stress on each rivet = $\frac{\text {shearing force}}{\text {area}}$= $\frac{W}{4 A}$
let W_max be the maximum permissible tension exerted by the riveted strip then $\frac{W_{\max }}{4 A}$ = $6.9 \times 10^{7} \Rightarrow W_{\max }$ = 4 A $\times$ 6.9 $\times$ 10^7 $\Rightarrow$ (1)
Given that diameter of each rivet d = 6.0 mm
area A = $\pi \mathrm{r}^{2}=\frac{\pi \mathrm{d}^{2}}{4}=\frac{3.14 \times\left(6 \times 10^{-3}\right)^{2}}{4}$
A = 28.26 $\times$ 10^‚àí6m^2
substitute in equation 1
W_max = 4 $\times$ 28.26 $\times$ 10^‚àí6 $\times$ 6.9 $\times$ 10^7
W_max = 7.8 $\times$ 10^3 N

Q15. The Marina trench is located in the Pacific Ocean, and in one place it is nearly eleven km beneath the surface of the water. The water pressure at the bottom of the trench is about 1.1 $ \times $ 10^8 Pa. A steel ball of initial volume 0.32 m^3 is dropped into the ocean and falls to the bottom of the trench. What is the change in the volume of the ball when it reaches the bottom? The bulk modulus of steel = 160 GPa.
    (A) 2.6 $ \times $ 10^‚àí4 m^3
    (B) 2.9 $ \times $ 10^‚àí4 m^3
    (C) 2.5 $ \times $ 10^‚àí4 m^3
    (D) 2.2 $ \times $ 10^‚àí4 m^3   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: bulk modulus is given by $B=\frac{\Delta P}{\frac{\Delta V}{V}} \Rightarrow \Delta V=\frac{\Delta P V}{B}$ $\Rightarrow$ (1)
volume of ball = 0.32 m^3
given water pressure at bottom = 1.1 $\times $ 10^8 pa
depth of marina trench = 11000 km bulk modulus of ball B = 160 Gpa = 160 $\times$ 10^9 pa
pressure at top of surface will be atmospheic pressure p_0 = P_atm
pressure at bottom will be p_2 = P_atm + P_water = P_atm + (1.1 $\times$ 10^8)
$\Delta P=p_{2}-p_{1}$ = 1.1 $\times$ 10^8pa
substitute values in equation 1
$\Delta V=\frac{1.1 \times 10^{8} \times 0.32}{160 \times 10^{9}}$
$\Delta V=2.2 \times 10^{-4} $m^3

Q1. What diameter should a 10-m-long steel wire have if we do not want it to stretch more than 0.5 cm under a tension of 940 N? Take Young's modulus of steel as 20 $ \times $ 10^10 Pa.
    (A) 3.2 mm
    (B) 3.0 mm
    (C) 3.6 mm
    (D) 3.4 mm   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Given length of wire L = 10 m
elongation in wire $\Delta L$ = 0.5 cm = 0.5 $\times$ 10^-2 m
restoring force in wire i.e. tension T = 940 N
young modulus of wire Y = 20 $\times$ 10^10N/m^2
using relation for young modulus $Y = \frac{s t r e s s}{s t r a i n} = \frac{T \times L}{A \times \Delta L}$
$A = \frac{T \times L}{Y \times \Delta L} = \frac{940 \times 10}{20 \times 10^{10} \times 0.5 \times 10^{-2}}$
$\pi \mathrm{R}^{2} = 940 \times 10^{-8}$
R = 17.30 $\times$ 10^-4
Diameter d = 2 R $\Rightarrow$ 2 $\times$ 17.30 $\times$ 10^-4
d = 34.60 $\times$ 10^-4 m $\approx$ 3.4 mm

Q2. A solid brass sphere is initially surrounded by air, and the air pressure exerted on it is 1.0 $ \times $ 10^5N/m^2 (normal atmospheric pressure). The sphere is lowered into the ocean to a depth at which the pressure is 2.0 $ \times $ 10^7 N/m^2. The volume of the sphere in the air is 0.50 m^3. By how much does this volume change once the sphere is submerged? ( take the modulus of brass as 61 GPa.)
    (A) -1.7 $ \times $ 10^-4 m^3
    (B) -1.5 $ \times $ 10^-4 m^3
    (C) -1.6 $ \times $ 10^-4 m^3   <-- CORRECT
    (D) -1.4 $ \times $ 10^-4 m^3
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Given initial pressure p_1 = 1.0 $\times$ 10^5 N/m^2
final pressure p_2 = 2.0 $\times$ 10^7 N/m^2
volume of sphere V = 0.50 m^3
bulk modulus B of brass = 61 Gpa = $61 \times 10^{9}$ pa
bulk modulus is given by $B=-\frac{\Delta p}{\frac{\Delta V}{V}}$
$\Delta V=-\frac{\Delta p \times V}{B}=-\frac{\left(p_{2}-p_{1}\right) \times V}{B}$ = $-\frac{\left(2.0 \times 10^{7}-1.0 \times 10^{5}\right) \times 0.50}{61 \times 10^{9}} $
$\Delta V=-1.6 \times 10^{-4} m^3$

Q3. A 200-kg load is hung on a wire with a length of 4.00 m, a cross-sectional area of 0.200 $ \times $ 10^‚àí4 m^2, and Young‚Äôs modulus of 8.00 $ \times $ 10^10 N/m^2. What is its increase in length?
    (A) 4.70 mm
    (B) 4.90 mm   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 4.80 mm
    (D) 5.00 mm
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Given load hung to a wire = 200 kg thus force applied to wire = weight W = 200 $\times$ g
W = 200 $\times$ 9.8 = 1960 N
at equilibrium restoring force = applied force
thus, tension T = 1960 N
length of wire L = 4.0 m
cross sectional area of wire = 0.200 $\times$ 10^-4 m^2
young modulus of wire Y = 8.00 $\times$ 10^10 N/m^2
also $Y = \frac{stress}{strain} = \frac{T \times L}{A \times \Delta L} $
$\Delta L$ = $\frac{T \times L}{A \times Y} = \frac{1960 \times 4.0}{0.200 \times 10^{-4} \times 8.00 \times 10^{10}} $
$\Delta L$ = 4.90 mm

Q4. A child slides across a floor in a pair of rubber-soled shoes. The frictional force acting on each foot is 20.0 N. The footprint area of each shoe‚Äôs sole is 14.0 cm^2, and the thickness of each sole is 5.00 mm. Find the horizontal distance by which the upper and lower surfaces of each sole are offset. The shear modulus of the rubber is 3.00 $ \times $ 10^6 N/m^2.
    (A) 2.58 mm
    (B) 2.38 mm   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 2.48 mm
    (D) 2.68 mm
    Answer: B
    Explanation: let restoring force be T
at equilibrium frictional force = restoring force
thus T = 20 N
area A of each sole = 14.0 cm^2 = 14.0 $\times$ 10^-4 m^2
thickness of each sole H = 5.00 mm = 5.00 $\times$ 10^-3 m
shear modulus of rubber $\eta$ = 3.00 $\times$ 10^6 N/m^2
let distance by which upper and lower surface are offset is x
shear modulus $\eta$ = $\frac{\text {shear stress}}{\text {shear strain}} $ = $ \frac{T \times H}{A \times x}$
x = $\frac{T \times H}{A \times \eta}$ = $\frac{20 \times 5 \times 10^{-3}}{14 \times 10^{-4} \times 3 \times 10^{6}}$= 2.38 mm

Q5. If the elastic limit of copper is 1.50 $ \times $ 10^8 N/m^2, determine the minimum diameter a copper wire can have under a load of 10.0 kg if its elastic limit is not to be exceeded?
    (A) 1.012 mm
    (B) 0.812 mm
    (C) 0.912 mm   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 0.712 mm
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Given mass to be hung m = 10.0 kg
elastic limit = 1.50 $\times$ 10^8 N/m^2
elastic limit i.e. pessure P = $\frac{\text {force}}{\text {area}} $ $\rightarrow$ (1)
force F = m g and area = $\pi \mathrm{r}^{2}$ (r $\rightarrow$ radius of wire)
using 1
1.50 $\times$ 10^8= $\frac{10.0 \times 9.8}{\pi \mathrm{r}^{2}}$
r^2 = 20.80 $\times$ 10^-8
r = 4.56 $\times$ 10^-4 m
d = 2r
d = 0.912 mm

Q6. If the shear stress exceeds about 4.00 $ \times $ 10^8 N/m^2, steel ruptures. Determine the shearing force necessary to shear a steel bolt 1.00 cm in diameter?
    (A) 3.14 $ \times $ 10^4 N   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 3.24 $ \times $ 10^4 N
    (C) 3.34 $ \times $ 10^4 N
    (D) 3.44 $ \times $ 10^4 N
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Given shear stress S = 4.00 $\times$ 10^8 N/m^2
diameter d = 1.00 cm
radius r = $d\over 2 $= 0.5cm
area (A) = $\pi$r^2 = 3.14 $\times$ (0.5 $\times$ 10^‚àí2)^2 = 0.785 $\times$ 10^‚àí4m^2
stress S = $force \over area$ $\Rightarrow$ force = S $\times$ area
force F = 4.00 $\times$ 10^8 $\times$ 0.785 $\times$ 10^‚àí4 F = 3.14 $\times$ 10^4N

Q7. If the shear stress exceeds about 4.00 $ \times $ 10^8 N/m^2, steel ruptures. Determine the shearing force necessary to punch a 1.00-cm-diameter hole in a steel plate 0.500 cm thick?
    (A) 5.98 $ \times $ 10^4 N
    (B) 6.18 $ \times $ 10^4 N
    (C) 6.28 $ \times $ 10^4 N   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 6.08 $ \times $ 10^4 N
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Given shear stress S = 4.00 $\times$ 10^8 N/m^2
diameter d = 1.00 cm
radius r = $d\over 2$ = 0.5cm
height h = 0.500 cm
force will be applied on area A = 2 $\pi$rh = 2 $\times $ 3.14 $\times $ (0.5 $\times$ 10^-2) $\times$ 0.5 $\times$ 10^-2 = 1.57 $\times$ 10^-4 m
area A = $\pi$r^2 = 3.14 $\times$ (0.5 $\times$ 10^‚àí2)^2 = 0.785 $\times$ 10^‚àí4m^2
stress S = $force \over area$ $\Rightarrow$ force = S $\times$ area
force F = 4.00 $\times$ 10^8 $\times$ 1.57$\times$ 10^‚àí4 
F = 6.28$\times$ 10^4N

Q8. When water freezes, it expands by about 9.00%. What would be the pressure increase inside your automobile‚Äôs engine block if the water in it froze? (The bulk modulus of ice is 2.00 $ \times $ 10^9 N/m^2.)
    (A) 1.1 $ \times $ 10^8 N/m^2
    (B) 2.4 $ \times $ 10^8 N/m^2
    (C) 1.8 $ \times $ 10^8 N/ m^2   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 4.16 $ \times $ 10^8 N/m^2
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Given $\frac{\Delta V}{V}$ = 9% = $\frac{9}{100}$
the bulk modulus of water = 2.00 $\times$ 10 ^9 N/m^2
$B=\frac{\Delta P}{\frac{\Delta V}{V}}$ $\Rightarrow $ $\Delta P=B \times \frac{\Delta V}{V}$
$ŒîP$ = 2.00 $\times$ 10^9 $\times$ $9\over 100$
$ŒîP$ = 1.8 √ó 10^8N/m^2

Q9. In a materials testing laboratory, a metal wire made from a new alloy is found to break when a tensile force of 90.8 N is applied perpendicular to each end. If the diameter of the wire is 1.84 mm, what is the breaking stress of the alloy?
    (A) 3.51 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{\rm{7}}}$ Pa
    (B) 3.61 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{\rm{7}}}$ Pa
    (C) 3.41 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{\rm{7}}}$ Pa   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 3.31 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{\rm{7}}}$ Pa
    Answer: C
    Explanation: given tensile force F = 90.8 N
diameter d = 1.84 mm
radius r = 1.84/2 = 0.92 mm = 0.92 $\times$10^‚àí3m
stress = $\frac{\text {tensile force}}{\text {area}}=\frac{90.8}{\pi \mathrm{r}^{2}}=\frac{90.8}{3.14 \times\left(0.92 \times 10^{-3}\right)^{2}}$
stress = 3.41 $\times$ 10^7 N/m^2

Q10. A steel cable with cross-sectional area 3.00 cm^2 has an elastic limit of 2.40 $ \times $ 10^8 Pa. Find the maximum upward acceleration that can be given a 1200-kg elevator supported by the cable if the stress is not to exceed one-third of the elastic limit?
    (A) 11.2 m/s^2
    (B) 9.2 m/s^2
    (C) 8.2 m/s^2
    (D) 10.2 m/s^2   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Given area of cable A = 3cm^2
elastic limit = breaking stress = 2.40 $\times$ 10^8pa mass of elevator m = 1200kg
given condition stress should not exceed $\frac13$ of elastic limit i.e.
$\text {stress}<\frac{\text {elastic limit}}{3}$
$\frac{\text {force}}{\text {area}}<\frac{2.4 \times 10^{8}}{3}$
force < $\frac{2.4 \times 10^{8} \times 3 \times 10^{-4}}{3}$
force < 2.4 $\times$ 10^4
but force = mass $\times$ acceleration 
net acceleration a_net^ = $\frac{2.4 \times 10^{4}}{1200}$
a_net = 20m/s^2 
when elevator moves upwards net acceleration = a + g
here 'a' is acceleration given to elevator and'g' is acceleration due to gravity
a + g = 20
a = 20 - 9.8
a = 10.2 m/s^2

Q11. You hang a flood lamp from the end of vertical steel wire. The flood lamp stretches the wire 0.18 mm and the stress is proportional to the strain. How much would it have stretched if the wire were twice as long?
    (A) 0.38 mm
    (B) 0.36 mm   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 0.40 mm
    (D) 0.34 mm
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Let length in the first case is L
extension $\Delta L$ = 0.18 mm
length in 2 nd case will be 2L
given that stress $\alpha$ strain $\Rightarrow $ stress = k $\times$ strain (k is proportionality constant)
as stress in both cases is same because same flood lamp is hung and
the diameter of wire remains same on increasing length to 2L thus force per unit area
i.e. stress will be the same. so
$\frac{\text {stress}_{1}}{\text {stress}_{2}}=\frac{k \times \text {strain}_{1}}{k \times \text {strain}_{2}}$ $\Rightarrow \frac{\operatorname{strain}_{1}}{\operatorname{strain}_{2}}$ = 1 (stress_1 = stress_2)
$\frac{\frac{\Delta L_{1}}{L_{1}}}{\frac{\Delta L_{2}}{L_{2}}}=1 \Rightarrow \Delta L_{2}=\frac{\Delta L_{1} \times L_{2}}{L_{1}}=\frac{0.18 \times 2 L}{L}$
$\Delta L_2$ = 0.36 mm

Q12. You hang a flood lamp from the end of vertical steel wire. The flood lamp stretches the wire 0.18 mm and the stress is proportional to the strain. How much would it have stretched if the wire had the same length but twice the diameter?
    (A) 0.065 mm
    (B) 0.045 mm   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 0.055 mm
    (D) 0.075 mm
    Answer: B
    Explanation: let length in the first case is L
extension $\Delta L$ = 0.18 mm
length in 2nd case will be 2L
given that stress $\alpha$ strain ...(1)
as stress in both cases is same because the diameter of wire is changed so the area of wire will change thus force per unit area
i.e. stress will be the same. so
stress = force/area
from (1)st equation :-
$$${F\over A} \propto {\Delta{L\over l}}$
${1\over A}\propto \Delta l$ ( because l is same )
${1\over r^2} \propto \Delta l$
$({r_2\over r_1})^2 = {\Delta l_1\over \Delta l_2}$
thus, $({2r\over r})^2 = {0.18\over \Delta l_2}$
$\Delta L_2$ = 0.045 mm

Q13. You hang a flood lamp from the end of vertical steel wire. The flood lamp stretches the wire 0.18 mm and the stress is proportional to the strain. How much would it have stretched for a copper wire of the original length and diameter? (young modulus of steel=2.0 $\times$ 10^11 N/m^2 and young modulus of copper=1.1 $\times$ 10^11 N/m^2)
    (A) 0.33 mm   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 0.37 mm
    (C) 0.18 mm
    (D) 0.39 mm
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Let the length of wire in both cases be L
extension in case of steel $\Delta L_{\text {steel}}$ = 0.18 mm
young modulus y = $\frac{\text {stress}}{\text {strain}}$
as stress in both case is same
$y_{\text {steel}}=\frac{\text {stress}}{\text {strain}_{\text {steel}}} \Rightarrow$ (1) 
$y_{\text {copper}}=\frac{\text {stress}}{\text {strain}_{\text {copper}}} \Rightarrow$ (2)
equation 1 divided by equation 
$$$\frac{y_{\text {steel }}}{y_{\text {copper }}}=\frac{\text { strain }_{\text {copper }}}{\text { strain }_{\text {steel }}}$
$\frac{2.0 \times 10^{11}}{1.1 \times 10^{11}}=\frac{\frac{\Delta L_{\text {copper}}}{L}}{\frac{\Delta L_{\text {steel}}}{L}}$
$\Delta L_{\text {copper}}=\frac{2.0 \times 0.18}{1.1}$
$\Delta L_{\text {copper}}$ = 0.327 mm $\approx$ 0.33 mm

Q14. A 12.0-kg mass, fastened to the end of aluminum wire with an unstretched length of 0.50 m, is whirled in a vertical circle with a constant angular speed of 120 rev/min. The cross-sectional area of the wire is 0.014 cm^2. Calculate the elongation of the wire when the mass is at the lowest point of the path (young modulus of steel = 0.70 $\times$ 10^11 N/m^2)?
    (A) 0.56 cm
    (B) 0.50 cm
    (C) 0.52 cm
    (D) 0.54 cm   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Given mass m = 12.0 kg
length of aluminium wire L = 0.50 m
angular speed $\omega$ = 120 rev/min = 120 $\times$ $\frac{2 \pi}{60} = 4 \pi$ rad/s
area of wire A = 0.014 cm^2 = 0.014 $\times$ 10^-4m^2
when whirled in vertical circle at lowest point let tension in wire be T
applying net force = centripetal force
T - mg = $\mathrm{m} \omega^{2} \mathrm{r}$
T= (12 $\times$ (4$\pi$)^2 $\times$0.50) + 12 $\times$ 9.8
T = 1064.12N
young modulus y = $\frac{\mathrm{stress}}{\mathrm{strain}}=\frac{\frac{\mathrm{T}}{\mathrm{A}}}{\frac{\Delta \mathrm{L}}{\mathrm{L}}}$ $\Rightarrow$ $\Delta \mathrm{L}=\frac{\mathrm{T} \times \mathrm{L}}{\mathrm{A} \times \mathrm{y}}$
$\Delta \mathrm{L}=\frac{1064.12 \times 0.50}{0.014 \times 10^{-4} \times 0.70 \times 10^{11}}$
$\Delta \mathrm{L}$ = 54291.91 $\times$ 10^-7 m = 0.54 cm

Q15. 12.0-kg mass, fastened to the end of aluminum wire with an unstretched length of 0.50 m, is whirled in a vertical circle with a constant angular speed of 120 rev/min. The cross-sectional area of the wire is 0.014 cm^2. Calculate the elongation of the wire when the mass is at the highest point of its path. ( young modulus of aluminum 0.70 $\times$ 10^11 N/m^2)
    (A) 0.40 cm
    (B) 0.54 cm   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 0.36 cm
    (D) 0.38 cm
    Answer: B
    Explanation: given mass m = 12.0 kg
length of aluminum wire L = 0.50 m
angular speed $\omega$ = 120 rev/min = 120 $\times$ $\frac{2 \pi}{60} = 4 \pi$ rad/s
area of wire A = 0.014 cm^2 = 0.014 $\times$ 10^-4m^2
when whirled in vertical circle at lowest point let tension in wire be T
applying net force = centripetal force
T - mg = $\mathrm{m} \omega^{2} \mathrm{r}$
T=(12 $\times$ (4$\pi$)^2 $\times$0.50) + 12 $\times$ 9.8
T = 1064.1216 N
young modulus y = $\frac{\mathrm{stress}}{\mathrm{strain}}=\frac{\frac{\mathrm{T}}{\mathrm{A}}}{\frac{\Delta \mathrm{L}}{\mathrm{L}}}$ $\Rightarrow$ $\Delta \mathrm{L}=\frac{\mathrm{T} \times \mathrm{L}}{\mathrm{A} \times \mathrm{y}}$
$\Delta \mathrm{L}=\frac{1064.1216\times 0.50}{0.014 \times 10^{-4} \times 0.70 \times 10^{11}}$
$\Delta \mathrm{L}$ = 54291.92 $\times$ 10^-7 m = 0.54 cm

Q1. The basic property of a fluid that makes it different from solids is that:
    (A) It can be compressed
    (B) It does not expand on heating
    (C) It can flow   <-- CORRECT
    (D) It has a large density
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Fluids can flow due to unbalanced forces between the atoms of fluids.

Q2. Density is defined as:
    (A) the volume of 10 kg of the material
    (B) the volume of 1 kg of the material
    (C) volume per unit mass
    (D) mass per unit volume   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Density is defined as the compactness of a substance.
Mathematically,
Density(D) = $Mass (M) \over Volume (V)$

Q3. The specific gravity of a material is:
    (A) the ratio of its mass to the mass of water
    (B) the ratio of its volume to the density of water
    (C) the ratio of its density to the density of water   <-- CORRECT
    (D) the ratio of its mass to the density of water
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The specific gravity of an object is the ratio between the density of an object to a reference liquid. Usually, this reference liquid is water, which has a density of 1 g/mL or 1 g/cm^3.
Water has a specific gravity equal to 1. Materials with a specific gravity less than 1 are less dense than water, and will float on the pure liquid; substances with a specific gravity more than 1 are denser than water and will sink.

Q4. Pressure p at any point in a fluid at rest is:
    (A) the force parallel to the area at that point per unit area
    (B) the normal force at that point per unit volume
    (C) the normal force at that point per unit area   <-- CORRECT
    (D) the normal force at that point per unit mass
    Answer: C
    Explanation: It is important to note that it is valid only for a fluid at rest. In the case of a moving fluid, pressures in different directions could be different depending upon fluid accelerations in different directions.

Q5. According to Pascal‚Äôs Law:
    (A) pressure in a fluid increases with temperature
    (B) pressure in a fluid decreases with temperature
    (C) pressure in a fluid at rest is the same at all points if they are at the same height   <-- CORRECT
    (D) pressure in a fluid at rest is the same at all points if they are at different heights
    Answer: C
    Explanation: According to Pascal's Law,
P-P_0 = hdg
from above
Change in pressure is directly proportional to a depth from the free surface.
At the same horizontal line, all points are at the same depth and have the same value of acceleration due to gravity and density of water as well.

Q6. The pressure at a depth of h in a fluid of density $\rho $ at a place where the acceleration due to gravity is g and the pressure at h = 0 is ${{\rm{p}}_0}$ is given by:
    (A) p = ${{\rm{p}}_0}$+ $3\rho $ gh
    (B) p = ${{\rm{p}}_0}$ + $2\rho $ gh
    (C) p = ${{\rm{p}}_0}$ + $\rho $ gh   <-- CORRECT
    (D) p = $\rho $ gh
    Answer: C
    Explanation: According to Pascal's Law,
Change in Pressure (P - P_0) = h$\rho $g
if h = 0 then P - P_0 = 0
and P = P_0

Q7. The units of pressure in SI system is:
    (A) Newton
    (B) Pascal   <-- CORRECT
    (C) Watt
    (D) Joule
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Pressure is defined as force per unit area. The SI unit for pressure is the pascal (Pa), equal to one newton per square meter (N/m^2, or kg m^-1 s^‚àí2). This name for the unit was added in 1971; before that, pressure in SI was expressed simply in newtons per square meter.

Q8. Gauge pressure at a point is:
    (A) the pressure at that point - atmospheric pressure   <-- CORRECT
    (B) the pressure at that point + atmospheric pressure
    (C) $\frac{\text { (pressure at that point }+ \text { atmospheric pressure) }}{2}$
    (D) $\frac{\text { (pressure at that point-atmospheric pressure) }}{2}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Gauge pressure is zero-referenced against ambient air pressure, so it is equal to absolute pressure minus atmospheric pressure. Negative signs are usually omitted. 
The difference between absolute pressure and atmospheric pressure is what we call gauge pressure (P_gauge). It can be calculated if we know the absolute and atmospheric pressures using this formula: 
P_gauge = P_abs ‚àí P_atm

Q9. According to Pascal‚Äôs law for transmission of fluid pressure, external pressure applied on any part of a fluid contained in a vessel is ________ in all directions.
    (A) not transmitted
    (B) transmitted undiminished and equally   <-- CORRECT
    (C) transmitted and decreased
    (D) transmitted and increased
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Pascal's principle is defined as a change in pressure at any point in an enclosed fluid at rest is transmitted undiminished to all points in the fluid.
This principle is stated mathematically as
$\Delta P = \rho g(\Delta h)$ 
$\Delta P$ is the hydrostatic pressure (given in pascals in the SI system), or the difference in pressure at two points within a fluid column, due to the weight of the fluid.

Q10. In a hydraulic lift, the force applied on the smaller cylinder of area A_1 is F_1. If the area of the larger cylinder is A_2 the maximum weight that can be lifted is:
    (A) F_1A_2
    (B) ${F_1}$
    (C) $\frac{A_2}{A_1} F_1$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{A_1}{A_2} F_1$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: According to Pascal's Law,
Pressure applied to any point inside the liquid is transmitted equally in all direction so,
Pressure applied to the smaller cylinder is equal to the pressure on the other cylinder, which is given by
$\frac{F_1}{A_1}=\frac{F_2}{A_2}$ 
So,
Maximum force on the other side is,
F_2 = $\frac{A_2}{A_1} \times F_1$

Q11. The streamline in a flow is a curve:
    (A) whose perpendicular at any point is in the direction of the fluid velocity at that point.
    (B) whose tangent at any point is in the direction of the fluid velocity at that point.   <-- CORRECT
    (C) which shows all points of equal pressure.
    (D) which shows all points of equal velocity.
    Answer: B
    Explanation: A streamline is one that drawn is tangential to the velocity vector at every point in the flow at a given instant and forms a powerful tool in understanding flows. This definition leads to the equation for streamlines.${du\over u}={dv\over v}={dw\over w}$ 
where u, v, and w are the velocity components in x, y, and z directions respectively as sketched.

Q12. Flow is said to be steady if:
    (A) the flow rate decreases 10$\% $ every second
    (B) the flow rate does not change with time   <-- CORRECT
    (C) the flow rate decreases 20% every second
    (D) the flow rate increases with time
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The flow velocity u of a fluid is a vector field u = u(x, t)
which gives the velocity of an element of fluid at a position x and time t.
The flow of a fluid is said to be steady if u do not vary with time. That is if ${\delta u \over \delta t}$ = 0.

Q13. If a pipe carrying incompressible liquid has an area A_1 and velocity V_1 at one point A_2 nd V_2 at another point, the equation of continuity gives the relation:
    (A) A_1V_1 = A_2V_2   <-- CORRECT
    (B) A_1 = A_2V_2
    (C) A_1V_1 = 2A_2V_2
    (D) A_1V_1 = A_1V_2
    Answer: A
    Explanation: we apply the principle of mass conservation. Since there is no flow through the sidewalls of the duct, what mass comes in overA_1 goes out of A_2, Over a short time interval $\Delta t$ 
Volume flow in over A_1 = A_1V_1$\Delta$t
Volume flow out over A_2 = A_2V_2$\Delta$t
Therefore
mass in over A = $\rho$A_1V_1$\Delta$t
mass out over A = $\rho$A_2V_2$\Delta$t
So:$\rho$ A_1V_1 = $\rho$A_2V_2$\Delta$t
As volume is the same so this equation can be written as|
A_1V_1 = A_2V_2
This equation is called the continuity equation for steady one-dimensional flow.

Q14. in laminar flow:
    (A) adjacent layers of fluid slide smoothly past each other and the flow is steady   <-- CORRECT
    (B) adjacent layers of fluid move in circle crossing each other and the flow is steady
    (C) adjacent layers of fluid do not slide smoothly past each other and the flow is unsteady
    (D) adjacent layers of fluid slide smoothly past each other and the flow is unsteady
    Answer: A
    Explanation: laminar flow (or streamline flow) occurs when a fluid flows in parallel layers, with no disruption between the layers. At low velocities, the fluid tends to flow without lateral mixing, and adjacent layers slide past one another like playing cards.

Q15. At large flow velocities, the flow of fluid becomes:
    (A) turbulent   <-- CORRECT
    (B) laminar
    (C) compressible
    (D) viscous
    Answer: A
    Explanation: When any liquid is flowing more than the velocity of its critical velocity then flow becomes turbulent.
