Q1. According to Bernoulli‚Äôs equation for a varying cross-section pipe carrying incompressible liquid, pressure p, density $\rho $ velocity v and height h at a place where the acceleration due to gravity is g.
    (A) P + $ \rho {v^2} + \rho gh $ = constant
    (B) P + $\frac{1}{2}\rho {v^2} + 2\rho gh$ = constant
    (C) P + $ \frac{1}{2}\rho {v^2} + \rho gh $ = constant   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $P + \frac{1}{3}\rho {v^2} + \rho gh $ = constant
    Answer: C
    Explanation: According to the conservation of energy,
The total energy of any system is always conserved.
And Bernoulli's Theorem says that at any point in the pipe in which the liquid is flowing the sum of all the energies (Pressure Energy, Kinetic Energy, Potential Energy) is always constant.

Q2. If a ball is spinning in an airflow it develops pressure difference between the lower and upper faces due to differences in the:
    (A) the viscosity of air giving rise to lift
    (B) the density of air giving rise to lift
    (C) velocities of air giving rise to lift   <-- CORRECT
    (D) height of air giving rise to lift
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The Magnus effect is an observable phenomenon that is commonly associated with a spinning object that drags air faster around one side, creating a difference in pressure that moves it in the direction of the lower-pressure side.
The Magnus effect often occurs when a spinning sphere (or cylinder) curves away from its principal flight path.
This phenomenon is important in the study of the physics of many ball sports. It is also an important factor in the study of the effects of spinning on guided missiles‚Äîand has some engineering uses, for instance in the design of rotor ships and Flettner aeroplanes.

Q3. The lift on an aircraft wing arises because of:
    (A) velocity increase and pressure increase on the lower side of the wing
    (B) velocity decrease and pressure increase on the lower side of the wing   <-- CORRECT
    (C) velocity increase and pressure decrease on the lower side of the wing
    (D) velocity decrease and pressure decrease on the lower side of the wing
    Answer: B
    Explanation: This is the application of Bernoulli's Theorem.
If air speeds up the pressure are lowered. Thus, a wing generates lift because the air goes faster over the top creating a region of low pressure, and thus lift.

Q4. The coefficient of viscosity for a fluid is defined as the ratio of:
    (A) compressive stress to the strain rate
    (B) tensile stress to the strain rate
    (C) shearing stress to the flow rate
    (D) shearing stress to the strain rate   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The degree to which a fluid resists flows under an applied force expressed as the ratio of the shearing stress to the velocity gradient. The coefficient of viscosity of liquids decreases as temperature increases because the bonds between molecules are weakened.

Q5. According to Stokes‚Äô law if $\eta $ is the coefficient of viscosity, 'R' the radius of the sphere and it is moving with a velocity v the viscous drag force F is given by:
    (A) F_d = $6\pi \eta R $
    (B) F_d = $ 6\pi \eta R v$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) F_d = $ 6\pi R v$
    (D) F_d = $ 2\pi \eta R v$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The force of viscosity on a small sphere moving through a viscous fluid is given by:
F_d = $ 6\pi \eta R v$
Where:
 F_d is the frictional force - known as Stokes' drag ‚Äì acting on the interface between the fluid and the particle
 Œ∑ is the dynamic viscosity 
 R is the radius of the spherical object
 v is the flow velocity relative to the object.
In SI units, F_d is given in Newtons, Œ∑ in Pa¬∑s, R in meters, and v in m/s.

Q6. If $\rho $ is the density of the fluid flowing with a speed v, D stands for the dimension of the pipe, and $\eta $ is the viscosity of the fluid Reynolds number R_e is given by:
    (A) R_e = $\rho vD\eta$
    (B) R_e = $\rho v \over\eta$
    (C) R_e = $\rho vD\over\eta$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $R_e= $ $ \rho D\over\eta$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: It is a dimensionless number comprised of the physical characteristics of the flow. An increasing Reynolds number indicates increasing turbulence of flow.
It is defined as: $R_e =$$ \rho vD\over\eta$ 
where;
v is the flow velocity
D is a characteristic linear dimension
œÅ fluid density(kg/m^3)
$\eta$ is dynamic viscosity (Pa.s)
ŒΩ kinematic viscosity (m^2/s), v = $\eta\over\rho$

Q7. The molecules on the surface, are:
    (A) The balanced attractions cause the surface to resist being stretched
    (B) The unbalanced attractions cause the surface to stretch less
    (C) The unbalanced attractions cause the surface to stretch more
    (D) The unbalanced attractions cause the surface to resist being stretched   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The surface tension of liquid results from an imbalance of intermolecular attractive forces, the cohesive forces between molecules: A molecule in the bulk liquid experiences cohesive forces with other molecules in all directions. A molecule at the surface of a liquid experiences only net inward cohesive forces. The unbalanced attraction of molecules at the surface of a liquid tends to pull the molecules back into the bulk liquid leaving the minimum number of molecules on the surface. It required energy to increase the surface area of a liquid because a larger surface area contains more molecules in the unbalanced situation and this may result as the surface being stretched.

Q8. Water drops are spherical because of:
    (A) surface tension   <-- CORRECT
    (B) viscosity
    (C) elasticity
    (D) low shear strain
    Answer: A
    Explanation: In fluids, there are primarily 2 forces cohesive force and adhesive force. Cohesive force is the one that acts between the same molecules and adhesive force is the one that acts between different molecules. 
The cohesive force gives rise to a phenomenon called surface tension. In this phenomenon a fluid surface tends to pull itself inwards, causing a reduction of the exposed surface.
For a water droplet that is left alone, cohesive forces undoubtedly dominate, leading to surface tension. Surface tension makes all the molecules of water to stick with each other closely, this results in its spherical shape.

Q9. If a film of width L is stretched in the longitudinal direction a distance d by force F, surface tension is given by:
    (A) S = $\frac{F}{L}$
    (B) S = $\frac{F}{2L}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) S = $\frac{F}{3L}$
    (D) S = $\frac{F}{4L}$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Let L be the width of the film. Since the film has two surfaces, the total length along which the surface force acts on the slider is 2L. The surface tension S in the film is defined as the ratio of the surface force F to the length d (perpendicular to the force) along which the force acts
$S=\frac{F}{d}$
Hence, in the case, d = 2L
$S=\frac{F}{2L}$

Q10. Capillary Rise of water is explained by:
    (A) low shear strain
    (B) elasticity
    (C) surface tension   <-- CORRECT
    (D) viscosity
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Capillary action(sometimes capillarity or wicking) is the ability of a liquid to flow in narrow spaces without the assistance of, or even in opposition to, external forces like gravity. The effect can be seen in the drawing up of liquids between the hairs of a paint-brush, in porous materials such as paper and plaster, in some non-porous materials such as sand and liquefied carbon fiber, or in a cell. It occurs because of intermolecular forces between the liquid and surrounding solid surfaces. If the diameter of the tube is sufficiently small, then the combination of surface tension (which is caused by cohesion within the liquid) and adhesive forces between the liquid and container wall act to propel the liquid.

Q11. An iron needle floats on the surface of the water. This phenomenon is attributed to:
    (A) Viscosity
    (B) Up thrust of liquid
    (C) Surface tension   <-- CORRECT
    (D) Gravitational forces
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The answer lies in the surface tension of the water, As the cohesive forces between the molecules are responsible for the surface tension. When the molecules on the glass or container do not have a surface to stick to, they cohere more strongly to the molecules they are associated with. Since the molecules on top have no neighbours on the top to cohere to, they associate with their other neighbours more strongly. This creates a strong bond between the top layer of molecules. The molecules inward face no net force because they all cancel out each other. However, the surface molecules face a net inward force because there is no force for them on the surface to cancel it. This causes the molecules on the surface to 'contract' and resists being broken or stretched.
When a small body like a needle is placed onto the water carefully, the surface tension will not break due to the small mass of the object. 
As long as the surface tension is undisturbed, the needle will continue to float. Agitating the water will cause the surface tension to break and hence the needle will then sink
.

Q12. The excess pressure across the surface of a soap bubble of radius R(with $\sigma$ as the surface tension of soap solution) is:
    (A) $\frac{2\sigma}R$
    (B) $\frac\sigma R$
    (C) $\frac{4\sigma}R$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{8\sigma}R$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Let the pressure of the air outside the bubble be P1, that within the soap solution be p' and that of the air inside the bubble be P_2. Looking at the outer surface the solution is on the concave side of the surface hence 
P‚Ä≤ - P_1 = $\frac{2\sigma}R$ 
Where R is the radius of the bubble. As the thickness of the bubble is small on a macroscopic scale, the difference in the radii of the two surfaces will be negligible. 
Similarly looking at the inner surface the air is on the concave side of the surface hence‚Äã
‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚ÄãP_2 ‚àí P‚Ä≤ = $\frac{2\sigma}R$ 
Adding two equations 
P_2 ‚àí P_1 = $\frac{4\sigma}R$

Q13. Two soap bubbles are blown simultaneously. In the first soap bubble, the excess pressure is 4 times of second soap bubble. The ratio of radii of first to second soap bubble is:
    (A) 2:1
    (B) 1:2
    (C) 1:4   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 4:1
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Using relation for excess pressure in the soap bubble
$P_2-P_1=\frac{4\sigma}R$ 
$\triangle P=\frac{4\sigma}R$
thus
$\triangle P \propto \frac{1}{R}$
given $$
$\triangle P_{1}=4 \triangle P_{2}$
$\frac{\triangle P_{1}}{\triangle P_{2}}=\frac{R_{2}}{R_{1}} \Rightarrow \frac{4 \triangle P_{2}}{\triangle P_{2}}=\frac{R_{2}}{R_{1}}$
$\frac{R_{1}}{R_{2}}$ = 1:4

Q14. A drop of oil is placed on the surface of the water. Which of the following statements is correct?
    (A) It will remain on it as a sphere
    (B) It will partly change to spherical droplets and partly as a thin film
    (C) It will spread as a thin layer   <-- CORRECT
    (D) It will float as a distorted drop on the water surface
    Answer: C
    Explanation: When any liquid is poured onto another liquid there are different phenomena that can take place depending on the properties of liquids, In case of crude oil and water:
The two liquids are non-miscible and the first is lighter than the second. The first liquid then spreads on the surface of the second liquid forming a thin layer. The thickness of this layer depends on the properties of the two liquids. This is the case for most hydrocarbons, including both refined products and crude oil of API gravity(API gravity is thus an inverse measure of a petroleum liquid's density relative to that of water (also known as specific gravity). greater than 10.

Q15. Two small drops of mercury, each of radius R coalesce to form a single large drop. The ratio of the total surface energies before and after the change is
    (A) $2^{\frac{1}{3}}: 1$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 2 : 1
    (C) $1: 2^{\frac{2}{3}}$
    (D) 1 : 2
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $2 \times \frac { 4 } { 3 } \pi r ^ { 3 } = \frac { 4 } { 3 } \pi R ^ { 3 }$ 
$2 ^ \frac{1}{3} r $ = R or $\frac { r } { R } = \frac { 1 } { 2 ^ \frac{1}{3 } }$
$\frac{\text { surface energy of } 2 \text { small drops }}{\text { surface energy of large drop }}$ $=\frac{2 \times 4 \pi \times r^2 \times T}{4 \pi \times R^2 \times T}$
$\frac{E_1}{E_2}=\frac{2 r^2}{R^2}=\frac{2}{2^{\frac{2}{3}}}=2^{1 / 3}: 1$

Q1. A wooden block is taken to the bottom of a deep, calm lake of water and then released. It rises up with a:
    (A) constant velocity
    (B) decreasing velocity
    (C) decreasing acceleration
    (D) constant acceleration   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The wooden block rises up with acceleration,
a = $Upthrust-weight \ of \ block\over mass\ of\ block$ 
Upthrust and mass of block will be constant. So the acceleration will also be a constant throughout the motion.

Q2. A man in a lift is holding a can filled with water but having a hole in its bottom. When will the drainage of water be maximum?
    (A) when the lift is stationary
    (B) when the lift goes down with uniform velocity
    (C) when the lift is going down with acceleration
    (D) when the lift is going up with the acceleration   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Force acting on the liquid contained in the cylinder in the vertical directions are
 $P_1\triangle S$ upward due to the liquid below it
 $P_2\triangle S$ downward due to the liquid above it
 Applying newton's second law
 ${P_1}\Delta S - {P_2}\Delta S - mg = m{a_0}$
 $({P_1} - {P_2})\Delta S = m(g + {a_0}) $ = $ \Delta Sz\rho (g + {a_0})$
 ${P_1} - {P_2} = \rho (g + {a_0})z$
 weight mg = $\triangle$S z $\rho$ g where $\rho$ is the density of liquid
 Net force = mass $\times$ acceleration
Thus pressure difference is greatest when lift moves upwards with acceleration a because for uniform motion $a_0$ will be zero and for lift moving downwards the term in the bracket would be (g - $a_0$). So due to the maximum pressure difference between top and bottom, drainage of water will be maximum in this case.

Q3. In old age, arteries carrying blood in the human body, become narrow resulting in an increase in blood pressure. This follows from:
    (A) Archimedes principle
    (B) Pascal‚Äôs law
    (C) Bernoulli's principle   <-- CORRECT
    (D) Stoke‚Äôs law
    Answer: C
    Explanation: According to the Bernoulli principle for fluids: in ideal state pressure and density are inversely related or in other words, a slow-moving fluid exerts more pressure than a fast-moving fluid. Due to narrowness and other obstruction, the velocity of the flow of blood gets decreased in old age. This results in increased pressure inside the blood vessel.

Q4. Soap helps in better cleansing of clothes because:
    (A) chemicals of soap change
    (B) it increases the surface tension of the solution
    (C) it reduces the surface tension of the solution   <-- CORRECT
    (D) it absorbs the dirt
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Surfactants are substances that help lower the surface tension of water, so as to increase the wetting ability of water which helps in cleaning the clothes.
Soaps (which are a type of detergent) contain surfactants in significant amounts, which help water spread uniformly over clothes.

Q5. If two soap bubbles of different radii are in communication with each other
    (A) Air flows from the larger bubble into the smaller one until the two bubbles are of equal size
    (B) The sizes of the bubbles remain unchanged
    (C) Air flows from the larger into the smaller bubble until the radius of the smaller one becomes half that of the larger one
    (D) Air flows from the smaller bubble into the larger one and large bubble grows at the expense of the smaller one   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Excess pressure in a soap bubble is given by $P _ { 2 } - P _ { 1 } = \frac { 4 S } { R }$ $\Delta P = \frac { 4 S } { R }$ $\Rightarrow \triangle P \propto \frac { 1 } { R }$ 
Thus pressure difference will be large in smaller bubble or we can say that pressure inside the smaller bubble will be greater than bigger bubble. As air always flow from a high pressure to low pressure end thus in this case air will flow from smaller bubble to larger bubble and larger bubble will grow at the expanse of smaller bubble.

Q6. More liquid rises in a thin tube because of:
    (A) Smaller value of S.T
    (B) A larger value of surface tension
    (C) A larger value of the radius
    (D) A smaller value of the radius   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The rise of liquid in a thin tube is caused by forces of attraction between the molecules of water and the glass walls and among the molecules of liquid themselves. The narrower the bore of the capillary tube, the higher the water liquid.

Q7. The surface tension of the soap solution is 25 $\times$ 10^-3 Nm^-1. The excess pressure inside a soap bubble of diameter 1 cm is:
    (A) 10 Pa
    (B) 20 Pa   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 15 Pa
    (D) 5 Pa
    Answer: B
    Explanation: given surface tension S = 25 $\times$ 10^‚àí3N/m
diameter = 1cm
thus radius r = $d\over 2$ = 0.5cm excess pressure inside soap bubble
$\triangle P=\frac{4 S}{r}=\frac{4 \times 25 \times 10^{-3} \times 100}{0.5}$.
$\Delta P$ = 20pa

Q8. When a soap bubble is charged, it:
    (A) Decreases
    (B) Does not undergo any change in size
    (C) Expands   <-- CORRECT
    (D) Disappears
    Answer: C
    Explanation: A charge will be distributed uniformly on the surface of a bubble if the bubble is charged. The bubble will expand because the charged particles uniformly distributed on it cause them to repel each other due to the electrostatic force. This will happen to both positive and negatively charged bubbles because of the charge on it.

Q9. A drop of oil is placed on the surface of the water. Which of the following statements is correct?
    (A) It will remain on it as a sphere
    (B) It will spread as a thin layer   <-- CORRECT
    (C) It will float as a distorted drop on the water surface
    (D) It will partly change to spherical droplets and partly as a thin film
    Answer: B
    Explanation: When any liquid is poured onto another liquid there are different phenomena that can take place depending on the properties of liquids, In case of crude oil and water:
The two liquids are non-miscible and the first is lighter than the second. The first liquid then spreads on the surface of the second liquid forming a thin layer. The thickness of this layer depends on the properties of the two liquids. This is the case for most hydrocarbons, including both refined products and crude oil of API gravity(API gravity is thus an inverse measure of a petroleum liquid's density relative to that of water (also known as specific gravity). greater than 10.

Q10. Small droplets of liquid are usually more spherical in shape than larger drops of the same liquid because:
    (A) the force of surface tension is equal and opposite to the force of gravity
    (B) the force of surface tension predominates the force of gravity   <-- CORRECT
    (C) the force of gravity and force of surface tension act in the same direction and are equal
    (D) the force of gravity predominates the force of surface tension
    Answer: B
    Explanation: In small drops effect of gravity can be ignored because all the points can be considered at the same height. Thus to acquire minimum energy they will acquire spherical shape while in larger drops due to finite radius of drop all the points cannot be assumed at same height thus drop will try to acquire spherical shape but due to effect of gravity, its shape will be a little distorted from the pure spherical shape.

Q11. Water rises to a height of 16.3 cm in a capillary of height 18 cm above the water level. If the tube is suddenly cut at a height of 12 cm.
    (A) water will stay at a height of 12 cm in the capillary tube   <-- CORRECT
    (B) the height of the water in the capillary will be 10.3 cm
    (C) water will come as a fountain from the capillary tube
    (D) water will flow down the sides of the capillary tube
    Answer: A
    Explanation: water will stay at a height of 12 cm in the capillary tube

Q12. A spherical soap bubble has a radius ‚Ä≤r‚Ä≤. The surface tension of the soap film is T. The energy needed to double the diameter of the bubble at the same temperature is:
    (A) $8\pi {r^2}\sigma$
    (B) $4\pi {r^2}\sigma$
    (C) $14\pi {r^2}T$
    (D) $24\pi\;r^2\sigma$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $24\pi\;r^2\sigma$

Q13. A liquid drop of radius R is broken up into N small droplets. The work done is proportional to( take $N^\frac13$ >> 1).
    (A) $N^\frac13$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) N
    (C) $N^{\frac{-2}{3}}$
    (D) $N^{ - 0.{\rm{5}}}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: When a droplet of radius R is broken into N small droplets total volume will remain constant. Let the radius of small droplets be r. Then
$\frac{4}{3} \pi R^{3}=N \frac{4}{3} \pi r^{3}$
$r=\frac{R}{{N} ^\frac{1}{3}}$
work done will be equal to the change in surface energy, thus
W = $S_{f}-S_{i}=N 4 \pi r^{2} T-4 \pi R^{2} T$
W = $N 4 \pi\left(\frac{R}{N^{\frac{1}{3}}}\right)^{2} T-4 \pi R^{2} T$
W = $4 \pi R^{2} T\left(N^{\frac{1}{3}}-1\right)$
if $N^\frac{1}{3}$ is very large thus it becomes
W = 4$\pi$R^2T$N^\frac{1}{3}$
thus
$W \propto N^{\frac{1}{3}}$

Q14. An air bubble of radius r is formed inside a tank of water at depth h. The atmospheric pressure is equal to a water column of height H. the pressure inside the bubble is:
    (A) $\rho g\left(h+H\right)+\frac{4T}r$
    (B) $\rho g\left(h+H\right)+\frac{8T}r$
    (C) $\rho g\left(h+H\right)+\frac{2T}r$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\rho g\left(h+H\right)+\frac Tr$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Excess pressure in an air bubble is given by
P_2 ‚àí P_1 = $\frac{2 T}{r}$ $\longrightarrow$ surface tension r $\longrightarrow$ the radius of the bubble if the bubble is at a depth h inside then
$P_{1}=P_{a t m}+h \rho g$
given $P_{a t m}=H \rho g$
$P_{1}=\rho g(H+h)$
thus
$P_{2}=\rho g(h+H)+\frac{2 T}{r}$

Q15. It is easier to spray water to which soap is added, because:
    (A) Addition of soap to water increases the surface tension of water
    (B) Addition of soap to water increases its density
    (C) Addition of soap to water makes surface tension of water zero
    (D) Addition of soap to water decreases the surface tension of water   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The addition of soap decreases the surface tension of water, as we know the energy of spraying is directly proportional to the surface tension.

Q1. A capillary tube remains dipped in a water container so that loss in weight of the capillary tube is:
    (A) less than the upward buoyant force
    (B) more than the upward buoyant force
    (C) equal to the upward buoyant force   <-- CORRECT
    (D) half of the buoyant force
    Answer: C
    Explanation: This is Archimedes principle when an object submerged in a liquid its weight is equal to the Buoyant force.

Q2. One thousand small water droplets of equal size combine to form a big drop. The ratio of the final surface energy to the total initial surface energy is:
    (A) 1:10   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 1:1000
    (C) 1000:1
    (D) 10:1
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Let 1000 small droplets of radius r combine to form a bigger drop of radius R. As total volume remains constant 
$1000 \times \frac{4}{3} \pi r^{3}=\frac{4}{3} \pi R^{3}$
$\Rightarrow$ R = 10r
initial surface energy
$S_{i}$ = surface area $\times$ surface tension
$S_{i}=1000 \times 4 \pi r^{2} \times T$
final surface energy
$S_{f}=4 \pi R^{2} \times T$
$S_{f}=100 \times 4 \pi r^{2} \times T$
taking ratio
$\frac{S_{f}}{S_{i}}=\frac{100 \times 4 \pi r^{2} \times T}{1000 \times 4 \pi r^{2} \times T}$
$\Rightarrow$ 1:10

Q3. The unit of surface tension in S.I units is given by:
    (A) dynes per cm^2
    (B) Newtons per meter   <-- CORRECT
    (C) dynes per cm
    (D) Newtons per meter^2
    Answer: B
    Explanation: surface tension is measured in force per unit length. The S.I unit is a newton per meter but the CGS unit dyne per centimeter is also used.

Q4. What will be the height of a liquid column in a capillary tube on the surface of the moon?
    (A) 36 times that on earth's surface
    (B) Six times that on the earth's surface   <-- CORRECT
    (C) It will remain unchanged
    (D) 1/6th of what was on earth's surface
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The height to which the liquid rises in a capillary tube is inversion all proportional to the acceleration due to gravity.
$h=\frac{2 S \cos \theta}{r \rho g}$
thus if all other parameters are fixed 
$h \propto\frac{1}{g}$
On the surface of the moon, acceleration due to gravity is $1\over6$th as that on the surface of the earth. Therefore the water will rise to a height 6 times that on earth's surface.

Q5. Two match sticks are laid side by side on the surface of the water. When a drop of light oil on the end of a third match stick is touched to the surface film between the two floating sticks, the two match sticks
    (A) will remain as they are
    (B) will be joined
    (C) will move apart rapidly   <-- CORRECT
    (D) will come closer
    Answer: C
    Explanation: They will move apart rapidly because the surface tension of oil is lesser than that of water.

Q6. Soldering of two metals possible because of the property of:
    (A) surface tension
    (B) cohesion   <-- CORRECT
    (C) osmosis
    (D) viscosity
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Cohesion is the attractive force among the same kind of materials. Thus soldering of two metals is possible due to cohesion.

Q7. When the adhesive force in the case of liquid and glass is greater than the cohesive forces between the liquid molecules, the shape of the meniscus of liquid in a capillary tube is?
    (A) Circular
    (B) Concave   <-- CORRECT
    (C) Convex
    (D) Plane
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The formation of meniscus depends on cohesive and adhesive forces in a liquid. For water, adhesive forces are stronger than the cohesive forces, therefore, water in a container stick to the wall of the container and owing to the capillary action rises a little bit and form a concave meniscus.
When liquid water is confined in a tube, its surface (meniscus) has a concave shape because water wets the surface and creeps up the side.

Q8. If the work done in blowing a bubble of volume V is W, then the work done in blowing a soap bubble of volume 2V will be
    (A) $\sqrt 2 W$
    (B) 2W
    (C) W
    (D) $\sqrt[3]4W$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Work done to blow a bubble will be equal to its surface energy 
W = S = surface area $\times$ surface tension S = A $\times$ T
Given that work done to blow a bubble of volume V is W. 
W = S = surface area $\times$ surface tension, S = A $\times$ T for volume V
S = 4$\pi$r^2 $\times$ T
S $\propto$ r^2 but radius r $\propto$ Volume$(v)^{1\over 3}$
thus S $\propto$ $(V)^{2\over 3}$
$\Rightarrow$ $W \propto V^{\frac{2}{3}}$
$\Rightarrow$ thus if the volume is doubled 
the W_2 becomes $(2)^{\frac{2}{3}} \Rightarrow(4)^{\frac{1}{3}} W_{1}$
thus $W_{2}=\sqrt[3]{4} W_{1}$

Q9. Two tubes of the same material but of different radii are dipped in a liquid. The height to which a liquid rises in one tube is 2.2cm and in the other is 6.6 cm. The ratio of their radii is:
    (A) 3:1   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 1:9
    (C) 9:1
    (D) 1:3
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Using the relation for the height of the liquid in a capillary tube 
$h=\frac{2 S \cos \theta}{r \rho g}$
thus if all other parameters are fixed
$h \propto \frac{1}{r}$
$\Rightarrow \frac{h_{1}}{h_{2}}=\frac{r_{2}}{r_{1}}$ given h_1 = 2.2 and h_2 = 6.6
$\frac{2.2}{6.6}=\frac{r_{2}}{r_{1}}$
$\frac{r_{1}}{r_{2}}$ = 3 : 1

Q10. A raft of wood of density 600kg/m^3 and mass 120 kg floats in water. How much weight can be put on the raft to make it just sink?
    (A) 80kg   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 40kg
    (C) 120 kg
    (D) 200kg
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Given mass of raft M = 120 k.g.
density of raft = 600 k.g./m^3
thus volume of raft V = 
$V = \frac{{Mass}}{{density}} = \frac{{120}}{{600}} = 0.2{m^3}$
when raft just sinks inside after placing extra mass m. thus weight of
(raft + extra mass m)
will be equal to the buoyant force. so
(M + m)g = $V\rho g$
$120 + m = 0.2 \times 1000$
m = 80 kg.

Q11. If a block of iron of unknown mass ‚Äã‚Äã‚Äãof size 5 cm $ \times $ 5 cm $ \times $ 5 cm was completely submerged in water, what is the mass of the block. (density of water = 1 gm/cm^3)
    (A) 100 ‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äãgm
    (B) 125 gm‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 90 ‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äãgm
    (D) 80 ‚Äã‚Äã‚Äãgm
    Answer: B
    Explanation: When it is submerged completely its weight will be balanced by the buoyant force which is equal to the mass of water displaced. Thus let the mass of block be m than at equilibrium
mg = VœÅg
m = 5 $\times$ 5 $\times$5 $\times$ 1
m = 125gm

Q12. A boy is carrying a bucket of water in one hand with a 0.05 kg cork piece in it. After some time he takes out the cork piece and holds it on the other hand. The boy will carry:
    (A) Same load as before   <-- CORRECT
    (B) Either less or more load depending on the density of plastic
    (C) Less load than before
    (D) More load than before
    Answer: A
    Explanation: as there is no change in total weight only the place of the cork piece has changed.

Q13. A beaker is partly filled with water, the beaker and the contents have a mass of 50 gm. A piece of wood having a volume of 5 cc. is floated in the beaker. The density of wood is 0.8 g/cc. the mass of the beaker and its contents is:
    (A) 54 g   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 46 g
    (C) 56.25 g
    (D) 50 g
    Answer: A
    Explanation: v = $m\over d$
m = v $\times$ d . . . . .(mass = density $\times$ volume)
total mass = 50 + 0.8 $\times$ 5 = 50 + 4 = 54 g

Q14. A bird, resting on the floor of an airtight box, which is being carried by a boy, starts flying. The boy will feel that the box:
    (A) Is now lighter
    (B) Is now heavier
    (C) Lighter in the beginning and heavier afterwards
    (D) Shows no change in weight   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: If the cage is completely closed, it doesn't make a difference if the bird is hovering inside it or if it sits on the ground. When flying, the bird pushes air to the ground which will exert a downward force on the cage exactly equal to the weight of the bird. This is a direct consequence of the conservation of momentum and Newton's second & third law. Since no additional external force is acting on the cage-bird-system when the bird is flying as compared to when it's not, the acceleration on the cage can be no different. The effect due to the flying of the bird only concerns the internal forces and since action = reaction, hence there is no change in the weight of the box.

Q15. A cube of side 10 cm is floating in equilibrium in a tank of water. When a mass of 10 gm is placed on the cube, the depth of the cube inside the water increases by: (g = 10 ms^‚àí2 density of water = 10^3 kg m^‚àí3)
    (A) 1mm   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 1 cm
    (C) 0.1 m
    (D) 0.1 mm
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Consider initially x c.m. height of cube is inside the water. For floatation, its weight will be balanced by a buoyant force which is equal to the weight of water displaced. Thus let the mass of cube is m. Given side of cube = 10 c.m. thus at equilibrium
Weight of cube = buoyant force
mg = V$\rho$g
where $\rho$ is the density of water and volume of cube inside will be $\left(\frac{10}{100}\right)^{2}$metre^3
m = $x\left(\frac{10}{100}\right)^{2} 1000$
m = 10x $\rightarrow$ (1)
now if a block of 10 g is placed on it then let the height of cube inside is (x + y)
thus at equilibrium
$\left(m+\frac{10}{1000}\right) g=(x+y)\left(\frac{10}{100}\right)^{2} 1000 g$
using(1)
(10x + $\frac{1}{{100}}$) = (x + y)10 $\Rightarrow$ 1000x + 1 = 1000x + 1000y
y = $1\over 1000$metre = 1mm
Thus after placing 10gm mass height of cube in water will increase by 1mm.

Q1. A wooden cube just floats inside the water when a 200gm mass is placed on it. When the mass is removed, the cube is 2 cm above the water level. The size of the cube is:
    (A) 15 cm
    (B) 20 cm
    (C) 5 cm
    (D) 10 cm   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Let the side of the cube be x, the density of water be $\rho$, the mass of cube is m, acceleration due to gravity be g.
When the weight is on the block,
Using Archimedes‚Äô Principle,
Weight of the block + Weight of the mass = Buoyant force (B_1)
Since Bouyant force is equal to the volume of liquid displaced by the body thus 
B_1 = $\rho g x^{3}$
thus (m + 200) g = $\rho$ gx^3
m = $\rho$x^3 - 200 $\rightarrow$ (1)
when mass is removed the wooden cube is 2 cm outside
thus volume of cube inside = volume of water displaced = (x - 2) x^2
again balance the buoyant force B_2 = $\rho$g(x - 2) x^2
$\rho$g(x - 2) x^2 = mg
$\Rightarrow$ $\rho$(x - 2) x^2 = m
substitute for m
$\rho$(x - 2) x^2 = $\rho$x^3 - 200
$\Rightarrow$ 2 $\rho$x^2 = 200
take $\rho$ = 1 gram/cm^3
$\Rightarrow$ x = 10 cm

Q2. Pressure applied to an enclosed fluid is:
    (A) increased in proportion to the mass of the fluid and then transmitted.
    (B) transmitted unchanged to every portion of the fluid and walls of the containing vessel.   <-- CORRECT
    (C) diminished and transmitted to the wall of the container.
    (D) increased and applied to every part of the fluid.
    Answer: B
    Explanation: According to Pascal's law (or the principle of transmission of fluid-pressure) is a principle in fluid mechanics that states that a pressure change occurring anywhere in a confined incompressible fluid is transmitted throughout the fluid such that the same change occurs everywhere.

Q3. A parrot is in a wire case, which is hanging from a spring balance. Initially, the parrot sits in the cage and in the second instance the parrot flies inside the cage, the reading of the balance will be:
    (A) Normal when the parrot flies in the cage
    (B) Remain unchanged
    (C) Lesser when the parrot flies in the cage   <-- CORRECT
    (D) Greater when the parrot flies in the cage
    Answer: C
    Explanation: For the bird to stay in the air, the wings must push down on the air under them. If the cage is airtight, the air exerts an equal force on the floor of the cage. The net force down on the scale will remain constant. If the cage is not airtight (i.e; the wire cage), the air will move down and horizontal. The force down will be less than the weight of the bird.

Q4. If there would be smaller gravitational effects, which of the following forces do you think would alter in some respect?
    (A) Electrostatic forces
    (B) Nuclear forces
    (C) Viscous forces
    (D) Buoyant forces   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Buoyancy force on an object is equal to the weight of the fluid displaced by the object, or the density of the fluid multiplied by the submerged volume times the gravitational acceleration.

Q5. If you float on your back on the water, your weight is:
    (A) Half to your normal weight
    (B) Equal to your normal weight
    (C) Greater than the weight of water you displace
    (D) Zero   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Due to the buoyant force of water, the buoyancy of water reduces your body weight by 90%. When you get into the pool, your body displaces a volume of water (the "hole" in the water that your body fits into). As long as your body displaces water weighs more than you do, you float. This is basically Archimedes' Law. You weigh less than the water you're in, because your lungs are full of air, like a balloon, and like a balloon, the air in your lungs lifts you to the surface naturally.

Q6. The height of the mercury column in a simple barometer is h. As the tube is inclined to the vertical at an angle $\alpha$ the length of the mercury column along the length of the tube is l, then:
    (A) l = h
    (B) l = h sin$\alpha$
    (C) $1=\frac{h}{\cos \alpha}$
    (D) l = h cos$\alpha$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: This will form a right-angled triangle with base l and hypotenuse h and the angle between base and hypotenuse is 
$\alpha$ thus cos$\alpha$ = $\frac{l}{h}$ 
l = h cos $\alpha$

Q7. A cylindrical vessel is filled with an equal mass of mercury and water. The overall height of the two layers is 43.8 cm. The relative density of mercury is 13.6. Then the pressure of the liquid on the bottom of the vessel is:
    (A) P_atm + 6 cm of Hg   <-- CORRECT
    (B) P_atm + 1.5 cm of Hg
    (C) P_atm + 16.6 cm of Hg
    (D) P_atm +‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã 3 cm of Hg
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Given total height H of vessel = 43.8 cm 
As mass of mercury and water is equal
$\rho_{h g} V_{h g}=\rho_{w} V_{w} $ given $ \rho_{h g}$ = 13.6 $\rho_{w} $)
13.6 $\times$ $\pi r^{2} h_{h g}$ = $\pi r^{2} h_{w}$
h_w = 13.6 $h_{h g}$ $\rightarrow$ (1)
given h_w + h_hg = 43.8 $\rightarrow$ (2)
from(1) and (2)
h_w = 40.8 cm and h_hg = 3 cm 
Thus pressure at bottom will be
P = P_atm + $\rho_{h g} g h_{h g} + \rho_{w} g h_{w} $
P = $P_{a t m} + \left(\rho_{h g} g \times 3\right) + \frac{\rho_{h g}}{13.6} g \times 40.8$ 
P = P_atm + 3cm of Hg + 3cm of Hg
P = P_atm + 6 cm of Hg

Q8. Two bodies are in equilibrium when suspended in water from the arms of a balance. The mass of one body is 36 gm and its density is 9 gm/cc. If the mass of the other is 48 gm, its density in gm/cm^3 is:
    (A) 5
    (B) 3.0   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac{3}{2}$
    (D) $\frac{4}{3}$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: W_1 = W_2 
$m_1 g -v_1\rho_\omega g = m_2g - v_2\rho_\omega g$
by simplifying the equation, we get 
‚Äã‚Äã36‚àí4 = 48‚àíV_2
V_2 = 48‚àí32 = 16m^3
the density of body is given by 
$\rho=\frac{m_2}{V_2}$
$ = \frac{48}{16}$ = 3g/cm^3

Q9. Three liquids of densities d, 2d, and 3 d are mixed in equal volumes. Then the density of the mixture is:
    (A) 5d
    (B) 2d   <-- CORRECT
    (C) d
    (D) 3d
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The density of the mixture is given by
$\rho_{\operatorname{mix}}=\frac{\text {total mass}}{\text {total volume}}=\frac{m_{1}+m_{2}+m_{3}}{v_{1}+v_{2}+v_{3}}$
asv_1 = v_2 = v_3
$\rho_{\operatorname{mix}}=\frac{\rho_{1} v+\rho_{2} v+\rho_{3} v}{v+v+v}$
$\Rightarrow \frac{d v+2 d v+3 d v}{3 v}$
$\rho_{m ix}$ = 2d

Q10. Three liquids of densities d, 2d and 3d are mixed in equal proportion of weights. If density of water is d, then the specific gravity of the mixture is
    (A) $\frac{13}{9}$
    (B) $\frac{23}{18}$
    (C) $\frac{11}{7}$
    (D) $\frac{18}{11}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: w_1 = w_2 = w_3
$\Rightarrow$ m_1 = m_2 = m_3 = m (say)
Then, v_1 = $\frac{m}{d}$, v_2 = $\frac{m}{2d}$, v_3 = $\frac{m}{3d}$
$\therefore$ d_max = $\frac{mass}{volume}$= $\frac{3m}{v_1+v_2+v_3}$ = $\frac{18}{11}d$
So, specific gravity of mixture = $\frac{d_{max}}{d_{water}}$ = $\frac{18}{11}$

Q11. What fraction of the volume of a solid piece of metal of sp gr 7.2 floats above the surface of a container of mercury of sp. gr. 13.6?
    (A) 0.53
    (B) 0.78
    (C) 0.47   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 0.75
    Answer: C
    Explanation: V = Volume of metal
V‚Äô = Volume of mercury displaced 
Weight of body = Weight of mercury displaced 
= 7.2 $\times$ 9810 $\times$ V = 13.6 $\times$9810 $\times$V‚Ä≤
$\Rightarrow$ ${V'\over V }= 0.53$ 
Fraction of volume above mercury = 1 - 0.53 = 0.47

Q12. Which one of the following statements is correct for fluid passing through the narrow part of the non-uniform pipe?
    (A) its velocity increases but its pressure decreases   <-- CORRECT
    (B) its velocity and pressure both decrease
    (C) its velocity decreases but its pressure increases
    (D) its velocity and pressure both increase
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Consider two points A and B in the pipe at the same horizontal level. At point A, let a_1 be the area of cross-section of the pipe, v_1 the velocity of fluid flow, and p_1 the fluid pressure. let a_2, v_2, p_2 be the corresponding quantities at point B. then from Bernoulli's theorem we have, 
$p_1 +{1\over 2}\rho v_1^2 = p_2 +{1\over 2}\rho v_2^2 $
$\Rightarrow (p_1-p_2) = {1\over 2} \rho (v_2^2-v_1^2) \ $....(1)
if a_2 < a_1, it follows from continuity equation a_1v_1= a_2v_2 that v_1 > v_2 from eq (1) it follows that p_2 < p_1, Hence at the narrow part of the pipe, the fluid velocity increase but its pressure decrease.

Q13. A cylindrical jar of cross-sectional area 0.01 m^2 is filled with water to a height of 50 cm. It carries a tight-fitting piston of negligible mass. What is the pressure at the bottom of the jar when the mass of 1 kg is placed on the piston? Take g = 10ms^‚àí2.
    (A) 2000 Pa
    (B) 6000 Pa   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 5000 Pa
    (D) 4000 Pa
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The pressure at the bottom of the jar is due to the weight of a column of water of height h = 50 cm = 0.5 m and the weight of a load of m = 1 kg.
The total force acting on the base = $h\rho g A+ mg $ 
= 0.5 $\times $ 1000 $\times $ 10 $\times $ 0.01 + 1 $\times $10 = 60N
$\therefore$ Pressure =$ {force \over area} = {60\over 0.01 } = 6000 \ Nm^{-2} \ $ or 6000 Pa

Q14. Two vessels A and B of different shapes have the same base area and are filled with water up to the same height h. The force exerted by water on the base is ${{\rm{F}}_{\rm{A}}}$ for vessel A and ${{\rm{F}}_{\rm{B}}}$ for vessel B. The respective weights of the vessels are ${{\rm{W}}_{\rm{A}}}$ and ${{\rm{W}}_{\rm{B}}}$. Then
    (A) F_A > F_B; W_A > W_B
    (B) F_A < F_B; W_A > WB
    (C) F_A = F_B; W_A > W_B   <-- CORRECT
    (D) F_A = F_B; W_A = W_B
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Since h is, the same for both vessels, pressure = $h\rho g$ is the same. Now force = $pressure \times base \ area $. Since the base area is the same, the force exerted by water on the base is the same for both vessels. Thus $F_A= F_B$.
The liquid exerts pressure, and hence force, not only on the base but also on the sides of the vessel. This force is normal to the sides of the vessel. For vessel B, this force has no component in the downward direction. but for vessel A, this force ( which is normal to the sides) has a non-zero component vertically downwards. Hence vessel A gives higher reading than vessel B when they are weighed on a weighing scale. Thus $W_A > W_B$

Q15. A tank with a square base of area 2.0 m^2 is divided into two compartments by a vertical partition in the middle. There is a small hinged door of face area 20 cm$^{\rm{2}}$ at the bottom of the partition. Water is filled in one compartment and acid, of relative density 1.7, in the other, both to a height of 4m. If g = 10 ms^-2 the force necessary to keep the door closed is:
    (A) 40.52 N
    (B) 54.88 N   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 20.92 N
    (D) 80.88 N
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Water compartment
P = $ h\rho g $
= 4 $\times$ 1.0 $\times$ 10^3 $\times$ 9.8
= 39.2 $\times$ 10^3Pa
Acid Compartment,
P' = $h\rho'g$
= 4 $\times$ 1.7 $\times$ 10^3 $\times$ 9.8
= 66.64 $\times$ 10^3Pa
Now,
P‚Ä≤ ‚àí P = 66.64 $\times$ 10^3 ‚àí 39.2 $\times$ 10^3
P‚Ä≤ ‚àí P = 27.44 $\times$ 10^3 Pa
A = 20 cm^2 = 20 $\times$ 10^‚àí4m^2
Force using pressure,
Pressure = $ {Force \over Area}$
Force = pressure $\times$ area F = 27.44 $\times$ 10^3 $\times$ 20 $\times$ 10^‚àí4
F = 54.88 N

Q1. Heat is a form of_______.
    (A) gas
    (B) liquid
    (C) solid
    (D) energy   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Heat is the amount of energy flowing from one body of matter to another spontaneously due to their temperature difference.

Q2. Heat flows from one body to another if:
    (A) there is a difference of material
    (B) there is a difference in temperatures   <-- CORRECT
    (C) there is a difference of density
    (D) there is no difference in temperature
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Heat is the amount of energy flowing from one body of matter to another spontaneously due to their temperature difference.

Q3. The ice and steam point have values 32^oF and 212^oF respectively, on the Fahrenheit scale. Corresponding values on the Centigrade scale are:
    (A) 5^oC and 102^oC
    (B) -5^oC and 95^oC
    (C) 0^oC and 100^oC   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 10^oC and 112^oC
    Answer: C
    Explanation: ${C \over 5} = {{F - 32} \over 9}$
 for ice , at 32 ^0F ; C= 0^ 0C
for steam , at 212^ 0F ; C= 5 $\times$ $212-32\over 9$= 100 ^0C

Q4. 180 equal intervals between two reference points on the Fahrenheit scale correspond to:
    (A) 50 equal intervals on the Celsius scale
    (B) 110 equal intervals on the Celsius scale
    (C) 100 equal intervals on the Celsius scale   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 90 equal intervals on the Celsius scale
    Answer: C
    Explanation: ${{\Delta {T_C}} \over {100}} = {{\Delta {T_F}} \over {180}}$

Q5. The correct equation for conversion between Fahrenheit temperature t_F and Celsius temperature t_C is:
    (A) $\frac{t_F-32}{160}=\frac{t_C}{100}$
    (B) $\frac{t_F+32}{180}=\frac{t_C}{100}$
    (C) $\frac{t_F-32}{180}=\frac{t_C}{120}$
    (D) $\frac{t_p-32}{180}=\frac{t_C}{100}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $\frac{T_Z-T_L}{T_U-T_L}=\frac{T_C-0}{100-0}=\frac{T_F-32}{212-32}$ 
TU = B.P. of water
TL = M.P. of ice

Q6. If P is the pressure, $\mu $ is the number of moles in the sample, T the temperature and V the volume of gas, the ideal-gas equation is:
    (A) PV = $\frac{\mu R}{T}$
    (B) PV = $\mu $RT   <-- CORRECT
    (C) PV = RT
    (D) PV = $\mu $T
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $V \propto \frac{NT}{P}$
$\frac{PV}{NT} = k$
$N = \mu {N_A}$
$\frac{PV}{{\mu T}} = {N_A}k = R$
PV = $ \mu RT$

Q7. Plots of pressure as a function of temperature for gas thermometers containing different types and quantities of gas converge to the same point when extrapolated to zero pressure. The temperature value to which converge is.
    (A) -212.00^oC
    (B) -273.15^oC   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 232.15^oC
    (D) 32.15^oC
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Absolute zero temp. = 0K

Q8. Relation between Kelvin (T) and Celsius scales (t_C) is
    (A) T = t_C + 32.15
    (B) T = t_C + 273.15   <-- CORRECT
    (C) T = t_C + 180.15
    (D) T = t_C + 212.15
    Answer: B
    Explanation: ${{{T_Z} - {T_L}} \over {{T_U} - {T_L}}} = {{{T_C} - 0} \over {100 - 0}} = {{{T_K} - 273.15} \over {373.15 - 273.15}}$

Q9. If the substance is in the form of a long rod, then for small change in temperature, $\Delta {\rm{T}}$, the fractional change in length, $\frac {\Delta l} {l}$, is
    (A) inversely proportional to log $\Delta {\rm{T}}$
    (B) directly proportional to log $\Delta {\rm{T}}$
    (C) inversely proportional to $\Delta {\rm{T}}$
    (D) directly proportional to $\Delta {\rm{T}}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $\alpha=\frac{\Delta l}{l \times \Delta T}$
$\frac{\Delta l}{l}=a \Delta T$
where $\alpha$ is the temperature coefficient of linear expansion

Q10. compared to glass copper expands:
    (A) two times less
    (B) five times more   <-- CORRECT
    (C) three times less
    (D) about the same
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The coefficient of linear expansion is more for copper than that of glass.

Q11. If V is the initial volume, $\Delta {\rm{V}}$ the fractional change in volume of a substance for temperature change $\Delta {\rm{T}}$, the coefficient of volume expansion, ${\gamma _{\rm{V}}}$ is defined as:
    (A) $\gamma_V=\left(\frac{\Delta V}{V}\right) \frac{1}{\Delta T}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\gamma_V=\left(\frac{V}{2 V}\right) \frac{1}{T}$
    (C) $\gamma_V=\left(\frac{2 V}{V}\right) \frac{1}{T}$
    (D) $\gamma_V=\left(\frac{V}{V}\right) \frac{1}{2 T}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Increase in volume per unit original volume per Kelvin rise in temperature is called coefficient of volume expansion, $\gamma_V=\left(\frac{\Delta V}{V}\right) \frac{1}{\Delta T}$

Q12. Which of these materials has a low coefficient of volume expansion?
    (A) water
    (B) pyrex glass   <-- CORRECT
    (C) alcohol
    (D) paraffin
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Glass is a ceramic material made from silica. So that it has a lower coefficient of volume expansion in comparison to metal and liquid.

Q13. Which of these materials have high coefficients of volume expansion ${\alpha _{\rm{V}}}$?
    (A) glass
    (B) invar
    (C) alcohol   <-- CORRECT
    (D) pyrex glass
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Alcohol has a higher coefficient of volume expansion in comparison to glass and Invar.

Q14. expansion of water is called anomalous because:
    (A) it contracts on heating from 0^oC to 4^oC   <-- CORRECT
    (B) it expands on heating from 0^oC to 4^oC
    (C) it contracts on cooling from 4^oC to 0^oC
    (D) it expands on cooling from 10^oC to 4^oC
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Liquids expand on heating and contract on cooling at moderate temperatures. But water exhibits exceptional behaviour. When it is heated from 0^oC, it actually contracts rather than expanding, up to 4^oC. The volume of water is minimum at 4^oC. Beyond 4^oC, water starts expanding. This behavior of water between 0^oC and 4^oC is called the anomalous expansion of water.

Q15. Animal and plant life in lakes and ponds are not destroyed because:
    (A) water expands on heating at all temperatures
    (B) water contracts on cooling at all temperatures
    (C) freezing takes place at the top first and bottom layers are not frozen as temperature in bottom layers is more than zero   <-- CORRECT
    (D) freezing takes place at the bottom first
    Answer: C
    Explanation: As and when water is cooled down to its freezing point the thickness T of the ice layer increases. The increase in thickness of ice decreases the rate of cooling of water and thus freezing process slows down.

Q1. For an ideal gas the coefficient of volume expansion at constant pressure is:
    (A) $\frac{1}{2T}$; T is the temperature
    (B) $\frac{1}{3T}$; T is the temperature
    (C) $\frac{2}{T}$; T is the temperature
    (D) $\frac{1}{T}$; T is the temperature   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $\gamma=\frac{\Delta V}{V \Delta T}$
PV = nRT
V = $\frac{n R T}{P}$
$P \Delta V=n R \Delta T$
$\Delta V=\frac{n R \Delta T}{P}$
$\gamma=\frac{\frac{n R \Delta T}{P}}{\frac{n R T}{P}} \times \frac{1}{\Delta T}$ = $\frac{n R \Delta T}{P} \times \frac{P}{n R T} \times \frac{1}{\Delta T}=\frac{1}{T}$

Q2. The coefficient of volume expansion is n times coefficient of linear expansion where n equals:
    (A) 3   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 4
    (C) 5
    (D) 2
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\alpha = \left( {{{\Delta l} \over l}} \right){1 \over {\Delta T}}$ and $\gamma = \left( {{{\Delta V} \over V}} \right){1 \over {\Delta T}}$ 
Volume V = L^3
expanded length = $L(1 + \alpha \Delta T)$ 
expanded Volume = ${\left[ {L(1 + \alpha \Delta T)} \right]^3} = {L^3}(1 + {\alpha ^2}\Delta {T^2} + 2\alpha \Delta T)(1 + \alpha \Delta T)$ 
very small term is neglected
expanded volume = $V(1 + 3\alpha \Delta T)$ 
$\frac{\Delta l}{l}=a \Delta T$
$\gamma = \left( {{{\Delta V} \over V}} \right){1 \over {\Delta T}} = {{3\alpha V\Delta T} \over V}{1 \over {\Delta T}}$ 
$ \gamma = 3\alpha$

Q3. The coefficient of area expansion is n times of coefficient of linear expansion. where n equals
    (A) 3
    (B) 2   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 5
    (D) 4
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $\alpha = \left( {{{\Delta l} \over l}} \right){1 \over {\Delta T}}$ and $\beta = \left( {{{\Delta A} \over A}} \right){1 \over {\Delta T}}$ 
area A = L^2
expanded length = $L(1 + \alpha \Delta T)$ 
expanded area = ${\left[ {L(1 + \alpha \Delta T)} \right]^2} $ = $ {L^2}(1 + {\alpha ^2}\Delta {T^2} + 2\alpha \Delta T)$ 
${\alpha ^2}\Delta {T^2}$ is very small
expanded area = $A(1 + 2\alpha \Delta T)$ 
$\Delta A = 2\alpha A\Delta T$
$\beta=\left(\frac{\Delta A}{A}\right) \frac{1}{\Delta T}=\frac{2 \alpha A \Delta T}{A} \frac{1}{\Delta T}$
$\beta=2 \alpha$ 
So that n = 2

Q4. Thermal stress is stress-induced in solids:
    (A) if thermal expansion is allowed by floating it‚Äôs ends
    (B) due to vibration of molecules
    (C) due to dropping in cold water
    (D) if thermal expansion is prevented by fixing its ends rigidly   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: If temperature deformation is permitted to occur freely, no load or stress will be induced in the structure. In some cases where temperature deformation is not permitted, internal stress is created. The internal stress created is termed as thermal stress.

Q5. If m is the mass of a substance, $\Delta {\rm{Q}}$ the energy required to raise the temperature by $\Delta {\rm{T}}$ the specific heat is given by:
    (A) s = $\Delta {\rm{Q}}$.$\Delta {\rm{T}}$
    (B) $s=\frac{1}{m} \frac{Q}{\Delta T}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) s = m$\Delta {\rm{Q}}$$\cdot$$\Delta {\rm{T}}$
    (D) $\mathrm{S}=\frac{\Delta \mathrm{Q}}{\Delta \mathrm{T}}$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The specific heat is the amount of heat per unit mass required to raise the temperature by one degree Celsius.

Q6. The latent heat of fusion is. (L_f)
    (A) the heat per unit mass required for changing a substance from liquid into solid, at the same temperature and pressure
    (B) the heat per unit mass required for changing a substance from solid into liquid, at high pressure and low temperature
    (C) the heat per unit mass required for changing a substance from solid into liquid, at high temperature and low pressure
    (D) the heat per unit mass required for changing a substance from solid into liquid, at the same temperature and pressure   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The enthalpy of fusion of a substance, also known as (latent) heat of fusion, is the change in its enthalpy resulting from providing energy, typically heat, to a specific quantity of the substance to change its state from a solid to a liquid at constant pressure.

Q7. The latent heat of vaporization $\left( {{{\rm{L}}_{\rm{v}}}} \right)$ is:
    (A) the heat per unit mass required to change a substance from liquid to the vapor state at high pressure and low temperature
    (B) the heat per unit mass required to change a substance from liquid to the vapor state without a change in the temperature and pressure.   <-- CORRECT
    (C) the heat per unit mass required to change a substance from vapor to the liquid state without a change in the temperature and pressure.
    (D) the heat per unit mass required to change a substance from liquid to the vapor state, at high temperature and low pressure
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The latent heat of vaporization is actually the total amount of enthalpy (a kind of energy/heat) necessary to accomplish a phase change for a liquid or gas at the boiling/condensation point. Phase changes are generally considered at constant pressure, rather than constant volume.

Q8. In which mode of heat transfer no medium is required?
    (A) none of the three
    (B) radiation   <-- CORRECT
    (C) conduction
    (D) convection
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Heat transfer through radiation takes place in the form of electromagnetic waves.

Q9. In which mode of heat transfer is there material movement?
    (A) convection   <-- CORRECT
    (B) radiation
    (C) conduction
    (D) emit
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Convection is the heat transfer due to the bulk movement of molecules within fluids.

Q10. If k is the thermal conductivity of the material of the bar of cross-section A whose ends are maintained at temperatures T_C and T_D, the rate of flow of heat H is:
    (A) $H=k A \frac{T_C+T_D}{L}$
    (B) $H=k A \frac{T_C+T_D}{2 L}$
    (C) $H=k A \frac{T_C-T_D}{2 L}$
    (D) H = $k A \frac{T_C-T_D}{L}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: rate of flow of heat H depends on
H $\propto$ A
H $\propto$ $\Delta$T
$H \propto \frac{1}{L}$ 
hence $H \propto \frac{A\Delta T}{L}$ 
$H = K\frac{A\Delta T}{L}$ 
K = thermal conductivity of the material

Q11. If T_1 is the temperature of the surrounding medium and T_2 is the temperature of the body, then according to Newton‚Äôs Law of Cooling, rate of cooling of the body is:
    (A) $\frac{dQ}{dt} = - k({T_2} + {T_1})$
    (B) $\frac{dQ} {dt} = - k({T_2} - {T_1})$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac{dQ} {dt} = k({T_2}^2 - {T_1}^2)$
    (D) $\frac{dQ} {dt} = k({T_2}^2 + {T_1}^2)$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Newton's law of cooling states that the rate of heat loss of a body is directly proportional to the difference in the temperatures between the body and its surroundings.

Q12. The average kinetic energy of the molecules in a substance is most closely associated with:
    (A) Contraction
    (B) heat
    (C) temperature   <-- CORRECT
    (D) expansion
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $E = {3 \over 2}kT$

Q13. The Celsius temperature at absolute zero is equal to:
    (A) 100^o C
    (B) -100^o C
    (C) 273^o C
    (D) -273^o C   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: ${{{T_C} - 0} \over {100 - 0}} = {{{T_K} - 273.15} \over {373.15 - 273.15}}$

Q14. Which of the following is true of the Celsius and Kelvin temperature scales?
    (A) Both the Celsius and Kelvin temperature scales have negative values.
    (B) A Kelvin degree is smaller in size than a Celsius degree.
    (C) A Kelvin degree is larger in size than a Celsius degree.
    (D) A Kelvin degree and a Celsius degree are equivalent in size.   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: A change of one degree Kelvin or one degree Celsius means an equal change in temperature.

Q15. In general, when a solid is heated, it:
    (A) contracts inversely proportionally to the change in temperature
    (B) expands proportionally to the change in temperature   <-- CORRECT
    (C) expands inversely proportionally to the change in temperature
    (D) contracts proportionally to the change in temperature
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $\Delta l=\beta V \Delta T $
$\Delta A=\alpha V \Delta T$
$\Delta V=\gamma V \Delta T$

Q1. A brass spring has a spring constant k. When the spring is heated, the spring constant will:
    (A) remain the same
    (B) increase
    (C) increase, then decrease
    (D) decrease   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: on heating spring will be expand
$k \propto {1 \over l}$

Q2. Which of the following statement(s) is/are true?
 Every substance contains heat.
 For heat to flow between two substances, they must be at different temperatures.
 The internal energy of a substance is equal to the kinetic energy of the molecules in the substance.
    (A) II only   <-- CORRECT
    (B) II and III only
    (C) III only
    (D) I and II only
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Heat is the form of energy that is transferred from one body to another as the result of a difference in temperature.

Q3. A washer consists of a 3.00 cm diameter circle of sheet metal with a 1.00 cm diameter circular hole in the middle. If the metal washer is heated until the diameter of the washer is 3.03 cm, then the diameter of the hole will be:
    (A) 0.98 cm
    (B) 0.97 cm
    (C) 1.00 cm
    (D) 1.01 cm   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $\alpha=\frac{\Delta l}{l \Delta T}$
$\frac{0.03}{3 \Delta T}=\frac{\Delta l}{1 \Delta T}$
$\Delta l$ = 0.01 cm
diameter of hole = 1 + 0.01 = 1.01 cm

Q4. Find the wrong statement about the specific heat of a material:
    (A) Has units as J/kg-K.
    (B) Is extensive property   <-- CORRECT
    (C) Constant for a material
    (D) Heat capacity per unit mass
    Answer: B
    Explanation: extensive properties (i.e. mass) are dependent upon the amount of a substance, while intensive properties (i.e. density) are independent of quantity.

Q5. Molar Specific Heat capacity has units of:
    (A) J.ohm/sec. K^2
    (B) J/mol.K   <-- CORRECT
    (C) J/kg.K
    (D) W/m.K
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Q = nC$\Delta $T
C = $Q\over{n\Delta T}$

Q6. Thermal conductivity has units of:
    (A) J m^-1 K^-1
    (B) J kg^-1 K^-1
    (C) Wm^-1 K^-1   <-- CORRECT
    (D) J ohm sec^-2 K^-2
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $H=\frac{K A \Delta \theta}{l}$
$K=\frac{H l}{A \Delta \theta}$
k- coefficient of thermal conductivity,
H - heat flowing per unit time,
l - length of solid material,
A- area of cross section, $\Delta \theta$ - temperature difference

Q7. The triple points of neon are 24.57 K. Express this temperature on the Celsius and Fahrenheit scales:
    (A) ‚Äì238.58^o C = ‚Äì 315.44^o F
    (B) ‚Äì248.58^o C = ‚Äì 415.44^o F   <-- CORRECT
    (C) ‚Äì208.58^o C = ‚Äì 355.44^o F
    (D) ‚Äì218.58^o C = ‚Äì 335.44^o F
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $\frac{T_{C}-0}{100-0}=\frac{T_{F}-32}{212-32}=\frac{T_{K}-273.15}{373.15-273.15}$
$\frac{T_{C}}{100}=\frac{T_{F}-32}{180}=\frac{T_{K}-273.15}{100}$
T_K = 24.57K
after solving 
T_C = ‚Äì248.58^o C
T_F = ‚Äì 415.44^o F

Q8. The triple points of carbon dioxide are 216.55 K. Express this temperature on the Celsius and Fahrenheit scales:
    (A) ‚Äì 86.60 ^oC = ‚Äì 150.88 ^oF
    (B) ‚Äì 56.60 ^oC = ‚Äì 69.88 ^oF   <-- CORRECT
    (C) ‚Äì 66.60 ^oC = ‚Äì 99.88 ^oF
    (D) ‚Äì 76.60 ^oC = ‚Äì 120.88 ^oF
    Answer: B
    Explanation: ${{{T_C} - 0} \over {100 - 0}} = {{{T_F} - 32} \over {212 - 32}} = {{{T_K} - 273.15} \over {373.15 - 273.15}}$
$ {{{T_C}} \over {100}} = {{{T_F} - 32} \over {180}} = {{{T_K} - 273.15} \over {100}}$
T_K = 216.55K
after solving
T_C = ‚Äì 56.60 ^oC
T_F = ‚Äì 69.88 ^oF

Q9. Two absolute scales A and B have triple points of water defined to be 200 A and 350 B. What is the relation between T_A and T_B?
    (A) T_A = ($\frac{4}{7}$) T_B   <-- CORRECT
    (B) T_A = ($\frac{5}{9}$) T_B
    (C) T_A = ($\frac{3}{7}$) T_B
    (D) T_A = ($\frac{2}{7}$) T_B
    Answer: A
    Explanation: for any temperature scale 
$\frac{X}{X_{T r}}$ = const.
$\frac{T_{A}}{200}=\frac{T_{B}}{350}$
$T_{A}=\frac{4}{7} T_{B}$

Q10. The electrical resistance in ohms of a certain thermometer varies with temperature according to the approximate law: R = R$_{\rm{o}}$ [1 + $\alpha $ (T ‚Äì T$_{\rm{o}}$ )] The resistance is 101.6 $\Omega $ at the triple-point of water 273.16 K, and 165.5 $\Omega $ at the normal melting point of lead (600.5 K). What is the temperature when the resistance is 123.4 $\Omega $?
    (A) 384.8 K   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 404.8 K
    (C) 344.8 K
    (D) 364.8 K
    Answer: A
    Explanation: It is given that:
R = R_0 [1 + $\alpha $ (T - T_0)] ‚Ä¶(i)
where,
R_0 and T_0 are the initial resistance and temperature respectively
R and T are the final resistance and temperature respectively 
$\alpha $ is a constant
At the triple point of water, T_0 = 273.15 K
Resistance of lead, R_0 = 101.6 $\Omega $
At normal melting point of lead, T = 600.5 K
The resistance of lead, R = 165.5 $\Omega $
Substituting these values in equation (i), we get:
R = R_o [1 + $\alpha $ (T - T_o)]
165.5 = 101.6 [ 1 + $\alpha $(600.5 - 273.15) ] 
1.629 = 1 + $\alpha $(327.35) 
‚à¥ $\alpha $ = $0.629 \over 327.35$ = 1.92 $\times$ 10^-3 K^-1
For resistance, R_1 = 123.4 $\Omega $
R_1 = R_0 [1 + $\alpha$ (T - T_0)]
where,
T is the temperature when the resistance of lead is 123.4 $\Omega $
123.4 = 101.6 [ 1 + 1.92 $\times$ 10^-3( T - 273.15) ]
Solving for T, we get
T = 384.8 K.

Q11. A steel tape 1m long is correctly calibrated for a temperature of 27.0^oC. The length of a steel rod measured by this tape is found to be 63.0 cm on a hot day when the temperature is 45.0 ^oC. What is the actual length of the steel rod on that day? Coefficient of linear expansion of steel = 1.20 $ \times $ 10^‚àí5 K^‚àí1.
    (A) 63.0116 cm
    (B) 63.0136 cm   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 63.0146 cm
    (D) 63.0126 cm
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $\Delta l = \alpha l\Delta T $ = 1.20 $\times$ 10^‚àí5 $\times$ 63 $\times$ 18 = 0.0136cm
final length = $l +\Delta l$ = 63.0136cm

Q12. A large steel wheel is to be fitted on to a shaft of the same material. At 27 $^\circ $C, the outer diameter of the shaft is 8.70 cm and the diameter of the central hole in the wheel is 8.69 cm. The shaft is cooled using ‚Äòdry ice‚Äô. At what temperature of the shaft does the wheel slip on the shaft? Assume the coefficient of linear expansion of the steel to be constant over the required temperature range: ${\alpha _{{\rm{steel}}}}$ = 1.20 $ \times $ 10^‚àí5 K^‚àí1.
    (A) ‚Äì 69 ^oC   <-- CORRECT
    (B) ‚Äì 79 ^oC
    (C) ‚Äì 89 ^oC
    (D) ‚Äì 59 ^oC
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\alpha=\frac{\Delta l}{l \Delta T}$
$\Delta T=\frac{\Delta l}{l \alpha}=\frac{0.01}{8.70 \times 1.2 \times 10^{-5}}=96 C^{\circ}$
final temp. = 27 ‚àí 96 = ‚àí69^oC

Q13. A hole is drilled in a copper sheet. The diameter of the hole is 4.24 cm at 27.0^oC. What is the change in the diameter of the hole when the sheet is heated to 227^oC? Coefficient of linear expansion of copper = 1.70 $ \times $ 10^-5 K^‚Äì1.
    (A) 1.24 $\times$ 10^‚àí2 cm.
    (B) 1.34 $\times$ 10^‚àí2 cm.
    (C) 1.44 $\times$ 10^‚àí2 cm.   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 1.54 $\times$ 10^‚àí2 cm.
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Change in diameter
$\Delta l = \alpha l\Delta T $
= 1.70 $\times$ 10^‚àí5 $\times$ 4.24 $\times$ (227 ‚àí 27) = 1.44 $\times$10^‚àí2 cm

Q14. A brass wire 1.8 m long at 27 ^oC is held taut with little tension between two rigid supports. If the wire is cooled to a temperature of ‚Äì39 $^\circ $C, what is the tension developed in the wire, if its diameter is 2.0 mm? Co-efficient of linear expansion of brass = 2.0 $ \times $ 10^‚àí5 K^‚àí1; Young‚Äôs modulus of brass = 0.91 $ \times $ 10^11 Pa.
    (A) 3.4 $\times$ 10^2 N
    (B) 3.2 $\times$ 10^2 N
    (C) 3.8 $\times$ 10^2 N   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 3.6 $\times$ 10^2 N
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Initial temperature, T_1 = 27^oC
Length of the brass wire at T_1, l = 1.8 m
Final temperature, T_2 = ‚Äì39^oC
Diameter of the wire, d = 2.0 mm = 2 $\times$ 10^‚Äì3 m
Tension developed in the wire = F
Coefficient of linear expansion of brass, Œ± = 2.0 $\times$ 10^‚Äì5 K^‚Äì1
Young‚Äôs modulus of brass, Y = 0.91 $\times$ 10^11 Pa
Young‚Äôs modulus is given by the relation:
Œ≥ = $Stress \over Strain$ = ${{F\over A} \over {\Delta L\over L}}$
$\Delta L = {{FL} \over {YA}}$ ....
Where,
F = Tension developed in the wire 
A = Area of a cross-section of the wire. 
$\Delta L$ = Change in the length, given by the relation:
$\Delta L = \alpha L({T_2} - {T_1})$ ‚Ä¶ (ii)
Equating equations (i) and (ii), we get:
$\alpha L\left(T_{2}-T_{1}\right)=\frac{F L}{Y A}$
$\alpha L\left(T_{2}-T_{1}\right)=\frac{F L}{Y \pi\left(\frac{d}{2}\right)^{2}}$
$F=\alpha\left(T_{2}-T_{1}\right) Y \pi\left(\frac{d}{2}\right)^{2}$
F = 2 $\times$ 10^-5 $\times$ (-39 - 27) $\times$ 3.14 $\times$ 0.91 $\times$ 10^11 $\times$ ($2 \times 10^{-3} \over 2$ )^2
= -3.8 $\times$ 10^2 N
(The negative sign indicates that the tension is directed inward.)
Hence, the tension developed in the wire is 3.8 $\times$10^2 N.

Q15. A brass rod of length 50 cm and diameter 3.0 mm is joined to a steel rod of the same length and diameter. What is the change in length of the combined rod at 250 $^\circ $C, if the original lengths are at 40.0 $^\circ $C? The ends of the rod are free to expand (Co-efficient of linear expansion of brass = 2.0 $ \times $ 10^‚àí5 K^‚àí1, steel = 1.2 $ \times $ 10^‚àí5 K^‚àí1).
    (A) 0.42 cm.
    (B) 0.34 cm.   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 0.38 cm.
    (D) 0.36 cm.
    Answer: B
    Explanation: For the expansion in the brass rod
$\frac{\Delta l_{1}}{l_{1}}=\alpha_{1} \Delta T$
$\Delta l_{1}=l_{1} \alpha_{1} \Delta T=50 \times 2.1 \times 10^{-5} \times 210$ = 0.2205 cm
For the expansion in the steel rod
$\frac{\Delta l_{2}}{l_{2}}=\alpha_{2} \Delta T$
$\Delta l_{2}=l_{2} \alpha_{2} \Delta T=50 \times 1.2 \times 10^{-5} \times 210=0.126 \mathrm{cm}$ 
Total change in the lengths of brass and steel
$\Delta l = \Delta {l_1} + \Delta {l_2} $ = 0.2205 + 0.126 = 0.346cm
Total change in the length of the combined rod = 0.346 cm

Q1. The coefficient of volume expansion of glycerin is 49 $ \times $ 10^‚àí5 /^ 0C. What is the fractional change in its density for a 30 ^oC rise in temperature?
    (A) 0.0157
    (B) 0.0147   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 0.0107
    (D) 0.0127
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Coefficient of volume expansion of glycerine,
Œ≥=49√ó10^-5/ ^0C
Rise in temperature, ŒîT=30^ 0C
As, $\gamma = {1\over \Delta T}$$({\Delta V\over V})$
$({\Delta V\over V})$= $\gamma\times \Delta T$
or ŒîV/V=(49√ó10^-5)√ó30=0.0147
Since mass remains constant,
Fractional change in density = fractional change in volume = 0.0147

Q2. A 10 kW drilling machine is used to drill a bore in a small aluminum block of mass 8.0 kg. How much is the rise in temperature of the block in 2.5 minutes, assuming 50 percent of power is used up in heating the machine itself or lost to the surroundings. Specific heat of aluminum = 0.91 J g^‚àí1K^‚àí1.
    (A) 103 ^oC   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 123 ^oC
    (C) 113 ^oC
    (D) 93 ^oC
    Answer: A
    Explanation: W = Pt = 10 $\times$ 10^3 $\times$ 2.5 $\times$ 60 = 150 $\times$ 10^4J
Energy used in heating = 50% = 75 $\times$ 10^4J
Q = ms$\Delta$T
$ \Delta T = {Q \over {ms}} $
$\Delta T = {{75 \times {{10}^4}} \over {8000 \times 0.91}}$
$\Delta T $ = 103^oC

Q3. A copper block of mass 2.5 kg is heated in a furnace to a temperature of 500 ^oC and then placed on a large ice block. What is the maximum amount of ice that can melt? (Specific heat of copper = 0.39 J g^‚àí1K^‚àí1; the heat of fusion of water = 335 J g^‚àí1).
    (A) 1.5 kg   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 1.8 kg
    (C) 1.6 kg
    (D) 1.7 kg
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Heat given by Cu = Heat Intake by ice
mC_us$\Delta$T = m_iceL
2500 $\times$ 0.39 $\times$ 500 = m_ice $\times$ 335
m_ice = 1.5Kg

Q4. In an experiment on the specific heat of a metal, a 0.2 kg block of the metal at 150 ^oC is dropped in a copper calorimeter (of water equivalent 0.025 kg) containing 150 cm^3 of water at 27 ^oC. The final temperature is 40 ^oC, the specific heat of the metal will be
    (A) 0.43 J g^‚àí1K^‚àí1   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 0.40 J g^‚àí1K^‚àí1
    (C) 0.46 J g^‚àí1K^‚àí1
    (D) 0.37 J g^‚àí1K^‚àí1
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Heat lost by the metal = Heat gained by the water and calorimeter system
$m_{1} c_{1} \Delta T_{1}=\left(m_{1}+m_{2}\right) c_{2} \Delta T_{2}$
200 $\times$ c_1 $\times$ 110 = (150 + 25) $\times$ 4.186 $\times$ 13
$c_{1}=\frac{175 \times 4.186 \times 13}{200 \times 110}$
c_1 = 0.43Jg^‚àí1K^‚àí1

Q5. A child running a temperature of 101^oF is given an antipyrin (i.e. medicine that lowers fever) which causes an increase in the rate of evaporation of sweat from his body. If the fever is brought down to 98 ^oF in 20 min, what is the average rate of extra evaporation caused, by the drug? Assume the evaporation mechanism to be the only way by which heat is lost. The mass of the child is 30 kg. The specific heat of human body is approximately the same as that of water, and the latent heat of evaporation of water at that temperature is about 580 cal g^‚àí1.
    (A) 4.3 g/min   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 5.2 g/min
    (C) 4.9 g/min
    (D) 4.6 g/min
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\Delta T = 101 - 98 = 3^\circ F = \left( {3 \times {5 \over 9}} \right)^\circ C$ 
Specific heat of the human body = Specific heat of water = 1000 cal/kg/ ^oC
The heat lost by the child is Q = $ mc\Delta T = 30 \times 1000 \times \left( {3 \times {5 \over 9}} \right) $ = 50000cal
Let m_1 be the mass of the water evaporated from the child‚Äôs body in 20 min. 
Loss of heat through water is
Q = m_1L
${m_1} = {Q \over L} = {{50000} \over {580}} $ = 86.2gm
The average rate of extra evaporation caused by the drug = $m_1 \over t$
= $86.2 \over 20$
= 4.3 g/min.

Q6. A ‚Äòthermal‚Äô icebox is a cheap and efficient method for storing small quantities of cooked food in summer in particular. A cubical icebox of side 30 cm has a thickness of 5.0 cm. If 4.0 kg of ice is put in the box, estimate the amount of ice remaining after 6 h. The outside temperature is 45 ^oC, and the coefficient of thermal conductivity of thermal is 0.01 J s^‚àí1m^‚àí1K^‚àí1. [Heat of fusion of water = 335 $ \times $ 10^3Jkg^‚àí1]
    (A) 3.6 kg
    (B) 3.7 kg   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 3.5 kg
    (D) 3.4 kg
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $Q = {{KA\Delta T} \over l}t$ 
A = Surface area of the box = 6s^2 = 6 $\times$ (0.3)^2 = 0.54 m^3
Time gap, t = 6 h = 6 $\times$ 60 $\times$ 60 s
$Q = {{0.01 \times 0.54 \times 45 \times 6 \times 60 \times 60} \over {0.05}} $ = 104976J
But Q= m'L
$\therefore$ m' = $Q\over L$
= $104976\over(335 \times 10^3)$ = 0.313 kg
Mass of ice left = 4 ‚Äì 0.3 = 3.7 kg
Hence, the amount of ice remaining after 6 h is 3.7 kg.

Q7. A brass boiler has a base area of 0.15 m^2 and thickness 1.0 cm. It boils water at a rate of 6.0 kg/min when placed on a gas stove. Estimate the temperature of the part of the flame in contact with the boiler. Thermal conductivity of brass = 109 J s^‚àí1m^‚àí1K^‚àí1 ; Heat of vaporization of water = 2256 $ \times $ 10^3Jkg^‚àí1.
    (A) 239 ^oC
    (B) 237 ^oC
    (C) 238 ^oC   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 240 ^oC
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The base area of the boiler, A = 0.15 m^2
Thickness of the boiler, l = 1.0 cm = 0.01 m
Boiling rate of water, R = 6.0 kg/min
Mass, m = 6 kg
Time, t = 1 min = 60 s
Thermal conductivity of brass, K = 109 J s ^‚Äì1 m^‚Äì1 K^‚Äì1
Heat of vaporisation, L = 2256 √ó 10^3 J kg^‚Äì1
The amount of heat flowing into the water through the brass base of the boiler is
$Q = {{KA({T_1} - {T_2})} \over l}t$ ......(1)
where,
T_1 = Temperature of the flame in contact with the boiler
T_2 = Boiling point of water = 100¬∞C 
Heat required for boiling the water
Q = mL....‚Ä¶(ii)
Equating equations (i) and (ii), we get:
mL = $KA(T_1 - T_2) t \over l $
T_1 - T_2 = $mLl \over KAt$
= $6 \times 2256 \times 10^3 \times 0.01 \over(109 \times 0.15 \times 60)$
= 137.98 ^oC
Therefore, the temperature of the part of the flame in contact with the boiler is 237.98^oC.

Q8. A body cools from 80 ^oC to 50 ^oC in 5 minutes. Calculate the time it takes to cool from 60 ^oC to 30 ^oC. The temperature of the surroundings is 20 ^oC.
    (A) 12 min
    (B) 9 min   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 6 min
    (D) 15 min
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Initial temperature (T_i‚Äã) = 80 ^oC
Final temperature (T_f) = 50 ^oC
Temperature of the surrounding (T_0) = 20 ^oC
t = 5 min
According to Newton's law of cooling,
Rate of cooling $\frac {dt}{dT}$‚Äã = $K({(T_i+T_F)\over 2}-T_0)$
$\frac {(T_f - T_i)}{t} = \frac {K[(80 + 50)]}{2-20}$
$\frac {80-50}{5}$‚Äã = K[65 ‚àí 20]
6 = K $\times$ 45
K = ${6\over 45}={2\over 15}$
In second condition,
initial temperature =T_i = 60 ^oC
Final temperature T_f‚Äã = 30 ^oC
Time taken for cooling is t
According to Newton's law of cooling 
$\frac {(60-30)}{t}$t ‚Äã =${2\over 15}[{(60+30)\over 2}-20]$
$\frac {30}{t}=(\frac {2}{15})(25)$
$\therefore$ t = 9 min

Q9. On a day when the temperature reaches 50^oF, what is the temperature in degrees Celsius and in kelvins?
    (A) 10^o C, 283 K   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 14^o C, 323 K
    (C) 8^o C, 273 K
    (D) 12^o C, 303 K
    Answer: A
    Explanation: ${{{T_C} - 0} \over {100 - 0}} = {{{T_F} - 32} \over {212 - 32}} = {{{T_K} - 273.15} \over {373.15 - 273.15}}$
${{{T_C}} \over {100}} = {{{T_F} - 32} \over {180}} = {{{T_K} - 273.15} \over {100}}$
T_F = 50^oF
after solving 
T_C = 10^oC
T_K = 283K

Q10. A steel railroad track has a length of 30.000 m when the temperature is 0.0^oC. What is its length when the temperature is 40.0$^\circ $ C? $\alpha$ = 11 $\times$ 10^‚àí6 (^oC)^-1.
    (A) 30.008 m
    (B) 30.013 m   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 30.016 m
    (D) 30.010 m
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $\Delta l = \alpha l\Delta \theta $ =11 $\times$ 10^‚àí6 $\times$ 30 $\times$ 40 = 0.013m
Total length after expansion = 30 + 0.013 = 30.013m

Q11. A steel railroad track has a length of 30.000 m when the temperature is 0.0^oC. Suppose that the ends of the rail are rigidly clamped at 0.0^oC so that expansion is prevented. What is the thermal stress set up in the rail if its temperature is raised to 40.0^oC? $\alpha $‚àí steel = 11 $\times$ 10^‚àí6 (^oC)^-1 Y-steel = 20GPa?
    (A) 8.1 $ \times $ 10^7N/m^2
    (B) 8.8 $ \times $ 10^6N/m^2   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 9.1 $ \times $ 10^7N/m^2
    (D) 8.4 $ \times $ 10^6N/m^2
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Thermal Stress $ = Y\alpha \Delta \theta $ = 20 $\times$ 10^9 $\times$ 11 $\times$ 10^‚àí6 $\times$ 40 = 8.8 $\times$ 10^6

Q12. An ideal gas occupies a volume of 100 ${\rm{c}}{{\rm{m}}^{\rm{3}}}$ at ${\rm{2}}0^\circ $ C and 100 Pa. Find the number of moles of gas in the container.
    (A) 4.60 $ \times $ 10^‚àí6
    (B) 4.10 $ \times $ 10^‚àí6 mol   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 4.30 $ \times $ 10^‚àí6
    (D) 4.80 $ \times $ 10^‚àí6
    Answer: B
    Explanation: number of moles according to gas equation
$n = {{PV} \over {RT}} = {{100 \times 100 \times {{10}^{ - 6}}} \over {8.31 \times 293}} = 4.10 \times {10^{ - 6}}$

Q13. A spray can contain a propellant gas at twice atmospheric pressure (202 kPa) and having a volume of 125 cm^3 is at 22^oC. It is then tossed into an open fire. When the temperature of the gas in the can reaches 195^oC, what is the pressure inside the can? Assume any change in the volume of the can is negligible.
    (A) 300 kPa
    (B) 320 kPa   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 260 kPa
    (D) 290 kPa
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $P \propto T$
$ {{{P_1}} \over {{P_2}}} = {{{T_1}} \over {{T_2}}}$
${P_2} = {{468 \times 202} \over {295}} $ = 320KPa

Q14. A student eats a dinner rated at 2000 Calories. He wishes to do an equivalent amount of work in the gymnasium by lifting a 50.0-kg barbell. How many times must he raise the barbell to expend this much energy? Assume that he raises the barbell 2.00 m each time he lifts it and that he regains no energy when he drops the barbell to the floor.
    (A) 8.34 $ \times $ 10^3
    (B) 8.74 $ \times $ 10^3
    (C) 8.54 $ \times $10^3 times   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 8.14 $ \times $ 10^3
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Total no. of times he lifts barbell = $Total \ energy \over energy \ in \ lifting \ barbell \ once$
$= {Q \over {mgh}}$
$= {{2000 \times 4.186 \times {{10}^3}} \over {50 \times 9.8 \times 2}} $ = 8.54 $\times$ 10^3 times

Q15. A 0.0500-kg ingot of metal is heated to 200.0^oC and then dropped into a beaker containing 0.400 kg of water initially at 20.0^oC. If the final equilibrium temperature of the mixed system is 22.4^oC, find the specific heat of the metal.
    (A) 4.53 J/kg. ^oC   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 4.63 J/kg. ^oC
    (C) 4.83 J/kg^oC
    (D) 4.73 J/kg. ^oC
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Heat given = Heat intake
m_1s_1$\Delta$T_1 = m_2 s_2$\Delta$T_2
m_1 = 0.05Kg
s_1 = ?
$\Delta$T_1 = 22.4 ^0C - 20 ^0C= 2.4^ 0C
$\Delta$T_2 = 200 ^0C-22.4 ^0C= 177.6 ^0C
${s_1} = {{{m_2}{s_2}\Delta {T_2}} \over {{m_1}\Delta {T_1}}}$ 
${s_1} = {{0.4 \times 4186 \times 2.4} \over {0.05 \times 177.6}} $ = 4.53J/Kg^oC

Thermal Properties of Matter Test 05  (id 581) [PAID]
