Q1. A cowboy fires a silver bullet with a mass of 2.00 g and with a muzzle speed of 200 m/s into the pine wall of a saloon. Assume that all the internal energy generated by the impact remains with the bullet. What is the temperature change of the bullet? Specific heat of silver is 234J/Kg^oC?
    (A) 81.5^oC
    (B) 83.5^oC
    (C) 78.5^oC
    (D) 85.5^oC   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Q = K
$mc\Delta T = {1 \over 2}m{v^2}$
$ \Delta T = {{{v^2}} \over {2c}} = {{200 \times 200} \over {2 \times 234}} = 85.5^\circ C $

Q2. What mass of steam initially at 130$^\circ $C is needed to warm 200 g of water in a 100-g glass container from 20.0$^\circ $C to 50.0^oC? Specific heat of container is 837J/Kg^oC 
Specific heat of steam is 2110J/Kg^oC 
Specific heat of water is 4186J/Kg^oC 
Latent heat of vapourisation is 2.26 $\times$ 10^6J/Kg
    (A) 10.9 g   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 12.9 g
    (C) 14.9 g
    (D) 16.9 g
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Heat given by steam = heat taken by water + heat taken by the container
$m{s_s}\Delta {T_1} + mL + m{s_w}\Delta {T_2} = m{s_w}\Delta {T_3} + m{s_c}\Delta {T_3}$
$\left( {m \times 2110 \times 30} \right) + \left( {m \times 2.26 \times {{10}^6}} \right) + \left( {m \times 4186 \times 50} \right) $$= \left( {0.2 \times 4186 \times 30} \right) + \left( {0.1 \times 837 \times 30} \right) $
after solving
mass of steam m = 10.9gm

Q3. Liquid helium has a very low boiling point, 4.2 K, and a very low latent heat of vaporization, 2.09 $\times$ 10^4 J/kg. If energy is transferred to a container of boiling liquid helium from an immersed electric heater at a rate of 10.0 W, how long does it take to boil away 1.00 kg of the liquid?
    (A) 37 min
    (B) 31 min
    (C) 35 min   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 33 min
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Q = mL= 1 $\times$ 2.09 $\times$ 10^4 = 2.09 $\times$10^4J
$\Delta t = {Q \over P} = {{2.09 \times {{10}^4}} \over {10}} $ = 2.09 $\times$ 10^3 sec = 35 min

Q4. The temperature of a silver bar rises by 10.0$^\circ $C when it absorbs 1.23 kJ of energy by heat. The mass of the bar is 525 g. Determine the specific heat of silver?
    (A) 0.214 kJ/kg. ^oC
    (B) 0.234 kJ/kg. ^oC   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 0.244 kJ/kg. ^oC
    (D) 0.254 kJ/kg. ^oC
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $s = {Q \over {m\Delta T}} = {{1.23} \over {0.525 \times 10}} = 0.234KJ/Kg^\circ C$

Q5. A 1.50-kg iron horseshoe initially at 600^oC is dropped into a bucket containing 20.0 kg of water at 25.0^oC. What is the final temperature? (Neglect the heat capacity of the container and assume that a negligible amount of water boils away.) Specific heat of iron = 448J/Kg^oC.
    (A) 28.6^oC
    (B) 30.6^oC
    (C) 18.6^oC
    (D) 29.6^oC   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: by the principle of calorimetry,
The heat has given by iron = Heat intake by water
$m_{F e} s_{F e} \Delta T_{1}=m_{w} s_{w} \Delta T_{2}$
1.5 $\times$ 448(600 - T) = 20 $\times$ 4186(T - 25)|
after solving
final temperature T = 29.6^oC

Q6. An aluminum calorimeter with a mass of 100 g contains 250 g of water. The calorimeter and water are in thermal equilibrium at 10.0$^\circ $C. Two metallic blocks are placed into the water. One is a 50.0-g piece of copper at 80.0$^\circ $C; the other block has a mass of 70.0 g and is originally at a temperature of 100$^\circ $C. The entire system stabilizes at a final temperature of 20.0$^\circ $C. Determine the specific heat of the unknown sample.
Specific heat of aluminium is 0.215cal/gm^oC 
Specific heat of copper is 0.0924cal/gm^oC
    (A) 0.465 cal/g. ^oC
    (B) 0.435 cal/g. ^oC   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 0.445 cal/g. ^oC
    (D) 0.455 cal/g. ^oC
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Heat given by Copper(1) + heat given by unknown metal(2) = heat gain by aluminum (3) + heat gain by water(4)
${m_1}{s_1}\Delta {T_1} + {m_2}{s_2}\Delta {T_2} $ = ${m_3}{s_3}\Delta {T_3} + {m_4}{s_4}\Delta {T_4} $ 
(50 $\times$ 0.0924 $\times$ 60) + (70 $\times$ s_2 $\times$ 80) = (100 $\times$ 0.215 $\times$10) + (250 $\times$ 1 $\times$10)
after solving 
specific heat of unknown metal s_2 = 0.435cal/gm^oC

Q7. A 3.00-g copper penny at 25.0$^\circ $C drops from a height of 50.0 m to the ground. (a) If 60.0% of the change in potential energy goes into increasing the internal energy, what is its final temperature?
    (A) 23.8^oC
    (B) 25.8^oC   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 27.8^oC
    (D) 21.8^oC
    Answer: B
    Explanation: mgh = 3 $\times$ 10^‚àí3$\times$10 $\times$ 50 = 1.5J
Heat used in increasing temp 
$Q=1.5 \times \frac{60}{100}=0.9 J$
Q = ms $\Delta$T_0.9 = 3 $\times$ 0.385 $\times$ (T‚àí298)
T = 298.8K = 25.8^oC

Q8. A water heater is operated by solar power. If the solar collector has an area of 6.00 m^2 and the power delivered by sunlight is 550 W/ m^2, how long does it take to increase the temperature of 1.00 m^3 of water from 20.0^oC to 60.0^oC?
    (A) 54.7 ks
    (B) 56.7 ks
    (C) 52.7 ks
    (D) 50.7 ks   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $Q = ms\Delta T$
$ P = {W \over t} = {{ms\Delta T} \over t} $ 
$t = {{ms\Delta T} \over P} = {{1000 \times 4186 \times 40} \over {550 \times 6}} $ = 50700 sec = 50.7 ksec

Q9. A 3.00-g lead bullet at 30.0^oC is fired at a speed of 240 m/s into a large block of ice at 0^oC, in which it becomes embedded. What quantity of ice melts? Latent heat of ice = 3.33 $\times$ 10^-5 J/Kg, the specific heat of lead = 128J/Kg^oC?
    (A) 2.54 g
    (B) 2.94 g   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 3.04 g
    (D) 2.74 g
    Answer: B
    Explanation: ${1 \over 2}{m_{Pb}}{v^2} + {m_{Pb}}{s_{Pb}}(30 - 0) = {m_{ice}}{L_{ice}}$
($1\over 2$ $\times$ 3 $\times$ 10^‚àí3 $\times$ 240 $\times$ 240)+(3 $\times$ 10^‚àí3 $\times$ 128 $\times$ 30) = m_ice $\times$ 3.33 $\times$ 10^5
m_ice = 2.94gm

Q10. A 1.00-kg block of copper at 20.0$^\circ $C is dropped into a large vessel of liquid nitrogen at 77.3 K. How many kilograms of nitrogen boil away by the time the copper reaches 77.3 K? (The specific heat of copper is 0.092 0 cal/g. $^\circ $C. The latent heat of vaporization of nitrogen is 48.0 cal/g.)
    (A) 0.434 kg
    (B) 0.414 kg   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 0.384 kg
    (D) 0.404 kg
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Heat is given by copper = Heat gain by nitrogen
ms$\Delta$T = mN_2L
1000 $\times$ 0.092 $\times$ 215.7 = mN_2 √ó 48
mN_2 = 414gm = 0.414Kg

Q11. In an insulated vessel, 250 g of ice at 0^oC is added to 600 g of water at 18.0^oC. What is the final temperature of the system?
    (A) 2^oC
    (B) -2^oC
    (C) 4^oC
    (D) 0^oC   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: maximum heat given by water is $Q = {m_{water}}s\Delta T $ =600 $\times$ 1 $\times$ (18 ‚àí 0) = 10800cal
Heat required to melt the complete ice = m_ice L = 250 $\times$ 80 = 20000cal
ice remains unmelt is given by = ${{20000 - 10800} \over {80}} = {{9200} \over {80}} $ = 115gm
Heat given by water is not sufficient to meet the complete ice. so that temp of the system is $0^\circ C$ with some ice remains unmelt

Q12. In an insulated vessel, 250 g of ice at 0$^\circ $C is added to 600 g of water at 18.0$^\circ $C. How much ice remains when the system reaches equilibrium?
given that latent heat of fusion of ice = 79.5 cal/gm, the specific heat of water = 1cal/gm^oC.
    (A) 87 g
    (B) 94 g
    (C) 114 g   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 134 g
    Answer: C
    Explanation: maximum heat given by water is
${Q_1} = {m_w}{s_w}\Delta T = 600 \times 1 \times (18 - 0) = 10800cal$
The heat required to melt the complete ice = Q_2 = m_ice L
Q_2 = 250 $\times$ 79.5 = 19875cal
Heat given by water is not sufficient to melt complete ice. Hence the temperature of the mixture will be $0^\circ C$ with some ice remains unmelt.
amount of ice melt = $m = {{{Q_1}} \over L} = {{10800} \over {79.5}} $ = 136gm
L = latent heat of fusion of ice = 79.5 cal/gm
Amount of ice which remains unmelt at equilibrium = 250 - 136 = 114 gm

Q13. If 90.0 g of molten lead at 327.3^oC is poured into a 300-g casting form made of iron and initially at 20.0^oC, what is the final temperature of the system? (Assume that no energy loss to the environment occurs.) Latent heat of fusion of lead = 2.45 $\times$ 10^4 J/kg, specific heat of lead = 128J/Kg^oC
specific heat of iron = 448J/Kg^oC.
    (A) 51.4^oC
    (B) 55.4^oC
    (C) 62.4^oC
    (D) 59.4^oC   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The melting point of lead is given by 327.3^o C.
Assume the final temperature of the system is T. Then the amount of energy released by the lead as it solidifies is given by 
ŒîQ = m_leadL_lead = 0.09 kg*(2.45*10^4 J/kg) = 2205 J,
and the amount of energy released as it cools is given by :-
ŒîQ = m_leadc_leadŒîT = 0.09 kg*(128 J/(kg^oC))(327.3 ^oC - T) = (11.52 J/^oC)(327.3 ^oC - T).
This energy is absorbed by the iron. Thus,
2205 J + (11.52 J/^oC) (327.3^oC - T) = m_ironc_ironŒîT = 0.3 kg*(448 J/(kg^oC))(T - 20 ^oC).
5975.5 J - (11.52 J/^oC)T = (134 J/^oC)T - 2688 J.
8663.5 J = (145.52 J/^oC)T.
T = 59.5^o C.

Q14. A surveyor uses a steel measuring tape that is exactly 50.000 m long at a temperature of 20$^\circ $C. The markings on the tape are calibrated for this temperature.
 What is the length of the tape when the temperature is 35^oC?
 When it is 35^oC, the surveyor uses the tape to measure a distance. The value that she reads off the tape is 35.794 m. What is the actual distance? $\alpha$ = 1.2 $\times$ 10^-5oC
    (A) 50.010 m, 35.900 m
    (B) 50.008 m, 35.600 m
    (C) 50.007 m, 35.750 m
    (D) 50.009 m, 35.800 m   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $\alpha = {{\Delta l} \over l}{1 \over {\Delta T}}$
$\Delta l = l\alpha \Delta T$ = 50 $\times$ 1.2 $\times$ 10^-5 $\times$ 15 = 0.009m
final length = 50 + 0.009m = 50.009m
if actual distance = x
then $\Delta x = x\alpha \Delta T$
x‚àí35.794 = x $\times$ 1.2 $\times$ 10^‚àí5 $\times$ 15
x = 35.800m

Q15. A 200- ${\rm{c}}{{\rm{m}}^{\rm{3}}}$ glass flask is filled to the brim with mercury at 20^oC. How much mercury overflows when the temperature of the system is raised to 100$^\circ $ C.The coefficient of linear expansion of the glass is 0.40 $\times$ 10^‚àí5/^oC. Coefficient of volume expansion of mercury is 1.82 $\times$ 10^-4/^oC.
    (A) 2.7 cm^3   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 2.5 cm^3
    (C) 2.3 cm^3
    (D) 2.9 cm^3
    Answer: A
    Explanation: coefficient of linear expansion of the glass ${\gamma _S} $ = 0.40 $\times$ 10^‚àí5/^oC
Coefficient of volume expansion of mercury ${a_L} $ = 1.82 $\times$ 10^-4/^oC
The number of mercury overflows
$\Delta V = ({\gamma _L} - {\gamma _S})V\Delta \theta$
${\gamma _S} $ = 3Œ± = 3 $\times$ 0.4 $\times$ 10^‚àí5 = 0.12 $\times$ 10^‚àí4
ŒîV = (1.82‚àí0.12) $\times$ 10^‚àí4 $\times$ 200 $\times$ 80 = 2.7cm^3
mercury overflows = 2.7cm^3

Thermodynamics Test 01  (id 582) [FREE]

Q1. The picture of heat as a fluid was discarded in favor of the modern concept of heat as a form of energy because
    (A) Count Rumford observed that boring of brass cannon generated a lot of heat   <-- CORRECT
    (B) Count Rumford observed that boring of brass cannon generated a lot of smoke
    (C) Count Rumford observed that boring of brass cannon generated a lot of noise
    (D) Count Rumford observed that boring of brass cannon generated a lot of dust
    Answer: A
    Explanation: If heat were actually a substance, as many scientists of the time believed, it was difficult to see how so much of it could be produced during the boring of a metal cannon. Rumford showed that the amount of heat was not related to the quantity of filings produced

Q2. Thermodynamics is the branch of physics that deals with:
    (A) the concepts of kinetic energy and temperature and the inter-conversion of mechanical and other forms of energy
    (B) the concepts of heat and temperature and the inter-conversion of heat and other forms of energy   <-- CORRECT
    (C) the concepts of heat and noise and the inter-conversion of noise and other forms of energy
    (D) the concepts of heat and dust and the inter-conversion of heat and other forms of matter
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Thermodynamics is a branch of physics concerned with heat and temperature and their relation to energy and work. It deals with the relations between heat and other forms of energy (such as mechanical, electrical, or chemical energy), and, by extension, of the relationships between all forms of energy.

Q3. The thermodynamic state of a system is an equilibrium state:
    (A) if the macroscopic variables that characterize the system do not change in time   <-- CORRECT
    (B) if the macroscopic variables that characterize the system change randomly
    (C) if the macroscopic variables that characterize the system change linearly in time
    (D) if the macroscopic variables that characterize the system change decrease in time
    Answer: A
    Explanation: In thermodynamic equilibrium, there are no net macroscopic flows of matter or of energy, either within a system or between systems.

Q4. Adiabatic wall is:
    (A) a thermally insulating wall that does not allow flow of heat from one body to another   <-- CORRECT
    (B) a thermally conducting wall that does not allow flow of heat from one body to many
    (C) a thermally conducting wall that does not allow the flow of heat from one body to another
    (D) a thermally insulating wall that allows the flow of heat from one body to another
    Answer: A
    Explanation: In thermodynamics, an adiabatic wall between two thermodynamic systems does not allow heat or matter to pass across it. In theoretical investigations, it is sometimes assumed that one of the two systems is the surroundings of the other.

Q5. The diathermic wall is:
    (A) a thermally conducting wall that allows flow of heat from one body to another   <-- CORRECT
    (B) a thermally conducting wall that does not allow flow of heat from one body to another
    (C) a thermally insulating wall that does not allow flow of heat from one body to another
    (D) a thermally insulating wall that allows flow of heat from one body to another
    Answer: A
    Explanation: A wall which prevents thermal interaction is known as adiabatic wall and a system enclosed within an adiabatic wall is called thermally isolated. If exchange of heat takes place between the system and surroundings through the boundary wall, the boundary is called diathermic wall.

Q6. Zeroth Law of Thermodynamics
    (A) states that two systems in thermal equilibrium with a third system simultaneously are never in thermal equilibrium with each other
    (B) states that two systems in thermal equilibrium with a third system separately cannot be in thermal equilibrium with each other
    (C) states that a system in thermal equilibrium with a third system separately is in thermal equilibrium with any other
    (D) states that two systems in thermal equilibrium with a third system separately are in thermal equilibrium with each other   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The zeroth law of thermodynamics states that if twothermodynamic systems are each in thermal equilibrium with a third, then they are in thermal equilibrium with each other.
More simply:
if $\eqalign{ & {{\rm{T}}_{\rm{A}}}{\rm{ = }}{{\rm{T}}_{\rm{B}}} \cr & {{\rm{T}}_{\rm{A}}}{\rm{ = }}{{\rm{T}}_{\rm{C}}} \cr & {\rm{Then}} \cr & {{\rm{T}}_{\rm{B}}}{\rm{ = }}{{\rm{T}}_{\rm{C}}} \cr} $
It is called the zeroth (0^th) law of thermodynamics because it was discovered after the others, but people thought it was more important so they wanted to give it a number before the other laws.

Q7. Internal energy of a system:
    (A) is the difference of the kinetic energies and potential energies of the molecules of the system
    (B) is the change in the sum of the kinetic energies and potential energies of the molecules of the system
    (C) is the sum of the kinetic energies and potential energies of the molecules of the system   <-- CORRECT
    (D) is the change in the difference of the kinetic energies and potential energies of the molecules of the system
    Answer: C
    Explanation: In thermodynamics, the internal energy of a system is the energy contained within the system. It is the energy in a system arising from the relative positions and interactions of its parts.

Q8. Internal energy of a system is:
    (A) a state variable   <-- CORRECT
    (B) a complex variable
    (C) a discrete variable
    (D) a random variable
    Answer: A
    Explanation: A state function describes the equilibrium state of a system. For example, internal energy, enthalpy, and entropy are state quantities because they describe quantitatively an equilibrium state of a thermodynamic system, irrespective of how the system arrived in that state.

Q9. First Law of Thermodynamics is based on the law of:
    (A) conservation of mass
    (B) conservation of energy   <-- CORRECT
    (C) conservation of angular momentum
    (D) conservation of momentum
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The first law of thermodynamics is the application of the conservation of energy principle to heat and thermodynamic processes
$\Delta$Q = $\Delta$U + $\Delta$W

Q10. If $\Delta $Q is the energy supplied to the system $\Delta $U the change in internal energy, and $\Delta $W the work done on the environment, First Law of Thermodynamics states that:
    (A) $\Delta $Q = $\Delta $U - $\Delta $W
    (B) $\Delta $Q = $\Delta $U + $\Delta $W   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\Delta $U + $\Delta $W = 0
    (D) $\Delta $Q = $\Delta $U $ \times $ $\Delta $W
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Heat given to a system is equal to the sum of an increase in its internal energy and the work done by the system against the surroundings.

Q11. For solids near room temperature the molar specific heat (R is the universal gas constant):
    (A) 3R   <-- CORRECT
    (B) R
    (C) 2R
    (D) 5R
    Answer: A
    Explanation: The molar specific heats of solids also demonstrate a marked temperature dependence. Solids have molar specific heats that generally decrease in a nonlinear manner with decreasing temperature and approach zero as the temperature approaches absolute zero. At high temperatures (usually above 300 K), the molar specific heats approach the value of 3R ‚âà 25 J/mol.K, a result known as the DuLong‚ÄìPetit law.

Q12. If C_p and C_v are molar specific heat capacities of an ideal gas at constant pressure and volume respectively and R is the universal gas constant, the relation between the three is:
    (A) C_p + C_v = R
    (B) C_p + C_v = 2R
    (C) C_p - C_v = R   <-- CORRECT
    (D) C_p - C_v = 2R
    Answer: C
    Explanation: This is Meyer's Equation
At constant volume as there is no work done on the gas(ŒîV = 0) all the heat supplied goes for the increase of internal energy. Hence the temperature of the body increases with less supply of heat. Whereas at constant pressure heat supplied accounts for both internal energy and work done on the gas.
if we increase temp. of 1 mole of gas by 1K or 1C then
PŒîV = nR$\Delta$T
n = 1mole
$\Delta$T = 1^oC
P$\Delta$V = R
W = P$\Delta$V
C_P > C_V
C_P‚àíC_V = W
C_P‚àíC_V = R
This is also called Meyer's formula.

Q13. In Thermodynamics Extensive variables depend:
    (A) on the ‚Äòentropy‚Äô of the system
    (B) on the ‚Äòpressure‚Äô of the system
    (C) on the ‚Äòsize‚Äô of the system   <-- CORRECT
    (D) on the ‚Äòtemperature‚Äô of the system
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The variables which depend on the size or amount of the substance are called extensive variables.
Example: Mass, volume etc.

Q14. In the case of the isobaric process, the volume of an ideal gas changes the work done by the gas is given by:
    (A) P - $\Delta $V
    (B) $\frac{P}{\Delta V}$
    (C) P $\Delta $V   <-- CORRECT
    (D) P + $\Delta $V
    Answer: C
    Explanation: dW = F.dy = PA.dy = P.dV
$ W = \int\limits_{{V_1}}^{{V_2}} {P.dV} $ 
W = P(V_2 ‚àí V_1) = P$\Delta$V

Q15. In a quasi-static process,
    (A) the temperature of the surrounding reservoir and the external pressure differ substantially from the temperature and pressure of the system
    (B) the temperature of the surrounding reservoir and the external pressure equal the temperature and pressure of the system
    (C) the temperature of the surrounding reservoir differ only infinitesimally from the temperature of the system
    (D) the temperature of the surrounding reservoir and the external pressure differ only infinitesimally from the temperature and pressure of the system   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: A quasi-static process is a thermodynamic process that happens slowly enough for the system to remain in internal equilibrium.

Thermodynamics Test 02  (id 583) [PAID]
CBSE > Class 11 > Physics > Thermodynamics
Questions: 15 | Answers: YES

Q1. In which type of process is the volume constant:
    (A) Isochoric process   <-- CORRECT
    (B) Isothermal process
    (C) Isobaric process
    (D) Adiabatic process
    Answer: A
    Explanation: An isochoric process is a thermodynamic process during which the volume of the closed system undergoing such a process remains constant.

Q2. The heat engine takes
    (A) heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{1}}}$ from a cold reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{1}}}$, releases heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{2}}}$ to a cold reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{2}}}$ and delivers work W to the surroundings
    (B) heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{1}}}$ from a hot reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{1}}}$ , releases heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{2}}}$ to a cold reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{2}}}$ and delivers work W to the surroundings   <-- CORRECT
    (C) heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{1}}}$ from a hot reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{1}}}$, releases heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{2}}}$ to a hot reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{2}}}$ and delivers work W to the surroundings
    (D) heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{1}}}$ from a cold reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{1}}}$, releases heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{2}}}$ to a hot reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{2}}}$ and delivers work W to the surroundings
    Answer: B
    Explanation: a heat engine converts heat energy to mechanical work by using the temperature difference between a hot "source" and a cold "sink". Heat is transferred from the source, through the "working body" of the engine, to the "sink", and in this process some of the heat changes into work by using the qualities of the gas or liquid inside the engine.

Q3. The efficiency $(\eta)$ of a heat engine is defined by (W is the output and ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{1}}}$ the heat input):
    (A) $\eta $ = $\frac{W}{{Q_1}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\eta $ = $\frac{W}{2{Q_1}}$
    (C) $\eta $ = $\frac{2W} {{Q_1}}$
    (D) $\eta $ = $\frac{{Q_1}}{W}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\eta=\frac{\text { WorkDone }}{\text { HeatInput }}=\frac{W}{Q_1}=\frac{Q_1-Q_2}{Q_1}$

Q4. A refrigerator's working substance
    (A) extracts heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{2}}}$ from the hot reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{2}}}$, some external work W is done by it and heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{1}}}$ is released to the cold reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{1}}}$
    (B) extracts heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{2}}}$ from the cold reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{2}}}$, some external work W is done on it and heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{1}}}$ is released to the hot reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{2}}}$
    (C) extracts heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{2}}}$ from the cold reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{2}}}$, some external work W is done on it and heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{1}}}$ is released to the hot reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{1}}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) extracts heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{2}}}$ from the hot reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{2}}}$, some external work W is done on it and heat ${{\rm{Q}}_{\rm{1}}}$ is released to the hot reservoir at temperature ${{\rm{T}}_{\rm{1}}}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: According to the second law of thermodynamics heat cannot spontaneously flow from a colder location to a hotter area; work is required to achieve this.

Q5. The coefficient of performance K of a refrigerator is given by:
    (A) K = $\frac{W}{{Q_2}}$
    (B) K = $W + {Q_2}$
    (C) K = $\frac{Q_2}{W}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) K = $W*{Q_2}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The performance of refrigerator is expressed by means of cofficient of performance
cofficient of performance = Heat extracted / work done = $\frac{Q_2}{W}$

Q6. Kelvin-Planck statement of Second Law of Thermodynamics states that
    (A) Processes are not possible whose sole result is the absorption of heat from a reservoir and the partial conversion of the heat into work.
    (B) No process is possible whose sole result is the absorption of heat from a reservoir and the partial conversion of the heat into work.
    (C) Processes are possible whose sole result is the absorption of heat from a reservoir and the complete conversion of the heat into work.
    (D) No process is possible whose sole result is the absorption of heat from a reservoir and the complete conversion of the heat into work.   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: It is impossible to devise a cyclically operating device, the sole effect of which is to absorb energy in the form of heat from a single thermal reservoir and to deliver an equivalent amount of work. This implies that it is impossible to build a heat engine that has 100% thermal efficiency.

Q7. Clausius statement of Second Law of Thermodynamics states that:
    (A) Many processes are possible whose sole result is the transfer of heat from a colder object to a hotter object
    (B) No process is possible whose sole result is the transfer of work from a hotter object to a hotter object
    (C) No process is possible whose sole result is the transfer of heat from a colder object to a hotter object   <-- CORRECT
    (D) No process is possible whose sole result is the transfer of heat from a hotter object to a colder object
    Answer: C
    Explanation: It is impossible for a self-acting machine unaided by any external agency to transfer heat from a cold to a hot reservoir. In other words, heat by itself cannot pass from a colder to a hotter body.

Q8. The spontaneous processes of nature are:
    (A) reversible
    (B) irreversible   <-- CORRECT
    (C) static
    (D) dynamic
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Spontaneous processes are irreversible because they can be reversed only by taking a different path to get back to their original state. A reversible process can take the same path to return to its original state.

Q9. A thermodynamic process (state i $ \to $ state f) is reversible
    (A) if the process can be turned back such that both the system and the surroundings return to their original states, with no other change anywhere else in the universe.   <-- CORRECT
    (B) if the process can be turned back such that both the system and the surroundings return to different states, with lots of change in the universe.
    (C) if the process can be turned back such that both the system and the surroundings return to their different states, with no other change anywhere else in the universe.
    (D) if the process can be turned back such that both the system and the surroundings return to their original states, with lots of change in the universe.
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Irreversible process is one which can be reversed in such a way that all changes taking place in the direct process are exactly replaced in the inverse order and opposite sense and no changes are left behind.

Q10. The Carnot cycle consists of:
    (A) Two isobaric processes connected by two adiabatic processes
    (B) Two isothermal processes connected by two adiabatic processes   <-- CORRECT
    (C) Two isochoric processes connected by two adiabatic processes
    (D) Two isothermal processes connected by two isobaric processes
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The Carnot cycle consists of the following four processes:
 A reversible isothermal gas expansion process
 A reversible adiabatic gas expansion process
 A reversible isothermal gas compression process
 A reversible adiabatic gas compression process

Q11. An engine operating between two temperatures:
    (A) can have a coefficient of performance greater than that of the Carnot engine
    (B) can have an efficiency greater than that of the Carnot engine
    (C) can not have an efficiency greater than that of the Carnot engine   <-- CORRECT
    (D) efficiency does not depend on the temperature
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The efficiency of an engine is given by
$\eta = 1 - {{{T_L}} \over {{T_H}}}$ 
T_L = is the temperature of the sink
T_H = is the temperature of source
maximum efficiency of the engine depends only on temperature.
so that any engine can not have an efficiency greater than that of the Carnot engine

Q12. A geyser heats water flowing at the rate of 3.0 liters per minute from 27 ^oC to 77 ^oC. If the geyser operates on a gas burner, what is the rate of consumption of the fuel if its heat of combustion is 4.0 $ \times $ 10^4 J/g?
    (A) 12 g per min
    (B) 18 g per min
    (C) 16 g per min   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 14 g per min
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Q = ms $\Delta$T = 3000 $\times$ 4.2 $\times$ 50
m = mass of water 
s = specific heat of the water
the heat of combustion is 4.0 $ \times $ 10^4 J/g
rate of consumption of the fuel = Q / Heat of combustion
$ = {{3000 \times 4.2 \times 50} \over {4 \times {{10}^4}}} $ = 16gm/min

Q13. What amount of heat must be supplied to 2.0 $ \times $ ${\rm{1}}{0^{-{\rm{2}}}}$ kg of nitrogen (at room temperature) to raise its temperature by 45 ^oC at constant pressure? (Molecular mass of N_2 = 28; R = 8.3 J mol^‚àí1 K^‚àí1.)
    (A) 974 J
    (B) 954 J
    (C) 904 J
    (D) 934 J   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $Q = n{C_v}\Delta T = {{2 \times {{10}^{ - 2}} \times {{10}^{ 3}}} \over {28}} \times {7 \over 2} \times 8.3 \times 45 $ = 934J
$n=\frac{\text { wtingm }}{\text { Molecularwt }}$
C_v = $7 \over 2$ R for Diatomic Gas

Q14. A cylinder with a movable piston contains 3 moles of hydrogen at standard temperature and pressure. The walls of the cylinder are made of a heat insulator, and the piston is insulated by having a pile of sand on it. By what factor does the pressure of the gas increase if the gas is compressed to half its original volume?
    (A) 2.84
    (B) 2.44
    (C) 2.64   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 3.04
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $P V^{\gamma}=\text {Const}$
$P V^{\gamma}=P_{f}\left(\frac{V}{2}\right)^{\gamma}$
For H_2, $\gamma$ = 1.4
P_f = P_2^1.4 = 2.6

Q15. In changing the state of a gas adiabatically from an equilibrium state A to another equilibrium state B, an amount of work equal to 22.3 J is done on the system. If the gas is taken from state A to B via a process in which the net heat absorbed by the system is 9.35 cal, how much is the net work done by the system in the latter case?
    (A) 17.9 J
    (B) 17.6 J
    (C) 16.8 J   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 17.2 J
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $\Delta Q = \Delta U + W$ 
In the adiabatic process $\Delta Q = 0$
$\Delta U = - W$ 
Given that W = -22.3J
$\Delta U $ = 22.3J
in second process $\Delta Q = 9.35 \times 4.18 $ = 39.083J
$\Delta Q = \Delta U + W$ 
ŒîQ = 9.35 $\times$ 4.18 = 39.083J
$\Delta$Q = $\Delta$U + W
39.083 = 22.3+W
W = 16.8J

Q1. Two cylinders A and B of equal capacity are connected to each other via a stopcock. A contains a gas at standard temperature and pressure. B is completely evacuated. The entire system is thermally insulated. The stopcock is suddenly opened. What is the final pressure of the gas in A and B?
    (A) 0.5 atm   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 0.6 atm
    (C) 0.55 atm
    (D) 0.45 atm
    Answer: A
    Explanation: PV = P_f.2V
P_f = $V \over 2V$
P_f = 0.5P

Q2. Two cylinders A and B of equal capacity are connected to each other via a stopcock. A contains gas at standard temperature and pressure. B is completely evacuated. The entire system is thermally insulated. The stopcock is suddenly opened. What is the change in internal energy of the gas?
    (A) 50 J
    (B) 100 J
    (C) 0.0 J   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 10 J
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Internal energy depends on temperature and temperature remains constant because the system is thermally insulated( as no work is done on//by the gas). so that change in internal energy will be zero.

Q3. Two cylinders A and B of equal capacity are connected to each other via a stopcock. A contains gas at standard temperature and pressure. B is completely evacuated. The entire system is thermally insulated. The stopcock is suddenly opened. What is the change in the temperature of the gas?
    (A) 0^oC   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 15^o C
    (C) -10^o C
    (D) 10^o C
    Answer: A
    Explanation: The temperature remains constant because the system is thermally insulated. Hence, the change in temperature will be zero.

Q4. Two cylinders A and B of equal capacity are connected to each other via a stopcock. A contains a gas at standard temperature and pressure. B is completely evacuated. The entire system is thermally insulated. The stopcock is suddenly opened. Do the intermediate states of the system (before settling to the final equilibrium state) lie on its P-V-T surface?
    (A) No   <-- CORRECT
    (B) yes
    (C) no for points on the left
    (D) yes for points on the left
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Pressure and volume will be changed.

Q5. A steam engine delivers 5.4$ \times $ 10^8 J of work per minute and services 3.6 $\times$ 10^9J of heat per minute from its boiler. What is the efficiency of the engine? How much heat is wasted per minute?
    (A) 14 percent, 3.0$ \times $10^9 J
    (B) 15 percent, 3.06$ \times $10^9 J   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 13 percent, 2.9$ \times $10^9 J
    (D) 12 percent, 2.8$ \times $10^9 J
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $\eta=\frac{\text { WorkDone }}{\text { HeatExtracted }}=\frac{5.4 \times 10^{8}}{3.6 \times 10^{9}}$ = 0.15 = 15 %
Heatwasted = Heat Extracted ‚àí Work Done
Heat Wasted = 3.6 $\times$ 10^9 ‚àí 5.4 $\times$ 10^8 = 3.06 $\times$ 10^9J

Q6. An electric heater supplies heat to a system at a rate of 100W. If the system performs work at the rate of 75 joules per second, at what rate is the internal energy increasing?
    (A) 35 W
    (B) 15 W
    (C) 25 W   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 20 W
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $\Delta$Q = $\Delta$U + W
$\Delta U = \Delta Q - W$
=100 ‚àí 75 = 25Watt

Q7. A thermodynamic system is taken from an original state to an intermediate state by the linear process shown in Figure. Its volume is then reduced to the original value from E to F by an isobaric process. Calculate the total work done by the gas from D to E to F
    (A) 470 J
    (B) 500 J
    (C) 450 J   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 480 J
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Work done by the gas is equal to the area under P-V curve. Total work done by the gas from D to E to F = Area of $\triangle$DEF is given by:-
Area of $\triangle$DEF is given by =($\frac {1}{2}$)DE $\times$ EF
Where,
DF = Change in pressure is
=600N/m^2 - 300N/m^2
= 300N/m^2
FE = Change in volume is
=5.0m^2 - 2.0m^2
= 3.0m^2
Area of $\triangle$DEF is given by = ($\frac {1}{2}$) $\times$ 300 $\times$ 3 = 450J
Therefore, the total work done by the gas from D to E to F is 450J.

Q8. A 1.0-mol sample of an ideal gas is kept at 0.0$^\circ $C during expansion from 3.0 L to 10.0 L. How much energy transfer by heat occurs with the surroundings in this process?
    (A) 3.0 $ \times $ 10^3 J
    (B) 2.8 $ \times $ 10^3 J
    (C) 2.7 $ \times $ 10^3 J   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 2.9 $ \times $ 10^3 J
    Answer: C
    Explanation: in the isothermal process $\Delta U = 0$ 
hence $\Delta Q = W$ 
$Q = nRT{\log _e}{{{V_f}} \over {{V_i}}}$ 
$Q = 1 \times 8.31 \times 273 \times 2.303{\log _{10}}{{10} \over 3} $ = 2.7 $\times$ 10^3J

Q9. A refrigerator is to maintain eatables kept inside at 9^o C. If the room temperature is 36^o C, calculate the coefficient of performance?
    (A) 12.4
    (B) 11.8
    (C) 11.4
    (D) 10.4   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $K = {{{T_L}} \over {{T_H} - {T_L}}} = {{282} \over {309 - 282}}$ = 10.4

Q10. A 1.0-mol sample of an ideal gas is kept at 0.0^oC during expansion from 3.0 L to 10.0 L. How much work is done by the gas during the expansion?
    (A) 3.0 $ \times $ 10^3 J
    (B) 2.7 $ \times $ 10^3 J   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 2.9 $ \times $ 10^3 J
    (D) 2.8 $ \times $ 10^3 J
    Answer: B
    Explanation: In the isothermal process
$W = nRT{\log _e}\left( {{{{V_f}} \over {{V_i}}}} \right)$
$W = 1 \times 8.31 \times 273 \times 2.303{\log _{10}}{{10} \over 3} $ = 2.7 $\times$ 10^3J

Q11. A 1.0-mol sample of an ideal gas is kept at 0.0^oC during expansion from 3.0 L to 10.0 L. If the gas is returned to the original volume by means of an isobaric process, how much work is done by the gas?
    (A) -1.3 $ \times $ 10^3
    (B) -1.4 $ \times $ 10^3
    (C) -1.6 $ \times $ 10^3 J   <-- CORRECT
    (D) -1.5 $ \times $ 10^3
    Answer: C
    Explanation: W = nR(T_f ‚àí T_i)
V $\propto$ T
$\frac{10}{273}=\frac{3}{T_{f}}$ 
T_f = 81.9K
T_i = 273K
W = 1 $\times$ 8.31 $\times$ (81.9 ‚àí 273) = ‚àí1.6 $\times$ 10^3J

Q12. A 1.0-kg bar of copper is heated at atmospheric pressure. If its temperature increases from 20^oC to 50^oC, what is the work done by the copper on the surrounding atmosphere? Given that for Cu $3\alpha $= 5.1 $\times$ 10^-5 /C, ${\rho _{Cu}} $ = 8.92 $\times$ 10^3Kg/m^3.
    (A) 1.8 $ \times $ 10^‚àí2 J
    (B) 1.9 $ \times $ 10^‚àí2 J
    (C) 1.7 $ \times $ 10^‚àí2 J   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 1.6 $ \times $ 10^‚àí2 J
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $\Delta V = 3\alpha {V_i}\Delta T = 5.1 \times {10^{ - 5}} \times 30 \times {V_i} = 1.5 \times {10^{ - 3}}{V_i}$
$\Delta V = 1.5 \times {10^{ - 3}}{m \over {{\rho _{Cu}}}} $ $= 1.5 \times {10^{ - 3}}{1 \over {8.92 \times {{10}^3}}} = 1.7 \times {10^{ - 7}}$
W = P$\Delta$V = 1.01 $\times$ 10^5 $\times$ 1.7 $\times$ 10^‚àí7 = 1.7 $\times$10^‚àí2J

Q13. A 3.00-g copper penny at 25.0^oC drops from a height of 50.0 m to the ground. (a) If 60.0 percent of the change in potential energy goes into increasing the internal energy, what is its final temperature? Specific heat of Cu = 0.385J/g ^0C.
    (A) 28.8^o C
    (B) 26.8^o C
    (C) 25.8^o C   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 27.8^o C
    Answer: C
    Explanation: mgh = 3 $\times$ 10^‚àí3 $\times$ 10 $\times$ 50 = 1.5J
Heat used in increasing temp 
$Q=1.5 \times \frac{60}{100}$ =0.9 J
Q = ms$\Delta$T
0.9 = 3 $\times$ 0.385 $\times$ (T ‚àí 298)
T = 298.8K = 25.8^oC

Q14. A water heater is operated by solar power. If the solar collector has an area of 6.00 m^2 and the power delivered by sunlight is 550 W/ m^2, how long does it take to increase the temperature of 1.00 m^3 of water from 20.0^oC to 60.0^oC?
    (A) 51.7 ks
    (B) 52.7 ks
    (C) 48.7 ks
    (D) 50.7 ks   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Q = $msŒîT$
$ P = {W \over t} = {{ms\Delta T} \over t} $
t = $ {{ms\Delta T} \over P} = {{1000 \times 4186 \times 40} \over {550 \times 6}} $ = 50700sec = 50.7Ksec

Q15. A sample of an ideal gas is expanded to twice its original volume of 1.00 m^3 in a quasi-static process for which P = $ \alpha {V^2}$with $\alpha $= 5.00atm/m^6. How much work is done by the expanding gas?
    (A) 1.18 MJ   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 1.28 MJ
    (C) 0.98 MJ
    (D) 1.08 MJ
    Answer: A
    Explanation: P = $\alpha$V^2
$\alpha$ = 5.00 $\times$ 1.01 $\times$ 10^5 Pa/m^6
V_i = 1.00 m^3
V_f = 2.00 m^3
Work done by the gas W = $\int_{1.00}^{2.00} P . d V=\int_{1.00}^{2.00} \alpha V^{2} \cdot d V=\left[\frac{\alpha V^{3}}{3}\right]_{1.00}^{2.00}$ = ${\alpha \over 3} \times (2^3-1^3)$ =1.18 MJ

Q1. One mole of an ideal gas is heated slowly so that it goes from PV state (P_i, V_i) to (3P_i, 3V_i) in such a way that the pressure of the gas is directly proportional to the volume. How much work is done in the process?
    (A) 2 P_i V_i
    (B) 4 P_i V_i   <-- CORRECT
    (C) P_i V_i
    (D) 3 P_i V_i
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $P=\frac{P_{i}}{V_{i}} V$ 
W =$ \int_{{V_i}}^{{V_f}} {P.dV} = \frac{{{P_i}}}{{{V_i}}}\int_{{V_i}}^{3{V_i}} {V.dV} = \frac{{{P_i}}}{{{V_i}}}{\left[ {\frac{{{V^2}}}{2}} \right]_{{V_i}}}^{3{V_i}} $ = 4P_i V_i

Q2. One mole of an ideal gas is heated slowly so that it goes from PV state (${{\rm{P}}_{\rm{i}}}$ , ${{\rm{V}}_{\rm{i}}}$) to (3 ${{\rm{P}}_{\rm{i}}}$ , 3 ${{\rm{V}}_{\rm{i}}}$) in such a way that the pressure of the gas is directly proportional to the volume. How is the temperature of the gas-related to its volume during this process?
    (A) $T=\left(\frac{4 P_i}{n R V_i}\right) V^2$
    (B) $T=\left(\frac{P_i}{n R V_i}\right) V^2$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $T=\left(\frac{2 P_i}{n R V_i}\right) V^2$
    (D) $T=\left(\frac{P_i}{n R V_i}\right) V^3$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $k=\frac{P_{i}}{V_{i}}$
$P=\left(\frac{P_i}{V_i}\right) V$
PV = nRT
$\left(\frac{P_{i}}{V_{i}}\right) $V . V = nRT
$T=\left(\frac{P_i}{n R V_i}\right) V^2$

Q3. An ideal gas is enclosed in a cylinder with a movable piston on top. The piston has a mass of 8000 g and an area of 5.00 cm^2 and is free to slide up and down, keeping the pressure of the gas constant. How much work is done as the temperature of 0.200 mol of the gas is raised from 20.0 ^oC to 300^oC?
    (A) 486 J
    (B) 456 J
    (C) 476 J
    (D) 466 J   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: in the isobaric process
W = nR(T_f ‚àí Ti)
W = 0.2 $\times$ 8.31(300 ‚àí 20) = 466J

Q4. A gas expands from I to F along three possible paths, as indicated in Figure. Calculate the work in joules done by the gas along the paths IAF, IF, and IBF
    (A) 820 J, 516 J, 213 J
    (B) 810 J, 506 J, 203 J   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 830 J, 526 J, 223 J
    (D) 840 J, 536 J, 233 J
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Work done by gas = area under P-V curve
 IAF
 Work done by the gas = Area under the graph IA = 4 $\times$ 1.013 $\times$ 10^5 $\times$ 2 $\times$ 10^-3 = 810 J
 IF
 Work done by the gas = area under the graph If = $\frac {1}{2}$(1 + 4) $\times$ 1.013 $\times$10^5 $\times$ 2 $\times$10^-3 = 506J
 IBF
 Work done by the gas= area under the graph BF = 1 $\times$ 1.013 $\times$ 10^5 $\times$ 2 $\times$ 10^-3 = 203 J

Q5. A thermodynamic system undergoes a process in which its internal energy decreases by 500 J. If, at the same time, 220 J of work is done on the system, what is the energy transferred to or from it by heat?
    (A) Q = -720 J   <-- CORRECT
    (B) Q = -740 J
    (C) Q = -700 J
    (D) Q = -730 J
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\Delta Q = \Delta U + W$ = ( ‚àí500) + (‚àí220) = ‚àí720J

Q6. Consider the cyclic process depicted in Figure If Q is negative for the process BC, and if $\Delta {{\rm{E}}_{{\rm{int}}}}$ is negative for the process CA, what are the signs of W and $\Delta {{\rm{E}}_{{\rm{int}}}}$ that are associated with BC?
    (A) 0, -   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 0, +
    (C) 0, -2
    (D) +, -
    Answer: A
    Explanation: W = area under P-V curve = 0
$\Delta$Q = $\Delta$E_int + W
W = 0, $\Delta$Q = -ve
$\Delta$E_int = -ve

Q7. Consider the cyclic process depicted in Figure if $\Delta {{\rm{E}}_{{\rm{int}}}}$ is negative for the process CA, what are the signs of Q and W are associated with CA?
    (A) -, -   <-- CORRECT
    (B) -, +
    (C) +, 0
    (D) -, 0
    Answer: A
    Explanation: W = PŒîV = ‚àíve(inCompression $\Delta$V = ‚àíve) 
Q = $\Delta$E_int + W
$\Delta$E_int = -ve
W = -ve
So that $\Delta$Q = -ve

Q8. Consider the cyclic process depicted in Figure If Q is negative for the process BC, and if $\Delta {{\rm{E}}_{{\rm{int}}}}$ is negative for the process CA, what are the signs of Q, W, and $\Delta {{\rm{E}}_{{\rm{int}}}}$ that are associated with AB?
    (A) -, +, -
    (B) +, 0, -
    (C) +, +, +   <-- CORRECT
    (D) -, 0, +
    Answer: C
    Explanation: W = +ve (Expansion)
U_c - U_a = -ve
U_b - U_c = -ve
hence U_b - U_A = -ve
U_a - U_b = + ve
$\Delta$Q = $\Delta$U + W
$\Delta$U = + ve
W = + ve
Hence $\Delta$Q = + ve

Q9. An ideal gas initially at 300 K undergoes an isobaric expansion at 2.50 kPa. If the volume increases from 1.00 m^3 to 3.00 m^3 and if 12.5 kJ of energy is transferred to the gas by heat, what is the change in its internal energy?
    (A) 7.80 kJ
    (B) 7.70 kJ
    (C) 7.60 kJ
    (D) 7.50 kJ   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: W = P$\Delta$V = 2.5 $\times$ 2 = 5KJ
$\Delta$Q = 12.5
$\Delta$Q = $\Delta$U + W
12.5 = $\Delta$U + 5
$\Delta$U = 7.5KJ

Q10. An ideal gas initially at 300 K undergoes an isobaric expansion at 2.50 kPa. If the volume increases from 1.00 m^3 to 3.00 m^3 and if 12.5 kJ of energy is transferred to the gas by heat, what is the change in its final temperature?
    (A) 850 K
    (B) 900 K   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 800 K
    (D) 950 K
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $\frac{V_{1}}{T_{1}}=\frac{V_{2}}{T_{2}}$
$T_{2}=\frac{V_{2}}{V_{1}} T_{1}=\frac{3.00}{1.00} \times 300$ = 900 K

Q11. A 2.00-mol sample of helium gas initially at 300 K and 0.400 atm is compressed isothermally to 1.20 atm. Assuming the behavior of helium to be that of an ideal gas, find the final volume of the gas.
    (A) 0.040 m^3
    (B) 0.039 m^3
    (C) 0.042 m^3
    (D) 0.041 m^3   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: PV = nRT
V = $\frac{n R T}{P}=\frac{2 \times 8.31 \times 300}{0.4 \times 1.01 \times 10^{5}}$
P_1V_1 = P_2V_2
V_2 = $\frac{P_{1} V_{1}}{P_{2}}=\frac{0.4 \times 1.01 \times 10^{5}}{1.2 \times 1.01 \times 10^{5}} \times \frac{2 \times 8.31 \times 300}{0.4 \times 1.01 \times 10^{5}}$ = 0.041m^3

Q12. A 2.00-mol sample of helium gas initially at 300 K and 0.400 atm is compressed isothermally to 1.20 atm. Assuming the behaviour of helium to be that of an ideal gas, find the work done by the gas?
    (A) -5.48 kJ   <-- CORRECT
    (B) -5.38 kJ
    (C) -5.58 kJ
    (D) -5.28 kJ
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $W = \mu RT{\log _e}{{{V_f}} \over {{V_i}}} $ = $ \mu RT{\log _e}{{{P_i}} \over {{P_f}}}$ 
$W = \mu RT \times 2.303{\log _{10}}{{{P_i}} \over {{P_f}}} $ = 2√ó8.31 $\times$ 300 $\times$ 2.303 $\times$ $ {\log _{10}}{1 \over 3} $ = -5.48 kJ

Q13. A 2.00-mol sample of helium gas initially at 300 K and 0.400 atm is compressed isothermally to 1.20 atm. Assuming the behavior of helium to be that of an ideal gas, find the energy transferred by heat:
    (A) -5.28 kJ
    (B) -5.48 kJ   <-- CORRECT
    (C) -5.58 kJ
    (D) -5.38 kJ
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $\Delta Q = \Delta U + W$ 
in the isothermal process
${\rm{ }}\left( {\Delta U = 0} \right)$
$ \Delta Q = W = \mu RT{\log _e}{{{V_f}} \over {{V_i}}} = \mu RT{\log _e}{{{P_i}} \over {{P_f}}} $
$Q = \mu RT \times 2.303{\log _{10}}{{{P_i}} \over {{P_f}}} = 2 \times 8.31 \times 300 \times 2.303 \times {\log _{10}}{1 \over 3} $ = - 5.48KJ

Q14. An ideal gas is carried through a thermodynamic cycle consisting of two isobaric and two isothermal processes, as shown in Figure net work done in the entire cycle is given by the equation?
    (A) W_net = P_1(V_2 - V_1) In $\frac{P_2}{P_1}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) W_net = P_1(V_2 ‚àí V_1)
    (C) W_net = RP_1(V_2 - V_1) In $\frac{P_2}{P_1}$
    (D) W_net = P_1(V_2 + V_1) In $\frac{P_2}{P_1}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: W = W_AB + W_BC + W_CD + W_DA
W = $\int_{A}^{B} P \cdot d V+\int_{B}^{C} P . d V+\int_{C}^{D} P . d V+\int_{D}^{A} P . d V$
W = nRT_1$\int_\limits{A}^{B}\left(\frac{d V}{V}\right)+P_{2} \int_\limits{B}^{C} d V+n R T_{2} \int_\limits{C}^{D}\left(\frac{d V}{V}\right)+P_{1} \int_{D} d V$
W = nRT_1log_e($\frac{V_B}{V_1}$) + P_2(V_C - V_B) + nRT_2log_e($\frac{V_2}{V_C}$) + P_1(V_A - V_D)
P_1V_A = P_2V_B
P_2V_C = P_1V_D
$\frac{V_B}{V_1}$ = $\frac{P_1}{P_2}$
$\frac{V_2}{V_c}$ = $\frac{P_2}{P_1}$
W = P_1(V_2 - V_1)log_e($\frac{P_2}{P_1}$)

Q15. Find the efficiency of a heat engine that absorbs 2000 J of energy from a hot reservoir and exhausts 1500 J to a cold reservoir?
    (A) 25 percent   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 30 percent
    (C) 20 percent
    (D) 35 percent
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\eta = {{{Q_H} - {Q_L}} \over {{Q_H}}} = {{500} \over {2000}} $ = 0.25 = 25%

Q1. A steam engine has a boiler that operates at 500 K. The energy from the burning fuel changes water to steam, and this steam then drives a piston. The cold reservoir‚Äôs temperature is that of the outside air, approximately 300 K. What is the maximum thermal efficiency of this steam engine?
    (A) 40 percent   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 25 percent
    (C) 30 percent
    (D) 45 percent
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\eta = {{{T_H} - {T_L}} \over {{T_H}}} = {{200} \over {500}} $ = 0.4 = 40%

Q2. The highest theoretical efficiency of a certain engine is 30 percent. If this engine uses the atmosphere, which has a temperature of 300 K, as its cold reservoir, what is the temperature of its hot reservoir?
    (A) 430 K   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 490 K
    (C) 470 K
    (D) 450 K
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\eta=\frac{T_{H}-T_{L}}{T_{H}}$
0.3 = $\frac{T_{H}-300}{T_{H}}$
T_H = 430K

Q3. One of the most efficient engines ever built (actual efficiency 42.0 percent) operates between 430^oC and 1 870^oC. What is its maximum theoretical efficiency?
    (A) 87.3 percent
    (B) 57.2 percent
    (C) 67.2 percent   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 99.2 percent
    Answer: C
    Explanation: T_H = 1870 ^0C = 1870 +273 = 2143K and T_L = 430 ^0C = 430+273 = 703K
$\eta = {{{T_H} - {T_L}} \over {{T_H}}} = {{1440} \over {2143}} $ = 0.672 = 67.2%

Q4. One of the most efficient engines ever built (actual efficiency 42.0 percent) operates between 430^oC and 1 870^oC. How much power does the engine deliver if it absorbs1.40 $ \times $ 10^5 J of energy each second from the hot reservoir?
    (A) 62.8 kW
    (B) 52.8 kW
    (C) 58.8 kW   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 58.1 kW
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $\eta = {{{\rm{Work (Output)}}} \over {{\rm{Heat Extracted}}}}$
0.42 = $ {W \over {1.40 \times {{10}^5}}}$
W = 58.8KJ
Power = Energy (or work) transfer per second = 58.8KW

Q5. A Carnot engine has a power output of 150 kW. The engine operates between two reservoirs at 20.0^oC and 500^oC. How much energy does it absorb per hour?
    (A) 632 MJ
    (B) 869 MJ   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 765 MJ
    (D) 855 MJ
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $\eta = {{{T_H} - {T_L}} \over {{T_H}}} $$= {{480} \over {773}} $ = 0.62
$\eta$ = $ {{{\rm{Work (Output)}}} \over {{\rm{Heat Extracted(input)}}}} $ 
Power = Energy output per second = 150KJ
Efficiency = Energy Output / Energy Absorbed
Energy absorbed per second = Energy output / Efficiency = $ {{150 \times {{10}^3}} \over {0.62}}J$ 
Energy absorbed per hour = $ = {{150 \times {{10}^3}} \over {0.62}} \times 3600 $ = 869MJ

Q6. A Carnot engine has a power output of 150 kW. The engine operates between two reservoirs at 20.0^oC and 500^oC. How much energy is lost per hour in its exhaust?
    (A) 320 MJ
    (B) 351 MJ
    (C) 330 MJ   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 220 MJ
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $\eta=\frac{T_{H}-T_{L}}{T_{H}}=\frac{480}{773}$ = 0.62
$\eta=\frac{\text { Work }(\text { Output })}{\text { HeatExtracted }(\text { input })}$
Power = Energy output per second = 150KJ
Efficiency = Energy Output / Energy Absorbed
Energy absorbed per second = $Energy \ output \over Efficiency$ = $ {{150 \times {{10}^3}} \over {0.62}}J$ 
Energy loss per hour = $ = {{150 \times {{10}^3}} \over {0.62}} $ ‚àí150 $\times$ 10^3 = 91935J
Energy loss per hour = 91935 $\times$ 3600 = 330MJ

Q7. An ideal gas is taken through a Carnot cycle. The isothermal expansion occurs at 250^oC, and the isothermal compression takes place at 50.0^oC. Assuming that the gas absorbs 1200 J of energy from the hot reservoir during the isothermal expansion, find the energy expelled to the cold reservoir in each cycle.
    (A) 115 J   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 110 J
    (C) 120 J
    (D) 105J
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\eta = {{{T_H} - {T_L}} \over {{T_H}}} = {{473} \over {523}}$
$\eta = {W \over {{Q_H}}}$
${{473} \over {523}} = {W \over {1200}}$
W = 1085J
W = Q_H ‚àí Q_L
Q_L = Q_H ‚àí W = 1200 ‚àí 1085 = 115J

Q8. An ideal gas is taken through a Carnot cycle. The isothermal expansion occurs at 250^oC, and the isothermal compression takes place at 50.0^oC. Assuming that the gas absorbs 1200 J of energy from the hot reservoir during the isothermal expansion, find the network done by the gas in each cycle:
    (A) 1080.4 J
    (B) 1000.78 J
    (C) 1090.86 J
    (D) 1085.22 J   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $\eta = {{{T_H} - {T_L}} \over {{T_H}}} = {{473} \over {523}}$
$\eta = {W \over {{Q_H}}}$
${{473} \over {523}} = {W \over {1200}}$
W = 1085.277J

Q9. A power plant operates at 32.0 percent efficiency during the summer when the seawater for cooling is at 20.0^oC. The plant uses 350^oC steam to drive turbines. Assuming that the plant‚Äôs efficiency changes in the same proportion as the ideal efficiency, what would be the plant‚Äôs efficiency in the winter, when the seawater is at 10.0^oC?
    (A) 31 percent
    (B) 34 percent
    (C) 32 percent
    (D) 33 percent   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $\eta = {{{T_H} - {T_L}} \over {{T_H}}}$
$\eta \propto \Delta T$
${{{\eta _1}} \over {{\eta _2}}} = {{\Delta {T_1}} \over {\Delta {T_2}}}$
${{32} \over \eta } = {{330} \over {340}}$
$\eta $ = 33%

Q10. A power plant that would make use of the temperature gradient in the ocean has been proposed. The system is to operate between 5.00^oC (water temperature at a depth of about 1 km) and 20.0$^\circ $C (surface water temperature). What is the maximum efficiency of such a system?
    (A) 5.12 percent   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 5.32 percent
    (C) 5.02 percent
    (D) 5.45 percent
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\eta = {{{T_H} - {T_L}} \over {{T_H}}}$ 
$\eta = {{293 - 278} \over {293}} $ = 0.0512 = 5.12%

Q11. A power plant that would make use of the temperature gradient in the ocean has been proposed. The system is to operate between 5.00^oC (water temperature at a depth of about 1 km) and 20.0$^\circ $C (surface water temperature). If the power output of the plant is 75.0 MW, how much energy is absorbed per hour?
    (A) 5.27 TJ/hr   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 5.57 TJ/hr
    (C) 5.07 TJ/hr
    (D) 5.37 TJ/hr
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\eta = {{{T_H} - {T_L}} \over {{T_H}}} = {{293 - 278} \over {293}} $ = 0.0512 = 5.12%
Efficiency = $Energy \ output \over Energy \ Absorbed$ 
Energy output per second = Power output = 75 MW
Energy absorbed per second = $Energy \ output \ per \ second \over Efficiency$ 
Energy absorbed per second $ = {{75 \times {{10}^6}} \over {0.0512}} = 1465 \times {10^6}J$ 
Energy absorbed per hour = 1465 $\times$ 10^6 $\times$ 3600 = 5.27 $\times$10^12 J = 5.27TJ/hr

Q12. A typical dorm room or bedroom contains about 2500 moles of air. Find the change in the internal energy of this much air when it is cooled from 35^oC to 26^oC at a constant pressure of 1.00 atm. Treat the air as an ideal gas with Œ≥ = 1.4.
    (A) $ - 4.48 \times {10^5}J$
    (B) $ - 4.78 \times {10^5}J$
    (C) $ - 4.58 \times {10^5}J$
    (D) ‚àí4.68 $\times $ 10^5J   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: ${C_V} = {R \over {\gamma - 1}} = {{8.31} \over {1.4 - 1}} = {{8.31} \over {0.4}}$
$\Delta U = m\times C_V\times \Delta T$
$\Delta U = 2500 \times {{8.31} \over {0.4}} \times (299 - 308) $ = ‚àí4.68 $\times$ 10^5J

Q13. A Carnot engine takes 2000 J of heat from a reservoir at 500 K, does some work, and discards some heat to a reservoir at 350 K. How much work does it do?
    (A) 500 J
    (B) 550 J
    (C) 650 J
    (D) 600 J   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $\eta = {{{T_H} - {T_L}} \over {{T_H}}} = {{500 - 350} \over {500}} = {{150} \over {500}} $ = 0.3 = 30% 
$\eta = {W \over {{Q_H}}}$
0.3 = $ {W \over {2000}}$
W = 600J

Q14. A Carnot engine takes 2000 J of heat from a reservoir at 500 K, does some work, and discards some heat to a reservoir at 350 K. How much heat is discarded?
    (A) -1100 J
    (B) -1200 J
    (C) -1300 J
    (D) -1400 J   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $\eta = {{{T_H} - {T_L}} \over {{T_H}}} = {{500 - 350} \over {500}} = {{150} \over {500}} $ = 0.3 = 30%
Heat Discarded = ${\rm{2000}} \times {{{\rm{70}}} \over {100}} $ = 1400 J

Q15. A Carnot engine takes 2000 J of heat from a reservoir at 500 K, does some work, and discards some heat to a reservoir at 350 K. what is its efficiency?
    (A) 30 percent   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 27 percent
    (C) 28 percent
    (D) 32 percent
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $\eta = {{{T_H} - {T_L}} \over {{T_H}}} = {{500 - 350} \over {500}} = {{150} \over {500}} $ = 0.3 = 30%

Kinetic Theory Test 01  (id 587) [FREE]

Q1. Kinetic theory explains the behavior
    (A) of solids and liquids based on the idea that they gas consist of rapidly vibrating atoms or molecules
    (B) of solids based on the idea that the solid consists of rapidly vibrating atoms or molecules
    (C) of gases based on the idea that the gas consists of rapidly moving atoms or molecules   <-- CORRECT
    (D) of liquids based on the idea that the liquids consist of rapidly moving atoms or molecules
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The theory for ideal gases makes the following assumptions
1. Gases consist of particles in constant, random motion. They continue in a straight line until they collide with something‚Äîusually each other or the walls of their container.
2. Particles are point masses with no volume. The particles are so small compared to the space between them, that we do not consider their size in ideal gases.
3. No molecular forces are at work. This means that there is no attraction or repulsion between the particles.
4. Gas pressure is due to the molecules colliding with the walls of the container. All of these collisions are perfectly elastic, meaning that there is no change in energy of either the particles or the wall upon collision. No energy is lost or gained from collisions.
5. The time it takes to collide is negligible compared with the time between collisions.
6. The kinetic energy of a gas is a measure of its Kelvin temperature. Individual gas molecules have different speeds, but the temperature and kinetic energy of the gas refer to the average of these speeds.
7. The average kinetic energy of a gas particle is directly proportional to the temperature. An increase in temperature increases the speed in which the gas molecules move.
8. All gases at a given temperature have the same average kinetic energy.
9. Lighter gas molecules move faster than heavier molecules.

Q2. Kinetic theory:
    (A) correctly explains the specific heat capacities of many liquids
    (B) correctly explains the specific heat capacities of many gases   <-- CORRECT
    (C) correctly explains specific heat capacities of many solids
    (D) correctly explains specific heat capacities of super cooled liquids
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The kinetic theory describes a gas as a large number of submicroscopic particles (atoms or molecules), all of which are in constant rapid motion that has randomness arising from their many collisions with each other and with the walls of the container. It will apply only to gases.

Q3. Kinetic theory relates measurable properties.
    (A) of super cooled liquids such as viscosity, creep and diffusion with molecular parameters, yielding estimates of molecular sizes and masses
    (B) of gases such as viscosity, conduction and diffusion with molecular parameters, yielding estimates of molecular sizes and masses   <-- CORRECT
    (C) of solids such as expansion, conduction and elongation with molecular parameters, yielding estimates of molecular sizes and masses
    (D) of liquids such as viscosity, conduction and diffusion with molecular parameters, yielding estimates of molecular sizes and masses
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Kinetic theory explains the behaviour of gases based on the idea that the gas consists of rapidly moving atoms or molecules. It also relates measurable properties of gases such as viscosity, conduction and diffusion with molecular parameters, yielding estimates of molecular sizes and masses.

Q4. According to Atomic Hypothesis:
    (A) All things are made of atoms - little particles that move around in circles
    (B) All things are made of atoms - little particles that stay put at fixed points
    (C) All things are made of atoms - little particles that move around in perpetual motion   <-- CORRECT
    (D) All things are made of atoms - little particles that keep oscillating
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The atomic hypothesis that all things are made of atoms-little particles that move around in perpetual motion, attracting each other when they are a little distance apart, but repelling upon being squeezed into one another.

Q5. According to Avogadro‚Äôs law:
    (A) Equal volumes of all gases at equal temperature and pressure have different numbers of molecules.
    (B) Equal volumes of all gases at equal temperatures have the same number of molecules.
    (C) Equal volumes of all gases at equal pressure have the same number of molecules.
    (D) Equal volumes of all gases at equal temperature and pressure have the same number of molecules.   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Avogadro's law states that "equal volumes of all gases, at the same temperature and pressure, have the same number of molecules"
For a given mass of an ideal gas, the volume and amount (moles) of the gas are directly proportional if the temperature and pressure are constant.
V $ \propto $ n
${V \over n} = k$

Q6. In which case are the atoms relatively rigidly fixed?
    (A) liquids and gases
    (B) gases
    (C) liquids
    (D) solids   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Atoms in a gas are well separated with no regular arrangement. Atoms vibrate and move freely at high speeds
Atoms in a liquid are close together with no regular arrangement. Atoms vibrate, move about, and slide past each other.
Atoms in a solid are tightly packed, usually in a regular pattern. Atoms vibrate (jiggle) but generally do not move from place to place.

Q7. The order of interatomic distance in liquids is about:
    (A) $2\mathop A\limits^o $   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $20\mathop A\limits^o $
    (C) $1000\mathop A\limits^o $
    (D) $100\mathop A\limits^o $
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Using the assumption of little spheres (for interatomic space) the spacing between the molecules is about 2 $\times$ 10^-10 m i.e.$2\mathop A\limits^o $

Q8. The average distance a molecule can travel without colliding is called the:
    (A) mean free distance
    (B) mean free path   <-- CORRECT
    (C) mean free length
    (D) mean free motion
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The mean free path is the average distance travelled by a moving particle (such as an atom, a molecule, a photon) between successive impacts (collisions), which modify its direction or energy or other particle properties.

Q9. In dynamic equilibrium, molecules collide and change their speeds during the collision:
    (A) but the r.m.s properties vary
    (B) but the peak properties vary
    (C) but the average properties are constant   <-- CORRECT
    (D) but the average properties vary
    Answer: C
    Explanation: All collisions between molecules among themselves or between molecules and the walls are elastic. So that total kinetic energy and total momentum are conserved. Also, the average properties of gas remain constant.

Q10. A cubic vessel (with faces horizontal + vertical) contains an ideal gas at NTP. The vessel is being carried by a rocket which is moving at a speed of 500ms^-1 in vertical direction. The pressure of the gas inside the vessel as observed by us on the ground
    (A) remains the same because the motion of the vessel as a whole does not affect the relative motion of the gas molecules and the walls.   <-- CORRECT
    (B) will be different on the top wall and bottom wall of the vessel.
    (C) will increase by a factor equal to $\frac{\left(V_{\mathrm{rms}}^2+(500)^2\right)}{v^{22}}$ where v_rms was the original mean square velocity of the gas.
    (D) remain the same because 500 ms^-1 is very much smaller than v_rms of the gas.
    Answer: A
    Explanation: According to the ideal gas law,
P = $\frac{nRT}{V}$, here the temperature of the vessel remains unchanged hence, the pressure remains the same from that point of view.
Now, let us discuss the phenomenon inside the vessel. The gas molecules keep on colliding among themselves as well as with the walls of containing vessel. These collisions are perfectly elastic.
The number of collisions per unit volume in a gas remains constant. So, the pressure of the gas inside the vessel remains the same because the motion of the vessel as a whole does not affect the relative motion of the gas molecules with respect to the walls.

Q11. Consider a mixture of non-interacting ideal gases: ${\mu _{\rm{1}}}$ moles of gas 1, ${\mu _{\rm{2}}}$ moles of gas 2, etc. in a vessel of volume V at temperature T and pressure P. It is then found that the equation of state of the mixture is :
    (A) PV = ($\mu _{\rm{1}}$ + ${\mu _{\rm{2}}}$ +‚Ä¶) RT   <-- CORRECT
    (B) PV = ($\mu _{\rm{1}}$ + ${\mu _{\rm{2}}}$ +‚Ä¶) R
    (C) V = ($\mu_ {\rm{1}}$ + ${\mu _{\rm{2}}}$ +‚Ä¶) RT
    (D) P = ($\mu_{\rm{1}}$ + ${\mu _{\rm{2}}}$+‚Ä¶) RT
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Total pressure = Sum of partial pressure 
= P_1 + P_2 +.........
P = $ {{{\mu _1}RT} \over V} + {{{\mu _2}RT} \over V} $ +........ 
PV = ($\mu_ {\rm{1}}$ + ${\mu _{\rm{2}}}$ +‚Ä¶) RT

Q12. According to Dalton‚Äôs law of partial pressures:
    (A) the total pressure of a mixture of real gases is the sum of factored individual pressures
    (B) the total pressure of a mixture of ideal gases is the difference of partial pressures
    (C) the total pressure of a mixture of ideal gases is the sum of pressures
    (D) the total pressure of a mixture of ideal gases is the sum of partial pressures   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: According to Dalton's law of partial pressures, at constant volume and temperature, the total pressure exerted by a mixture of gases is equal to the sum of the partial pressures of the component gases
P = P_1 + P_2 + .......
P = $\frac{\mu_{1} R T}{V}$ + $\frac{\mu_{2} R T}{V}$ + ........
PV = $(\mu_{1}+\mu_{2}+.....)$ RT
PV = $\mu $RT

Q13. Considering a gas in a parallelopiped, in a small time interval $\Delta {\rm{t}}$, a molecule with x-component of velocity v_x will hit the wall?
    (A) If it is outside or beyond the distance v_x $\Delta {\rm{t}}$ from the wall
    (B) If it is outside or beyond the distance 2v_x $\Delta {\rm{t}}$ from the wall
    (C) If it is within the distance v_x $\Delta {\rm{t}}$ from the wall   <-- CORRECT
    (D) If it is within the distance 2v_x $\Delta {\rm{t}}$ from the wall
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Collision by molecule to the wall of container assumed to be perfectly elastic.
By the principle of conservation of momentum, the momentum imparted to the wall in the collision = 2mv_x
So the magnitude of the force on the wall from each collision is
$F = {{2m{v_x}} \over {\Delta t}}$ 
distance travelled in time $\Delta t$ 
$x = {v_x}\Delta t$

Q14. If there are n number of molecules per unit volume and m is the mass of each, v_x is the x-component of velocity, pressure can be written as:
    (A) P = $
 m\overline {v_x^2} $
    (B) P = $ 2nm\overline {v_x^2} $
    (C) P = $ n\overline {v_x^2} $
    (D) P = $ nm\overline {v_x^2} $   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Pressure is due to collision of molecule with the wall of container.
Collision by molecule to the wall of container assumed to be perfectly elastic.
By the principle of conservation of momentum, the momentum imparted to the wall in the collision = 2mv_x 
the number of molecules hitting the wall in time $\Delta t$ = $nA{v_x}\Delta t$ 
n = no. of molecule per unit volume
A = Area of container
But, on the average, half of these are moving towards the wall and the other half away from the wall. Thus, the number of molecules with velocity (v_x , v_y, v_z) hitting the wall in time ‚àÜt is ¬ΩA v_x‚àÜt n, where n is the number of molecules per unit volume. The total momentum transferred to the wall by these molecules in time ‚àÜt is: Q = (2mv_x ) (¬Ω n A v_x ‚àÜt )
The total momentum transferred to the wall by these molecules in $\Delta t$ time = $m{v_x}\left( {nA{v_x}\Delta t} \right)$ 
Pressure P = $F\over A$ = rate of momentum transfer per unit area
P = $ {{m{v_x}\left( {nA{v_x}\Delta t} \right)/\Delta t} \over A} $ = $nm{v^2}_x$ 
all molecules in a gas do not have the same velocity; there is a distribution in velocities. The above equation therefore, stands for pressure due to the group of molecules with speed v_x in the x-direction and n stands for the number density of that group of molecules. The total pressure due to all groups 
P = $ nm\overline {v_x^2} $

Q15. The average kinetic energy of a molecule:
    (A) is inversely proportional to the molecular mass of the gas
    (B) is inversely proportional to the absolute temperature of the gas
    (C) is proportional to the absolute temperature of the gas   <-- CORRECT
    (D) is not dependent on the absolute temperature of the gas
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $E = {3 \over 2}kT$ 
E $\propto$ T

Q1. According to the law of equipartition of energy, the total energy is equally distributed in all possible energy modes, with each mode having average energy equal to:
    (A) $\frac{3}{4}$ k_B T
    (B) k_BT
    (C) $\frac{1}{4}$ k_B T
    (D) $\frac{1}{2}$k_BT   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The law of equipartition of energy states that for any dynamical system in thermal equilibrium, the total energy is equally divided among the degrees of freedom. The energy associated with a molecule for one degree of freedom is $\frac{1}{2}{\text{ }}{{\text{k}}_{\text{B}}}{\text{T}}$

Q2. The number of degrees of freedom a diatomic molecule is:
    (A) 5   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 3
    (C) 6
    (D) 4
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Degrees of freedom of a system refers to the possible independent motions a system can have.
the total degrees of freedom describing the motion of a diatomic molecule is 5.
3 for translation and 2 for rotation

Q3. The number of degrees of freedom a monoatomic molecule have:
    (A) 1
    (B) 3   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 4
    (D) 2
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Degrees of freedom of a system refers to the possible independent motions a system can have.
Monoatomic gas molecule can have 3 independent motion and hence have 3 degrees of freedom (all are translational).

Q4. The molar specific heat at constant volume, C_v for monatomic gases is:
    (A) $\frac{3}{2}$R   <-- CORRECT
    (B) R
    (C) $\frac{7}{2}$R
    (D) $\frac{5}{2}$R
    Answer: A
    Explanation: C_V = $\frac{1}{2}Rf$ 
for monatomic gases f = 3
C_V = $\frac{3}{2}R$

Q5. The molar specific heat at constant volume, C_v for diatomic gases is:
    (A) $\frac{3}{2}$R
    (B) $\frac{5}{2}$R   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac{7}{2}$R
    (D) R
    Answer: B
    Explanation: C_V =$ \frac{1}{2}$ Rf 
for diatomic gases f = 5
C_V = $\frac{5}{2}R$

Q6. For a rope of yield strength S_y loaded in tension with weight Mg the minimum area A of the rope should be:
    (A) $\mathrm{A} \geq \frac{M g}{\mathrm{~S}_{\mathrm{y}}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) A $ \geq $ $\frac{M g}{2 S_y}$
    (C) A $ \geq $ $\frac{M g}{3 S_y}$
    (D) A $ \geq $ MgS_y
    Answer: A
    Explanation: We know that Yield strength times the Area gives the weight of the body.
As Yield strength = $\frac{\text { weight of body }}{\text { area }}$
For safety purposes
Yield strength $ \geq $ $\frac{\text { weight of body }}{\text { area }}$
${{\rm{S}}_{\rm{y}}}$ $ \geq $ $\frac{Mg}{A}$
A $ \geq $ $\frac{Mg}{{\rm{S}}_{\rm{y}}}$

Q7. Mean free path is the:
    (A) minimum distance between collisions
    (B) (maximum distance + minimum distance )/ 2 between collisions
    (C) average distance between collisions   <-- CORRECT
    (D) maximum distance between collisions
    Answer: C
    Explanation: the mean free path is the average distance traveled by a moving particle (such as an atom, a molecule, a photon) between successive impacts (collisions), which modify its direction or energy or other particle properties.

Q8. An approximate equation for calculating the mean free path is:
    (A) $\lambda=\frac{1}{n d^2}$
    (B) $\lambda=\frac{1}{n \pi d^3}$
    (C) $\lambda = \frac{1}{{n\pi {d^2}}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\lambda=\frac{1}{\pi d^2}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: If the molecules have diameter d, then the effective cross-section for a collision can be A = $ \pi {d^2}$ 
l = distance travelled
n = no. of molecule per unit volume
V = volume of interaction
The mean free path could be taken as the length of the path divided by the number of collisions.
mean free path
$\lambda=\frac{l}{V \times n}=\frac{l}{(A l) \times n}=\frac{\bar{v} t}{\left(\pi d^2 \times \bar{v} t\right) \times n}$
$\lambda=\frac{1}{\pi d^2 n}$

Q9. Estimate the fraction of molecular volume to the actual volume occupied by oxygen gas at STP. Take the diameter of an oxygen molecule to be 3 $\mathop A\limits^0 $.
    (A) 3.8 $ \times $ 10^‚àí4   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 5 $ \times $ 10^‚àí4
    (C) 3.5 $ \times $ 10^‚àí4
    (D) 4.5 $ \times $ 10^‚àí4
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Radius = $1.5 \mathop A\limits^o $ 
molecular volume of oxygen 
$V = {N_A} \times {4 \over 3}\pi {r^3} $ = 6.023 $\times$ 10^23 $\times$ $4\over 3$ $\times$ 3.14 $\times$(1.5 $\times$10^‚àí8)^3 = 8.51cm^3
Actual volume of oxygen at STP = 22400cm^3
ratio of molecular volume to the actual volume is given by = ${{8.51} \over {22400}} = 3.8 \times {10^{ - 4}}$

Q10. Molar volume is the volume occupied by 1 mol of any (ideal) gas at standard temperature and pressure (STP : 1 atmospheric pressure, 0 ^oC). The value of Molar volume is:
    (A) 22.4 litres   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 20.4 litres
    (C) 23.7 liters
    (D) 24.4 litres
    Answer: A
    Explanation: PV = nRT
$V $ = $ {{nRT} \over P} = {{1 \times 8.31 \times 273} \over {1.01 \times {{10}^5}}} $ = 0.02224m^3 = 22.4lit

Q11. The figure shows a plot of $\frac{PV}{T}$versus P for 1.00 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 kg of oxygen gas at two different temperatures. The dotted plot corresponds to
    (A) ‚Äòideal‚Äô gas behavior   <-- CORRECT
    (B) ‚Äòreal‚Äô gas behavior
    (C) ‚Äòsuper heated‚Äô gas behavior
    (D) ‚Äòsuper cooled‚Äô gas behavior
    Answer: A
    Explanation: For 1 mole of ideal gas, according to ideal gas equation‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã
PV = RT
$\frac{PV}{T}$ = RT = const.
Hence the graph must be with zero slopes. So that dotted line show ‚Äòideal‚Äô gas behaviour and the curved line shows deviation from ‚Äòideal‚Äô gas behaviour.

Q12. The figure shows a plot of $\frac{PV}{T}$ versus P for 1.00$ \times $ 10^‚àí3 kg of oxygen gas at two different temperatures. Comparing T_1 and T_2.
    (A) T_1 > T_2   <-- CORRECT
    (B) T_1 = T_2
    (C) T_1 < T_2
    (D) T_1 $\ge$ T_2
    Answer: A
    Explanation: For 1 mole of an ideal gas, according to the ideal gas equation
‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚ÄãPV = RT
$\frac{PV}{T}$ = R = constant
Hence the graph must be with zero slopes. So that dotted line show ‚Äòideal‚Äô gas behaviour and the curved line show deviation from ‚Äòideal‚Äô gas behaviour.
A real gas behaves as an ideal gas at high temperature. Temperature T_1 is close to the dotted line. 
so that T_1 > T_2

Q13. The figure shows a plot of $\frac{PV}{T}$ versus P for 1.00$ \times $ 10^‚àí3 kg of oxygen gas at two different temperatures. What is the value of$\frac{PV}{T}$ where the curves meet on the y-axis?
    (A) 0.26 J K^‚àí1   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 0.22 JK^-1 ‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã
    (C) 0.24 JK^-1‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã
    (D) 0.28 JK^-1 ‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã
    Answer: A
    Explanation: PV = nRT
$\frac{PV}{T}$ = nR = $\frac{1}{32}$ $\times$ 8.314 = 0.26 JK^-1
Hence the value of $\frac{PV}{T}$ where the curves meet on the y-axis is 0.26 J K^‚àí1

Q14. An oxygen cylinder of volume 30 liters has an initial gauge pressure of 15 atm and a temperature of 27 ^oC. After some oxygen is withdrawn from the cylinder, the gauge pressure drops to 11 atm and its temperature drops to 17 ^oC. Estimate the mass of oxygen taken out of the cylinder (R = 8.31 J mol^-1 K^-1, the molecular mass of O_2 = 32 u).
    (A) 0.16 kg
    (B) 0.13 kg   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 0.12 kg
    (D) 0.10 kg
    Answer: B
    Explanation: V_1 = 30lit = 30 $\times$ 10^‚àí3m^3
P_1 = 15atm = 15 $\times$ 1.01 $\times$ 10^5 = 15.15 $\times$ 10^5Pa
T_1 = 27^oC = 300K
P_1V_1 = n_1RT_1
$n_{1}=\frac{P_{1} V_{1}}{R T_{1}}=\frac{15.15 \times 10^{5} \times 30 \times 10^{-3}}{8.31 \times 300}$ = 18.276
$n_{1}=\frac{m_{1}}{M}$
m_1 = n_1M = 18.276 $\times$ 32 = 584.84gm
When some oxygen is withdrawn from the cylinder 
V_2 = 30lit = 30 $\times$ 10^‚àí3m^3
P_1 = 11atm = 11 $\times$ 1.01 $\times$ 10^5 = 11.11 $\times$ 10^5Pa
T_1 = 17^oC = 300K
P_2V_2 = n_2RT_2
$n_{2}=\frac{P_{2} V_{2}}{R T_{2}}=\frac{11.11 \times 10^{5} \times 30 \times 10^{-3}}{8.31 \times 290}$ = 13.86
${n_2} = {{{m_2}} \over M}$ = 
m_2 = n_2M = 13.86 $\times$ 32 = 453.1gm
the mass of oxygen taken out of the cylinder = m_1 ‚àí m_2 = 584.84 ‚àí 453.1 = 131.74gm = 0.13Kg.

Q15. An air bubble of volume 1.0 cm^3 rises from the bottom of a lake 40 m deep at a temperature of 12 ^oC. To what volume does it grow when it reaches the surface, which is at a temperature of 35 ^oC?
    (A) 5.3 $ \times $ 10^‚àí6 m^3   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 5.5 $ \times $ 10^‚àí6 m^3
    (C) 5.1 $ \times $ 10^‚àí6 m^3
    (D) 4.9 $ \times $ 10^‚àí6 m^3
    Answer: A
    Explanation: P_1 = P_2 +h$\rho$g
V_1 = 1.0cm^3
h = 40m
T_1 = 35^oC = 308K , density of water is = 10^3 kg m^-3
Pressure at surface P_2 = 1atm = 1.013$\times$ 10^5Pa
Pressure at depth of 40 meter = P_1 = 1atm + h$\rho$g = (1.013 $\times$ 10^5) + 40 $\times$ 10^3 $\times$ 9.8 = 493300Pa
${{{P_1}{V_1}} \over {{T_1}}} = {{{P_2}{V_2}} \over {{T_2}}} $
${V_2} = {{{P_1}{V_1}{T_2}} \over {{P_2}{T_1}}} $ = $ {{493300 \times 1.0 \times {{10}^{ - 6}} \times 308} \over {1.013 \times {{10}^5} \times 285}} $ = 5.3 $\times$ 10^‚àí6m^3.
