Q1. Estimate the total number of air molecules (inclusive of oxygen, nitrogen, water vapor and other constituents) in a room of capacity 25.0 m^3 at a temperature of 27 ^oC and 1 atm pressure.
    (A) 6.50 $ \times $ 10^26
    (B) 6.10 $ \times $ 10^26   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 6.30 $ \times $ 10^26
    (D) 6.60 $ \times $ 10^26
    Answer: B
    Explanation: PV = NkT
N = $\frac{P V}{k T}$ = $\frac{1.01 \times 10^{5} \times 25}{1.38 \times 10^{-23} \times 300}$ = 6.1 $\times$ 10^26

Q2. Estimate the average thermal energy of a helium atom at room temperature (27 ^oC)
    (A) 6.0 $ \times $ 10^‚àí21 J
    (B) 6.2 $ \times $ 10^‚àí21 J   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 6.4 $ \times $ 10^‚àí21 J
    (D) 5.7 $ \times $ 10^‚àí21 J
    Answer: B
    Explanation: E = $ {3 \over 2}kT $ = $3\over 2$ $\times$ 1.38 $\times$10^‚àí23 $\times$ 300 = 6.2 $\times$ 10^‚àí21J
k = Boltzmann constant

Q3. Estimate the average thermal energy of a helium atom at the temperature on the surface of the Sun (6000 K)
    (A) 1.35 $ \times $ 10^‚àí19 J
    (B) 1.28 $ \times $ 10^‚àí19 J
    (C) 1.24 $ \times $ 10^‚àí19 J   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 1.20 $ \times $ 10^‚àí19 J
    Answer: C
    Explanation: E = $ {3 \over 2}kT $ = 32 $\times$ 1.38 $\times$ 10^‚àí23 $\times$ 6000 = 1.24 $\times$ 10^‚àí19J
k = boltzman constant

Q4. Estimate the average thermal energy of a helium atom the temperature of 10 million Kelvin (the typical core temperature in the case of a star).
    (A) 2.1 $ \times $ 10^‚àí16 J   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 2.3 $ \times $ 10^‚àí16 J
    (C) 2.0 $ \times $ 10^‚àí16 J
    (D) 1.9 $ \times $ 10^‚àí16 J
    Answer: A
    Explanation: E = $3\over2$kT = $3\over2$ $\times$ 1.38 $\times$ 10^‚àí23 $\times$ 10 $\times$ 10^6 = 2.07 $\times$ 10^‚àí16J 
k = Boltzmann constant

Q5. Three vessels of equal capacity have gases at the same temperature and pressure. The first vessel contains neon (monatomic), the second contains chlorine (diatomic), and the third contains uranium hexafluoride (polyatomic). The number of molecules:
    (A) is the greatest in the vessel with neon
    (B) is the greatest in the vessel with chlorine
    (C) is the same in all three vessels   <-- CORRECT
    (D) is the greatest in the vessel with uranium hexafluoride
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Avogadro's law states that "equal volumes of all gases, at the same temperature and pressure, have the same number of molecules".

Q6. Three vessels of equal capacity have gases at the same temperature and pressure. The first vessel contains neon (monatomic), the second contains chlorine (diatomic), and the third contains uranium hexafluoride (polyatomic). In which case is v_rms the largest?
    (A) v_rms is the same in all vessels.
    (B) v_rms is largest for chlorine.
    (C) v_rms is largest for uranium hexafluoride.
    (D) v_rms is the largest for neon.   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: ${v_{rms}} = \sqrt {{{3RT} \over M}}$
${v_{rms}} \propto {1 \over {\sqrt M }}$ 
Neon has smaller molecular wt in comparison to chlorine and uranium hexafluoride. So that v_rms s largest for neon.

Q7. At what temperature is the root mean square speed of an atom in an argon gas cylinder equal to the rms speed of a helium gas atom at ‚Äì 20 ^oC? (Atomic mass of Ar = 39.9 u, of He = 4.0 u).
    (A) 2.77 $ \times $ 10^3 K
    (B) 2.63 $ \times $ 10^3 K
    (C) 2.52 $ \times $ 10^3 K   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 2.26 $ \times $ 10^3 K
    Answer: C
    Explanation: v_Ar = v_He
$\sqrt{\frac{3 R T}{39.9}}=\sqrt{\frac{3 R \times 253}{4}}$
T = $ {{39.9 \times 253} \over 4} $ = 2.52 $\times$ 10^3K

Q8. Estimate the mean free path and collision frequency of a nitrogen molecule in a cylinder containing nitrogen at 2.0 atm and temperature 17^oC. Take the radius of a nitrogen molecule to be roughly 1.0 $\mathop A\limits^0 $.
    (A) 2.0 $ \times $ 10^-7 m., 5.9 $ \times $ 10^2 s^-1.
    (B) 1.5 $ \times $ 10^-7 m., 5.6 $ \times $ 10^9 s^-1.
    (C) 1.1 $ \times $ 10^-7 m., 4.6 $ \times $ 10^9 s^-1.   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 2.5 $ \times $ 10^-7 m., 6.1 $ \times $ 10^2 s^-1.
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $\lambda = {1 \over {\sqrt 2 \pi {d^2}{n_V}}}$ 
n_V = no.of molecule per unit volume ($N\over V$)
PV = NkT
${n_V} = {N \over V} = {P \over {kT}}$
$\lambda = {{kT} \over {\sqrt 2 \pi {d^2}P}} $ = $ {{1.38 \times {{10}^{ - 23}} \times 290} \over {1.41 \times 3.14 \times {{\left( {2 \times {{10}^{ - 10}}} \right)}^2} \times \left( {2 \times 1.01 \times {{10}^5}} \right)}}$
$\lambda = 1.11 \times {10^{ - 7}}m$ 
collision frequency${{{v_{rms}}} \over \lambda }$ 
${v_{rms}} = \sqrt {{{3RT} \over M}} = \sqrt {{{3 \times 8.31 \times 290} \over {28 \times {{10}^{ - 3}}}}} $ = 508.26m/sec 
collision frequency $ = {{508.26} \over {1.11 \times {{10}^{ - 7}}}} $ = 4.58 $\times$ 10^9s^‚àí1

Q9. In constructing a large mobile, an artist hangs an aluminum sphere of mass 6.0 kg from a vertical steel wire 0.50 m long and 2.5 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 cm^2 in a cross-sectional area. On the bottom of the sphere, he attaches a similar steel wire, from which he hangs a brass cube of mass 10.0 kg. Compute the tensile strain?
    (A) 3.2 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 upper, 2.3 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 lower
    (B) 3.3 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 upper, 2.4 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 lower
    (C) 3.4 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 upper, 2.5 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 lower
    (D) 3.1 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 upper, 2.0 $ \times $ 10^‚àí3 lower   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Given mass of aluminium sphere m_sph = 6.0 KG
length of steel wire L_steel = 0.50 m area of steel wire A_steel = 2.5 $\times$ 10^-3 cm^2
mass of cube m_cube = 10 KG
net restoring force in lower wire i.e. tension T_2 will be equal to weight
of cube i.e. T_2 = m_cube = 10g
net restoring force in upper wire i.e. tension T_1
T_1 = m_sph + T_2 = 6g + 10g = 16g
standard value for young modulus of steel y = 2 $\times$ 10^11 N/m^2
y = $\frac{\text { tensile stress }}{\text { tensile strain }}$ for lower wire
$\operatorname{strain}_{l w}=\frac{\text {tensile stress}}{y}=\frac{10 \times 9.8}{2.5 \times 10^{-3} \times 10^{-4} \times 2.0 \times 10^{11}}$
$\text {strain}_{l w} = 1.96 \times 10^{-3} \approx 2.0 \times 10^{-3} $ $\rightarrow$(1)
now for upper wire
$\operatorname{strain}_{u p} = \frac{\text {tensile stress}}{y} = \frac{16 \times 9.8}{2.5 \times 10^{-3} \times 10^{-4} \times 2.0 \times 10^{11}}$
strain_up = 3.14 $\times$ 10^‚àí3 $\Rightarrow$ (2)

Q10. A tank used for filling helium balloons has a volume of 0.300 m^3 and contains 2.00 mol of helium gas at 20.0 ^oC. Assuming that the helium behaves like an ideal gas, What is the average kinetic energy per molecule?
    (A) 6.28 $ \times $ 10^-21 J
    (B) 5.53 $ \times $ 10^-21 J
    (C) 6.95 $ \times $ 10^-21 J
    (D) 6.07 $ \times $ 10^-21 J   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: E = $3\over 2$kT = $3\over 2$ $\times$ 1.38 $\times$ 10^‚àí23 $\times$ 293 = 6.07 $\times$ 10^‚àí21J

Q11. Air at 20.0 ^oC in the cylinder of a diesel engine is compressed from an initial pressure of 1.00 atm and a volume of 800.0 cm^3 to a volume of 60.0 cm^3. Assume that air behaves as an ideal gas with $\gamma $ = 1.4 and that the compression is adiabatic. Find the final pressure of the air.
    (A) 38.6 atm
    (B) 39.6 atm
    (C) 36.6 atm
    (D) 37.6 atm   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $P_{1} V_{1}^{\gamma}=P_{2} V_{2}^{\gamma}$
P_2 = $P_{1}\left(\frac{V_{1}}{V_{2}}\right)^{\gamma}=1 \times\left(\frac{800}{60}\right)^{1.4}$ = 37.6atm

Q12. Air at 20.0$^\circ {\rm{C}}$ in the cylinder of a diesel engine is compressed from an initial pressure of 1.00 atm and a volume of 800.0 cm^3 to a volume of 60.0 cm^3. Assume that air behaves as an ideal gas with $\gamma = 1.4$ and that the compression is adiabatic. Find the final temperature of the air.
    (A) 826 K   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 898 K
    (C) 679 K
    (D) 765 K
    Answer: A
    Explanation: T_1 is = 20 ^0C = 20 +273 = 293 K
$T_{1} V_{1}^{\gamma-1}=T_{2} V_{2}^{\gamma-1}$
T_2 = $T_{1}\left(\frac{V_{1}}{V_{2}}\right)^{\gamma-1}=293 \times\left(\frac{800}{60}\right)^{1.4-1}$ = 826 K

Q13. Nine particles have speeds of 5.00, 8.00, 12.0, 12.0, 12.0, 14.0, 14.0, 17.0, and 20.0 m/s. find the particles‚Äô average speed.
    (A) 8.7 m/s
    (B) 12.7 m/s   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 16.7 m/s
    (D) 10.7 m/s
    Answer: B
    Explanation: v_av = $ {{\sum v } \over n} $$= {{5.0 + 8.0 + 12.0 + 12.0 + 12.0 + 14.0 + 14.0 + 17.0 + 20.0} \over 9} $
v_av = 12.7m/sec

Q14. Nine particles have speeds of 5.00, 8.00, 12.0, 12.0, 12.0, 14.0, 14.0, 17.0, and 20.0 m/s. What is the rms speed?
    (A) 12.3 m/s
    (B) 13.3 m/s   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 14.7 m/s
    (D) 9.35 m/s
    Answer: B
    Explanation: ${v_{rms}} = \sqrt {{{{v_1}^2 + {v_2}^2 + {v_3}^2 + .......{v_n}^2} \over n}}$
${v_{rms}} $ = $ \sqrt {{{25 + 64 + 144 + 144 + 144 + 196 + 196 + 289 + 400} \over 9}} $ = $ \sqrt {{{1602} \over 9}}$
v_rms = 13.3 m/sec

Q15. Approximate the air around you as a collection of nitrogen molecules, each of which has a diameter of 2.00 $ \times $ 10^-10 m. How far does a typical molecule move before it collides with another molecule?
    (A) 2.25 $ \times $ 10^-7 m   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 2.25 $ \times $ 10^-60 m
    (C) 3.16 $ \times $ 10^-70 m
    (D) 2.52 $ \times $ 10^-70 m
    Answer: A
    Explanation: no. of molecule per unit volume 
n_V = ${N \over V} = {P \over {kT}} = {{1.01 \times {{10}^5}} \over {1.38 \times {{10}^{ - 23}} \times 293}} $ = 2.5 $\times $ 10^25
mean free path 
$\lambda = {1 \over {\sqrt 2 \pi {d^2}{n_V}}} = {1 \over {1.41 \times 3.14 \times {{\left( {2 \times {{10}^{ - 10}}} \right)}^2} \times 2.5 \times {{10}^{25}}}} $ = 2.25 $\times $ 10^‚àí7m

Q1. Approximate the air around you as a collection of nitrogen molecules, each of which has a diameter of 2.00 $ \times $ 10^‚àí10 m. On average, how frequently does one molecule collide with another at 298K?
    (A) 1.98 $ \times $ 10^9 /s
    (B) 3.53 $ \times $ 10^9 /s
    (C) 1.32 $ \times $ 10^9 /s
    (D) 2.28 $ \times $ 10^9 / s   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: collision frequency $ = {1 \over \tau }$ = $ {{{v_{rms}}} \over \lambda }$ 
no. of molecule per unit volume 
n_V = $ {N \over V} = {P \over {kT}} = {{1.013 \times {{10}^5}} \over {1.38 \times {{10}^{ - 23}} \times 293}} $ = 2.5 $\times$ 10^25
mean free path 
$\lambda $ = $ {1 \over {\sqrt 2 \pi {d^2}{n_V}}} = {1 \over {1.41 \times 3.14 \times {{\left( {2 \times {{10}^{ - 10}}} \right)}^2} \times 2.5 \times {{10}^{25}}}} $ = 2.25 $\times$ 10^‚àí7m
${v_{rms}} = \sqrt {{{3RT} \over M}} = \sqrt {{{3 \times 8.31 \times 298} \over {28 \times {{10}^{ - 3}}}}} $= 515m/sec
 hence, collision frequency is given by $ = {1 \over \tau } = {{{v_{rms}}} \over \lambda } = {{515} \over {2.25 \times {{10}^{ - 7}}}} $ = 2.28 $\times$ 10^9/sec

Q2. In a 30.0-s interval, 500 hailstones strike a glass window with an area of 0.600 m^2 at an angle of 45.0^o to the window surface. Each hailstone has a mass of 5.00 g and a speed of 8.00 m/s. If the collisions are elastic, what are the average force and pressure on the window?
    (A) 0.978 N
    (B) 0.943 N   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 1.043 N
    (D) 0.913 N
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $F = 500 \times {{\Delta p} \over {\Delta t}} $
$= 500 \times {{2mv\cos 45^\circ } \over {\Delta t}} $
$= {{500 \times 2 \times 5 \times {{10}^{ - 3}} \times 8 \times 1} \over {30 \times 1.41}} $
F = 0.943N

Q3. In a 30.0-s interval, 500 hailstones strike a glass window with an area of 0.600 m^2 at an angle of 45.0^o to the window surface. Each hailstone has a mass of 5.00 g and a speed of 8.00 m/s. If the collisions are elastic, what is the pressure on the window?
    (A) 1.97 Pa
    (B) 1.57 Pa   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 1.32 Pa
    (D) 1.13 Pa
    Answer: B
    Explanation: $ P = {F \over A}$
$F = 500 \times {{\Delta p} \over {\Delta t}} $
$= 500 \times {{2mv\cos 45^\circ } \over {\Delta t}} $
$= {{500 \times 2 \times 5 \times {{10}^{ - 3}} \times 8 \times 1} \over {30 \times 1.41}}$
A = 0.6 m^2
P = $ {{500 \times 2 \times 5 \times {{10}^{ - 3}} \times 8 \times 1} \over {30 \times 1.41 \times 0.6}} $ = 1.57Pa

Q4. A 14.5 kg mass, fastened to the end of a steel wire of unstretched length 1.0 m, is whirled in a vertical circle with an angular velocity of 2 rev/s at the bottom of the circle. The cross-sectional area of the wire is 0.065 cm^2. Calculate the elongation of the wire when the mass is at the lowest point of its path.
    (A) 0.8 mm
    (B) 1.4 mm
    (C) 1.1 mm
    (D) 1.9 mm   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: given mass m = 14.5 kg
length of steel wire L = 1.0m
angular velocity $\omega$ = 2rev/s $\Rightarrow$ 2 $\times$ 2$\pi$rad/s = 4$\pi$rad/s cross sectional area of steel wire = 0.065cm^2 = 0.065 $\times$10^‚àí4m^2
let us consider $\Delta$L be the elongation in steel wire when mass is at lowest point during its motion in vertical circle and T be the tension in wire apply in g_net force = centripetal force
T ‚àí mg = m$\omega$^2r
T = (14.5 $\times$ 9.8) + 14.5 $\times$ (4$\pi$)^2 $\times$ 1
T = 2429.5N now young modulus of steel wire y = 2.0 $\times$ 10^11pa
also $\mathrm{y}=\frac{\mathrm{stress}}{\operatorname{strain}}=\frac{\mathrm{T} \times \mathrm{L}}{\mathrm{A} \times \Delta \mathrm{L}}$
$\Delta \mathrm{L}=\frac{\mathrm{T} \times \mathrm{L}}{\mathrm{A} \times \mathrm{y}}=\frac{2429.5 \times 1.0}{0.065 \times 10^{-4} \times 2.0 \times 10^{11}}$
$\Delta$L = 1.9 $\times$ 10^‚àí3m = 1.9mm

Q5. A spherical balloon with a volume of 4 .0 x 10^3 cm^3 contains helium at an (inside) pressure of 1.20 $ \times $ 10^5 Pa. How many moles of helium are in the balloon if each helium atom has an average kinetic energy of 3.60 $ \times $ 10^-22 J?
    (A) 3.12 mol
    (B) 3.42 mol
    (C) 3.32 mol   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 3.82 mol
    Answer: C
    Explanation: We know that ,$P = {2 \over 3}{N \over V}\left( {\bar K} \right)$
$N = {3 \over 2}{{PV} \over {\left( {\bar K} \right)}} = {3 \over 2} \times {{\left( {1.20 \times {{10}^5}} \right)\left( {4.00 \times {{10}^{ - 3}}} \right)} \over {3.60 \times {{10}^{ - 22}}}} $ = 2 $\times$ 10^24
n = $ {N \over {{N_A}}} = {{2 \times {{10}^{24}}} \over {6.023 \times {{10}^{23}}}} $ = 3.32mole

Q6. How many atoms of helium gas fill a balloon of diameter 30.0 cm at 20.0^oC and 1.00 atm?
    (A) 3.33 $ \times $ 10^23 atoms
    (B) 3.13 $ \times $ 10^23 atoms
    (C) 3.53 $ \times $ 10^23 atoms   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 3.73 $ \times $ 10^23 atoms
    Answer: C
    Explanation: PV = NkT
N = $\frac{P V}{k T}$ = $\frac{1.01 \times 10^{5} \times\left(\frac{4}{3} \times 3.14 \times(0.15)^{3}\right)}{1.38 \times 10^{-23} \times 293}$ = 3.53 $\times$ 10^23

    (B) 5.79 $ \times $ 10^‚àí21 J

Q8. What is the root-mean-square speed of helium atoms in a balloon of diameter 30.0 cm at 20.0^oC and 1.00 atm?
    (A) 1.49 km/s
    (B) 1.15 km/s
    (C) 1.57 km/s
    (D) 1.35 km/s   <-- CORRECT

Q9. A cylinder contains a mixture of helium and argon gas in equilibrium at 150^oC. What is the average kinetic energy for each type of gas molecule?
    (A) 3.76 $\times$ 10^‚àí21 J helium and 5.76 $\times$10^‚àí21 J argon
    (B) 8.76 $\times$ 10^‚àí21 J both   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 2.76 $\times$ 10^‚àí21 J helium and 6.76 $\times$ 10^‚àí21 J argon
    (D) 4.76 $\times$ 10^‚àí21 J helium and 5.76 $\times$ 10^‚àí21 J argon
    Answer: B

Q10. Anvils made of single crystals of diamond, with the shape as shown in Figure, are used to investigate the behaviour of materials under very high pressures. Flat faces at the narrow end of the anvil have a diameter of 0.50 mm, and the wide ends are subjected to a compression force of 50,000 N. What is the pressure at the tip of the anvil?
    (A) 2.1 $ \times $ 10^11 Pa
    (B) 3.2 $ \times $ 10^11 Pa
    (C) 2.5 $ \times $ 10^11 Pa   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 2.9 $ \times $ 10^11 Pa
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Given compression force F = 50000 N
Radius will be r = $\frac{diameter}{2}$ = $\frac{0.50}{2}$ = 0.25 mm = 0.25 $\times$ 10^-3 m
Area at which compression is applied A = $\pi$r^2 = 3.14 $\times$ (0.25 $\times$ 10^-3)^2 = 0.2 $\times$ 10^-6 m^2 
Pressure at the tip P = $\frac{Force}{area}$ = $\frac{50000}{0.2 \times 10^{-6}}$
P = 2.5 $\times$ 10^11 pa

Q11. Calculate the change in internal energy of 3.00 mol of helium gas when its temperature is increased by 2.00 K.
    (A) 95.0 J
    (B) 85.0 J
    (C) 65.0 J
    (D) 75.0 J   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Helium is a monoatomic gas.(C_V = 1.5R)
change in internal energy 
$\Delta U $ = $ n{C_V}\Delta T $ 
= 3 $\times$ 1.5 $\times$ 8.31 $\times$ 2 = 75J

Q12. One mole of hydrogen gas is heated at constant pressure from 300 K to 420 K. Calculate the energy transferred by heat to the gas
    (A) 3.86 kJ
    (B) 3.26 kJ
    (C) 3.49 kJ   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 3.66 kJ
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Hydrogen is a diatomic gas. (C_P = 3.5R)
energy transferred by heat to the gas
Q = $ n{C_P}\Delta T $ = 1 $\times$ 3.5 $\times$ 8.31 $\times$ 120 = 3490J = 3.49KJ

Q13. One mole of hydrogen gas is heated at constant pressure from 300 K to 420 K. Calculate the increase in its internal energy.
    (A) 2.65 kJ
    (B) 2.85 kJ
    (C) 2.15 kJ
    (D) 2.49 kJ   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Hydrogen is a diatomic gas. (C_V = 2.5R)
Change in internal energy 
$\Delta U = n{C_V}\Delta T $ = 1 $\times$ 2.5 $\times$ 8.31 $\times$ 120 = 2493J = 2.49KJ

Q14. One mole of hydrogen gas is heated at constant pressure from 300 K to 420 K. Calculate the work done by the gas.
    (A) 0.98kJ   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 1.27 kJ
    (C) 1.11 kJ
    (D) 0.93 kJ
    Answer: A
    Explanation: An isobaric process 
W = nR(T_F ‚àí T_I) = 1 $\times$ 8.31 $\times$ (420 ‚àí 300) = 0.98kJ

Q15. A house has well-insulated walls. It contains a volume of 100 m^3 of air at 300 K. Calculate the energy required to increase the temperature of this diatomic ideal gas by 1.00 ^oC. $\left(C_P=\frac{7}{2} R\right)$
    (A) 131 kJ
    (B) 118 kJ   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 101 kJ
    (D) 139 kJ
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Q = $ n{C_P}\Delta T $ = $\frac 7 2 Rn\Delta T $ = $\frac{7}{2}\left(\frac{P V}{T}\right) \Delta T$ (because, PV=nRT thus n = $\frac {PV}{ RT}$)
Q = $\frac{7}{2} \times\left(\frac{1.01 \times 10^5 \times 100}{300}\right) \times 1$ = 118KJ

Q1. A house has well-insulated walls. It contains a volume of 100 ${{\rm{m}}^{\rm{3}}}$ of air at 300 K. If the energy required to lift an object of mass m through a height of 2.00 m equals the energy required to increase the temperature of this air by 1.00^oC., what is the value of m? $\left(C_P=\frac{7}{2} R\right)$
    (A) 6.03 $ \times $ 10^3 kg   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 6.67 $ \times $ 10^3 kg
    (C) 5.87 $ \times $ 10^3 kg
    (D) 6.18 $ \times $ 10^3 kg
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Q = $n C_{P} \Delta T=\frac{7}{2} R n \Delta T=\frac{7}{2}\left(\frac{P V}{T}\right) \Delta T$
Q = $\frac{7}{2} \times\left(\frac{1.01 \times 10^{5} \times 100}{300}\right) \times 1$ = 118 K J
U = mgy
m = $\frac{U}{g y}=\frac{118 \times 10^{3}}{9.8 \times 2}$ = 6.03 $\times$ 10^3Kg

Q2. One mole of an ideal monatomic gas is at an initial temperature of 300 K. The gas undergoes an isovolumetric process, acquiring 500 J of energy by heat. It then undergoes an isobaric process, losing this same amount of energy by heat. Determine the new temperature of the gas:
    (A) 333 K
    (B) 316 K   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 301 K
    (D) 343 K
    Answer: B
    Explanation: For monoatomic gas C_V = 1.5R, C_P = 2.5R
At const volume, 
Q = 500 J
Q = nC_V$\Delta$T
500 = 1 $\times$ 1.5 $\times$ 8.31(T_1 - 300)
T_1 = 340K
At const pressure Q = 500 J
Q = nC_P$\Delta$T
500 = 1 $\times$ 1.5 $\times$ 8.31(340 - T_2)
T_2 = 316K

Q3. One mole of an ideal monatomic gas is at an initial temperature of 300 K. The gas undergoes an isovolumetric process, acquiring 500 J of energy by heat. It then undergoes an isobaric process, losing this same amount of energy by heat. Determine the work done on the gas.
    (A) 123 J
    (B) 333 J
    (C) 200 J   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 231 J
    Answer: C
    Explanation: At constant volume, 
Q = 500 J
Q = $n{C_P}\Delta T$
500 = 1 $\times$ 2.5 $\times$ 8.31$\Delta T$
$\Delta T$ = 24.06
work done on gas is W = nR$\Delta T$ = 1 $\times$ 8.31 $\times$ 24.06 = 200 J

Q4. 1 mole of a monoatomic gas is mixed with 3 moles of a diatomic gas. What is the molecular specific heat of the mixture at constant volume?
    (A) 22.6 J/mol K
    (B) 18.7 J / mol K   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 15.2 J/mol K
    (D) 12.5 J/mol K
    Answer: B
    Explanation: for monoatomic gas 
${C_{V_1}} = {3 \over 2}R$
 for diatomic gas
${C_{V_2}} = {5 \over 2}R $ 
from conservation of energy we get,
C_V = $ {{{n_1}{C_{{V_1}}} + {n_2}{C_{{V_2}}}} \over {{n_1} + {n_2}}} = {{\left( {1 \times 1.5R} \right) + \left( {3 \times 2.5R} \right)} \over {1 + 3}} = {9 \over 4}R$
${C_V} = {9 \over 4} \times 8.31 $ = 18.7J/mol K

Q5. Two moles of an ideal gas ($\gamma = 1.4$) expands slowly and adiabatically from a pressure of 5.00 atm and a volume of 12.0 L to a final volume of 30.0 L. What is the final pressure of the gas?
    (A) 1.09 atm
    (B) 1.19 atm
    (C) 1.39 atm   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 1.59 atm
    Answer: C
    Explanation: $P_{1} V_{1}^{\gamma}=P_{2} V_{2}^{\gamma}$
P_2 = $P_{1}\left(\frac{V_{1}}{V_{2}}\right)^{\gamma}=5 \times\left(\frac{12}{30}\right)^{1.4}$ = 5 $\times$ (0.4)^1.4 = 1.39 atm

Q6. Two moles of an ideal gas ($\gamma $ = 1.4) expands slowly and adiabatically from the pressure of 5.00 atm and a volume of 12.0 L to a final volume of 30.0 L. What are the initial and final temperatures?
    (A) 346 K, 243 K
    (B) 406 K, 273 K
    (C) 386 K, 263 K
    (D) 366 K, 253 K   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: ${T_i} = {{{P_i}{V_i}} \over {nR}} = {{\left( {5 \times 1.01 \times {{10}^5}} \right) \times \left( {12 \times {{10}^{ - 3}}} \right)} \over {2 \times 8.31}} $ = 366K
${P_i}{V_i}^\gamma = {P_f}{V_f}^\gamma $
${P_f} = {P_i}{\left( {{{{V_i}} \over {{V_f}}}} \right)^\gamma } = 5 \times {\left( {{{12} \over {30}}} \right)^{1.4}} $ = 5 $\times$ (0.4)^1.4 = 1.39atm
${T_f} = {{{P_f}{V_f}} \over {nR}} = {{\left( {1.39 \times 1.01 \times {{10}^5}} \right) \times \left( {30 \times {{10}^{ - 3}}} \right)} \over {2 \times 8.31}} $ = 253K

Q7. Two moles of an ideal gas ($\gamma = 1.4$) expands slowly and adiabatically from the pressure of 5.00 atm and a volume of 12.0 L to a final volume of 30.0 L. Find Q, W, and $\Delta {{\rm{E}}_{{\rm{int}}}}$.
    (A) 100 J, 4.88 kJ, -4.88 kJ
    (B) 10 J, 4.44 kJ, -4.44 kJ
    (C) 1 J, 4.22 kJ, -4.22 kJ
    (D) 0, 4.66 kJ, -4.66 kJ   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Process is adiabatic. so that Q = 0
${T_i} = {{{P_i}{V_i}} \over {nR}} = {{\left( {5 \times 1.01 \times {{10}^5}} \right) \times \left( {12 \times {{10}^{ - 3}}} \right)} \over {2 \times 8.31}} $ = 366K
$P_{1} V_{1}^{\gamma}=P_{2} V_{2}^{\gamma}$
$P_{2}=P_{1}\left(\frac{V_{1}}{V_{2}}\right)^{\gamma}=5 \times\left(\frac{12}{30}\right)^{1.4}$ = 5 $\times$ (0.4)^1.4 = 1.39atm
${T_f} = {{{P_f}{V_f}} \over {nR}} = {{\left( {1.39 \times 1.01 \times {{10}^5}} \right) \times \left( {30 \times {{10}^{ - 3}}} \right)} \over {2 \times 8.31}} $ = 253K
For the adiabatic process ; q=0 J
$\Delta U = n{C_V}\Delta T $ 
$\Delta U$= 2 $\times$ 2.5R(253 ‚àí 366) = 2 $\times$ 2.5 $\times$ 8.31 $\times$ 13 = ‚àí4.66KJ
From first law of thermodynamics ; Q = $\Delta$U + W
0 = ‚àí4.66 + W
W = 4.66KJ

Q8. The air in a thundercloud expands as it rises. If its initial temperature was 300 K, and if no energy is lost by thermal conduction on expansion, what is its temperature when the initial volume has doubled?${\gamma _{air}} $ = 1.41
    (A) 237 K
    (B) 257 K
    (C) 227 K   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 247 K
    Answer: C
    Explanation: ${{{T_1}} \over {{T_2}}} = {\left( {{{{V_2}} \over {{V_1}}}} \right)^{\gamma - 1}}$
${{300} \over {{T_2}}} = {\left( 2 \right)^{^{1.41 - 1}}}$
T_2 = 227K

Q9. Four liters of a diatomic ideal gas ($\gamma = 1.4$) confined to a cylinder is subject to a closed cycle. Initially, the gas is at 1.00 atm and at 300 K. First, its pressure is tripled under constant volume. Then, it expands adiabatically to its original pressure. Finally, the gas is compressed isobarically to its original volume. Determine the volume of the gas at the end of the adiabatic expansion.
    (A) 8.79 litres   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 8.23 litres
    (C) 8.55 litres
    (D) 8.97 litres
    Answer: A
    Explanation: $P_{1} V_{1}^{\gamma}=P_{2} V_{2}^{\gamma}$
$V_{2}^{\gamma}=\frac{P_{1}}{P_{2}} V_{1}^{\gamma}$
$V_{2}=\left(\frac{P_{1}}{P_{2}}\right)^{1 \over \gamma} V_{1}=\left(\frac{3}{1}\right)^{1 \over 1.4} \times 4$ = 8.79 lit

Q10. Four liters of a diatomic ideal gas ($\gamma = 1.4$) confined to a cylinder is subject to a closed cycle. Initially, the gas is at 1.00 atm and at 300 K. First, its pressure is tripled under constant volume. Then, it expands adiabatically to its original pressure. Finally, the gas is compressed isobarically to its original volume. Find the temperature of the gas at the start of the adiabatic expansion.
    (A) 984 K
    (B) 900 K   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 789 K
    (D) 865 K
    Answer: B
    Explanation: ${{{P_1}} \over {{P_2}}} = {{{T_1}} \over {{T_2}}}$
$\frac{1}{3}=\frac{300}{T_{2}}$
T_2 = 900K

Q11. Four liters of a diatomic ideal gas ($\gamma = 1.4$) confined to a cylinder is subject to a closed cycle. Initially, the gas is at 1.00 atm and at 300 K. First, its pressure is tripled under constant volume. Then, it expands adiabatically to its original pressure. Finally, the gas is compressed isobarically to its original volume. Find the temperature at the end of the cycle.
    (A) 276 K
    (B) 285 K
    (C) 332 K
    (D) 300 K   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: After Isochoric process
${{{P_1}} \over {{P_2}}} = {{{T_1}} \over {{T_2}}}$
${1 \over 3} = {{300} \over {{T_2}}}$
T_2 = 900K
after one whole cycle: T_1 = 300K

Q12. Four liters of a diatomic ideal gas ($\gamma $ = 1.4) confined to a cylinder is subject to a closed cycle. Initially, the gas is at 1.00 atm and at 300 K. First, its pressure is tripled under constant volume. Then, it expands adiabatically to its original pressure. Finally, the gas is compressed isobarically to its original volume. What was the network done for this cycle?
    (A) 376 J
    (B) 316 J
    (C) 327 J   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 356 J
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Work done in the isochoric process W_1 = 0
Work done in the adiabatic process
${W_2} = {1 \over {\gamma - 1}}\left( {{P_2}{V_2} - {P_3}{V_3}} \right)$ 
$P_{2} V_{2}^{\gamma}=P_{3} V_{3}^{\gamma}$
$V_{3}^{\gamma}=\frac{P_{2}}{P_{3}} V_{2}^{\gamma}$
V_3 = $\left(\frac{P_{2}}{P_{3}}\right)^{1 \over \gamma} V_{2}=\left(\frac{3}{1}\right)^{1 \over 1.4} \times 4$ = 8.79 litres
${W_2} = {1 \over {\gamma - 1}}\left( {{P_2}{V_2} - {P_3}{V_3}} \right) = {1 \over {1.4 - 1}} $ $\times$ (12 ‚àí 8.79) $\times$ 1.01 $\times$ 10^5 $\times$ 10^‚àí3 = 810J
Work done in the isochoric process 
W_3 = P$\Delta$V = 1 $\times$ 1.01 $\times$ 10^5 $\times$ (4 ‚àí 8.79) $\times$10^‚àí3 = ‚àí483J
Net work done for cycle
W = W_1 + W_2 + W_3 = 0 + 810 ‚àí 483 = 327J

Q13. Consider 2.00 mol of an ideal diatomic gas. Find the total heat capacity at constant volume and at constant pressure, if the molecules rotate but do not vibrate
    (A) 9.45 cal/K, 13.2 cal/K
    (B) 9.95 cal/K, 13.9 cal/K   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 9.65 cal/K, 13.5 cal/K
    (D) 9.85 cal/K, 13.7 cal/K
    Answer: B
    Explanation: for diatomic gas C_V = 2.5 R, C_P = 3.5 R
Heat capacity = mc = nC
at const. volume Heat capacity = nC_V = 2 $\times$ 2.5 $\times$ 1.986 = 9.95cal/K
at const. pressure Heat capacity = nC_P = 2 $\times$ 3.5 $\times$ 1.986 = 13.9cal/K

Q14. According to Atomic Hypothesis: little particles of atom
    (A) repel each other when they are at small distance apart, but repel upon being squeezed into one another
    (B) repel each other when they are at small distance apart, but attract upon being squeezed into one another
    (C) repel each other when they are at large distance apart, but attract upon being separated from one another
    (D) attract each other when they are at small distance apart, but repel upon being squeezed into one another   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: At room temperature (=300K) the noble gases are all in the gas phase, they are banging around and colliding into one another like little pool balls. At this temperature, when the atoms collide they appear to elastically bounce off of one another, but this bounce is actually a result of atomic repulsion. The atoms are traveling so fast and they approach each other so quickly that their momentum 'squeezes' them together until the atomic repulsion pushes them back apart.

Q15. The perfect gas equation can be written as:
    (A) PV = k_B T
    (B) PV = k_B N
    (C) P = k_B NT
    (D) PV = k_B NT   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: PV = nRT
PV = nk_BN_AT
R = k_BN_A, N = nN_A
PV = Nk_BT
n = no. of mole
${{\rm{k}}_{\rm{B}}}$= Boltzmann const
N = no. of molecule
N_A = Avogadro no.

Q1. The pendulum of a wall clock executes
    (A) an oscillatory motion   <-- CORRECT
    (B) a translatory motion
    (C) a circular motion
    (D) a rotary motion
    Answer: A
    Explanation: It executes simple harmonic motion because in this motion restoring force is directly proportional to the displacement towards the mean position at any time.

Q2. Oscillatory motion is best described by
    (A) a rotational motion about an axis
    (B) combination of translational motion and rotational motion
    (C) a zig zag motion about a straight line
    (D) periodic to and fro motion   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: In oscillatory motion object moves to and fro about mean position periodically or after a particular interval of time. All oscillatory motions are periodic in nature.

Q3. Periodic motion is described as the motion that
    (A) repeats itself at irregular intervals of time.
    (B) repeats itself at regular intervals of time.   <-- CORRECT
    (C) does not repeat itself at regular intervals of time.
    (D) changes at random intervals of time.
    Answer: B
    Explanation: A motion of a body that repeats itself at regular intervals of time on the same path like rotation of earth around sun in 365.25 days is known as periodic motion.

Q4. Period of a periodic motion is the ________ interval of time after which the motion is __________.
    (A) smallest, repeated   <-- CORRECT
    (B) average, repeated
    (C) largest, repeated
    (D) mean, not repeated
    Answer: A
    Explanation: The time period of oscillatory motion is the smallest interval of time for one complete oscillation. As when we apply the force on the bob, bob moves from mean position to extreme position then from extreme position to mean position and then again from mean to the extreme in opposite direction and at last come to mean position. The time taken by bob in this whole process is called period of oscillation.

Q5. Frequency of the periodic motion is
    (A) time taken to complete one oscillation
    (B) the reciprocal of the period of oscillation   <-- CORRECT
    (C) twice the period of oscillation
    (D) the reciprocal of the wave velocity
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Frequency is defined as number of oscillation completed by an oscillating body in unit time i.e. in one second during oscillatory motion.
 $\nu=\frac1T$

Q6. For any periodic motion with a period T and displacement f(t), then
    (A) f(t) = f(t + T - 1)
    (B) f (t) = f (2t + T)
    (C) f(t) = f(t + T)   <-- CORRECT
    (D) f (t) = f (t + 2T + 1)
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The periodic function is that function which repeats itself in equal interval of time or periods.
The function have same value for every 
f(t) = f(t + T) = f(t + nT)
e.g. Trigonimetric function sin(x) is periodic with periodicity of $2\mathrm\pi$

Q7. Any periodic function can be expressed as a superposition of:
    (A) polynomial functions of different time periods with suitable coefficients.
    (B) sine and cosine functions of fixed time periods with suitable coefficients.   <-- CORRECT
    (C) sine and cosine functions of increasing integral time period with suitable coefficients.
    (D) any trigonometric functions of real time periods with suitable coefficients.
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Because sine and cosine functions both are periodic in nature and according to Fourier theorem a" Fourier series" is a way to represent a function as the sum of simple sine waves. More formally, it decomposes any periodic function or periodic signal into the sum of a (possibly infinite) set of simple oscillating functions, namely sines and cosines, or in simple terms any periodic function can be expressed as sum or superposition of sine and cosine functions with fixed time periods.

Q8. Simple harmonic motion is a motion represented by
    (A) x (t) = A ($\omega {\rm{t}}$ + $\varphi $)
    (B) x (t) = A cos ($\omega {\rm{t}}$ + $\varphi $)   <-- CORRECT
    (C) x (t) = A exp ($\omega {\rm{t}}$ + $\varphi $)
    (D) x (t) = A log ($\omega {\rm{t}}$ + $\varphi $)
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Equation of simple harmonic motion is given by
$x(t) = A\cos (\omega t + \phi )$
Where x(t) is the displacement of the body at any instant of time, A is the amplitude of the body and
$ (\omega t + \phi )$ is the phase angle at the instant of time.

Q9. Simple harmonic motion is the projection of ___ of the circle in which the latter motion takes place.
    (A) Uniform circular motion on a diameter   <-- CORRECT
    (B) Uniform circular motion on a chord
    (C) Non uniform circular motion on a diameter
    (D) Non uniform circular motion on a chord
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Simple harmonic motion is the projection of uniform circular motion. In the above diagram P is the position of particle at at any instant of time. This motion follows the condition of SHM. Simple harmonic motion is a type of periodic motion or oscillation motion where the restoring force is directly proportional to the displacement and acts in the direction opposite to that of displacement,

Q10. For a particle executing simple harmonic motion represented by x(t) = A cos ($\omega {\rm{t}}$ + $\phi$), the the velocity v(t) is given by
    (A) v (t) = - $\omega {\rm{A}}$ cos ($\omega {\rm{t}}$ + $\phi $)
    (B) v(t) = - $\omega {\rm{A}}$ sin ($\omega {\rm{t}}$ + $\phi$)   <-- CORRECT
    (C) v(t) = - sint$A t$ ($\omega {\rm{t}}$ + $\phi $)
    (D) v (t) = - $\omega {\rm{A}}$$\varphi $ sin ($\omega {\rm{t}}$ + $\phi $ )
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Displacement, x(t) = A cos($\omega$t + $\phi$)
Velocity, v(t) = $\frac{dv}{dt}$ = $\frac{d[A \cos (‚Äã‚Äã\omega t+\phi)]}{d t}$
= $A \frac{d \cos (\omega t+\phi)}{d t}$ , (because A is constant)
= A(-$\omega$ sin ($\omega$t + $\phi$)) , $\left[\frac{d(\cos a x)}{d t}=-a \sin (a x)\right]$
= -$\omega$A sin ($\omega$t + $\phi$)

Q11. For a particle executing simple harmonic motion represented by x (t)= A cos ($\omega {\rm{t}}$ + $\varphi $) , the the acceleration a(t) is given by
    (A) a(t) = - 2 ${\omega ^{\rm{2}}}$x(t)
    (B) a(t) = - ${\omega ^{\rm{2}}}$x(t)   <-- CORRECT
    (C) a(t) = ${\omega ^{\rm{2}}}$x(t)
    (D) a(t) = - $\omega^2 v(t)$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Displacement of particle is given by
x (t) = A cos ($\omega {\rm{t}}$ + $\varphi $)
Then velocity,
$\begin{array}{l} v(t)= \frac{{dx}}{{dt}}\\ v(t) = A\frac{{d\cos (\omega t + \varphi )}}{{dt}}\\ v(t)= - A\;\omega \sin (\omega t + \varphi ) \end{array}$
Now,acceleration,
$a(t)=\frac{{dv}}{{dt}}$
$\begin{array}{l} a(t) =-\omega A \frac{{ \ d\sin (\omega t + \varphi )}}{{dt}} \\a(t)=-\omega^2A\,cos(\omega t+\varphi)\;\;, \;\;\;\;\;(\because\,x(t)=A\,cos(\omega t+\varphi)) \\ \therefore a(t) = - {\omega ^2}x(t) \end{array}$

Q12. For a particle of mass m executing simple harmonic motion represented by x (t) = A cos ($\omega {\rm{t }} + {\rm{ }}\varphi $) and a restoring force F = -kx, then the natural frequency is given by:
    (A) $\omega=\sqrt{\frac{k}{m}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\omega=\sqrt{\frac{2 k}{m}}$
    (C) $\omega=-\sqrt{\frac{k}{m}}$
    (D) $\omega=-\sqrt {{k}} $
    Answer: A
    Explanation: According to Newton's second law of motion
F = ma
= $-m \omega^{2} x$ .....(i) (Because a = $‚àí\omega^2x$, maximum acceleration of a particle executing S.H.M.)
F = -kx .....(ii) (Given)
Comparing (i) and (ii), we have
m$\omega^2$x = ‚àíkx
m$\omega^2$ = k
$\omega^2$ = $\frac{k}{m}$
$\omega$ = $\sqrt{\frac{k}{m}}$
where k is the force constant and $\omega $ is the natural frequency.

Q13. The spring restoring force F = ‚Äìkx is a conservative force, with associated potential energy.
    (A) U(x) = $\;\frac12kx^2$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) U(x) = $\frac32kx^2$
    (C) U(x) = kx^2
    (D) U(x) = $-\frac12kx^2$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Potential energy is 
U(x) = $-\int {{F.dx}}$
Now, F = ‚àíkx
Then, $U(x)=-\int F \cdot d x=-\int-k x \cdot d x$
$=k \int x d x$
$=k\left[\frac{x^{2}}{2}\right]$
$=\frac{1}{2} k x^{2}$
So, the potential energy is 
U(x) = $\;\frac12kx^2$

Q14. The total mechanical energy of a harmonic oscillator:
    (A) Decreases linearly with time
    (B) Increases linearly with time
    (C) Is independent of time   <-- CORRECT
    (D) Increases quadratically with time
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The total mechanical energy is the sum of kinetic energy(KE) and potential energy(PE), is constant.
E = KE + PE = $\frac{1}{2}$mv^2 + $\frac{1}{2}$kx^2 = Constant
Here the figure depicts the energy of a linear harmonic oscillator vs displacement, the horizontal axis represents the displacement x.
|From the graph, it is clear that total energy is constant with displacement and displacement is a function of time. So, total energy (mechanical energy) is independent of time.

Q15. The time period T of a spring system of mass m and spring constant k is given by:
    (A) ‚Äã‚Äã $T=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{m}{k}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $T=\pi \sqrt{\frac{k}{m}}$
    (C) $T=\sqrt{\frac{m}{k}}$
    (D) ‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã $T=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{k}{m}}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: If the restoring force of a vibrating or oscillatory system is proportional to the displacement
of the body from its equilibrium position and is directed opposite to the direction of displacement,
the motion of the system is simple harmonic and it is given by
x(t) = A$cos \omega t$
where A, the maximum value of the displacement, is called the amplitude of the motion. If
T is the time for one complete oscillation, then
x(t + T) = x(t)
$Acos\omega(t+T)=Acos\omega t$
$\omega T=2\pi$
or $T=\frac{2 \pi}{\omega}$
 As the angular frequency $\omega $ is given by $T=\frac{2 \pi}{\omega}$ and T = ${2\pi\over \omega }$ 
Then,time period of oscillation of mass m is given by
$T=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{m}{k}}$

Q1. The time period of a simple pendulum is given by ( l is the length of pendulum and g the acceleration due to gravity).
    (A) $T=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{l^2}{g}}$
    (B) $T=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{l}{g}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $T=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{l}{g^2}}$
    (D) ‚Äã $T=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{g}{l}}$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The figure shows the motion of a simple pendulum
The restoring force is $F=-mg\,sin \,\theta $ as the angle $\theta $ is small so $sin \, \theta\,\simeq \theta$ 
we have $F=-mg\, \,\theta $ from S H M and also F = -kx 
$\therefore mg\theta $ = kx
$\frac{m}{k}=\frac{g \theta}{x}$
as $l = \frac{\theta }{x}$
$\frac{m}{k}=\frac{g}{l}$
As $\omega=\sqrt{\frac{k}{m}}$
So $T=\frac{2 \pi}{\omega}=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{m}{k}}$
$=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{l}{g}}$

Q2. The time period of a physical pendulum of mass m and moment of inertia I is given by (l is the length of pendulum and g the acceleration due to gravity):
    (A) ‚Äã $T=\pi \sqrt{\frac{I}{m g l}}$
    (B) ‚Äã‚Äã $T=4 \pi \sqrt{\frac{I}{m g l}}$
    (C) $T=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{I}{m g l}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $T=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{l}{m g I}}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Torque;$\tau=I \alpha $, a= angular acceleration
$\tau = -mgl sin\theta$
$\therefore \alpha \simeq \frac{m g l \theta}{I}$ 
$\alpha=-\omega^2 \theta$
$\omega=\sqrt{\frac{m g l}{I}}$
So, Time period T is :
$\mathrm{T}=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{I}{m g l}}$

Q3. In a simple pendulum the restoring force is due to:
    (A) the tangential component of the gravitational force.   <-- CORRECT
    (B) the radial component of the tension in the string.
    (C) the radial component of the gravitational force.
    (D) the tangential component of the tension in the string.
    Answer: A
    Explanation: The tangential component of the gravitational field is F = ‚àímgsin$\theta$
that acts to the direction towards the mean position making it the restoring force for the oscillation.

Q4. The total mechanical energy of a damped system is:
    (A) Remains the same
    (B) Continuously increases
    (C) Continuously absorbed
    (D) Continuously dissipated   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The energy is dissipated as it decreases in the exponential form, making the amplitude to zero ultimately.
every pendulum in nonideal condition behaves like damped system, where damping force which is proportional to velocity is offered it by air resistance the fig shows the damped motion 
where the amplitude of the successive oscillation is damped by an exponential function of time.

Q5. Damping is due to:
    (A) Reactive forces like tension
    (B) Resistive forces like air drag, friction etc.   <-- CORRECT
    (C) Conservative forces like gravity
    (D) Restoring force
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Damping is proportional to the velocity of the mass through the medium, so it can be said that air drag, friction offers to damp.

Q6. In simple harmonic motion the damping force is:
    (A) Directly proportional to velocity   <-- CORRECT
    (B) Directly proportional to acceleration
    (C) Inversely proportional to acceleration
    (D) Inversely proportional to velocity
    Answer: A
    Explanation: In real oscillators, friction, or damping, slows the motion of the system. Due to frictional force, the velocity decreases in proportion to the acting frictional force. 
$F= -c \,{dx\over dt}$ 
where c is the damping constant.

Q7. The damped system differential equation is:
    (A) $m \frac{d^2 x}{d^2 t}$ + b + kx = 0
    (B) $m \frac{d^2 x}{d^2 t}$ + kx = 0
    (C) $m \frac{d^2 x}{d^2 t}+b \frac{d x}{d t}=0$
    (D) $m \frac{d^2 x}{d^2 t}+b \frac{d x}{d t}$ + kx = 0   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The forces on the system is restoring force F = -kx and the damping force is F = $-b v=-b \frac{d x}{d t}$ where 'k' is the force constant and 'b' is the damping constant
The net force is F= m*a which is equal to the sum of restoring force F = ‚àíkx and the damping force is $F=-b v=-b \frac{d x}{d t}$ 
$m \frac{d^2 x}{d t^2}=-k x-b \frac{d x}{d t}$
$\therefore m \frac{d^2 x}{d t^2}+k x+b \frac{d x}{d t}$ = 0

Q8. The damped natural frequency of a Damped system is:
    (A) lower than the natural frequency   <-- CORRECT
    (B) higher than natural frequency
    (C) Different as natural frequency
    (D) same as natural frequency
    Answer: A
    Explanation: The damped natural frequency f_d is related to the natural frequency f_n by a relation 
$f_d =f_n \sqrt {1-\zeta^2} $ 
where damping ratio $ \zeta $ = 1 
so f_d is less than the natural frequency

Q9. In forced oscillations apart from acceleration forces, damping and spring forces there is:
    (A) Centripetal attractive force
    (B) Restoring force
    (C) The gravitational force acting in the line of action
    (D) An external exciting force that changes the energy   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The free damping oscillations contain the forces as acceleration forces, damping, and spring forces, But in the case of forced oscillations, there is an external force that acts to maintain the vibrations that usually get dumped due to presence of the damping forces like friction, air drag, etc. The presence of the force promotes Total Mechanical Energy relations.

Q10. At resonance conditions when the frequency of the forced vibration matches the natural frequency of the system, amplitude ________ the natural frequency of the oscillator.
    (A) increases when the driving force is close to   <-- CORRECT
    (B) decreases when the driving force is close to
    (C) oscillates when the driving force is close to
    (D) increases when the driving force is far from
    Answer: A
    Explanation: When the mass and spring have no external force acting on them they transfer energy back and forth at a rate equal to the natural frequency. In other words, to efficiently pump energy into both mass and spring requires that the energy source feed the energy in at a rate equal to the natural frequency. Applying a force to the mass and spring is similar to pushing a child on a swing, a push is needed at the correct moment to make the swing get higher and higher. As in the case of the swing, the force applied need not be high to get large motions, but must just add energy to the system.
The damper, instead of storing energy, dissipates energy. Since the damping force is proportional to the velocity, the more the motion, the more the damper dissipates the energy. Therefore, there is a point when the energy dissipated by the damper equals the energy added by the force. At this point, the system has reached its maximum amplitude and will continue to vibrate at this level as long as the force applied stays the same. If no damping exists, there is nothing to dissipate the energy, and, theoretically, the motion will continue to grow into infinity.

Q11. What is constant in simple harmonic motion?
    (A) Time period   <-- CORRECT
    (B) Potential energy
    (C) Kinetic motion
    (D) Restoring force
    Answer: A
    Explanation: The time period of the SHM is given by T = $2\pi\sqrt{m\over k}$ where 'm' be the mass of the body (constant), 'k' restoring force constant as T depends on 'm' and 'k' and they are constant for the system, so the corresponding Time period of the motion is Constant.

Q12. A particle of mass 10 g is executing simple harmonic motion with an amplitude of 0.5 m and a periodic time of $\frac \pi 5 $ seconds. The maximum value of the force acting on the particle is:
    (A) 5 N
    (B) - 5N
    (C) 0.5 N
    (D) -0.5 N   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Given, T = $\frac \pi5 $s
amplitude, x = 0.5 m
mass , m = 10g = 0.01kg ,as we know that:
$T= 2\pi \sqrt {\frac m k}$
${\pi\over 5}= 2\pi\sqrt{\frac {0.01}{ k}}$
or k = 1 N/m
The maximum force is obtained at the maximum displacement i.e. amplitude 
F = ‚àíkx 
hence, F = ‚àí1(0.5) = ‚àí0.5N
The negative value of force shows the attractive nature.

Q13. A body of mass 5 g is executing simple harmonic motion about a point O with an amplitude of 10 cm. Its maximum velocity is 100 cm/s. Its velocity will be 50 cm/s at a distance (in cm) from O.
    (A) 10$\sqrt 2 $
    (B) 10$\sqrt 3 $
    (C) 5$\sqrt 2 $
    (D) 5$\sqrt3$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: $ v=\omega \sqrt{A^2-x^2} $
$ 50=10 \sqrt{10^2-x^2} $
$ 5=\sqrt{100-x^2} $
$ 25=100-x^2 $
$ x^2=75 $
$ x=\sqrt{75}=\sqrt{25 \times 3}=5 \sqrt{3} $

Q14. The maximum velocity and maximum acceleration of a body moving in a simple harmonic oscillation are 2 m/s and 4 m/s^2. The angular velocity is:
    (A) 2 rad/sec   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 4 rad/sec
    (C) 1 rad/sec
    (D) 5 rad/sec
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Given that :-v_max = a$\omega$ = 2m/s
a_max = a$\omega^2$_^ =_^ 4m/s^2
On dividing the two, we get $\omega$= 2 radians/sec

Q15. A particle is executing simple harmonic motion with an amplitude of 0.02 meter and frequency 50 hertz. The maximum acceleration of the particle is:
    (A) 100 m/s^2
    (B) 100 $\pi $ m/s^2
    (C) 200$\pi^2$ m/s^2   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 200 $\pi $ m/s^2
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Given frequency f = 50 Hz 
Thus w = 2$\pi$f = 100$\pi$
Acceleration
a = w^2x at max displacement x = A
a = (100$\pi$)^2 $\times$ 0.02
a = 200$\pi ^2$m/s^2

Q1. The angular velocities of three bodies in simple harmonic motion are ${{\rm{\omega }}_1}$, ${{\rm{\omega }}_2}$, ${{\rm{\omega }}_3}$ with the respective amplitudes as ${A_1}$ ${A_2}$ ${A_3}$ . If all the three bodies have the same mass and velocity, then
    (A) ${{\rm{A}}_{\rm{1}}}{\omega ^{\rm{2}}}_{\rm{1}}$ = ${{\rm{A}}^{\rm{2}}}_{\rm{2}}{\omega ^{\rm{2}}}_{{\rm{2}} = }{{\rm{A}}^{\rm{2}}}_{\rm{3}}{\omega ^{\rm{2}}}_{\rm{3}}$
    (B) ${{\rm{A}}^{\rm{2}}}_{\rm{1}}{\omega _{\rm{1}}}$ = ${{\rm{A}}^{\rm{2}}}_{\rm{2}}{\omega _{{\rm{2}} = }}{{\rm{A}}^{\rm{2}}}_{\rm{3}}{\omega _{\rm{3}}}$
    (C) ${{\rm{A}}_{\rm{1}}}{\omega _{\rm{1}}}$ = ${{\rm{A}}_{\rm{2}}}{\omega _{{\rm{2}} }=}{{\rm{A}}_{\rm{3}}}{\omega _{\rm{3}}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) ${{\rm{A}}^{\rm{2}}}_{\rm{1}}{\omega ^{\rm{2}}}_{\rm{1}}$ = ${{\rm{A}}^{\rm{2}}}_{\rm{2}}{\omega ^{\rm{2}}}_{{\rm{2}} {=} }{{\rm{A}}^{\rm{2}}}_{\rm{3}}{\omega ^{\rm{2}}}_{\rm{3}}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The velocity of a body performing SHM is given by: v = œâ$\sqrt {A^2-x^2}$
Maximum velocity is achieved when x=0.
All three bodies are at maximum velocity. Therefore, we can say that x=0 for all these bodies. 
Maximum velocity, Vmax‚Äã = Aœâ
Velocities are same so that A_1œâ_1 = A_2‚Äãœâ_2 ‚Äã= A_3‚Äãœâ_3

Q2. The kinetic energy of a body executing S.H.M. is $\frac 1 3$ of the potential energy. Then, the displacement of the body is x percent of the amplitude, where x is:
    (A) 86.5%   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 50%
    (C) 33.5%
    (D) 67%
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Given kinetic energy = $\frac 1 3$ potential energy
$\frac{1}{2} m(A \times \omega \times \cos \omega t)^{2}$ = $\frac{1}{3} \times \frac{1}{2} \times m \times(A \times \omega \times \sin \omega t)^{2}$
$\tan (\omega t)=\sqrt{3}$
$\omega t=\frac{\pi}{3}$
x = Asin($\omega$t)
x = $A \sin \left(\frac{\pi}{3}\right)$
x = $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} A$
for the percentage
$\frac{\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2} A}{{A}} \times 100$
= 86.5%

Q3. The total energy of a particle, executing simple harmonic motion is:
    (A) independent of x   <-- CORRECT
    (B) $\propto { x^2}$
    (C) $\propto \frac{x}{2}$
    (D) $\propto {x}$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: x= A cos( $\omega t+\phi$)
E = KE + PE
$PE=\frac{1}{2}kA^2cos^2(\omega t+\phi)$ and KE= $\frac{1}{2}kA^2sin^2(\omega t+\phi)$
$E=\frac{1}{2}kA^2$ $(sin^2(\omega t+\phi)+cos^2(\omega t+\phi))$ = $\frac{1}{2}$kA^2 Therefore, total energy is independent of x. 
Thus the total energy of the oscillator remains constant as displacement is regained after every half cycle.
no energy is dissipated then all the potential energy becomes kinetic and vice versa.
The figure below shows the variation of kinetic energy and potential energy of harmonic oscillator with time where phase œÜ is set to zero for simplicity

Q4. A spring with spring constant k, when stretched through 1 cm, has a potential energy U. If it is stretched by 4 cm, the potential energy will become:
    (A) 32U
    (B) 8U
    (C) 4U
    (D) 16U   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The potential energy of a spring is given by
$U=\frac12kx^2$
so U $\propto$ x^2
thus for x = 4x
U become 16 U

Q5. For a particle executing S.H.M having amplitude A, the speed of the particle is one-half of its maximum speed when its displacement from the mean position is:
    (A) x = $\frac{\sqrt3}2A$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) x = $\frac A2$
    (C) x = 2A
    (D) x = $\sqrt3A$
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Given velocity is one half of its max. velocity thus using relation
$v=\omega \sqrt{A^{2}-x^{2}}$
as v_max = $\omega$A
Put v = $\frac{\omega A}{2}$
$\frac{\omega A}{2}=\omega \sqrt{A^{2}-x^{2}}$
$\frac{\omega^{2} A^{2}}{4}=\omega^{2} A^{2}-\omega^{2} x^{2}$
on solving x = $\frac{\sqrt{3}}{2}A$

Q6. The time period of a spring oscillating simple harmonically is:
    (A) $\mathrm{T}=2 \pi \sqrt{\left\{\frac{K}{m}\right\}}$
    (B) $\mathrm{T}=2 \pi \sqrt{\left\{\frac{m}{K}\right\}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\mathrm{T}=2 \pi \sqrt{\left\{\frac{m}{2 K}\right\}}$
    (D) $T=2 \pi \sqrt{\left\{\frac{2 m}{K}\right\}}$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The time period is given by
$T=\frac{2 \pi}{\omega}$
 where $\omega=\sqrt{\frac{k}{m}}$
thus $\mathrm{T}=2 \pi \sqrt{\left\{\frac{m}{K}\right\}}$

Q7. A spring has a certain mass suspended from it and its period for vertical oscillations is T_1. The spring is now cut into two equal halves and the same mass is suspended from one of the halves. The period of vertical oscillations is now T_2. The ratio of $\frac {T_1}{T_2}$ is:
    (A) 2$\sqrt 2 $
    (B) 1   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\sqrt 2 $
    (D) $\frac 1 2$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: As the spring constant $k={\frac F x}$ i.e. restoring force per unit displacement remains the same thus time period in both cases will be same. Thus their ratio will be equal to 1.

Q8. A simple spring has length l and forces constant K. It is cut into two springs of lengths l_1 and l_2 such that l_1 = n.l_2 (where n is an integer), the force constant of the spring of length l_2 is
    (A) $\frac{K}{(n+1)}$
    (B) $\frac{K}{n(1+n)}$
    (C) $\frac{2 K}{(n+1)}$
    (D) K (1 + n)   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: As spring constant is inversely proportional to the length of spring L i.e. 
$k \propto \frac{1}{L} $ $\rightarrow$ (1)
given L_1 = nL_2 
L_1 + L_2 = L $\Rightarrow$ nL_2 + L_2 = L
$L_{2}=\frac{L}{n+1}$
thus using equation (1)
$\frac{K}{K_{2}}=\frac{\frac{L}{n+1}}{L}$
K_2 = (n + 1)K

Q9. In the arrangement shown in the figure, if the block of mass m is displaced, the frequency is given by:
    (A) f = $\frac{1}{2 \pi} \sqrt{\frac{\left(k_1-k_2\right)}{m}}$
    (B) f = $\frac1{2\pi}\sqrt{\frac{k_1+k_2}m}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) f = $\frac1{2\pi}$ ‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã$\sqrt {\left[ {({k_1} - {k_2})m} \right]} $
    (D) f = $\frac1{2\pi}$$\sqrt {\left[ {({k_1} - {k_2})m} \right]} $
    Answer: B
    Explanation: When the block is displaced slightly the restoring force acting on it in the same direction will be 
k_1x and k_2x
thus its equation of motion will be 
m$\frac{d^2x}{dt^2}$ = k_1x + k_2x $\rightarrow$ (1)
thus compare with the standard equation
$\frac{d^2x}{dt^2}$ = $\omega^2$x $\rightarrow$ (2) where $\omega$ = $\sqrt{\frac{k}{m}}$
on compare (1) and (2)
$\omega$ = $\sqrt{\frac{k_1 + k_2}{m}}$
f = $\frac{\omega}{2\pi}$ = $\frac{1}{2\pi}$$\sqrt{\frac{k_1 + k_2}{m}}$

Q10. Two pendulums of length I meter and 16 meters start vibrating one behind the other from the same stand. At some instant, the two are in the mean position in the same phase. The time period of the shorter pendulum is T. The minimum time after which the two threads of the pendulums will be one behind the other is:
    (A) $\frac{2T}{5}$
    (B) $\frac{T}{4}$
    (C) $\frac{4T}{3}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{T}{3}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Given the length of 1st pendulum L_1 = 1 cm and that of 2nd pendulum L_2 = 16 cm as the time period is given by
T = $2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{L}{g}}$
the time period of 1st pendulum T
the time period of 2nd pendulum will be
T_2 = 4T
When two pendulums become again one behind the other the phase difference between them again becomes 2$\pi$.
$\left(\omega_{1} t-\omega_{2} t\right)=2 \pi$
$\left(\frac{2\pi}T-\frac{2\pi}{4T}\right)t=2\pi$
$\left(\frac{2\pi}{T_1}\;-\frac{2\pi}{T_2}\right)t=2\pi$
$t=\frac{4 T}{3}$

Q11. For all practical purposes, the motion of a simple pendulum is SHM:
    (A) Only if the maximum angle which the string makes with the vertical is less than I.
    (B) Only if the length of its string is at least one metre.
    (C) Only if the maximum angle which its string makes with the vertical is less than 342.
    (D) None of these   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The motion of a simple pendulum is Simple Harmonic Motion(SHM) if a restoring force is proportional to the displacement, a relationship often known as Hooke's Law when applied to springs.

Q12. A rubber ball with water, having a small hole in its bottom is used as the bob of a simple pendulum. The time period of such a pendulum:
    (A) First increases and then decreases finally having the same value as at the beginning   <-- CORRECT
    (B) Increases with time
    (C) Is constant
    (D) Decreases with time
    Answer: A
    Explanation: First increases and then decreases finally having the same value as at the beginning

Q13. A mass M is suspended from a spring of negligible mass. The spring is pulled a little and then released so that the mass executes simple harmonic oscillations with a time period T.
    (A) T = $\begin{array}{l}4\pi\sqrt{\frac mk}\\\end{array}$
    (B) T = $8\pi\sqrt[{}]{\frac mk}$
    (C) T = $\begin{array}{l}2\pi\sqrt{\frac mk}\\\end{array}$   <-- CORRECT
    (D) T = $10\pi\sqrt[{}]{\frac mk}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The time period is defined as
$T=\frac{2 \pi}{\omega}$
and $\omega = \sqrt{\frac{k}{m}}$
thus $T=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{m}{k}}$

Q14. The bob of a simple pendulum has a mass of 20 g. The period of oscillations of the pendulum is 0.75 s. When the mass of bob is increased to 40 g, without any other change, the time period now becomes:
    (A) 1.5 s
    (B) 0.75 $\sqrt 2 $
    (C) 0.75 s   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $\frac{0.75}{\sqrt{2}}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: The time period of motion of the pendulum is independent of its mass.
$\mathrm{T}=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{L}{g}}$

Q15. A pendulum suspended from a ceiling of a train has a period T when the train is at rest. When the train is accelerating with a uniform acceleration Œ±, the period of oscillation will:
    (A) Remain unaffected
    (B) Increase
    (C) Become infinite
    (D) Decrease   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Due to the acceleration of train the effective acceleration become 
g_eff = $\sqrt{a^2+g^2}$
thus time period become
T = $2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{L}{g_{e f f}}}$
T = $2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{L}{\sqrt{a^{2}+g^{2}}}}$
thus the time period will decrease as compared to the rest state of the train.

Q1. If the length of a simple pendulum is increased by 1percent, then the new time-period:
    (A) Increases by 2.0 percent
    (B) Increases by 0.5 percent   <-- CORRECT
    (C) Increases by 1 percent
    (D) Decreases by 0.5 percent
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The time period of a simple pendulum is given by
T = $2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{L}{g}}$
new length L_1 = $L+\frac{L}{100}$
$L_{1}=\frac{101}{100} L$
$T_{1}=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{L_{1}}{g}}$
$T_{1}=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{\frac{101}{100} L}{L}}$
T_1 = 1.004988 T
change = $\frac{T_{1}-T}{T} \times 100$
change = $\frac{1.004988-1}{1} \times 100$
change = 0.4988%
Thus it increases by 0.5%

Q2. Two pendulums have time periods T and $5T\over4$. They are in phase at their mean positions at some instant of time. What will be their phase difference when the bigger pendulum completes one oscillation?
    (A) 30^o
    (B) 90^o   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 45^o
    (D) 60^o
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Assuming bigger pendulum as the pendulum of bigger time period i.e.having time period $5T\over4$ 
As the two pendulums start at the same time in simple harmonic motion from the mean position, their initial phase angles are zero
The bigger pendulum completes its one oscillation in time $t={5T\over 4}$ and comes back to the mean position. During this time the point on reference circle associated with SHM rotates by an angle 2$\pi$
If œâ represents the angular velocity of the reference point associated with SHM of the first pendulum of time period T, then the phase of this pendulum after $5T\over 4$ s will be
${\omega5T \over 4}=\omega T +{\omega T\over 4} $ 
${5T\over 4} = T+{T \over 4}$
This means that when the second pendulum completes 1 oscillation the first one completes 1+1/4
The first pendulum completes extra 1/4‚Äã oscillation.
Hence the phase difference = $\pi\over 2$ = 90^o

Q3. A particle is executing S.H.M of amplitude A with a time period T sec. The time taken by it to move from a positive extreme position to half the amplitude is:
    (A) $\frac{6 T}{12}$ sec
    (B) $\frac{T}{6}$ sec   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac{T}{12}$ sec
    (D) $\frac{3 T}{12}$ sec
    Answer: B
    Explanation: In this case, equation of motion will be given by
x = A cos($\omega$t)
time taken for x = 0 to x = $\frac{A}{2}$
using $\omega$ = $\frac{2\pi}{T}$
$\frac{A}{2}=A \cos \left(\frac{2 \pi}{T} \times T_1\right)$
$\frac{1}{2}=\cos \left(\frac{2 \pi T_{1}}{T}\right)$
$\frac{\pi}{3}=\frac{2 \pi T_{1}}{T}$
T_1 = $\frac{T}{6}$

Q4. Time period of a simple pendulum will be doubled, if we
    (A) decrease the length 4 times
    (B) decrease the length 2 times
    (C) increase the length 2 times
    (D) increase the length 4 times   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: When the time period is doubled length has to become 4 times as it is coming in square root.
T = $2\pi\sqrt{\frac Lg}$

Q5. A mass M is suspended by two springs which are connected in series. If the spring constants are K_1 and K_2 then the time period of the vertical vibrations is:
    (A) T = $2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{M\left(k_1 k_2\right)}{\left(k_1+k_2\right)}}$
    (B) T = $2\pi \sqrt{\frac{M\left(k_1+k_2\right)}{\left(k_1 k_2\right)}}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) T = $2 \pi \sqrt{\left\{\frac{M}{\left(k_1+k_2\right)}\right\}}$
    (D) T = $2 \pi \sqrt{\left\{\frac{M}{\left(k_1-k_2\right)}\right\}}$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Effective spring constant when two springs are connected in series is given by
$\frac{1}{k_{e q}}=\frac{1}{k_{1}}+\frac{1}{k_{2}}$
$k_{e q}=\frac{k_{1} k_{2}}{k_{1}+k_{2}}$
time period T = 2$\pi$$\sqrt{\frac{m}{k_{e q}}}$
T = $ 2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{m\left(k_{1}+k_{2}\right)}{k_{1} k_{2}}}$

Q6. Whose motion is an ideal case of SHM?
    (A) A simple pendulum with very Large angle of swing
    (B) An electric dipole placed stably in a uniform electric field and then given a very small angular displacement   <-- CORRECT
    (C) A simple pendulum with very small angle of swing
    (D) Shadow of a point mass having uniform circular motion, on the vertical diameter
    Answer: B
    Explanation: When an electric dipole will pe placed in an electric field and given a small angular displacement it will oscillate about its mid point due to different line of forces that give rise to torque.

Q7. A particle executes SHM of time period T, the time period with which the total energy changes is:
    (A) T/2
    (B) 2T
    (C) Independent   <-- CORRECT
    (D) T
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Total mechanical energy E does not depend on time period T. It is given by
E = $\frac12m\omega^2A^2$

Q8. The length of a second‚Äôs pendulum decreases by 0.1 percent, then the clock:
    (A) Loses 30 seconds per day
    (B) Loses 37.8 seconds per day
    (C) Gains 43.2 seconds per day   <-- CORRECT
    (D) Loses 13.5 seconds per day
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Initial time period T= 2 s
newlengthL‚Ä≤ = L - $L\over 1000$
$L^{\prime}=\frac{999}{1000} L$
new time period T‚Ä≤ = $2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{L^{\prime}}{g}}$
$T^{\prime}=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{\frac{999 L}{1000}}{g}}$
T‚Ä≤ = 0.9995T
In one day there are 86400 seconds
After reducing length new time will be
0.9995 $\times$ 86400 = 86356.8
Thus change is $\Delta$T = 86400 - 86356.8= 43.2s
Thus clock gains 43.2 s per day

Q9. A ball of mass 50 gm is suspended by a spring with a force constant K = 49 N/m. The ball is raised to the position at which the spring is not tensioned and is released without a push. The ball will fall through a maximum distance of:
    (A) 0.141 m   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 0.032 m
    (C) 0.042 m
    (D) 0.029 m
    Answer: A
    Explanation: At maximum stretching 
mgx = 0+ $\frac12$kx^2
x = $\frac{2mg}k=\frac{2\times50\times9.8}{49\times1000}$
x = $\frac2{100}$
x = 0.02m

Q10. A cylinder floats vertically in a tank full of water. The period of oscillation of the cylinder when depressed and released is dependent on:
    (A) Density of the liquid
    (B) The area of cross-section of the cylinder
    (C) The mass of the cylinder
    (D) Length of cylinder immersed in the liquid at equilibrium   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Consider a cylinder of length L and density $\rho$ floating in a liquid of density $\sigma$ . Let the cylinder have a cross-sectional area A and let a length h be below the surface when the cylinder is at rest. (See Figure 1)
According to the law of floatation, weight of the body = weight of liquid displaced 
 AL$\rho g$ = Ahag
 $l$ =$ha\over\rho$
The cylinder is now pushed downwards a little (x) and allowed to bob up and down, the forces causing the oscillation being gravity and the varying up-thrust on the cylinder.
Extra up-thrust = extra weight of liquid displaced $ = A\sigma gx$
Therefore restoring force $ = A\sigma gx$ = ma
Acceleration (a) = $ - \frac{{A\sigma gx}}{m} = - \frac{{A\sigma gx}}{{A\rho L}} = - \left[ {\frac{{\sigma g}}{{\rho L}}} \right]x$
The acceleration is therefore directly proportional to the displacement (x) and so the cylinder, therefore, moves with simple harmonic motion.
The value of œâ^2 for this system is œÉg/œÅL so the period T is:
$T = \frac{{2\pi }}{\omega } = 2\pi \sqrt {\frac{h}{g}} $= $2\pi\sqrt {\rho L\over\sigma g}$
thus $T\propto \sqrt h $

Q11. A particle is oscillating according to the equation X = 7 cos 0.5 $\pi $t, where t is in seconds. The point moves from the position of equilibrium to maximum displacement in time
    (A) 4.0 seconds
    (B) 1.0 seconds   <-- CORRECT
    (C) 0.5 seconds
    (D) 2.0 seconds
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Given x = 7 cos (0.5$\pi$t)
Comparing it with standard equation x = A cos($\omega$t)
$\omega$ = 0.5$\pi$ 
Thus, $ T=\frac{2\pi}\omega=\frac{2\pi}{0.5\pi}$ = 4 sec
thus the time taken for movement from the position of equilibrium to maximum displacement is, t = 4/4 = 1.0 sec

Q12. A weightless spring that has a force constant k oscillates with frequency n when a mass m is suspended from it. The spring is cut into two equal halves and a mass 2m is suspended from it. The frequency of oscillation will now become:
    (A) $\frac{n}{\sqrt{2}}$
    (B) n   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac{2}{\sqrt{2}}$
    (D) 2n
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Frequency of oscillation is given by
$n=\frac{1}{2 \pi} \sqrt{\frac{k}{m}}$
the spring constant is inversely proportional to the length
$k \propto \frac{1}{L}$ 
Thus if the length is halved spring constant will be doubled. Also, mass is also doubled
n' = $\frac{1}{2 \pi} \sqrt{\frac{2 k}{2 m}}$
$n'=\frac1{2\pi}\sqrt{\frac km}$
n' = n

Q13. The displacement of a particle executing S.H.M is given by y = 0.25 sin 200 t cm. The maximum speed of the particle is:
    (A) 50 cm/sec   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 200 cm/sec
    (C) 0.25 cm/sec
    (D) 100 cm/sec
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Given y = 0.25 sin(200t) 
compare this equation with the standard equation
y = Asin($\omega$t)
amplitude A = 0.25cm
$\omega$ = 200
v_max = $\omega$A
v_max = 200 $\times$ 0.25
v_max = 50cm/s

Q14. Which of the following equations does not represent simple harmonic motion:
    (A) Y = A sin $\omega $t + Bcos $\omega $t
    (B) Y = A sin $\omega $ t ‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã
    (C) Y = A sin $\omega $ t cos $\omega $ t
    (D) Y= A sin^2 $\omega $ t   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: Because this is not a periodic function.

Q15. The vertical extension in a light spring by a weight of 1 kg suspended from the wire is 9.8 cm. The period of oscillation is:
    (A) $\frac{2\pi}7$ sec‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã
    (B) $\frac{2\pi}{10}$ sec‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac{2\pi}8$sec
    (D) $\frac{2\pi}9$ sec‚Äã
    Answer: B
    Explanation: At equilibrium 
mg = kx
$k=\frac{1\times9.8\times100}{9.8}$
k = 100N/m^2
$T=2\pi\sqrt{\frac mk}$
$T=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{1}{100}}$
$T=\frac{2\pi}{10}\;$second

Q1. The maximum displacement of a particle executing S.H.M is 1 cm and the maximum acceleration is (1.57)^2 cm/sec, then the time period is:
    (A) 4.00 sec   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 1.57$^{\rm{2}}$ sec
    (C) 1.57 sec
    (D) 0.25 sec
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Maximum acceleration
$a_{\max }={\omega}^{2} A$
(1.57)^2 = $\omega^2 $ $\times$ 1
$\omega$ = 1.57
T = $\frac{2\pi}\omega=\frac{2\times3.14}{1.57}$
T = 4second

Q2. A body is executing S.H.M When its displacement from the mean position is 4 cm and 5 cm, the corresponding velocities of the body are 10 cm per sec and 8 cm per sec. Then the time period of the body is:
    (A) 4$\pi $ ‚Äã‚Äã‚Äãsec
    (B) $\pi $ sec   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac{3 \pi}{2}$ sec
    (D) 2 $\pi $ sec
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Given
x_1 = 4cm
x_2 = 5cm
v_1 = 10cm/s
v_2 = 8cm/s
Using relation
$v=\omega\sqrt{A^2-x^2}$
$10=\omega\sqrt{A^2-(4)^2}$
8 = $\omega\sqrt{A^2\;-\;\left(5\right)^2}$ 
Square and subtract these equations
36 = 9$\omega^2$
$\omega$ = 2
time period T = $\frac{2 \pi}{\omega}=\frac{2 \pi}{2}$
T = $\pi$seconds

Q3. The ratio of maximum acceleration to the maximum velocity of a particle performing S.H.M is equal to:
    (A) Square of amplitude
    (B) Amplitude
    (C) Angular velocity   <-- CORRECT
    (D) Square of angular velocity
    Answer: C
    Explanation: a_max = $\omega^2$A
v_max = $\omega$A
thus
$\frac{a_{\max }}{v_{\max }}=\frac{\omega^{2} A}{\omega A}$
$\Rightarrow$ $\omega$

Q4. A particle is executing S.H.M with an amplitude of 4 cm and a time period 12 sec. The time taken by the particle in going from its mean position to a position of displacement equal to 2 cm is T_1. The time taken from this displaced position of 2 cm to reach the extreme position is T_2. Therefore, $\frac {T_1}{T_2}$will be
    (A) 1
    (B) $\frac 1 3$
    (C) 2
    (D) 0.5   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: In s.h.m position of a body is given by
x = A sin ($\omega$t + $\Delta$) where $\Delta$ is the phase difference
given T = 12s and A = 4cm
$\omega=\frac{2 \pi}{T}=\frac{2 \pi}{12}=\frac{\pi}{6}$
at mean position $\Delta$ = 0
thus for interval x = 0 to t = 2
2 = $4 \sin \left(\frac{\pi}{6} T_{1}\right)$
$\frac{1}{2}=\sin \left(\frac{\pi}{6} T_{1}\right)$
$\frac{\pi}{6}=\frac{\pi T_{1}}{6}$
T_1 = 1 seconds
For interval x = 2 to x = 4 phase difference
$\phi=\frac{\pi}{6}$
4 = $4 \sin \left(\omega T_{2}+\phi\right)$
$1=\sin \left(\frac{\pi}{6} T_{2}+\frac{\pi}{6}\right)$
$\frac{\pi}{2}=\frac{\pi}{6} T_{2}+\frac{\pi}{6}$
T_2 = 2 seconds
$\frac{T_1}{T_2}=\frac12$ = 0.5

Q5. If the period of oscillation of mass M suspended from a spring is one second, then the period of mass 4 M will be:
    (A) 4s
    (B) 2s   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $\frac12$s
    (D) $\frac14$s
    Answer: B
    Explanation: The relation for the time period is
$T=2\pi\sqrt{\frac mk}$
for constant k 
$T \propto \sqrt{m}$
$\frac{T_{1}}{T_{2}}=\frac{\sqrt{m_{1}}}{\sqrt{m_{2}}}$
given m_2 = 4m and T_1 = 1s
$\frac{1}{T_{2}}=\frac{\sqrt{m}}{\sqrt{4 m}}$
T_2 = 2second

Q6. The length of the second‚Äôs pendulum on the surface of the earth is 1m. The length of the second‚Äôs pendulum on the surface of the moon, where g is $\frac 1 6$th the value on earth is:
    (A) 6m
    (B) 36m
    (C) $\frac {1} {36}$m
    (D) $\frac 1 6$m   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: The time period of a second pendulum is 1 s. The relation for the time period is
$T=2\pi\sqrt{\frac Lg}$
if T remains constant 
L $\propto$ g
$\frac{L_{\text {earth }}}{L_{\text {moon }}}=\frac{g_{\text {earth }}}{g_{\text {moon }}}$
given L_earth = 1 m
$\frac{1}{L_{\text {moon }}}=\frac{g}{\frac{g}{6}}$
L_moon = $\frac{1}{6} $m

Q7. If the maximum velocity and acceleration of a particle executing S.H.M are equal in magnitude, the time period will be:
    (A) 12.56 s
    (B) 3.14 s
    (C) 6.28 s   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 1.57 s
    Answer: C
    Explanation: v_max = $\omega$A
a_max = $\omega^2$A
given they are equal
$\omega^2$A = $\omega$A
$\Rightarrow$$\omega$ = 1
time period
T = $2\pi \over \omega$
T = 2 $\times$ 3.14
T = 6.28s

Q8. A particle executes S.H.M along a straight line with amplitude ‚ÄòA‚Äô. The potential energy is maximum when the displacement is:
    (A) Zero
    (B) $ \pm $ A$\sqrt 2 $
    (C) $ \pm $ A   <-- CORRECT
    (D) $ \pm {A\over2}$
    Answer: C
    Explanation: Potential energy in s.h.m. is given by
$U=\;\frac12kx^2$
Maximum possible values of x is equal to amplitude $\pm$A. I.e. maximum displacement on both sides. Thus maximum P.E. is when x = $\pm$A.

Q9. The potential energy of a particle with displacement x is V(x). The motion is simple harmonic when (k is a positive constant):
    (A) V = kx^2
    (B) V = k
    (C) V = kx
    (D) V = k$\frac{x^2}{2}$   <-- CORRECT
    Answer: D
    Explanation: In s.h.m. equation of motion is F = -kx thus work done by force F during a displacement from x to x + dx is 
dW = F $\times$ dx
Total work done in a displacement from x = 0 to x is
$W=\int_\limits0^x\;\left(-kx\right)dx$
W = $-\frac12kx^2$
thus change in P.E.
U(0) ‚àí U(x) = W
let U(0) = 0
then U(x) = $\frac{1}{2}$kx^2
P.E = W = $\frac{1}{2}$kx^2
‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚Äã‚ÄãP.E. will be maximum when x = $\pm$A i.e. amplitude of motion.
And will be zero at x = 0 i.e. at the mean position. Thus motion will be s.h.m.

Q10. If x, F, and V denote the displacement, force acting, and the potential energy of a particle then F = ?
    (A) $\frac{1 d v}{x d x}$
    (B) $\frac{-d V}{d x}$   <-- CORRECT
    (C) + $\frac{d V}{d x}$
    (D) Vx
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Potential energy V is given by
$V=\frac{1}{2} k x^2$
and force f = -kx
thus f = $-\frac{d V}{d x}$
f = $-\frac{d}{d x}\left(\frac{1}{2} k x^2\right)$
f = -kx
Hence proof.

Q11. What is the ratio between the height H of a mountain and the depth h of a mine if a pendulum swings with the same period at the top of the mountain and at the bottom of the mine?
    (A) $\frac12$   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 4
    (C) 6.0
    (D) 1
    Answer: A
    Explanation: Gravity above the earth surface is given by 
g = $g_0(1-\frac{2H}R)$ 
And below the earth surface is given by
g = $g_0(1-\frac hR)$ 
Where h and d are height and depth below the earth's surface. As given time period at both location is same 
T_1 = T_2
$2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{L}{g_{u p}}}=2 \pi \sqrt{\frac{L}{g_{d a c m}}}$
$\Rightarrow$ g_up = g_down
$g_{0}\left(1-\frac{2 H}{R}\right)=g_{0}\left(1-\frac{h}{R}\right)$
$\Rightarrow$ 2H = h
$\frac{H}{h}=\frac{1}{2}$

Q12. The time period of a simple pendulum of length L at a place where the acceleration due to gravity is g is T. What is the period of a simple pendulum of the same length at a place where the acceleration due to gravity is 1.02 g.
    (A) 0.99T   <-- CORRECT
    (B) 1.02T
    (C) T
    (D) 1.01T
    Answer: A
    Explanation: The relation for time period of a pendulum is 
$T=2\pi\sqrt{\frac Lg}$
thus T $\propto$ $\frac{1}{\sqrt{g}}$
so $\frac{T_{1}}{T_{2}}=\frac{\sqrt{g_{2}}}{\sqrt{g_{1}}} \Rightarrow \frac{T}{T_{2}}=\frac{\sqrt{1.02 g}}{\sqrt{g}}$
$T_{2}=\frac{T}{\sqrt{1.02}}$
T_2 = 0.99T

Q13. A particle executes S.H.M with a period of 6 seconds and amplitude 3 cm. Its maximum speed in cm/sec is:
    (A) $3\pi $
    (B) $\pi $   <-- CORRECT
    (C) $2\pi $
    (D) $\pi \over2$
    Answer: B
    Explanation: Given time period T = 6s and amplitude A = 3 cm/s
Using relation
angular frequency 
$\omega=\frac{2 \pi}{T}$
$\omega=\frac{2 \pi}{6}$
v_max = $\omega\times A$
v_max = $\frac{2 \pi}{6} \times 3$
v_max = $\pi$ cm/s

Q14. Which one of the following equations does not represent S.H.M?
    (A) Y= A sin $\omega $ t + B cos $\omega $ t
    (B) Y = A sin $\omega $ t
    (C) Y= A sec $\omega $ t + B cosec $\omega $ t   <-- CORRECT
    (D) Y = A cos $\omega $ t + B
    Answer: C
    Explanation: simple harmonic motion is a type of periodic motion or oscillation motion where the restoring force is directly proportional to the displacement and acts in the direction opposite to that of displacement. We can only state SHM in terms of sine and cosine functions of the fixed time period.

Q15. A 0.5 kg weight attached to a light spring elongates it by 0.981 mm. The natural frequency of the system should be approximately ________. (the mass hangs from spring vertically).
    (A) 8 Hz
    (B) 36 Hz
    (C) 16 Hz   <-- CORRECT
    (D) 5 Hz
    Answer: C
    Explanation: When mass elongates the spring the restoring force must be equal to wt. Of block at equilibrium 
kx = mg
$k=\frac{0.5 \times 9.81}{0.981 \times 10^{-3}}$
k = 5 $\times$ 10^3N/m
frequency
$f=\frac{1}{2 \pi} \times \sqrt{\frac{k}{m}}$
$f=\frac{1}{2\times3.14} \times \sqrt{\frac{5 \times 10^{3}}{0.5}}$
f = 15.92 Hz $\cong$ 16 Hz
